Telecommunications (A Special Report): Keeping the Customer---Fighting the Fickle:New technology is helping phone companies battle one of ...
Telecommunications (A Special Report): Keeping the Customer --- Fighting the Fickle: New
technology is helping phone companies battle one of their biggest enemies: customer turnover
By Ross Snel
1,483 words
18 September 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
R36
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Churn.
Forget how to spell it: It's the telecommunications industry's four-letter word. It means lost customers. It
means high costs. And, as competition in the industry gets more intense, it's spreading.
The problem has long been a serious one for wireless and long-distance telephone companies. For instance,
nearly one-third of all wireless phone customers changed carriers at least once in 1998, says International
Data Corp., a technology market-research firm in Framingham, Mass. IDC expects defections to hit 57% in
2003, as wireless competition intensifies. IDC estimates that each lost customer cost wireless companies an
average of $676 in 1998.
Meanwhile, Yankee Group, a Boston research and consulting firm, figures 23.5% of long-distance customers
changed phone companies at least once last year.
But now churn is also starting to plague the Baby Bells as they begin competing for long-distance business
and lose their monopolies on local phone service.
"The way the Baby Bells compete is changing dramatically," says Jeff Kagan, an independent
telecommunications analyst in Atlanta. "They're about to get thrown into the same pool of churning customers
that long-distance companies have faced for years."
To battle the problem, some companies have begun paying more attention to their arsenals of customer data
-- working to amass better information about who their customers are and "mining" it in ways that help them
identify and keep the ones they consider most valuable.
Entailing sophisticated software and computer models, so-called data mining is neither simple nor particularly
cheap. But for some companies the payoff -- the ability to slice and dice data on customers in just about any
way conceivable -- is a powerful incentive.
Information from a database, for instance, can be fed into computer models, which apply either complex
algorithms or artificial intelligence to deliver predictions about consumer behavior or observations about
groups of customers. Which customers are most likely to switch to another carrier? The computer model
knows. Moreover, it can identify which of those customers are making the most phone calls at the highest
rates. That lets the company zero in on the most profitable customers, those it wants to keep. Let the churn
take the unprofitable ones to competing carriers.
To get a glimpse at how data mining can help reduce churn, consider the experience of Verizon Wireless and
one of its parents, Verizon Communications Inc., New York.
At Verizon Wireless, a joint venture of Verizon Communications and Britain's Vodafone Group, the attack on
churn can be as simple as making a phone call to a new customer. Almost as soon as new customers get
their first monthly bills, Verizon Wireless customer-service representatives are on the phone, asking how they
like the service.
More important, in that same call a Verizon representative asks what parts of the service a customer isn't
using, and why. If someone isn't yet using voice mail, for instance, the representative will offer to help get it
set up and working.
"Asking them whether they're happy, asking, `Is there anything I can do?' absolutely decreases the likelihood
they will defect," says Laura Knoop, vice president of marketing operations at Verizon Wireless.

--- Page 6 ---
Page 6 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In this example of data mining, Verizon Wireless uses a software program to search the company's database
and flag customers who have just received their first bills to get what the company calls "welcoming calls."
At the time of this call, company representatives already have a certain amount of information about the
customers -- such as which services they have signed up for. And by the end of the call the representatives
may have gathered even more information for the computerized files.
Ms. Knoop says one way to keep customers happy, and lessen the churn, is to use computer analyses to see
whether customers use calling plans that best suit their needs. In one example, Verizon Wireless has used
data-mining software to identify customers who use mobile phones heavily on weekends but use a calling
plan with relatively high weekend rates.
The company mails those customers promotions suggesting other calling plans better suited to their needs.
The computer prompts Verizon Wireless customer-service representatives to make such suggestions when
they are on the phone to customers, pulling up information on their accounts.
"We'll say, `We see here that because of your heavy use on weekends, this other plan might be better for
you,'" Ms. Knoop says. "That's a proactive way of getting in front of customers' needs."
The beauty of data-mining software in such applications, says Ms. Knoop, is that it identifies only those
customers who could benefit from switching plans so that the company doesn't waste money sending direct
mail to customers who wouldn't be interested.
"You have to go after those customers you feel are worth making that recommendation to," says Ms. Knoop.
"You need to understand who needs it the most. You have to make sure it's people who are using the phone
a lot."
Ms. Knoop declines to provide information on how much such programs have reduced churn, or what Verizon
Wireless's overall churn level is. She also won't say how much Verizon Wireless has invested in data-mining
technology and staff.
But one of its parents -- Verizon Communications -- is more open about its own commitments to data mining.
The former Bell Atlantic Corp., which merged with GTE Corp. to form Verizon Communications, paid $5
million over the past two years for new tools to reduce churn and improve the efficiency of marketing
campaigns, according to Carol Nieditch, who led Bell Atlantic's team in charge of data mining and related
marketing. Verizon Communications plans to spend a similar amount over the next year, says Ms. Nieditch,
who is now director of customer-relationship management at Verizon Communications.
So far, says Ms. Nieditch, the investments in data mining are working, increasing the return on investment for
a typical marketing campaign by 25%.
The company relied on SAS Institute Inc., Cary, N.C., for data-mining software; MicroStrategy Inc., Vienna,
Va., for software that compiles the results of promotional campaigns; and Exchange Applications, Boston, for
software that manages those campaigns. The company built its own mathematical models.
Verizon Communications has used its data-mining tools and expertise to identify service combinations that
will satisfy different segments of customers. For example, if a group of customers appear likely to go for a
combination of high-speed DSL service for home Internet use, local phone service and wireless service,
Verizon Communications builds a package that includes all of those services and then markets it to that
group of customers.
In one marketing campaign, the company aimed a "loyalty program" at customers who made lots of regional
calls, rewarding them with points similar to airline frequent-flier miles. Customers could use the points to buy
merchandise from stores near their homes.
The loyalty program wasn't aimed specifically at customers likely to churn. But, says Ms. Nieditch, results
showed that customers who had received the offer churned 23% less often than those who hadn't.
Now Verizon Communications is working on models designed to home in on customers most likely to defect
and make special offers to them. Ms. Nieditch expects that to reduce churn at least 10% to 15% initially.
Both Verizon Communications and Verizon Wireless say they are getting their money's worth out of data
mining and related marketing. But some outsiders think the industry, as a whole, has barely scratched the
surface so far.
"Data mining is the big untapped resource," says Mr. Kagan, the analyst in Atlanta. "It's the gold mine that
just hasn't been opened yet."

--- Page 7 ---
Page 7 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
One obstacle for data mining, Mr. Kagan says, is that too many telecommunications companies are
structured according to product lines. That makes it tough to put together joint marketing efforts aimed at
meeting multiple needs of a single customer.
According to Lightbridge Inc., a consulting firm in Burlington, Mass., some companies invest in data-mining
technology but don't make the most out of it because they are too focused on other concerns, such as
building up their networks.
A company, for instance, might not get around to using its data-mining technology to identify its biggest
customers or follow up with special efforts to safeguard them from churn, says Carla Marcinowski, vice
president of consulting services at Lightbridge. She emphasizes that the number of customer defections often
matters less than the kinds of customers who are leaving. "You can't retain everybody, Ms. Marcinowski says,
"but you can most certainly try to retain those that you need to keep."
---
Mr. Snel is a reporter for Dow Jones Newswires in New York.

More Figures Show a Slowing Economy---Retail and Jobless NumbersBack Idea That InflationIsn't an Imminent Threat
Economy
More Figures Show a Slowing Economy --- Retail and Jobless Numbers Back Idea That Inflation Isn't
an Imminent Threat
By Nicholas Kulish
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
769 words
15 September 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
WASHINGTON -- Back-to-school shoppers took it easy in August, according to fresh data that support the
view of a slowing economy and tame inflation.
Retail sales were slower than expected last month while wholesale prices fell. New claims for unemployment
benefits edged higher last week. Together, the three latest reports back up the notion that the economy is
headed for the much-heralded "soft landing" of sustainable growth and low inflation.
"This is an inflation performance worthy of a gold medal," said Ken Mayland, chief economist at ClearView
Economics in Pepper Pike, Ohio. "A good case can be made that this is something fundamentally cyclical,
and we're on the side of the cycle where the economy's decelerating."
Signs of slowing economic activity also showed up in the latest survey of small businesses by the National
Federation of Independent Business. The NFIB said yesterday that small businesses, which tend to react
more quickly than large businesses to changes in the economy, reported slower sales, earnings and capital
spending in August. About 35% of the respondents complained of having a hard time filling job openings, the
highest percentage in the survey's 26-year history. And companies are having a harder time raising prices:
About 19% of companies raised prices over the past three months, the lowest since January.
The survey also found that small business owners remain optimistic about the future. In fact, NFIB's index of
small-business optimism in August hit its highest level, at 101.9, since January and above the average of
101.2 in 1999. The NFIB is the largest U.S. small-business lobbying group, with more than 500,000
members.
The Federal Reserve raised interest rates six times in little more than a year to try to slow the economy's
breakneck pace, but a string of recent reports suggest their work may be done. Recent signs of a cooler
economy have prompted talk in financial markets that the Fed may change its policy bias from inflationary to
neutral.
Such talk is a bit premature, some say. "My judgment is that they will not [change the bias], because the
public face that the Fed wants seen is one of caution and conservatism," said Mr. Mayland.
The Commerce Department said retail sales rose a seasonally adjusted 0.2% in August from July levels. That
figure compares with increases of 0.9% in both June and July. A dip of 0.4% in auto sales helped put the
brakes on retail sales, while general-merchandise stores saw an increase of just 0.2%.
Producer prices fell at a seasonally adjusted rate of 0.2% over the same period, after a flat reading in July,
the Labor Department said. A sharp drop of 0.7% in food prices and a small dip of 0.2% in energy prices
contributed to the decrease.
Food and energy are both volatile components that are likely to shoot back up this month -- especially
considering high oil prices. But the core rate, which excludes food and energy, rose just 0.1% for the second
month in a row.
On a year-over-year basis, August retail sales rose a strong 7%, a more sustainable pace than the first
quarter when retail sales rose at an annual pace of 13.1%.
The employment picture also supports the view of long-awaited slowdown. Although weekly unemployment
claims typically are volatile, they have been moving steadily higher in recent weeks.

--- Page 9 ---
Page 9 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The Labor Department said initial claims for unemployment benefits rose 13,000 to 311,000 last week,
following a decline of 7,000 the week before. The four-week moving average, which smooths out weekly
fluctuations, rose 2,500 to 318,500. A year earlier, the four-week average was just 290,500. For the week
ended Sept. 2, continuing claims rose 81,000 to 2,245,000, and the four-week moving average rose 37,250 to
2,179,000.
---
Rodney Ho in Atlanta contributed to this article.

Fed Official Says Rates Could Soon Move Lower
Economy
Fed Official Says Rates Could Soon Move Lower
By Steve Liesman
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
640 words
12 September 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
CHICAGO -- One of the Federal Reserve's leading New Economy advocates said in an interview that
interest rates could soon start heading lower.
"Once we get over the energy hump, I wouldn't be surprised if inflation declines and interest rates are going
to come down," Robert McTeer, president of the Federal Reserve Bank of Dallas, said after his speech at the
National Association for Business Economics conference here.
He said market rates, such as the 10-year Treasury note, would be the first to fall. "The federal-funds rate
probably won't lead" market rates down but "eventually will follow."
Mr. McTeer's remarks suggest that, aside from support inside the central bank for ending the recent
rate-raising campaign, there is even some talk of moving to ease monetary policy. Some economists have
suggested that the next Fed move would be an easing but that it wouldn't happen for several months or even
well into next year.
Behind that talk of an easing are expectations that inflation will ease once oil prices, which rose $1.51
yesterday to $35.14 a barrel, begin to decline. The federal-funds rate, which is the rate banks charge each
other for overnight lending, is currently set at 6.5%. But the 10-year Treasury yield, despite rising Monday,
remains well below at 5.76%.
The recent overall drop in bond yields, along with stagnant gold prices and a strong dollar, suggests to Mr.
McTeer declining inflationary expectations in the market.
The real federal-funds rate, which subtracts inflation, is at an 11-year high of around 4.8%.
Still, that doesn't mean the Fed will be reversing course soon. Mr. McTeer has always been among the Fed's
leading interest-rate doves. When the Fed began raising rates in 1999, he twice opposed the moves. Each
time, he was the lone dissenter against tighter money because of his belief that higher productivity brought on
by technology has changed the economic fundamentals. "Technology has made growth less inflationary," he
said.
In his speech, he urged the Fed not to target growth rates or the unemployment rate because of surging
productivity gains. "With the limits to productivity acceleration uncertain, I think the most important thing to
avoid in conducting monetary policy is to avoid basing policy on real growth," he said.
"You shouldn't tighten monetary policy because growth is too fast or because the unemployment rate is too
low. You don't fight prosperity with monetary policy."
He said the Fed's one and only target should be inflation and it shouldn't act until it sees firm signs of rising
prices.
Mr. McTeer's term as a voting member of the Fed's rate-setting committee ended at the beginning of this year
when he was replaced by another of the Fed's 12 regional presidents.
Though he continues to sit in on monetary-policy sessions, he won't vote again on rates until 2002.
Mr. McTeer's views were echoed in the NABE Outlook, released yesterday, in which economists revised
upward their growth projections from their last report in May. The Outlook, a consensus forecast made by a
30-member panel, estimates that 2000 gross domestic product will rise by 5.2%, compared with a previous
forecast of 4.9%, and 2001 GDP would increase by 3.3%, compared with 3%.

--- Page 11 ---
Page 11 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
More than half the survey respondents said they expected the current technology boom to continue to boost
U.S. productivity growth for more than five years. Yet the economists also revised upward their inflation
estimates, with the consumer-price index expected to rise 3.3% this year and 2.7% in 2001, largely because
of higher oil prices.

How LTCM Came to the Edge of the Abyss---Hedge Fund's LossesSeemed to ImperilFinancial Markets
How LTCM Came to the Edge of the Abyss --- Hedge Fund's Losses Seemed to Imperil Financial
Markets
3,187 words
11 September 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
The 1998 meltdown of Long-Term Capital Management was a singular debacle. Markets around the globe
plunged and the financial system itself seemed in peril -- all on account of a tiny band of secretive bond
traders who had been envied as the best and brightest of Wall Street.
Markets had been in an increasing state of panic ever since Russia had defaulted on loans in August 1998.
Then, in the ensuing five weeks, LTCM suffered horrific losses, bringing it to the verge of bankruptcy. With
the prospects of a forced liquidation of its assets -- which totaled a staggering $100 billion -- and the sudden
unwinding of its trading positions, which linked it to every major financial house, William J. McDonough,
president of the New York Fed, feared that markets would "possibly cease to function." Thus, on Sept. 22 and
23, Mr. McDonough orchestrated an unprecedented rescue by 14 private banks and defused the crisis.
The following are excerpts from "When Genius Failed: The Rise and Fall of Long-Term Capital Management,"
by former Wall Street Journal reporter Roger Lowenstein. As the excerpts begin, LTCM, based in Greenwich,
Conn., had enjoyed a fabulous run, with nary a serious downturn. With bond markets obligingly balmy, LTCM
had quadrupled its money (before deducting fees to its partners) in a mere four years. And two of LTCM's
academic superstars, Robert C. Merton and Myron S. Scholes, had recently been awarded the Nobel Prize in
economics. By 1998, however, John W. Meriwether, the acclaimed trader and founder of LTCM, was
increasingly troubled by the lack of opportunities in bond arbitrage, LTCM's stock in trade. Its returns for the
first quarter were flat -- the fund's first dry spell -- and Wall Street traders were beginning to get nervous.
The end of April, approximately, was the low point for bond spreads, the high point for confidence, and the
high point for Long-Term Capital Management. In the manner of markets, the first hints of trouble were
scattered, small and seemingly unrelated. Lloyd Blankfein, a partner at Goldman Sachs, complained to Peter
Fisher, who ran trading activities at the New York Fed, that people weren't distinguishing among risks,
meaning that yield differentials between riskier and less risky bonds were narrowing to the vanishing point.
For the moment, everything was trading like a Treasury bill.
For Steve Freidheim of Bankers Trust, the alarm sounded during a spring trip to Singapore and Hong Kong.
What Freidheim saw in Asia shook him: "A lot of big players" were taking money off the table. Freidheim
returned to the United States in a pessimistic mood. "We began to short the market after that," he recalled. In
the U.S., credit spreads -- that is, the premium that traders charge for less risky bonds -- had never been
tighter. There was only one way for them to go, Freidheim felt, especially if the still fragile condition of Asia
should become apparent.
Imperceptibly at first, other Wall Street traders started to reach similar conclusions. Banks and securities firms
began to cut back their inventories of riskier, less liquid bonds -- which were the very types of bonds in
Long-Term's portfolio.
By June, spreads between bonds had begun to widen, exactly the opposite of what LTCM's computer models
had forecast. In short, LTCM, relying on historical models, had bet that perceived risk would diminish, but
prices were moving the opposite way. Jim McEntee, J.M.'s friend and the one partner who relied on his nose,
as distinct from a computer, sensed the trade winds changing. He repeatedly urged his partners to lower the
firm's risk -- lower, that is, their potent leverage and derivative exposure -- but McEntee was ignored as a
nonscientific, old-fashioned gambler.
Since moving to Connecticut, the partners, who no longer had to jostle with the throngs on Wall Street every
day, had become even more isolated from the anecdotal, but occasionally useful, gossip that traders pass
around. They found it easy to brush off McEntee's alarms, particularly since McEntee's trades had been
losing money. Increasingly frustrated, McEntee met James Rickards, Long-Term's general counsel, after
work one night at the Horseneck Tavern in Greenwich. Rickards was leaving the next morning on an
expedition to climb Mount McKinley in Alaska. "By the time you get back, the world will have completely
changed," McEntee predicted darkly.

--- Page 13 ---
Page 13 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
LTCM suffered a 6% loss in May and a troubling 10% drop in June. The partners insisted that losses were to
be expected after their prolonged success, and predicted a speedy recovery. But then, on Aug. 17, Russia
defaulted. By the end of the month, the stunned partners were frantically looking for new investors.
By Friday, August 21, traders everywhere were panicking. Stock markets in Asia and in Europe plunged. The
Dow fell 280 points before noon, then recouped most of its losses. In Greenwich, on that golden late-August
Friday, Long-Term's office was largely deserted. Most of the senior partners were on vacation; it was a sultry
morning, and the staff was moving slowly. McEntee, the colorful "sheik" whose doleful warnings had been
ignored, was minding the store. Bill Krasker, the partner who had constructed many of the firm's models, was
anxiously monitoring markets, clicking from one phosphorescent page to the next. When he saw the
quotation for U.S. swap spreads, a standard barometer of credit market anxiety, he stared at his screen in
disbelief.
On an active day, Krasker knew, U.S. swap spreads might change by as much as a point. But on this
morning, swap spreads were wildly oscillating over a range of 20 points. Krasker couldn't believe it. He
sought out Matt Zames, a trader, and Mike Reisman, the firm's repo man, and heatedly broke the news. The
traders hadn't seen a move like that -- ever. True, it had happened in 1987 and again in 1992. But
Long-Term's models didn't go back that far. As far as Long-Term knew, it was a once-in-a-lifetime occurrence
-- a practical impossibility -- and one for which the fund was totally unprepared. One of the analysts called a
trader at home and asked, "Would you like to guess where swap spreads are?" When the analyst told him,
the trader snapped, "F--- you -- don't ever call me at home again!"
Nothing in any market went right that day. Long-Term lost money in Russia, Brazil -- even the U.S. stock
market. The skeleton crew in Greenwich tracked Meriwether down at a dinner in Beijing, and the boss took
the next flight home. Before he left, J.M. called Jon Corzine, the chief executive of Goldman Sachs, a major
Long-Term trading partner, at Corzine's home. "We've had a serious markdown," Meriwether advised him,
"but everything is fine with us."
But everything was not fine. Long-Term, which had calculated with such mathematical certainty that it was
unlikely to lose more than $35 million on any single day, had just dropped $553 million -- 15% of its capital --
on that one Friday in August. Since the end of April, it had lost more than a third of its equity.
The following Monday, a week after Russia's default, the partners started dialing for dollars. With their
gilt-edged roster of contacts, their brilliant record, their lustrous reputations, no modern Croesus was beyond
their reach. Greg Hawkins tapped a friend in George Soros's fund and set up a breakfast for Meriwether,
Soros and Stanley Druckenmiller, the billionaire's top strategist.
Meriwether argued, calmly but persuasively, that Long-Term's markets would snap back; for those with deep
pockets, the opportunities were great. Soros listened impassively, but Druckenmiller peppered J.M. with
questions. He smelled a chance to recoup Soros's recent losses in Russia. Then, Soros boldly said he would
be willing to invest $500 million at the end of August -- that is, a week later -- provided that Long-Term could
restore its capital by raising another $500 million in addition.
But as their losses mounted, the dream of raising money from Mr. Soros faded.
The partners began to sense that they might not make it. "We were desperate at the end of August," Eric
Rosenfeld admitted. Their tone changed to disbelief and bitterness at having left themselves exposed to such
needless humiliation. They were much too rich to have gotten into so much trouble.
Badly in need of a lift, Meriwether called an old friend, Vinny Mattone, who had been the fund's first contact at
Bear Stearns, LTCM's clearing broker. Mattone, who had retired, was everything that J.M.'s elegant
professors were not. He wore a gold chain and a pinkie ring, and he showed up at Long-Term in a black silk
shirt, open at the chest. He looked as if he weighed 300 pounds. Unlike J.M.'s strangely wooden partners,
Mattone saw markets as exquisitely human institutions -- inherently volatile, ever-fallible.
"Where are you?" Mattone asked bluntly.
"We're down by half," Meriwether said.
"You're finished," Mattone replied, as if this conclusion needed no explanation.
For the first time, Meriwether sounded worried. "What are you talking about? We still have two billion. We
have half -- we have Soros."
Mattone smiled sadly. "When you're down by half, people figure you can go down all the way. They're going
to push the market against you. They're not going to roll [refinance] your trades. You're finished."

--- Page 14 ---
Page 14 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
September was even worse. And with the fund losing money day after day, the partners' spirits began to
suffer.
In every class of asset and all over the world, the market moved against the hedge fund in Greenwich. The
correlations had gone to one; every roll was turning up snake eyes. The mathematicians at Long-Term had
not foreseen this. Random markets, they had thought, would lead to standard distributions -- to a normal
pattern of black sheep and white sheep, heads and tails, and jacks and deuces, not to staggering losses in
every trade, day after day after day. The professors had ignored the truism -- of which they were well aware --
that in markets, rare events nonetheless occur.
Stuck in their glass-walled palace far from New York's teeming trading floors, they had forgotten that traders
are not random molecules, or even mechanical logicians such as themselves, but people moved by greed
and fear, capable of the extreme behavior and swings of mood so often observed in crowds. And in the late
summer of 1998, the bond-trading crowd was extremely fearful, especially of risky credits.
Thursday, September 10 was a very bad day. Swap spreads -- Long-Term' s biggest bond market trade --
jumped another seven points; other credit spreads widened, too. In a spot of bad luck -- or was it a portent? --
a rocket ferrying a dozen Globalstar satellites fell out of the sky and exploded. Long-Term, as it happened,
owned Globalstar bonds. The partners almost laughed: Even the heavens were against them. In the
risk-management meeting, the traders went around the table, each reporting his results. When it became
clear that every trader had lost, David Mullins, a partner and previously the U.S. Fed's vice chairman,
demanded sarcastically, "Can't we ever make money -- just for one day?" So far in September, they hadn't;
not once.
The fund was immobilized by its sheer mass. The smaller fish around it were liquidating every bond in sight,
but Long-Term was helpless, a bloated whale surrounded by deadly piranhas. The frightful size of its
positions put the partners in a terrible bind. If they sold even a tiny fraction of a big position -- say, of swaps --
it would send the price plummeting and reduce the value of all the rest. Ever since his Salomon Brothers
days, LTCM's Victor Haghani had pushed his colleagues to double and even quadruple their positions. Now
he saw that size extracts a price.
As the firm's losses mounted, the latent divide between the inner group of partners and the others fermented
into sour wine. Inevitably, the lesser partners blamed Lawrence Hilibrand and Haghani for blowing so much
wealth. Hawkins and Hilibrand, never the best of friends, became bitter toward each other, and Scholes and
Hilibrand were barely speaking. The unhappy partners resented not just Hilibrand's and Haghani's investment
calls but their domineering, insensitive style. Scholes felt that the secretive Hilibrand hadn't really been a
partner; Larry [Hilibrand] hadn't trusted the others. Ironically, it was the same complaint that the banks had
been making all along. McEntee, meanwhile, was increasingly absent from the firm. He was immobilized by
an aching back, a symbolic expression of the partners' despair, and increasingly bitter that Meriwether hadn't
heeded his warnings.
Given their frayed nerves and their enormous losses, it was only human for the partners to show some
tension. They were losing not only their fortunes but also their reputations. Meriwether was facing the
unbearable indignity of a second disaster in a once shining career. Eric Rosenfeld, the most emotional of the
group and the most devoted to the firm, was touchingly affected. At least he was not afraid to scream when
the anguish overcame him. Merton, his former teacher, was upset, too; he was distraught and not himself.
Worrying that Long-Term's collapse would undermine the standing of modern finance -- all he had really
worked for -- Merton repeatedly broke into tears. The professor was wonderfully human, after all.
After Wall Street's leading CEOs met in two historic and heated meetings at the Fed, a consortium of 14
banks announced that they would invest $3.65 billion and take over the fund. But unknown to the world, the
deal was far from assured, and LTCM was still facing imminent bankruptcy.
The LTCM partners got copies of the consortium contract early Saturday, two days before the deadline for
signing. They immediately exploded. It was indentured servitude, they hollered -- it denied them bonuses,
incentives, liability protection and freedom to start anew. Accustomed to the extraordinary lives of the
superrich, the partners could not conceive of working for a salary, and one of merely $250,000 at that. They
had lived in a bubble so long they had forgotten the recent event -- their own impending bankruptcy -- that
had brought them to this pass. On Wednesday, J.M. had been "appreciative"; by Saturday, the gang was
refusing to sign. There was nothing in it for them.
This show of petulance carried a serious threat. The consortium could not invest without the partners'
signatures. And the consortium was now publicly committed to doing the deal. By contrast, the partners had
little left to lose. They could always, they intimated, let the fund blow up, seek cover in personal bankruptcy,
and go get seven-figure jobs on Wall Street.

--- Page 15 ---
Page 15 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Skadden, Arps, Slate, Meagher & Flom, the law firm representing the consortium, had set out two big
conference rooms and a series of private suites on the 33rd floor to accommodate an expected onslaught of
lawyers and bankers. By Saturday morning, 140 lawyers were scurrying back and forth in near
pandemonium, trying to get a handle on the hedge fund's numbingly complex assets, its debts, its structure,
its management company. The lawyers had discovered that the agreement they had come to negotiate
actually didn't exist; too many issues divided them.
J. Gregory Milmoe, the Skadden partner in charge, was in a terrible bind. Trying to craft a contract that would
be acceptable to fourteen banks and each of the partners all within 72 hours, Milmoe naturally had to fashion
compromises. As the day wore on, the bank lawyers began to feel that Milmoe was conceding too much to
Greenwich. At one point, striving for a consensus, the congenial attorney said, "I'd like to represent LTCM's
demands." John Mead, a lawyer for Goldman, snapped, "How can they have demands? They're going
bankrupt on Monday!"
Over the weekend, the fragile consortium came undone several times, only to be patched together by Merrill
Lynch banker Herbert Allison. Finally, on Monday, the absolute last day for signing, the rescue faced a final
threat -- from LTCM's stubborn star trader, Mr. Hilibrand.
Meriwether, not quite satisfied, said, "Our guys still won't sign until it says we can go out and do a new fund."
That was their goal now -- to get free of Long-Term.
Allison, who knew the banks would never invest $3.65 billion if the partners were plotting to desert, pulled
J.M. aside and explained that he couldn't put it into writing. The partners would have to commit to three years
each; markets would have little faith in the new consortium unless it was seen as truly "long term." But in
time, Allison ventured, the partners might be free to leave. J.M. heard this as a promise; he had gotten all he
could.
The waivers were in, and the Federal Reserve had been alerted to keep its electronic-payments wire open
past its usual 6:30 closing time. The Fed was expecting wires from fourteen banks totaling $3.65 billion.
The partners were ready to sign. They stood in a knot at the far end of the room, a cathedral-sized space that
seemed to dwarf the small band of diminished arbitrageurs. Hilibrand was reading the contract, which was
hopelessly illegible to all but the lawyers. The margins were jammed with penciled revisions and crossed-out
sentences and arrows up and down, as though the contract were a visual representation of the confusion of
the last three days. Rickards and a couple of other attorneys were trying to tell Hilibrand what it meant, but he
didn't want to listen, he wanted to read it himself, and he could barely see it through the tears that were
streaming down his face.
He didn't want to sign, he wailed; there was nothing in it for him, better to file for bankruptcy than be someone
else's indentured servant with no hope of ever earning his way out. Meriwether took Hilibrand aside and
talked to him about the group, and how the others were in it and needed him to be in it, and still, Hilibrand,
who had never needed anyone and who had once rebelled at paying for his share of the company cafeteria
but now couldn't pay his debts, refused. Then Allison talked to him and said they were trying to restore the
public's faith in the system and not to destroy anybody, and J.M. said, "Larry, you better listen to Herb." And
Hilibrand signed, and the fund was taken over by 14 banks.

Cry Havoc, and Unleash the Dogs of Populism!
Global View
Cry Havoc, and Unleash the Dogs of Populism!
By George Melloan
1,161 words
5 September 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
A35
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Al Gore seems to be having some political success after having reinvented himself as William Jennings
Bryan. But American voters would do well to dip into their history books to explore the potential dangers to
their own well-being from following the siren song of populism. They don't have to go back to the 1890s for
those lessons. The 1970s will do.
Mr. Gore's has a modern version of spellbinder Bryan's "Cross of Gold" speech. The vice president attacks
(who else?) "big business." Greedy oil companies are to blame for high gasoline prices. Greedy
pharmaceutical companies are robbing the poor and the elderly. Greedy HMOs are denying sick people their
rights as patients. The question of why all this greed has broken out during the Clinton-Gore administration is
never addressed. Why spoil such resonant terms as "Big Oil" with nettlesome particulars? Big business has
simply gotten greedy and something must be done about it.
There is the rub. The federal government could do something about prices. It has police powers. Congress
could legislate price controls and send federal officers out to enforce them, breaking the arms of gas station
attendants pumping regular at above a federally mandated price. "Economic" crimes, a favorite target of the
old Soviet Union and 1970s American bureaucrats, would once again be punished.
Mr. Gore's speeches are mostly blather, but should he be elected president and should high prices for some
consumer goods persist, who knows? He is the sort of megalomaniacal politician who might actually believe
that he can command markets to behave. George W. Bush and Dick Cheney, whose business experience
has taught them something of the difficulties of that task, would be far less likely to make that mistake. They
would know that markets are like dragons. When political popinjays wield the sword of government power
against them, they turn around, snort a bit of fire and incinerate their tormentors.
The dragon always wins. Always. Americans learned that at great cost in the 1970s. But that was 20 years
ago and people forget. So let's go over it just once more.
President Richard Nixon slapped price controls on the U.S. economy in August 1971 to stifle the inflationary
effects of his trashing of the Bretton Woods gold exchange monetary standard. He was under heavy
pressure to do so from a Democratic Congress concerned about the already existing inflation generated by
federal over-spending during the Lyndon Johnson regime. The price controls seemed effective for awhile.
They, along with continued monetary stimulus by the Federal Reserve, had the country rolling in a false
prosperity by November 1972. Mr. Nixon won easy reelection.
Then the dragon retaliated. Mr. Nixon's controls had discouraged oil production in the U.S. With the roaring
U.S. economy sending energy demand up, the lid blew off of the world oil market in 1973. There followed a
series of energy shortages and weird improvisations by Washington to try to reconcile low, controlled
domestic oil prices with high, uncontrolled, foreign oil. Price controls on other products ultimately withered in
the face of a rising rate of inflation, but that was no bargain either. As inflation gained steam, corporations
were decapitalized by an inability to retain adequate depreciation reserves; stocks went into slumber; interest
rates soared, crippling the bond and mortgage markets. A combination of inflation and a steeply graduated
personal income tax schedule destroyed worker incomes and personal savings.
It was not until 1981 that the combined efforts of Ronald Reagan and Paul Volcker saved the sinking ship. Mr.
Volcker firmed up the dollar. Mr. Reagan launched a tax reform and removed the remaining price controls on
energy. The price of gasoline, by the way, dropped.
Now, let's look at the energy situation today. Commodity prices are cyclical. Heavy demand causes prices to
rise. Higher prices act as an incentive to producers to create more capacity. But the sharp run-up in fuel
prices this year signals that there is more going on than just the usual cycles. Al Gore and his radical

--- Page 17 ---
Page 17 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
environmentalist constituency have played their role in boosting fuel prices as part of their continuing
campaign to wean the nation from the nasty habit of driving around in cars.
Prime areas for oil drilling in Alaska and the Western U.S. have been put off limits by the Gore-Clinton policy
of consigning more and more federal land to "wilderness" status. Punitive Environmental Protection Agency
anti-pollution rules have shut down new refinery construction. A confusing variety of new EPA
"environmentally-friendly" fuel additive requirements for different regions has disrupted gasoline distribution,
sending prices soaring in some parts of the Midwest.
These factors have combined with the normal supply-demand cycle that was predictable after world crude oil
prices dropped to $10 a barrel, discouraging new oil field development. Now prices near $30 have caused
drilling to pick up, with drillers and their rigs heading out in search of any promising areas that haven't been
closed off by Clinton-Gore ukases.
If the oil market were left alone, high prices would cure the supply-demand imbalance. Oil industry analysts
figure that the equilibrium price is around $18 a barrel, and that is where the price will return unless Mr. Gore
and/or Mr. Clinton interfere further with the natural processes in their demagoguing of oil producers in this
election year. Recent investigations by the Federal Trade Commission and the General Accounting Office of
Congress have found no evidence of oil industry collusion to raise prices, by the way.
Mr. Gore's attack on the pharmaceutical industry is of a different nature. In support of his sweeping plan for a
government takeover of the nation's medicine cabinet, he is claiming price gouging by the industry. Should
his plan succeed, the U.S. government would have a buying monopoly, or monopsony. Governmental
budgeting being what it is, the Feds would undoubtedly squeeze drug companies for lower prices.
The same thing would happen to drugs that happened to fuel when the government squeezed fuel prices in
the 1970s. There will be less new drug development and ultimately, shortages of certain drugs as producers
found it unprofitable to supply them. Of course, Mr. Gore, should he be elected, probably would be out of
office before the worst consequences hit, just as Richard Nixon was long gone when his price control policies
finally rebounded against the hapless Jimmy Carter. That is one reason economic interventionism is always
tempting to opportunistic politicians. They look like heroes until their meddling creates economic chaos.
Populist blather didn't win the presidency for Bryan and it may not for Al Gore. But it sure can be scary when
introduced into a political campaign.

WSJ Sept. 14, 2000)

Fear of Economic Slowdown Stops InvestorsFrom Mining Canada Natural-Resource Stocks
Heard on the Street
Fear of Economic Slowdown Stops Investors From Mining Canada Natural-Resource Stocks
By Mark Heinzl and Christopher J. Chipello
Staff Reporters of The Wall Street Journal
1,015 words
5 September 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Canadian natural-resource stocks are on sale, but shoppers aren't in a bargain-hunting mood.
That's how it is in the once-powerful sector of Canada's booming stock market. Shares of many of Canada's
big oil, metals and forest-products companies haven't been cheaper in recent memory. But fear of economic
slowdown and weakening commodity prices -- against a backdrop of investor infatuation with technology and
financial stocks -- are stopping investors from scooping up the apparent values.
"Rocks and trees" used to be a major driver of the Canadian stock market, collectively accounting for 20% of
the Toronto Stock Exchange 300's market capitalization two years ago and 29% a decade ago. Now they
account for less than 15% of the Canadian market, and are a glaring exception to the country's explosive
stock-market rally. Powered by soaring tech shares, financial stocks and others, the TSE 300 is up 35% this
year, easily the world's top-performing major market.
Take Noranda Inc., the big Toronto metals producer. Analysts expect the company's earnings to more than
double this year over last year, partly because of stringent cost cutting. Prices for nickel are at a high level
while copper, aluminum and zinc ones are relatively firm. Yet Noranda's shares, which closed Friday at 14.70
Canadian dollars (US$10), are down from above C$21 at the start of the year and C$25 three years ago. The
current level is about 10 times per-share earnings forecast by analysts for this year, and the stock carries a
dividend yield of more than 5%.
"It's a disappointment," says a Noranda spokesman, especially since the company has been tirelessly
meeting with investors, analysts and others during the past year to tout its achievements and planned
production increases for copper, zinc, magnesium and other metals. Recent signs of fading interest in certain
tech stocks could boost interest in resource stocks, he adds.
But with global economic growth expected to slow down during the next 12 months, investors "expect
commodity prices to come off, and the resource sector to not fare well," says Len Racioppo, president of
money-management firm Jarislowsky Fraser Ltd. in Toronto.
That sentiment is echoed by Paul Wong, portfolio manager with Talfund Management in Toronto, who says
Canada's resources sector is "cheap, but the problem is it keeps getting cheaper." Mr. Wong says he's
recently added to positions in a few Canadian producers of oil and natural gas; those are among the
commodities enjoying the strongest prices currently. Of Talisman Energy Inc., an oil producer with growing
natural-gas production, he says, for instance, "That thing is darn cheap" and well-managed. But he's largely
stayed away from mining and forestry shares.
Mr. Wong names a handful of forest-products companies with unusually low valuations: Canfor Corp.
currently trades at a little more than five times earnings expected this year; Slocan Forest Products Ltd.
trades at about four times this year's expected earnings and Tembec Inc. trades at about six or so times
expected earnings. Though the valuations are tempting, the problem in the forest-products sector is "in the
last little while you're starting to see downward revisions" of long-term expected earnings, he says.
Rising interest rates and a slowing U.S. housing market have dimmed the outlook for building products.
Even the possible end of restraints on Canadian lumber exports to the U.S. next March, when a bilateral pact
expires, doesn't excite many investors here. That's because higher Canadian lumber shipments into the U.S.
probably would mean lower product prices.
Many money managers view newsprint as the forestry industry's most attractive segment right now. Supplies
are tight and a price increase of $50 a metric ton this month is expected to stick, raising prices to around
$605 a metric ton. A metric ton is equal to 2,204.62 pounds.

--- Page 19 ---
Page 19 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Even so, the stock price of Abitibi-Consolidated Inc., the world's biggest newsprint maker, remains well below
its January high of more than C$19. The stock closed Friday at C$16.60. (The Montreal company's shares
also trade on the New York Stock Exchange.)
In the Canadian oil sector, many stocks are trading at two to four times cash flow, well below traditional levels
of five to seven times, fund managers say. Low-valued stocks include Talisman, at three or so times expected
cash flow this year, and Crestar Energy Inc., at a little more than two times. Talisman's low price is due partly
to political controversy in North America over the company's stake in a Sudanese oil project. But many
investors are reluctant to bid up oil stocks, in general, skeptical that oil prices can sustain recent levels above
$30 a barrel.
Increased takeovers in the international mining industry could spark a rally in long-depressed shares of
base-metals producers, analysts say. Billiton PLC, a United Kingdom mining company, recently offered $1.2
billion to acquire Canadian copper producer Rio Algom Ltd. The per-share price of the offer is 49% higher
than Rio Algom's trading price before Noranda made a previous bid.
Toronto nickel miner Inco Ltd. is trading at an "unusually low" multiple of four to five times expected cash flow
this year, says Terence Ortslan, a Montreal mining analyst. The multiple would normally be about seven,
given Inco's current strong market share, rising earnings and steadily growing nickel markets, he says.
As for the gold-mining sector, some have given up hope for a turnaround. The metal's long-sliding price has
dragged the Toronto exchange's gold-stock index down to about 4100 from above 7000 late last year and
13000 a few years ago. "It's too embarrassing," one gold-fund manager says in declining to talk about gold
stocks.

Burning Research:Scientists Fight FiresBy Trying to OutwitA Very Persistent Weed---Cheatgrass Provides the FuelFor Vast Western Blazes...
Burning Research: Scientists Fight Fires By Trying to Outwit A Very Persistent Weed --- Cheatgrass
Provides the Fuel For Vast Western Blazes; Can Smut Fungus Help? --- Next: a Medusahead Threat
By Mark Robichaux
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,966 words
1 September 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
PROVO, Utah -- Firefighters in labcoats, a world away from those on the TV news, battle blazes in a different
way here at the federal Shrub Sciences Laboratory.
Instead of axes and shovels, they use microscopes and test tubes. They target not the flames but the fuel: a
weed called cheatgrass.
A fire-loving annual plant from the deserts of Eurasia, cheatgrass has turned the American rangelands into a
tinderbox. It dies before other grasses, in early summer, and quickly dries into papery leaves and flammable
stalks. It has infested an estimated 100 million acres across the West, claiming more turf every year and
accelerating the natural fire cycle. What was once a sea of sage and native grasses providing cover for
wildlife is now a continuous foot-high carpet of cheatgrass.
This summer may turn out to be one of the worst for fires, with more than six million acres of forests and
rangeland already consumed -- more than twice the average. While the country focuses on efforts to douse
flames from Montana to New Mexico, a struggle no less important is taking place at the shrub-sciences lab,
where at times lately, fires have burned as close as five miles away.
Susan Meyer, a plant ecologist with the lab, is investigating the cheatgrass-annihilation potential of smut. A
type of "castrating fungus" also native to parts of Europe and Asia, smut stops cheatgrass from making
seeds.
Two other federal researchers are traversing south-central Russia, seeking to learn more about cheatgrass in
its native environment. Durant McArthur, research director of the lab, which is part of the U.S. Forest Service,
is working on a fire-resistant sagebrush. Government scientists also are testing a promising cheatgrass
herbicide.
None of these endeavors will diminish this year's inferno, however. Complicating matters, some of the most
basic efforts to revive areas devastated by cheatgrass fires have sparked objections and litigation by
environmentalists and Native Americans. And there is a chronic shortage of more-desirable grass seed for
postfire replanting.
Cheatgrass reached critical mass after decades of slow, silent accretion. The spread of the weed, whose
spiky seeds cling to socks on humans and fur on livestock and wild game, tells a good part of the story of the
West. Following gold and silver strikes in the Northwest in the 1850s, settlers moved toward the Pacific,
bringing cattle that overgrazed perennial grasses and shrubs. In the 1880s, grain shipments from Europe
arrived, and hidden among the seeds were those of cheatgrass, known formally as Bromus tectorum.
The weed isn't all bad. It can provide forage for animals in the spring and fall, when it briefly turns green. But
it earned its name because it "cheated" farmers out of a full yield. Eventually, as the railroad boom hastened
its spread, it became a menace throughout the West, flourishing in an arid climate similar to that of its
homeland.
Cheatgrass burns more frequently and unpredictably than such native grasses as bluebunch wheatgrass and
Indian ricegrass. Its dense growth pattern makes cheatgrass ignite more easily, whether from man-made
flame, errant spark or lightning. Once burning, a cheatgrass fire can turn and surge in almost any direction
because the prolific plant's stalks are massed so close together.
Ranges that once burned every 100 years now catch fire every three or four. And while cheatgrass is
associated with the open range, even fires in forested upland often spread there from abutting cheatgrass
fields.

--- Page 21 ---
Page 21 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Ms. Meyer, the plant ecologist, says smothering its ability to make seeds could be the key to eradicating
cheatgrass. The powerful annual weed gets a jump on native perennial plants because its seeds germinate
earlier, often in winter. It sends roots deep, monopolizing water and nutrients before native grasses have a
chance to start.
On a recent morning, Ms. Meyer, 51 years old, examines smut-infected cheatgrass stalks growing in a small
wooden box at the shrub-sciences lab. The plant looks relatively healthy, except for a sooty coating on its
impotent seed heads. Under a microscope, a single spore of smut resembles a tiny black balloon.
Smut and other fungi have been deployed against other pest plants, but this is its debut as a potential
cheatgrass-control weapon. The thorniest problem Ms. Meyer confronts is matching its more than 20 strains
with the types of cheatgrass they can neuter.
"If we could make all the strains, and apply them all at the same time, maybe we can infect" the cheatgrass
effectively, says Ms. Meyer. "But this is a hypothesis," she admits, and testing will take years. Some types of
smut attack native wheat, so farmers probably would oppose its release on the range.
As an interim strategy, government scientists are seeding some Western rangeland with another foreign
species that can crowd out cheatgrass. Crested wheatgrass, a hardy plant from Russia, is less flammable
and favored by landowners as forage for livestock. But the wheatgrass has a drawback: It tends to overrun
native perennial shrubs and grasses, as well, denying winter forage and shelter to rangeland animals.
Following a different course, Mr. McArthur, the shrub lab's research director, estimates he is about a year
away from developing a fire-resistant sagebrush. After eight years of genetic testing and cross-breeding
sagebrush varieties, he thinks he has nailed down the proper variables. "But it's still a gleam in this scientist's
eye," he says with a sigh.
A fixture in traditional Western movies, sagebrush is critical to the range ecosystem. When it burns,
cheatgrass takes over, and native wildlife reliant on sagebrush disappears. Mule deer, sage grouse and
pygmy rabbits, for example, depend on sagebrush for food and cover. Without big game, hunters stay home,
keeping in their pockets millions of dollars they might have spent on equipment and lodging.
Soil erosion and flooding also commonly follow rangeland fires. "We're losing not just plants; whole
ecosystems are collapsing," says Jayne Belnap, an ecologist with the U.S. Geological Survey.
Funding for the Forest Service shrub-sciences lab has barely kept pace with inflation over the past decade.
The current annual budget of $920,000 provides operating funds of less than $5,000 per scientist at the lab.
Since that amount often doesn't cover experiments, supplies and travel, the lab seeks supplementary money
from other government agencies.
The federal government will probably spend more than $1 billion on fire suppression this year, 25% more
than in 1999, according to the National Interagency Fire Center in Boise, Idaho. Researchers at the
shrub-sciences lab maintain that a relatively modest increase today in anti-cheatgrass spending could save
many millions in fire suppression later. But the reality is that Congress has an easier time focusing on putting
out the fires than on preventing them.
Once fire spreads across a range, the best time to reseed is soon after the flames are snuffed and before the
first snowfall, which is when cheatgrass typically germinates. But such rehabilitation efforts are expected to
cover only a third of the burned rangelands this year, despite efforts by the Forest Service, the Bureau of
Land Management and various Western state agencies. Beyond familiar shortages of money and workers,
officials say they lack one other critical ingredient: seed.
The market for native-grass seed hasn't been predictable or substantial enough to attract the attention of
major seed companies. It's hard to cultivate rangeland grasses efficiently on a farm and difficult to gather the
seeds from dispersed plants in the wild.
A couple of dozen small companies in the West collect native-grass seed, but their arduous techniques are
only "a step up from beating a bush with a tennis racket," says Scott Walker, a biologist with the Utah Division
of Wildlife Resources.
The tight supply has driven up seed prices. A 50-pound bag of cliffrose, a native shrub, goes for $2,500.
"We're not ordering any now; [the Bureau of Land Management] won't approve it at that price," says Pat
Fosse, assistant field manager of the bureau's regional office in Utah.
To fill the need for seed, state and federal officials in Utah are cultivating a vast research garden of native
grasses 50 miles south of Provo. From the open window of his government-issued Ford Explorer, Mr. Walker
points to sage, basin wildrye and other shrubs and grasses, which look oddly out of place in long neat rows.

--- Page 22 ---
Page 22 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
He hopes this research will establish more-efficient cultivation techniques that lure national seed companies
into the market for native-grass and shrub seed.
Even with seed in hand, though, replenishing torched rangeland is a Sisyphean task. Less than 5% of seeds
typically thrive after being dropped from airplanes. Most dry out or blow away. Seeds are also dispensed by
devices pulled behind tractors.
"Chaining" can increase seed-survival odds. Pairs of bulldozers pull a massive battleship-anchor chain, which
scrapes up charred ground and covers newly laid seed. The practice also knocks down dead trees, leaving
desolate-looking fields, but the land is better prepared for new growth.
Not everyone approves of chaining, however. Four years ago, cheatgrass fires burned more than 200,000
acres in west-central Utah. Planes dropped more than a million pounds of new plant seed, and the Bureau of
Land Management reconstructed more than 240 miles of fence to protect the seedlings from livestock.
But in early 1997, when only about half of the planned chaining was finished, the Paiute Indian Tribe of Utah,
assisted by the Southern Utah Wilderness Alliance, an environmental group, obtained a restraining order
from a federal court in Salt Lake City that temporarily halted work in that area.
The Native Americans argued that chaining disturbed archeological sites, and the wilderness alliance
asserted that chaining "doesn't let the natural process govern," in the words of Larry Young, the wilderness
group's executive director. By the time the court permitted chaining to resume, with some restrictions, in
west-central Utah in the spring of 1998, cheatgrass had taken over tens of thousands of acres.
Some "backseat ecologists" may want Mother Nature to take care of herself, says Mrs. Fosse of the
land-management bureau, but "weeds will come in, and it's a man-made problem." Chaining continues in
Utah and elsewhere, although the bureau says it now makes more intensive archeological surveys of burned
land.
In some heavily infested fields of cheatgrass, the bureau has had good luck so far with Oust, a herbicide
made by DuPont Co. Oust kills cheatgrass but leaves native perennial grasses standing. Used in limited
amounts, the chemical hasn't had any ill effects on other living things, either, says Stephen Monsen, a
botanist with the shrub-sciences lab.
But Oust won't become a quick fix, Mr. Monsen says. The Forest Service has been testing the chemical for
six years. "We're moving with caution on this," he says.
Defeating cheatgrass, sad to say, wouldn't end the threat of rangeland fire. Other weeds, just as aggressive,
are moving in after cheatgrass. These include Russian knapweed, medusahead and jointed goatgrass. Some
have thicker stalks than cheatgrass, meaning they potentially provide even better fuel.
Mr. Monsen frets that while forest fires command attention because they burn closer to private property and
towns, sometimes threatening human life, rangeland fires seem distant and irrelevant to many people. "We all
still think we have plenty of land left out here," he says. "When the public finally realizes the magnitude of this
problem, it'll be too late."

Zale's Beryl Raff Is Named ChairmanAs DiNicola Retires Amid Record Profit
Marketing & Media
Zale's Beryl Raff Is Named Chairman As DiNicola Retires Amid Record Profit
By Ann Zimmerman
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
733 words
31 August 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
B2
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Zale Corp. Chairman Robert DiNicola, credited with engineering a sparkling turnaround, said he will retire at
age 52 and turn the jewelry retailer over to Chief Executive Beryl Raff.
The news came as the company reported record profit, with fiscal fourth-quarter earnings increasing 36% and
earnings for the year jumping 38%. The results were fueled, in part, by diamond sales that remained strong
even as rising interest rates and gas-price increases slowed general consumer spending.
Ms. Raff, 49 years old, became president and CEO last year. She, in turn, will turn over the president's title to
Alan Shore, 41, who is executive vice president and chief operating officer. Mr. DiNicola will remain on the
board.
"I'm not going to change anything," Ms. Raff said. "We have a great formula that's been highly successful and
we will continue to evolve it."
Mr. DiNicola said he is stepping down having completed his two primary objectives for the year. Last month,
the company sold its private-label credit-card business to a subsidiary of Associates First Capital Corp. for
$575 million. With the proceeds, two weeks ago, Zale agreed to purchase Piercing Pagoda Inc., a mallbased
jewelry kiosk business, for about $210.7 million.
"I'm leaving with a high level of comfort that there is a great team in place that can continue to build the
business the right way," said Mr. DiNicola.
The moves completed a well-defined strategic plan Mr. DiNicola devised when he took the helm of Zale
almost seven years ago. At the time, Zale was struggling and the future of mall-based jewelry retailers was in
question.
The company's troubles began in 1986 when Peoples Jewellers, a Toronto-based chain, and Swarovski
International Holdings of Switzerland acquired Zale in a leveraged buyout for $550 million. Lumbering under
the debt load, Zale filed for bankruptcy protection in 1992. In 1993, Peoples also filed for bankruptcy-court
protection and lost control of Zale, which became an independent company again.
A 30-year veteran of the retail business, Mr. DiNicola was chairman and chief executive of the Seattle-based
Bon Marche department store chain when Zale hired him. Six months later he hired Ms. Raff, the senior vice
president responsible for jewelry and lingerie at Macy's East, where she had converted leased jewelry
departments into a thriving store-owned business.
"Bob was the visionary and Beryl was the merchant in the trenches who executed his vision," said Michael
Exstein, retail analyst with Credit Suisse First Boston.
Mr. DiNicola began by restoring the power of the company's core franchise, the Zale brand, focusing on key
fashion items, re-establishing pricing legitimacy by moving away from heavy discounting, increasing inventory
and training. He applied the same philosophy to the company's midpriced Gordon's stores and the upper-end
Baily Banks & Biddle Fine Jewelers.
By summer 1999, the company was strong enough to begin acquisitions, ironically beginning with the
purchase of Peoples's 176-store chain in June 1999. Zale also began broadening its reach by launching a
Web site and opening factory outlet stores.
The strategy has proven successful, with the company meeting or exceeding Wall Street's earnings
expectation for 24 consecutive quarters.

--- Page 24 ---
Page 24 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
For the period ended July 31, Zale posted net income of $11.6 million, or 32 cents a diluted share, compared
with $8.5 million, or 23 cents, in the year-ago period. A First Call/Thomson Financial survey of analysts
expected the company to earn 29 cents. Revenue increased 15% to $373.8 million from $326 million a year
ago.
Ms. Raff said while the company has noticed some spending tightening, its earnings prospects for the coming
year remain strong. "When times are tighter, a customer might have to choose between spending $100 on a
cashmere sweater or a gold necklace. The jewelry is more alluring, because it is perceived as having a
lasting value," she said.
In 4 p.m. New York Stock Exchange composite trading, Zale shares fell 63 cents to $37, well off its 52-week
high of $51.75.
Credit Suisse First Boston's Mr. Exstein blamed the negative sentiment over retail shares for Zale's recent
stock-market performance.

Some Stock Investors Wait for a Better Valuation Picture
Abreast of the Market
Some Stock Investors Wait for a Better Valuation Picture
By Gregory Zuckerman
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,447 words
28 August 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Like an Olympic track-and-fielder, the stock market keeps jumping over imposing hurdles. First inflation
worries, then earnings jitters and lately the Federal Reserve. In fact, stock valuations are getting much better,
and stock prices haven't looked this good in some time, according to some analysts.
So with all this good news, why can't stocks pick up much ground?
Stock prices have come so far, so fast in recent years that much of the market might not be able to rally until
earnings really catch up and valuations get even better, according to a number of investors. And that could
take several months, putting a damper on the market for the foreseeable future.
"The risk isn't the market's fundamentals, it's that stock prices could march in time until the valuation excess
is burned off," Richard Cripps, chief market strategist at Baltimore's Legg Mason Wood Walker, said.
The good news first: The bond market has rallied, pushing down bond yields to their lowest levels in more
than a year, easing pressures on corporate-borrowing costs. Earnings have been impressive and should
keep growing. And last week, the Fed kept interest rates unchanged, even issuing an encouraging
statement suggesting more increases may not be in the cards.
Meanwhile, important measures of stock valuations are improving. Thanks to impressive earnings and a flat
stock market, the ratio of stock prices to their earnings has been falling, especially in the technology sector.
Stocks in the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index now trade at a multiple of 23.6 times their expected
operating earnings over the next 52 weeks. In other words, investors are spending $23.60 for each dollar of
corporate earnings. That's down from a price-to-earnings ratio of 25.1 in early April, close to the all-time high,
according to IBES International, an earnings-research firm.
It is happening around the globe too. The Morgan Stanley World Index stands at 26 times last year's
earnings, down from a record 30 in December 1999. With inflation low, and earnings prospects good, a high
price/earnings multiple might be justified, many argue.
A widely followed valuation model that some say is favored by the Fed, and includes bond yields, stock prices
and earnings, shows the stock market is 37% overvalued. That sure is high, but it is down from a stunning
63% at the beginning of the year, according to Edward Yardeni, chief global economist and investment
strategist at Deutsche Bank Securities, who tracks the index.
"We're going in the right direction, and with bond yields falling and earnings expectations rising, the market
can correct its overvaluation without a crash," Mr. Yardeni said.
Last week, the Dow Jones Industrial Average rose 1.3%, to 11192.63, while the Nasdaq Composite Index
rose 2.9% to retake the 4000 mark, and the S&P gained 1% to regain its own milestone, of 1500.
But for all the good news about earnings and such, and even with last week's gains, the stock market's
performance for 2000 so far won't win any gold medals. Thus far this year, the industrial average is down
2.7%, while the Nasdaq has fallen 0.7% and the S&P 500 -- while nearer to its record high than the other
indexes -- is up a meager 2.5%. The results so far this year add up to the worst performance for stock
markets around the globe since 1992, according to Morgan Stanley.
More stocks have been rallying lately, even as major stock indexes tread water. But the results for the year
are not impressive. So far in 2000, some 976 stocks have gained ground on the New York Stock Exchange,
while 878 have fallen, according to Salomon Smith Barney. But on Nasdaq, 1,337 stocks have gone up this
year, while 1,795 have fallen.

--- Page 26 ---
Page 26 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The valuation concerns are a big change for investors, many of whom shifted last year into Internet
companies that weren't valued as a multiple of earnings -- often because they didn't have any.
"There's a greater sensitivity to valuation this year, while it was growth at any price in the past few years,"
Marshall Acuff, market strategist at Salomon Smith Barney, said.
Some say a serious rally is just around the corner, perhaps when investors come back from summer
vacations. Or maybe it will take a signal from the Fed that it is done raising rates. Some say the rally will
come after the Olympic games come to a close, and investors focus on equities, not equestrians. Believe it or
not, it's a legitimate issue, say some analysts.
"The Olympics have been a distraction in the past," Alfred E. Goldman, chief market strategist at A.G.
Edwards, said.
But the bullish talk might just be wishful thinking. Stock indexes can gain ground and perhaps climb another
5% from these levels by year end. But much of the market is still priced sky-high. The S&P's forward
price/earnings multiple, at 23.6, is still within earshot of its all-time high of 25.3 in December of last year. By
comparison, the average price/earnings multiple for S&P 500 stocks during the past decade is a meager 16,
and the average is just 19 during the past five years.
Mr. Cripps argues that just as Wal-Mart Stores' shares quadrupled between the mid-1980s and early 1990s,
then plateaued until 1997 as earnings caught up to expectations, "a lot of tech stocks, like Cisco and Oracle,
will go through a prolonged trading range as fundamentals catch up."
It is already happening. Dell Computer is trading at roughly the same price it was at the end of 1998, even as
its earnings doubled and its price/earnings ratio fell to 42 from more than 90.
Another reason for concern: Companies wishing to brace investors for poor third-quarter earnings will
probably do so in the next few weeks. And while the so-called Fed model suggests valuations are getting
better, it does say the market is 37% overvalued, not undervalued.
"There's still a historically high degree of overvaluation in the market that suggests you shouldn't expect
outsize returns without [embracing] a lot of risk," Mr. Yardeni said. "The Fed model has shown an impressive
degree of accuracy over the years." (Indeed, the model said stocks were 18% undervalued in January 1996;
stocks have surged 132% since.)
"It may be that 2000 marks the start of a period of more mundane equity returns as multiples compress,"
Robert Pelosky, Morgan Stanley's global strategist, said.
Others argue that the gains will be in the small- and mid-capitalization stocks that have been leading the
charge lately, not the favorite tech and big-name stocks that starred in recent years. "The average stock's
valuation is very reasonable, if not compelling, but I would not overweight technology," Mr. Cripps said.
Friday's Market Activity
Market averages finished little changed in a session that, aside from the stock-trading hoax that victimized
Emulex, lived up to expectations that Wall Street would endure some typically sleepy summer Friday trading.
The Nasdaq Composite Index didn't relinquish the momentum it has shown in finishing higher in 10 of the 11
trading sessions coming into Friday. The index did finish lower, but by just 10.60 points, a decline of 0.26%,
to 4042.68. The Dow Jones Industrial Average close was 11192.63, up 9.89.
Coca-Cola lost $1.94 to $56 after Salomon Smith Barney cut its volume projections for the Atlanta soft-drink
company, citing softness in several markets around the globe, including North America.
Northrop Grumman gained $1.98 to 78. SG Cowen boosted its rating on the Los Angeles defense contractor,
citing an outlook for strong third-quarter earnings, as well as other factors.
Lernout & Hauspie Speech Products (Nasdaq) lost 4.63 to 30.06. The Belgium-based developer of
speech-recognition technology confirmed a report in Friday's Wall Street Journal that Gaston Bastiaens will
step down as president and chief executive.
Honeywell added 69 cents to 39.88 after saying it would sell its automotive consumer-products group
business, which includes antifreeze, spark plugs, filters and other car-care products.
Wind River Systems (Nasdaq) gained 2.75 to 39.69. The Alameda, Calif., Internet software developer was
named by Standard & Poor's to the S&P MidCap 400 Index.
