There's a MarketFor News WithoutA Liberal Slant
There's a Market For News Without A Liberal Slant
By Bob Zelnick
885 words
24 August 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
A22
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
After years of dodging liberal bullets, John Stossel, the ABC News correspondent who specializes in
debunking liberal social and political mythology, finally provided his opponents with an easy target. In a
February report, "The Food You Eat," he suggested that organic produce offers nothing in the way of health
benefits to justify the higher price. He also reported that tests conducted for ABC News "surprisingly found no
pesticide residue on the conventional samples or the organic."
But when the gadfly Environmental Working Group checked with the two scientists who served as project
consultants, both denied conducting such tests. Despite that information, Mr. Stossel foolishly repeated his
claim in a live conversation with anchor Cynthia McFadden when the report was rebroadcast in July. With
serious critics now wondering what was going on, ABC launched its own investigation. It found that Mr.
Stossel had been wrong: There had been no testing for pesticide residue on either the organic or
conventional produce.
Environmental activists and their allies called for his head. But Mr. Stossel got away with a reprimand and an
on-air retraction. His producer was suspended for a month with no pay.
Contrary to the claims of liberal activists, Mr. Stossel does not systematically distort or misrepresent the
news. He can point to a closet filled with 19 Emmys and numerous other awards for distinguished journalism.
But his most lasting contribution to network television news may well lie in demonstrating that there is a mass
viewing audience out there for reports that approach important subjects from a conservative perspective.
Mr. Stossel, once a consumer advocate of the Ralph Nader stripe, has become a cash cow for ABC News. In
the early 1990s, to fend off a Rupert Murdoch bid for his services, ABC reluctantly guaranteed Mr. Stossel
four prime time hour-long specials per year in addition to his regular "20/20" reports. His now classic
inaugural effort, "Are We Scaring Ourselves to Death?" an assault on the over-reaction to threats from
everything from crime to chemicals, stunned ABC executives with its healthy audience rating. Subsequent
Stossel reports also played to good numbers. His organic vs. conventional food segment drew an audience of
12 million viewers.
This should surprise no one. In polls conducted during the current political campaign, 60% of those
responding describe themselves as at least "somewhat conservative," twice the percentage acknowledging
liberal views. And when a cable network like Mr. Murdoch's Fox News Channel offers an outlet for such
distinguished conservative journalists as Brit Hume, Tony Snow and Fred Barnes, audience ratings gallop
ahead. While the more traditional MSNBC edged Fox in the numbers battle during the Democratic
convention, Fox clobbered MSNBC during the GOP convention. Moreover, Fox's audience is growing faster
than either MSNBC's or CNN's, and there have been recent evenings when the audience for "Special
Report," anchored by Mr. Hume, has matched the combined viewership of MSNBC and CNN in that time slot.
Why haven't more network news executives recognized the gold in them there hills? Perhaps because they
are creatures of a liberal news establishment that study after study documents as being far to the left of the
audience it seeks to attract. Discerning no professional need for editorial balance -- as opposed to racial and
ethnic diversity -- in the newsroom, they recognize no potential economic benefit either.
As professional descendents of those who covered the civil rights movement, Vietnam, Watergate, the
feminist insurrection, Roe v. Wade and the birth of the environmental movement, liberal reporters are too
often weighted with the intellectual baggage of an earlier generation. Their view of African- and
Hispanic-Americans is frozen in a time of victimhood. Many are today nearly incapable of covering education,
affirmative action, church-burning or alleged police brutality without their preconceptions showing.
They are skeptical of the military and adversarial toward political leaders, while trusting of nearly any exercise
of government power. Industry and pollution are one and the same. The notion of risk assessment or
cost-benefit analysis is an alien concept smacking of cop-out. That the question of abortion can raise serious

--- Page 29 ---
Page 29 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
moral issues is fundamentalist religious nonsense. That few legal scholars saw it as a constitutional right prior
to 1973 is irrelevant.
As most who have mingled with my former colleagues have come to conclude, they are generally fine, highly
intelligent, immensely talented and dedicated people who come to work each day determined to do an honest
job. Most would never approach a story with conscious bias, and certainly not with any intention to distort the
facts. But they are what they eat, and most have been nurtured on a diet of liberal gruel spoon fed by older
colleagues, professors and their favorite think tanks and interest groups. They would be better journalists if
their employers made a conscious effort to recruit and hire a reasonable number of qualified conservative
journalists.
They would also be richer for the experience. And as the case of John Stossel suggests, the networks would
be richer too.
---
Mr. Zelnick, a professor of journalism at Boston University, worked for ABC News from 1977 to 1998.

Japan's Central Bank Has Been Vilified for BoostIn Rates, but Some See Pot of Gold Longer Term
World Stock Markets
Japan's Central Bank Has Been Vilified for Boost In Rates, but Some See Pot of Gold Longer Term
By Michael R. Sesit
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,351 words
22 August 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
C14
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
LONDON -- The alarm went off too early? Your hangover is killing you?
Could be worse. You could be governor Masaru Hayami or one of his eight colleagues at Japan's central
bank.
Earlier this month, these officials -- or, at least, seven of them -- voted to increase the bank's key overnight
interest rate to 0.25% from effectively zero. The rate rise, which ended Japan's 18-month experiment with
trying to pump-prime its economy with zero rates, was the first in a decade. Since then, the bank's board has
been vilified. Japan's government labeled the decision premature and ruling Liberal Democratic Party
politicians called for Mr. Hayami's head. The International Monetary Fund warned of a new recession.
Meanwhile, Tokyo's stock market, as measured by the Nikkei 225-stock index, remains as crippled as ever,
down 23% from its April highs and off 15% for the year to date.
"The reasons given to investors [for the rate rise] aren't quite convincing," says Ernst Glanzmann, an equity
analyst at Bank Julius Baer & Co. in Zurich. "We still have an economy that's sinking. . . . It's difficult to
understand why the central bank is raising rates." Adds Morgan Stanley Dean Witter & Co. in a report to
clients: "The BOJ will now be under much more pressure to explain itself."
Others say the 75-year-old Mr. Hayami should get credit for asserting the bank's independence from political
influence. The government had sought vainly to delay the interest-rate decision by a month.
"People have totally overreacted," says Michael Belkin, who heads his own investment-advisory firm in New
York.
"It's not like the central bank is pushing the economy back over a cliff." A quarter of a percentage point, he
adds, "is an insignificant move. Who knows? It might even be stimulative."
In the short-run, fund managers use epithets like "nervous" and "confused" to describe the Tokyo stock
market. Further out, though, might lie a pot of gold.
Expecting corporate earnings forecasts to be raised, Edmund Brandt, a Japanese equity manager at Fleming
Asset Management, says his firm projects a 32% rise in the broad Topix index of Tokyo stocks in 12 months,
to 1950.
Despite the rate increase, he contends that monetary policy remains easy. Favoring the technology,
telecom and service sectors, Mr. Brandt is high on Canon, Internet security provider Trend Micro and Rohm.
At Global Asset Management, Richard Worts, head of the United Kingdom client division, advises ignoring
indexes and focusing on "quality companies."
About 10% of GAM's private-client portfolios are invested in an in-house Japan fund run by stock picker Paul
Kirkby.
The picker's top picks: Orix, Matsushita Electric Industrial, Sony, Rohm, TDK, Daito Trust Construction,
Canon, Yamanouchi Pharmaceutical, Credit Saison and Nikko Securities.
Japan's stock market reminds Michael Hughes, the chief investment officer at Baring Asset Management Ltd.,
of the U.K. market in the 1970s: boom, collapse, then five years of restructuring during which stocks were in
"a trading market" -- fluctuating between well-defined highs and lows -- "not a growth market."

--- Page 31 ---
Page 31 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"At the end of that time, you had a little change in the form of Mrs. [Margaret] Thatcher, and the beginning of
a new era for the market," says the Englishman. "The hope is that the same will happen in Japan."
When do Japan's five years of purgatory end? "Arguably quite soon," Mr. Hughes says.
Like almost everyone else, Baring likes Canon. Other favorites include Fanuc, NEC, Takeda Chemical
Industries and Toshiba. "That gives you a tech play, a global play and a currency play [on dollar revenue],"
Mr. Hughes says.
A final word from one of the many skeptics: Credit Suisse Asset Management has been selling Japanese
shares for the past six months. Relative to the MSCI World index, the firm is three percentage points
underweighted toward Tokyo, down from being five points overweight.
"The process on restructuring until March had been very impressive, but has since fallen off a cliff; and we're
concerned that corporations -- now that they have decent earnings growth coming back -- have become
complacent about restructuring," says Kevin Hebner, the firm's global strategist in Tokyo.
What's more, he worries about the headwinds facing economic recovery: slowing global growth, higher
energy prices, less public spending and a strong yen.
Given these, "the last thing we need now is a tighter monetary policy," Mr. Hebner says.
Yesterday's Market Activity
Most Asian markets ended moderately weaker, while most European benchmark indexes were little changed.
The Dow Jones World Stock Index fell 0.12 point, or 0.05%, to 243.04. Excluding the U.S., the index fell 1.15
points, or 0.66%, to 173.88.
In TOKYO, the Nikkei 225 index fell 1.5%, or 240.31 points, to 16040.18, as investors sold select blue-chip
stocks in thin trading. Among the losers were technology blue chips. Sony fell 180 yen to 10,430 yen, and
Advantest dropped 960 yen to 19,920 yen. A few technology shares rallied, however, on expectations that
demand for computer memory chips would lift earnings. Toshiba rose 29 yen to 1,034 yen, and NEC climbed
45 yen to 2,995 yen.
In HONG KONG, the Hang Seng Index added 0.4%, or 61.48 points, to 17501.48, lifted by gains in the
banking sector on investor confidence that the U.S. interest rates won't rise this week. HSBC rose 1.50
Hong Kong dollars to HK$110, Dah Sing Financial jumped HK$1.50, to HK$37, and Hang Seng Bank rose 75
Hong Kong cents to HK$84.75. Hutchison Whampoa fell 50 HK cents to HK$113, as investors continued to
sell after the company's decision to give up plans to run a third-generation wireless network in Germany.
In LONDON, the FTSE 100 index ended just slightly lower, off 1.5 points to 6542.2 in lethargic trading as
some investors locked in profits. Bookham Technology, for example, was down 4% to GBP 38 after takeover
bid rumors had moved the stock higher Friday. Boots rose 3.2% to 516 pence on speculation that U.S. retail
giant Wal-Mart Stores was on the verge of a GBP 6 billion bid for the U.K. drugstore chain.
In FRANKFURT, the Xetra DAX Index declined 0.5%, or 33.08 points, to 7199.34. Index heavyweight
Deutsche Telekom dragged the market lower, down 5.3% to 43.67 euros, as investors worry increasingly
over the "rationale" behind having paid such high prices for German universal mobile telephone services
licenses. Siemens, on the other hand, climbed 2.5% to 174.21 euros, lifted by investor confidence that the
company will win a string of orders to build the UMTS network infrastructure.
In PARIS, the CAC 40 index eased down 0.3%, or 22.72 points, to 6571.63. Suez Lyonnaise des Eaux led
the stock exchange lower, dropping 3.2% to 181.60 euros, as investors bailed out of the water utility on news
of a possible tie-up with Germany's E.on.

EUROPE/MIDEAST
France Outlines Sale
Of Mobile Licenses
For $18.2 Billion
The French government published long-awaited details of its $18.2 billion plan to sell off four third-generation
mobile-phone licenses, setting a Jan. 31 deadline for bids. Opting for a so-called beauty contest in which
bidders will be judged by the strength of business plans rather than the amount they are ready to spend for
licenses, the Finance Ministry made it clear there would be no change in the 32.5 billion franc ($4.54 billion)
price of each license. Winning bidders would pay half the fee in 2001-02 and the rest over the next 13 years.
Friday's details of the process were released a day after Germany's auction of third-generation licenses
ended with total bids of 50.52 billion euros ($46.33 billion).
Separately, France Telecom's chief financial officer said the company won't directly pay the 8.4 billion euros
for the German third-generation license it won last week with its partner MobilCom of Germany. He said
France Telecom's 50% stake in MobilCom Multimedia, the consortium that won the German license, will be
transferred to MobilCom as part of the two companies' agreement when France Telecom acquired a 28.5%
stake in MobilCom. As a result, the cost of the German license will be consolidated in MobilCom's accounts.
Euro Zone's Core Inflation Rose in July
Consumer prices in the 11-nation euro zone rose 2.4% from a year earlier in July -- the same pace as in June
-- and were up 0.2% from the month before, less than the 0.5% month-to-month rise in June, the European
Union's statistics office said. Excluding energy prices, consumer prices rose 1.4% in July from a year before,
up from 1.2% core inflation in June, Eurostat said. The data put further pressure on the European Central
Bank to raise its key short-term refinancing rate, now at 4.25%, when its policy-making council meets Aug.
31. Analysts had expected year-to-year inflation in the euro zone to remain unchanged at 2.4% or to rise
slightly to 2.5%. Either reading is well above the ECB's inflation-tolerance threshold of 2.0%.
Iran Announces New Oil, Gas Discoveries
Iran has made new discoveries of 1.07 billion barrels of oil and 800 billion cubic feet of natural gas, the
exploration director of state-owned National Iranian Oil, Mahmoud Mohaddes, was quoted as saying by state
television. He said more reserves could be found after drilling appraisal wells in the Changuleh field in
western Ilam province. The finds were the second major hydrocarbon discoveries announced in Iran in a
week. Iran announced on Aug. 13 that it had discovered a new onshore gas field, called Homa, with
recoverable reserves of 4.7 trillion cubic feet of natural gas and 58 million barrels of gas liquids in Fars
province.
Ricardo Agrees to Reduced QXL Offer
British Internet auctioneer QXL.com said Ricarde.de and its majority shareholders agreed to a revised offer
for the German company, cutting the amount QXL would pay by about 22%. QXL said Friday it had revised
its offer because of a 20% decline in Ricardo's sales for the quarter ended June 30. QXL said that under the
revised deal, the maximum amount payable to Ricardo shareholders will be GBP 171.2 million ($256.7
million) based on QXL's closing share price on Thursday of 59 pence a share. Ricardo shareholders will
receive 34 QXL ordinary shares for each Ricardo share held.

-- German taxpayers reacted angrily to the disclosure that 2.4 billion marks ($1.13 billion) of public money will
be used to cover mounting losses at the Expo 2000 world fair in Hanover. The government and Lower

THE AMERICAS
U.S. Launches WTO Telecom Complaint
The U.S. has formally launched a World Trade Organization case that it hopes will force Mexico to open its
domestic telecommunications market, according to diplomats in Geneva. They said the U.S. complaint,
seeking consultations with Mexico on the dispute, was filed last week and accuses Mexico of violating its
obligations under the WTO's agreement on trade in services. U.S. Trade Representative Charlene
Barshefsky, apparently responding to pressure from companies such as AT&T and WorldCom, this month
accused Mexico of allowing Telefonos de Mexico to charge rival carriers unfairly high fees for connections to
the Mexican network and barring them from access to bandwidths needed for provision of Internet services.
Telmex has rejected the accusations.
Output Halted at VW's Mexican Plant
Talks between Volkswagen's Mexican unit and unions representing 12,600 workers were to continue last
night, union officials said, as production was halted for the third day at the only factory in the world producing
the German auto maker's popular New Beetle. The strike at the plant in Puebla, 60 miles east of Mexico City,
began when talks over pay temporarily collapsed Friday after 10 days. Unions are demanding a 35% wage
increase, while VW is offering a raise closer to expected Mexican inflation this year of about 9%. VW is
Mexico's largest auto exporter, and the Puebla plant produced a record 410,000 vehicles last year, up from
339,000 in 1998.
Canada to Comply on Generic Drugs
Canada said it would obey a World Trade Organization ruling that it must change its drug-patent law by Oct.
7. A WTO arbitrator issued the deadline Friday after Canada and the European Union, which had launched
the trade complaint, failed to agree on "a reasonable period of time" for implementing a March ruling. The
panel said Canada must change a provision in its law that allows generic-drug makers to start manufacturing
and stockpiling products six months before patents expire on brand-name drugs.
U.S. to Boost Ecuador, Bolivia Outlays
U.S. Secretary of State Madeleine Albright, concluding a South American tour Saturday, said the U.S. will
seek $15 million in additional aid for Ecuador to handle refugees that might spill into the country because of
the U.S.-backed antidrug initiative in Colombia. Congress already approved $20 million for Ecuador as part of
its $1.3 billion aid package, known as Plan Colombia. She praised Bolivia's efforts Friday against cocaine
output and said $110 million in additional funds have been approved for alternative projects.

-- Foreign investment in Colombia fell 85% in the first quarter from a year before to $59.3 million, according to
provisional central-bank figures. The fall was largely the result of foreign oil firms reducing their investments
by $424 million, compared with a $69.7 million increase a year before.

The combined first-half earnings of 13 listed companies of Hyundai Group, South Korea's largest

accused of abuses. "This is a major defeat for the reform movement," said analyst Dede Oetomo.

EUROPE/AFRICA
German, Irish Prices
Increase Chances
Of ECB Rate Move
German wholesale prices in July were up 5.8% from a year earlier -- the steepest rise in 11 years -- and rose
0.3% from the month before, renewing expectations that the European Central Bank will raise euro-zone
interest rates when its policy-making council meets Aug. 31. Excluding fuel costs, German wholesale prices
rose 3.2% year to year, the government said. Economists had forecast wholesale prices would rise by 5.6%
year to year and 0.1% from June. Meanwhile, Ireland's consumer inflation surged to 6.2% in July, the
highest level since February 1985 and more than twice the average inflation rate for the euro zone's 11
countries. Prices also rose 0.3% from the month before, as summer sales were more than offset by increased
costs of fuel, food and mortgages.
The harmonized index of consumer prices, which allows comparison with other European Union countries,
showed Irish inflation at 5.9% in July; in June, annual inflation for the euro zone was 2.4%. Elsewhere,
Finnish data showed consumer prices in July rose by a higher-than-expected 3.7% from a year before.
Lukoil, Tyumen Oil Seek to Bid for Onako
Two more Russian oil companies have filed preliminary documents to bid in the privatization sale of Onako,
one of the country's last state-owned oil companies, boosting competition for the asset. Lukoil, Russia's
largest oil company, and Tyumen Oil, the No. 5 oil producer, confirmed they filed papers with antitrust
authorities to receive permission to place a bid in the Sept. 14 sale. A winner is to be announced Sept. 19.
Onako is Russia's No. 11 oil company, with production of 160,000 barrels a day. In one sign that Russia's
privatization standards are improving, the government has set the starting price for the 85% stake in Onako at
$425 million, a level analysts said is fair. Russian oil companies Sibneft and Yukos and gas-pipeline builder
Stroitransgas last week said they would unite to place a joint bid for Onako.
Shell to Merge Global Trading Activities
Royal Dutch/Shell Group said it will integrate its global trading activities into a single business called Shell
Trading. Effective Jan. 1, Shell Trading will include the company's trading platforms for global crude-oil and oil
products, as well as chemical feedstocks in Europe and the U.S. It will also involve the company's natural-gas
and power-trading platforms in North America through Coral Energy and in Europe through Shell Energy.
Shell's world-wide shipping operations for crude oil and oil products and Coral Energy's marketing business
will also be included.
Euro Would Help Britain, Eichel Says
German Finance Minister Hans Eichel said the strong pound is hurting British exporters and Britain would do
well to adopt Europe's common currency, the euro. "I think Great Britain would be economically better off if it

were a member of the euro zone," he told business leaders in Bratislava, Slovakia, in response to a question.
Since the euro's introduction in January 1999, the pound has strengthened considerably against the euro,
making Britain's exports more expensive in most of the European Union, its biggest market.
Nigeria-U.S. Direct Flights Relaunched
Direct flights between Nigeria and the U.S. resumed after a six-year gap with a South African Airways
promotional flight from Lagos to New York. The carrier sealed a deal to operate the lucrative Lagos-New York
route on behalf of ailing flag carrier Nigeria Airways. The U.S. ended a ban on direct air links with Nigeria in
December, a move analysts said stemmed from the country's return to democracy after 15 years of military
rule. Commercial flights are slated to begin Oct. 29.

China Unicom Asset Injection Is Delayed
China's government will delay for at least three years a plan to inject a telecommunications network controlled

Confirming a report in China NetworkWorld, a state-run magazine, the official said the ministry's telecom

last week for not "clearly and explicitly" disclosing the level of public subscriptions to the offering by
eWorldofSports.com.
Bank of Japan Upgrades Outlook a Bit
The Bank of Japan left its overall assessment of Japan's economy unchanged in its monthly report, but
slightly upgraded its evaluation of household income, saying compensation of employees has stopped
declining. It said gradual recovery is likely to continue unless there are major adverse external shocks. The
upgrade of household-income conditions in August is important because it provides some clues as to why the
central bank's policy board arrived at its decision last week to end its zero-interest-rate policy.

through a combination of the joint venture, a debt-to-equity swap by the banks, a divestiture of assets and

company to reduce its debt load itself, he said.

Hong Kong Pushes Companies on Retirement Program---Controversies Swirl Around Ad Campaign by Competing Fund Firms
Hong Kong Pushes Companies on Retirement Program --- Controversies Swirl Around Ad Campaign
by Competing Fund Firms
By Sarah McBride
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,057 words
14 August 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
No Page Citation
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Hong Kong is in the throes of one of the most expensive marketing campaigns of the year, but not many are
buying the product.
The city is requiring all employers to sign up for a retirement program by Dec. 1. By the end of the year,
financial-services companies will have spent some 500 million Hong Kong dollars (US$64.1) chasing a pot of
gold estimated to reach HK$1 trillion over the next 15 years. But so far, these firms have signed up just 30%
of the target market of 300,000 employers.
Moreover, controversies involving advertisements and the plethora of freebies being handed out have swirled
around the campaign since the beginning of the year, when firms were allowed to start courting customers.
All the promotion is certainly increasing awareness of the retirement program -- called the Mandatory
Provident Fund -- but, critics say, it may be turning potential customers off and bewildering them to boot.
"The MPF is supposed to be whiter than white, cleaner than clean, and instead we've got this tawdry
competition," complains Ian Perkin, chief economist of the Hong Kong General Chamber of Commerce, which
has launched its own MPF plan with CMG First State. "The average employer is getting confused," says
Stewart Aldcroft, of Standard Chartered Bank, which also offers an MPF plan.
Most companies in Hong Kong are small businesses that don't have pension programs. The roughly 3.3
million employees who work for these companies will pay 5% of their salaries and then have it matched with a
5% company contribution, with monthly contributions capped at HK$2,000. The MPF now has 20 authorized
providers, but asset managers say there is enough business for only six to a dozen firms. To become one of
that select group, companies have to fight hard.
The race started late last year, when companies started hunting down customers, even though they couldn't
yet sign them up to a plan. Manulife Provident Funds started its Manulife Assistance Program to provide
employers with MPF information while building up a database on potential customers; any company that
signed up got a shot at a lucky draw for a free holiday.
Now the insurer is offering an "Early Bird Special." Companies that sign up get HK$500 credits to MPF
accounts, or free dental care or low-price outpatient services for the company's employees. Nicholas Crouch,
president of Manulife Provident Funds, says regulators asked about the promotion in the wake of complaints
from competitors, but didn't ask Manulife to withdraw or alter it. "Our attitude is we are trying to encourage
people to sign up early," he says.
HSBC, meanwhile, offers free subscriptions to Time magazine and discounts at a local wine vendor for
companies that sign up with their plans.
"It does sound a bit over-commercial for something that is very serious," says Michael Button of consultancy
Watson Wyatt Hong Kong Ltd. "Once you get down to a certain level of less sophisticated employer, they
may be swayed by free holidays and raffles." Steve Butler of William Mercer Ltd. is more blunt: "It would
seem a bit like bribery."
Indeed, in May the Hong Kong Monetary Authority, which governs banks, issued rules on bank conduct that
addressed the issue of integrity in MPF activity.
For the average consumer, it's the flood of advertisements that has heralded the arrival of MPF. ACNielsen
Media International says MPF advertising spending this year through June totaled HK$207 million, mostly on
television ads, with millions more to come this year.

--- Page 40 ---
Page 40 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Despite those sums, standing out from the crowd is hard because advertisers are in the unusual position of
promoting a product that doesn't yet exist. Contributions to the funds won't begin until the turn of the year,
which rules out the usual fund advertising mainstay of performance figures. Participating fund companies,
scrambling to sign up customers to funds that haven't started investing, can create ads that boost their name
and reputation and not much else.
HSBC has run ads stating, "Your MPF could be worth millions." Younger workers with 40 years of retirement
savings ahead of them may end up with about HK$3.4 million at retirement if they invest conservatively,
calculates Michael Button of Watson Wyatt Worldwide; he adds that after inflation, it will be worth much less
than it would be today. But the average worker is 39 years old, and will have about HK$1 million, not adjusted
for inflation, at retirement.
The MPFA says it has received just two complaints concerning advertisements that resulted in any action on
its part, and those two cases were resolved. While the MPFA won't identify the parties or issues involved, or
say how those issues were cleared up, Manulife says one of its promotions has been the subject of regulatory
inquiry.
MPF promotion is especially tricky -- not only are advertisers selling an intangible product with no record, but
they're trying to sell it to less sophisticated customers. Big companies likely already have a pension plan and
are sticking with that provider for their MPF; the battle now is over the medium-size and smaller employers,
most of whom are unfamiliar with pension issues and haven't yet chosen providers.
Employers seem to be putting off their decisions until the last minute, meaning "every company in the market
will be turning up the volume toward the end of the year," says Mr. Robson of Bates.
An alternative: They may not choose at all. "One of the big problems come Dec. 1 is going to be compliance,"
says Mr. Perkin of the Chamber of Commerce.
It may well be the customers that have the last laugh. Financial-service providers say some savvy companies
have signed up with a variety of MPF providers, scooping up free gifts at every turn. After the Dec. 1
deadline, it will become more difficult to change providers, but until then, employers have the prerogative to
change companies as many times as they like, and many are. They may take all the freebies they're offered,
says Mr. Perkin. "But I don't think they'll remain loyal."

Page 41 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Is ZimbabweDevaluationJust a Start?---Farmers, BusinessmenWelcome 24% MoveBut Demand Change
International
Is Zimbabwe Devaluation Just a Start? --- Farmers, Businessmen Welcome 24% Move But Demand
Change
By Robert Block
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
706 words
3 August 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
A9
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
JOHANNESBURG, South Africa -- Economists, businessmen and farmers took a wait-and-see attitude
yesterday toward Zimbabwe's surprise 24% devaluation of its currency against the U.S. dollar.
On Tuesday night, Zimbabwe's new finance minister, Simba Makoni, bowing to months of pressure from
industry, announced that the government had agreed to peg the Zimbabwean dollar at 50 for each U.S. dollar
from 38 previously. The artificially high 38 to the U.S. dollar rate had been a source of friction between the
private sector and the government for a year. Until now, though, President Robert Mugabe had refused to
yield, even in the face of collapsing businesses, dwindling foreign-exchange reserves, 70% inflation and
higher interest rates.
"The devaluation is an indicator that we could be seeing the return of common sense taking over again," said
John Robertson, an independent economist in Zimbabwe's capital, Harare.
Colin Cloete, vice president of the Commercial Farmers' Union, which represents the country's mostly white
commercial landowners, also welcomed the currency move. "It means I actually might be able to cover my
costs for next season's inputs," Mr. Cloete said.
But both said the move didn't go far enough, as the Zimbabwean dollar continued to trade on the black
market at 65 to the U.S. dollar. "There must be a controlled slide of the currency. We can't float it, because
there just aren't any foreign-currency reserves. But the government must continue to bring the rate down
further if it is to have any lasting effect. Still, it's a first step," Dr. Robertson said.
What makes the optimistic reaction to the government's move after months of resistance unusual is that it
came in the midst of a one-day national general strike that shut down much of Zimbabwe yesterday. Shops
and factories were closed and streets deserted in the three major cities -- Harare, Bulawayo and Masvingo --
as farmers, workers and the political opposition delivered the most broadly based challenge yet to Mr.
Mugabe's 20-year rule.
The Zimbabwe Congress of Trade Unions called the strike to protest months of escalating violence ahead of
the recent elections, and continued violent occupations of white-owned farms by self-styled veterans of the
former Rhodesia's 1970s liberation war. The stoppage also had the backing of white commercial farmers,
many businesses and the main opposition Movement for Democratic Change.
The government has warned that it intends to step up its land-resettlement program to include almost 75% of
the country's 4,500 white-owned farms. The government says it will give the land to 500,000 poor blacks
before October.
In the run-up to parliamentary elections in June, Mr. Mugabe made land redistribution a major plank of his
campaign -- a policy that led to invasions of white-owned farms and widespread violence. The farms at the
center of the dispute over land reform provide the nation's economic backbone; agriculture is thought to make
up 20% of gross domestic product, and tobacco exports bring in about $400 million annually, about 30% of
Zimbabwe's foreign-currency revenue. About 60% of Zimbabwe's manufacturing industry is linked to
agriculture, and the banking and insurance sectors have huge exposure in commercial farming.
Mr. Cloete said that while the strike had been peaceful in the cities, at least half a dozen farms reported
troubles. There were threats against farmers by squatters, and one farmer was badly beaten, he said.
Dr. Robertson said that while the devaluation might help Zimbabwe's battered export sector and stem the tide
of bankruptcies -- at least six Zimbabwe gold mines have closed in the past two months because they

--- Page 42 ---
Page 42 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
weren't able to cover their production costs at the 38-to-the-dollar exchange rate -- a real economic
turnaround was unlikely until the land occupations stopped.
"Devaluation is meaningless by itself. It must be attached to the rule of law, respect for property rights and the
end of this stupid idea that land can be expropriated and given away for free," he said.

Worthless Rubles HaveTheir Uses in KeepingRoofs From Leaking---With Cloth and WastepaperIn Short Supply, CurrencyFills the Bill in Russia...
Worthless Rubles Have Their Uses in Keeping Roofs From Leaking --- With Cloth and Wastepaper In
Short Supply, Currency Fills the Bill in Russia Now
By Andrew Higgins
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,098 words
26 July 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
ULYANOVSK, Russia -- Dmitri Nikiforov, a capitalist convert, closely watches Russia's money supply: The
survival of his factory depends on it.
Sitting beneath a portrait of Vladimir Lenin, leader of the Communist revolution and this Volga River city's
most famous son, Mr. Nikiforov scribbles figures to explain why pumping too many rubles into the system
causes havoc, while closing the spigot too tightly means trouble, too. "It's a delicate balance," he says.
So what does he consider the optimum level?
"As a rule I like to see it at about 100 to 150 tons a month," says the 53-year-old manager. "But last year, it
went up to as much as 400 tons a month because of a shortage of rags."
Tons? Rags? Such terms don't figure prominently in the texts of Milton Friedman and other monetarists of the
Chicago School, but they dominate the pioneering work of Mr. Nikiforov and his colleagues at the Ulyanovsk
School -- better known as the Ulyanovsk Roofing Material Factory.
When Russia's financial markets buckled in August 1998 and the ruble collapsed, Mr. Nikiforov had a
brainchild. Already wrestling with severe shortages of old cloth and wastepaper, his basic raw materials, he
proposed an unorthodox way to mop up Russia's excess money supply.
"We'd already tried using wood chips and even straw, but to no avail," says Mr. Nikiforov. "We found that
bank notes worked much better."
Today, his company is awash in money -- an unusual development in an impoverished region still named
after the Bolshevik leader (Lenin's original surname was Ulyanov) and blighted by a lingering faith in his
bankrupt economic creed.
Soaked in water and mashed into a porridge, millions of rubles in shredded bank notes cascade through a
network of pipes and concrete ducts. Mixed with scraps of paper and shredded cloth to form a thick paste, the
rubles are rolled into sheets and coated with tar to form a roofing material called ruberoid.
Russia has more money than it can cope with. It has mountains of the stuff, the legacy of a decade of political
tumult and financial promiscuity that has seen the Central Bank issue three different sets of bank notes and
has left warehouses bulging with unwanted and damaged notes. Frequent "monetary reform" has caused
agony for millions of Russians holding abruptly suspended, worthless paper money. For the roofing industry,
though, it has been a godsend.
A ton of rubles costs less than $15, not even a third as much as scrap paper. The rock-bottom prices have
Mr. Nikiforov reaching for the stars.
Not only do rubles help plug leaky roofs, he says; they also eventually could revolutionize personal hygiene.
He shows off certificates from the health ministry and epidemiological control department certifying that bank
notes pose no health hazard as toilet paper. He has even passed his Geiger counter over them, and knows
that they aren't radioactive.
Toilet tissue made of rubles -- known in the trade as MBS, a Russian acronym for "Special Waste Paper" --
would be "a bit rough" and not particularly absorbent, he says, but it would be cheap.
In the factory's main rolling room, Natalya Strada, a supervisor, fingers a heavy chunk of freshly minted
ruberoid specked with tiny strips of purple 500-ruble notes. "It's good that useless money has been given a

--- Page 44 ---
Page 44 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
new life" on roofs, she says. But to use it as toilet paper, she says, is "going too far." She would rather just
have "a few extra rubles in my pocket."
Mr. Nikiforov's exact recipe for ruberoid is a secret. He wants to patent it before providing details, adding that
a rival factory near Moscow tried to copy his idea but had trouble making it work. The proportions are
important, he says: A ruble content of more than 30% leaves his roofing products brittle and prone to leaks.
Mr. Nikiforov's ruble revolution began with a train trip to Moscow in late 1998, where he met Yuri Babichev,
the chain-smoking head of Mosoblvtorresurs, a state-owned scrap dealer. A veteran wholesaler of old socks,
dogeared schoolbooks and other recyclable waste, Mr. Babichev had cut a deal with Russia's Central Bank
to dispose of damaged and defunct rubles. (The bank was burning old money but stopped because
environmentalists complained.)
"They cried with joy when I said I'd take some of the money off their hands," recalls Mr. Nikiforov. Offered a
free sample, he returned to Ulyanovsk with two bricks of compressed shredded rubles in his briefcase. A few
weeks later, he got a truckload of money and started a trial production run.
The tests started out badly. Rubles didn't dissolve easily and kept floating to the surface of a big vat of
bubbling water. Then came problems with clotting: Ribbons of rubles tended to congeal into lumpy pellets.
Workers, many of whom hadn't been paid in months, were also trouble: "They wanted to glue the notes back
together," as if they might then be worth something, recalls the factory's general director, Yuri Titienko.
But Mr. Nikiforov persevered and finally prevailed. His technicians worked out the kinks in a maze of pipes
and and in the production process. And, thanks to a barter deal with the local government, the factory also
found a way to pay its back taxes. To dispatch some of its obligations, it now supplies schools and a clinic in
the nearby town of Novoulyanovsk with free ruberoid. Recycling has come full circle: "Money is the root of all
evil. It should be turned into roofs," says the mayor, Anatoly Arkhangelsky, who covered his own garage with
ruberoid. Each week, Mr. Nikiforov sends two big Kamaz trucks on a 450-mile journey to Moscow to collect
about 30 tons of rubles. And he is scouting for bank notes in Siberia. He also has heard of a ruble stockpile in
Boris Yeltsin's hometown, Yekaterinburg. The Central Bank won't discuss the size or location of its ruble
rubbish dumps.
"Marx and Lenin predicted we wouldn't need gold and would one day make toilets out of it," says Valery
Perfilov, director of a complex of dusty museums in the center of town dedicated to Lenin. "We don't have
golden toilets yet, but we have roofs covered with money. Who knows what might happen next?"

Page 45 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
A Genesis, of Sorts, in Downtown Los Angeles---Developers Rush to BuildIn Long-Shunned AreasAs City Offers Subsidies
The Property Report
A Genesis, of Sorts, in Downtown Los Angeles --- Developers Rush to Build In Long-Shunned Areas
As City Offers Subsidies
By Linda Sandler
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,082 words
26 July 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
B12
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
IN THE SOUTH Central district of Los Angeles, developer Kyle Arndt, president of Capital Vision Equities
LLC, is prowling the crowded aisles of a ramshackle clothing mart, where an outdated pair of Nike running
shoes goes for $60. Outside are massed Toyotas, Camrys, Cadillacs -- further evidence of "a huge pent-up
retail demand," he exults.
Mr. Arndt has reason to research buying habits at the Slauson SuperMall. One-and-a-half miles away, at
Slauson and Western avenues, where bulldozers have leveled an abandoned dairy, Capital Vision and its
partner, Katell Properties LLC, are preparing to build a $60 million mall called Chesterfield Square that will
have 250,000 square feet of retail stores, landscaping and a public plaza. To be anchored by a Home Depot
Inc. store and Food 4 Less Supermarkets, it will be the largest project in this inner-city area since the 1980s,
and one of the few to feature a village-style design.
"The suburbs wouldn't accept a strip mall. Why should they here?" asks Mr. Arndt.
Capital Vision, until now a low-income housing developer, and Katell, a specialist in suburban office and
industrial parks, aren't the only ones rushing to build in areas of Los Angeles where few used to tread.
Proposals have poured in for many of the 15 abandoned industrial sites made eligible in the past year for
subsidies by Mayor Richard Riordan under a plan called Genesis L.A., says Rocky Delgadillo, a deputy
mayor.
The Genesis plan just recently added six more sites. Officials also revealed that an independent for-profit
fund, managed by Roy Disney's family investment vehicle, Shamrock Holdings Inc., has raised $71 million of
institutional money for inner-city projects. "We aim to do well and do good," says Stanley P. Gold, a
Shamrock money manager.
Suitors for inner-city sites range from former Los Angeles Lakers basketball star Magic Johnson, whose
company, Johnson Development Corp., recently opened a movie theater in New York's Harlem, to Edward P.
Roski's Majestic Realty Co., developer of the new Staples Center arena downtown, and Texas-based
Trammel Crow Co. Most proposals, like Majestic's for Chinatown, involve light manufacturing, from food and
garments to high tech.
One reason for their interest: "Land everywhere else is overpriced," says Steve Soboroff, a California
developer and a mayoral candidate for 2001.
For decades, developers mostly shunned the inner cities. In South Los Angeles, where some stores
damaged in the 1992 riots still stand empty, there are 65% fewer grocery stores per capita than in the rest of
Los Angeles County, according to a study by Pepperdine University in Malibu, Calif.
But today, developers see work skills and enormous buying power concentrated there. "This is really urban
suburban," says Capital Vision's Mr. Arndt, pointing to the well-kept cottages, Spanish-style homes and
avenues of palm trees that surround the rubble-strewn site that will be Chesterfield Square, expected to be
completed early next year.
While the average household's income of $35,000 in South Los Angeles is roughly half as much as in
metropolitan areas, population density is three or four times as great, "so purchasing power is at least as
high" as in metro areas, says Don Spivack, deputy administrator of the Community Redevelopment Agency,
charged with reviewing development proposals for inner city sites.
But byzantine politics in South Los Angeles can stymie even the most experienced inner-city developer. Mr.
Johnson ran into roadblocks last year with his proposal to redevelop Santa Barbara Plaza, a rundown mall

--- Page 46 ---
Page 46 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
with decrepid tailor's and upholsterer's shops in the Crenshaw area that stands across the road from one of
his art deco movie theaters. He's now exploring joining forces with Capital Vision, which has options on most
of the plaza's 36 stores that must be bought before demolition begins. Capital Vision's politically savvy
founder and chairman, Chris Hammond, has run campaigns for former Los Angeles Mayor Tom Bradley and
Jesse L. Jackson.
Executives at Johnson Development couldn't be reached for comment.
Typically, inner-city developers demand subsidies. Though their land costs are cheaper than in, say, Beverly
Hills, demolition and cleanup can be exorbitant. Capital Vision's bulldozers razed not only the dairy but also
an auto-wrecking yard and a pickled-cabbage manufacturing facility. It knew of two fuel-storage tanks buried
on its site, which served various industrial purposes after 1900; it found three more tanks, one of which had
leaked. "You never know what you'll find. We half expected to find Jimmy Hoffa," jests Mr. Arndt, referring to
the late Teamsters chief.
Another problem: Inner-city landlords can't charge top rents because there's little competition for their space.
For Chesterfield Square, Gerald Katell, Capital Vision's partner, says he courted Vons, Albertson's and
Walgreen supermarkets, but was "turned down." Food 4 Less Supermarkets, a subsidiary of Ralph's Grocery
Co., Compton, Calif., accepted. Vons, now owned by Safeway Inc., had big plans to expand in the area after
the riots but later cut them back.
A spike in interest rates, or a slowing in the economy, could stop some projects. "The inner city is the last
resort of developers. And the last time they got interested, in the early 1990s, rates backed up and we went
backward again," says Linda Griego, a restaurateur in Los Angeles's business district and former deputy
mayor.
Besides the cost of inner-city development, developers must go through a long and arduous process of
building support in the community. Off and on for a year, starting in 1998, Capital Vision's Mr. Arndt knocked
on doors around the Slauson-Western site and attended community meetings, asking the locals what they
wanted. For one woman, it was a knitting shop -- not feasible, he says, but "not so different from middle-class
desires."
Some say Majestic ran into environmental opposition on its Chinatown project, an industrial business park on
32 acres of former rail yard, because it lacked experience in building community support. But John Semcken,
a Majestic vice president, says the opposition came from "groups that want to stop development at all cost."
He expects the $65 million project to be completed by the end of next year.

Page 47 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Longtime Losers: Some Funds Have SpentMuch of Their Lives on the Down Side
Fund Track
Longtime Losers: Some Funds Have Spent Much of Their Lives on the Down Side
By Karen Damato
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,513 words
21 July 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
You've seen those charts in mutual-fund ads, showing how $10,000 invested at a fund's launch would have
multiplied into a mountain of money over time.
Well, here is a group of charts you aren't likely to see: At some woebegone funds, that hypothetical initial
investment has declined in value -- sometimes by half and even more. While these funds usually have had
their moments in the sun, they have spent much more time in performance darkness to produce overall
negative results since their launches.
The fund with the longest money-losing longevity? It is Ameritor Industry Fund, founded way back in 1959
and still in business today despite a cumulative loss of 42.90%, according to fund tracker Lipper Inc. The tiny
fund was previously known as Steadman American Industry Fund and is a member of a fund family that for
many years was known for poor performance and large annual expenses.
Runners-up in this mostly bad-from-birth competition: U.S. Global Investors Gold Shares Fund, launched in
1970, followed by other precious metals funds introduced in the early 1980s. U.S. Global Gold has delivered
a negative 87.8% return since inception, according to Lipper, meaning that an initial $10,000 stake now would
be down to $1,220.
A look at long-longevity losers serves as a cautionary tale about some individual funds investors may want to
avoid. But more than that, it is a reminder of fund fads and once-popular strategies that didn't pan out for
many investors.
Back in the 1980s, for instance, many financial advisers routinely suggested that investors park a small
percentage of their assets in gold bullion or mining-stock funds as a hedge against runaway inflation or
other financial crises. "That was the nonthinking advice. It was everywhere," recalls Roy Weitz, publisher of
the Fundalarm.com Web site.
Such advice is rare today, after years of far-from-golden results. These days, Mr. Weitz says, investing in
gold "seems more like a philosophy than an investment."
Another once-hot category with plenty of since-inception losers: emerging-markets funds, which buy stocks in
the developing nations of Asia and Latin America. Such funds multiplied most dramatically in 1994 -- only to
be clobbered by the Mexican peso crisis in December of that year and the Asian financial crisis that unfolded
in 1997 and 1998. Numerous emerging-markets funds that appeared in 1994 and 1995 are still in the red
since inceptioneven after a huge 1999 when the average emerging-markets fund gained more than 70%,
according to Lipper.
In hindsight, there is a warning for investors about rushing into a suddenly hot sector. Most of the
emerging-markets funds appeared only after one of the category's best years, 1993. "Funds can come out at
precisely the wrong time," at the tail end of a period of strong results, notes Edward Rosenbaum, director of
research at Lipper, a unit of Reuters Group PLC. It can take months to get a new fund up and running, he
explains, and by then the hot performance that spurred the fund's creation may have cooled.
Another type of fund showing up on the longest-losers list: bear-market funds that are positioned to shield
investors from -- or perhaps profit from -- declining stock prices. One of the granddaddies of this category is
Gabelli Comstock Capital Value Fund, down a cumulative 18% from its start in 1985.
A far younger bear-market fund has posted the absolute steepest-since-inception negative return, according
to Lipper: ProFunds UltraShort OTC Fund has declined 96.39% since its June 1998 launch, meaning that an
initial $10,000 now would be worth only $361. Of course, that is not a big surprise given the fund's mandate: it

--- Page 48 ---
Page 48 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
aims to move in the opposite direction as the Nasdaq 100 index, and with twice the magnitude. Over the past
few years, the tech-heavy Nasdaq 100 has been one of the hottest market measures around.
To be sure, there are few investors who hold any fund continuously from inception. And a negative return
since inception doesn't mean a fund is fatally flawed, or that it hasn't proved profitable for some investors. For
instance, in market swoons including the 1987 crash, the Gabelli Comstock fund "has done what it is
designed to do, which is to make money," says Henry Van der Ebb, head of a group of
"nonmarket-correlated" funds at Gabelli Funds, Rye, N.Y. Bearish funds also starred for a while this past
spring, when stock-market barometers tumbled.
Still, a long negative record racked up by a fund should give investors pause. In the case of bear-market
funds, for instance, it is a reminder that, historically, stocks have gone up more often than down. Bear-market
funds will certainly have periods of strong performance, says Scott Wells, a financial adviser in Coral Gables,
Fla. But, he adds, "I don't think people are going to be able to time the market" and get in and out of those
funds at the right times.
All told, Lipper counts 491 individual stock funds or stock-fund share classes with negative returns since their
inception-6.5% of a total of 7,598 vehicles for which the data firm has since-inception results. (Many funds
have multiple share classes that differ in their sales commissions and annual expenses.) Not surprisingly,
many of the funds are far smaller than they were years ago.
Because relatively few diversified U.S.stock funds are on the list, it is interesting to look at the ones that are.
After Ameritor Industry, the oldest such funds with negative results are Frontier Equity Fund, down a
cumulative 58.73% from its April 1992 start, and Apex Mid Cap Growth Fund, down 9.79% from its December
1992 start. One of the best-known managers on the list is Donald Yacktman, whose Yacktman Focused Fund
is down 16.09% since its launch three years ago, in April 1997.
How do such losing funds manage to hold on to investors? A 1997 page one story in The Wall Street Journal
profiled investors who held shares in various Steadman funds for decades, despite their dismal record. Some
investors were driven by inertia or an unwillingness to part with fund shares received as a gift for a special
occasion. In other cases, the shares had been forgotten and ended up on state unclaimed-property lists.
Today at Ameritor Financial Corp. in Washington, new company President Jerome Kinney, son-in-law of the
deceased Charles Steadman, says he and his wife, Carole, are working to turn things around for the three
small Ameritor funds. Last month Ameritor hired portfolio manager Paul Dietrich, who also is manager of
Dominion Insight Growth Fund. Moving to less-expensive offices is one of the steps Mr. Kinney says he has
taken to trim "horrendous" expenses.
Over the years, Frontier Equity, which invests in volatile stocks of tiny companies, has tended to careen
between the top and the bottom of fund-performance charts. Manager James Fay says the fund's biggest
problem has been its "terrible" expenses, recently around 14% of assets a year. The fund has under $1
million in assets, like Ameritor Industry, so fixed costs take a big bite out of investors' returns.
Suresh Bhirud, manager of Apex Mid Cap, didn't return phone calls yesterday.
At Yacktman Focused, Mr. Yacktman says he continues to be enthusiastic about big holdings, including
tobacco giant Philip Morris Co. "We'll have other periods where we will look like rocket scientists, I'm sure,"
he says, adding, "But, hey, right now we look like dummies."
SEC CHARGES MANAGER: The Securities and Exchange Commission filed regulatory charges against
former fund manager Steven H. Adler of Tampa, Fla., for allegedly fraudulently diverting more than $2.2
million intended for the ASM Index 30 Fund into the Adler-owned company managing the fund. Mr. Adler
couldn't be reached to comment. Last year, the fund's directors fired Mr. Adler. The fund was merged into the
new Orbitex Focused 30 Fund, with different management.

EUROPE/AFRICA
U.K.'s Equitable Life
To Be Put on Block
After Legal Ruling
The chief executive of Britain's Equitable Life Assurance Society, Alan Nash, said he expects the large
mutual insurer to attract plenty of interest from potential buyers, after a legal ruling by Britain's House of Lords
forced the insurer to put itself up for sale. The ruling, which requires Equitable Life to make payouts to 90,000
guaranteed-annuity-rate policyholders, could cost the company as much as GBP 1.5 billion ($2.25 billion) and
would weaken it if were to remain independent. Equitable Life isn't related to AXA Group's Equitable Life
Assurance Society unit in the U.S. Mr. Nash indicated Equitable Life members are unlikely to see large
windfall payments as a result of any sale.
The House of Lords ruled that Equitable, founded in 1762 and the world's oldest mutual life insurer, isn't
entitled to differentiate -- when setting final bonuses -- between policyholders on the basis of whether or not
their policies contain guaranteed annuity rates, nor regarding the form in which benefits are taken. Equitable
has been embroiled in the dispute with the 90,000 policyholders for two years.
Ireland's `Tiger' Economy Grew 9.8%
Ireland's gross domestic product grew by 9.8% last year, confirming the strong economic expansion of the
so-called Celtic Tiger. The country's gross national product, which includes the value of goods and services
produced by the nation abroad as well as at home, rose by 7.8% in 1999 and has increased by 57% at
constant prices in the six years that began in 1994, the Central Statistics Office said. The government this
week reported consumer prices rose by 5.5% in June from a year earlier. The strong inflation numbers,
coupled with the buoyant growth figures, are likely to reinforce fears that the Irish economy is near to
overheating.
Deutsche Post to Face EU Charges
Within two weeks, Deutsche Post will face formal charges from the European Commission based on the
long-running allegations that the company abused its dominant position in Germany's postal market, say
people familiar with the situation. The charges revolve around allegations that Deutsche Post used predatory
pricing to squeeze its competitors from the German market. Deutsche Post has denied engaging in such
activities. United Parcel Service of the U.S. complained about the alleged practice to the European
Commission's antitrust authorities years ago.
Regulator Talks to France Telecom
France's stock-market regulator said it began discussions with France Telecom about possible violations of
regulations related to the initial public offering of its Internet unit Wanadoo. The Wall Street Journal Europe
reported yesterday that France Telecom communicated some details of a planned Wanadoo capital increase
to some analysts and fund managers before making them public yesterday. The regulator declined to provide
details of its contact with the firm, which has said it didn't make any selective disclosure. Wanadoo began
Paris trading Wednesday.
Rescued Penguins Escape Another Peril

--- Page 51 ---
Page 51 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Penguins evacuated from islands off South Africa to save them from an oil spill last month were shot at by
fishermen who feared the penguins would chase away fish, the local Eastern Province Herald reported. About
20,000 penguins have been trucked about 470 miles from Cape Town to Port Elizabeth and released to swim
home, a passage rescuers hoped would allow time to clean up the tanker-wreck spill.

-- The European Central Bank, as expected, left its key interest rates steady as it continues to monitor
potential risks of inflation. Most economists expect an ECB interest-rate increase in September.

beverage-can manufacturer American National Can Group after the companies agreed to sell three European

ASIA/PACIFIC
India's Reliance Posts 20% Profit Rise
Indian petrochemical and textiles company Reliance Industries, the country's largest private-sector company,
said fiscal first-quarter earnings rose 20% to 6.12 billion rupees ($136.7 million), despite pressure on margins
because of higher oil prices. Net sales jumped 72% to 66.15 billion rupees. Reliance's exports rose more than
eightfold to 13.11 billion rupees. Production of petrochemical products surged 37% from a year before to 2.6
million tons. The company's managing director said it expects record full-year production that will exceed 10
million tons.
U.S. Court Reverses Pacific Dunlop Ruling
Investors dumped shares of Pacific Dunlop, an Australian manufacturer of health-care products, apparel and
tires, after a U.S. court reversed an approved settlement relating to faulty pacemaker leads made by the
company's Accufix Research Institute unit. Pacific Dunlop told the Australian Stock Exchange that the U.S.
Court of Appeals for the Sixth Circuit reversed approval of a settlement initially approved by the federal court
in Cincinnati in March 1999. That settlement covered Accufix Research Institute, Pacific Dunlop and other
affiliates regarding all claims in the U.S. arising from the pacemaker leads, formerly made by the institute.
The parties had agreed to a 1998 settlement providing for payments of as much as A$144.6 million. The
company said the parties are now "considering their respective positions" in light of the ruling. Pacific Dunlop
shares sank 10.6% in Australian trading.
BRIEFLY:
-- Salomon Smith Barney Australia, a unit of Citigroup, said it plans to buy the Australian retail
stock-brokerage business of HSBC Bank Australia, a unit of Britain's HSBC Holdings. Financial terms of the
planned acquisition weren't disclosed.
-- South Korean private spending in the first quarter rose 11.2% from a year earlier, mainly because of
increased spending on cars and personal computers, the Bank of Korea said.
-- Thai Farmers Bank surprised analysts with second-quarter profit of 2.2 billion baht ($54.8 million),
compared with a loss of 19.29 billion baht a year earlier.

most gold producers, Barrick has been hampered by the fluctuating price of gold, which traded between
$252.80 and $326.25 an ounce in the past year. It was trading at about $279 yesterday.

Progenitor of the Paper Millionaires---John Law's Currency SystemDevastated Royal France,Transformed World Trade
Progenitor of the Paper Millionaires --- John Law's Currency System Devastated Royal France,
Transformed World Trade
2,447 words
19 July 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
THROUGHOUT HISTORY, the people who have become very wealthy have had two things in common:
Each had a great gift or idea, and each lived in a society where that gift was highly valued. A brawny and
aggressive man with state-of-the-art spears probably wouldn't get rich in modern America, but he might have
been the Bill Gates of the year 1000. Similarly, today's brainy Internet entrepreneur might very well have been
a slave 1,000 years ago.
In 18th-century France, the scarcity of gold impaired the country's ability to trade its surpluses with other
nations. Many of the French enjoyed great affluence, yet they and the profligate government of Louis XIV
always seemed to be scrounging for gold.
It was the great fortune of a man named John Law that he could introduce his ideas about paper money to
the country. "My secret is to make gold out of paper," he once said. The result was famously lucrative and
then even more famously catastrophic for the French, but Law's demonstration of the flexibility afforded by
paper currency and by the creation of money by bank or government fiat has been the basis of finance ever
since. His exploits gave the world a word it cherishes to this day: millionaire. The following excerpts from a
new book by Cynthia Crossen about the amassing of great fortunes through the ages shows linkages
between Law's epoch and our own.
---
Born in 1671, John Law grew up in Edinburgh, where his father was a goldsmith who lent money on the side.
As a child, Law displayed an aptitude for arithmetic, geometry and algebra. When he was 13 years old, his
father died, and his mother took over the family business. Law worked for the firm for three years, learning
the principles of banking, and after work he studied tennis and fencing. Before he left to seek his fortune in
London, he was described as "nicely expert in all manner of debaucheries."
In London, Law gambled, studied finance, and practiced what was then called "gallantry" -- all at considerable
expense to his widowed mother. He was a good athlete, quick-witted, eloquent, and fastidious in dress. He
was also a great favorite among women, who called him Beau Law. For reasons lost to history, Law began
feuding with a man named Beau Wilson, another London dandy. The two arranged to fight a duel, which
lasted only a few seconds: In Law's one pass, he fatally pierced Wilson's chest with his sword.
Because duels were assumed to be premeditated, Law was charged with murder and convicted. Imprisoned
while awaiting a public hanging, he escaped. For the next 10 years, Law drifted around the Continent,
supporting himself by gambling. In Venice, Genoa and Amsterdam, he also studied fledgling banking
companies. His mathematical mind was persistently reflecting on the intricacies of high finance.
At Europe's many gambling houses -- Paris alone had more than 60 at the time -- Law became known as an
expert at calculating mathematical odds. Eventually, he was winning so much money that he commissioned
the casting of a large gold coin to facilitate the handling of his stakes. He began to travel around in a polished
coach attended by men in livery. Charming and wealthy, Law was able to get letters of introduction to the
drawing rooms of powerful Europeans. At one home in Paris, he met the Duke of Orleans, who a few years
later became regent of France.
Law learned the most about banking and finance in Holland, where the Bank of Amsterdam was already 100
years old. In Law's time, the bank primarily handled transfers -- merchants brought coins to the bank and
received credit for their value in the form of banknotes. Because the bank simply transferred money among
accounts, under normal circumstances it wouldn't be caught short of metal reserves.
---

--- Page 54 ---
Page 54 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In the early 1700s, Law continued moving among the Continent's big cities, gambling for his living. In 1715,
he and the woman he lived with moved to Paris, where Louis XIV had recently died. Louis had passed France
to his five-year-old great-grandson, Louis XV, whose regent, the Duke of Orleans, was Law's friend.
In Paris at the time, wealth was seen as an unalloyed good, and the wealthy pampered themselves with
grotesque and silly magnificence. The mistress of a rich nobleman wanted to exceed the legendary Lucullus,
who swallowed a diamond worth 100,000 francs, so she ate a bill worth 500,000 francs. While some of the
wealthy were children of the landed aristocracy, others made their fortunes by milking the national treasury.
Louis XIV had been living on loans for years, borrowing from his subjects and issuing worthless paper that
went by different names to promote confusion. In the last 14 years of his reign, Louis XIV had spent two
billion livres more than he had collected in taxes. The coinage had been so debased that it was almost
worthless. Tens of thousands of workers were unemployed, and agriculture was in distress. Trade was
almost at a standstill.
Law believed he could fix France with his "system." He began by proposing a bank that would issue notes
and hold and transfer deposits, much as the Bank of Amsterdam did. As an incentive to the government to
endorse his bank, Law would sell shares of the new bank for a combination of cash and government
securities, which, although then trading at a deep discount, he would honor at face value.
In 1716, Law established a private bank called Law & Co. So sure was he of his system's promise that he
invested his entire fortune in it and promised to give 500,000 livres to charity if the project failed.
Many others had tried to replace metal with paper, but it was difficult to persuade people they should
exchange coins, which had a known value, for the promise of other coins in the future. Coinage everywhere
was subject to sudden and arbitrary devaluation or augmentation, making its purchasing power somewhat
hypothetical. But Law made a promise to the French people that their government hadn't: His notes were
redeemable on demand for coin "of the weight and standard of the day of issue." That meant the value of the
notes would be unaffected by future fluctuations in coinage.
Gradually trust in the bank began to build. In 1717, the notes were made receivable for taxes and other royal
revenue. The following year, Law's bank became the state-royal-bank. One of the nationalized bank's first
actions was to change the terms of its notes to be redeemable for "current coins" -- subjecting Law's notes to
fluctuating values.
Law had become one of the most revered people in France -- he was a financial pied piper, and the nation
danced to his music. He said what his audience wanted to hear: He could make them rich merely by printing
paper.
In 1717, Law proposed establishing a company that would have the exclusive rights to trade with and exploit
the resources of the Mississippi River, the Louisiana Territory and Canada's fur trade -- all under French
control at the time. Another part of the scheme: Law would pay down some of the government's enormous
debt from the company's profit.
The regent and Parliament approved the plan, and the Company of the West was established. Shares in the
company sold slowly at first: For nearly two years, they could be bought below par. To attract public attention,
Law declared that in six months he would "call" for a small number of shares of the company at par, even
though the shares were then selling at about half of face value. Share prices began to rise.
Law's company gradually began to acquire other contracts for government business, occasionally issuing
more shares to finance its acquisitions. It bought the tobacco monopoly in 1718, when consumption of
tobacco was rapidly escalating. In 1719, the French government gave the company the right of coinage. John
Law now controlled the mint, public finances, the bank, the sea trade and the Louisiana, tobacco, and salt
revenues.
At the same time, Law was encouraging holders of government annuities -- whose debt financing was
crippling the crown -- to exchange their instruments for shares in the company. He issued 200,000 shares of
his company and again accepted discounted paper at face value. Several months later, he issued another
50,000 shares. All were snapped up enthusiastically, so Law issued still another 50,000 shares. Then, in
September 1719, Law announced that he would buy the entire debt of France by issuing more shares of his
company and basically swapping dividends. By the end of the year, he had sold 600,000 shares in the
company.
To keep the share price rising, Law published exaggerated accounts of Louisiana's riches, mineral resources,
and people. He created duchies, earldoms, and marquisates in Louisiana (and gave himself the duchy of
Arkansas).

--- Page 55 ---
Page 55 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The reality, of course, was quite different. Much of the Mississippi valley was unconquered wilderness. But in
Paris, no one knew this. The only thing the French knew with certainty was that prices of the company's
shares would never stop climbing. Stock that had come on the market at 500 livres was selling for 10,000
livres. This price unnerved even Law himself, although he had become one of the richest men in the world.
Meanwhile, the regent ordered more money printed. In one year, the supply of money almost doubled.
Inflation soared.
In Paris, people of every class enjoyed the novelty of making a fortune while they slept. Rags became riches,
and riches became inordinate riches. So many people were becoming rich that the French needed a new
word to describe them, and millionaire was it.
In 1720, Law, the national hero, was appointed France's comptroller general of finances, making him the de
facto prime minister. The day after he assumed office, shares of his company reached an all-time high. Using
his trading profit, Law bought dozens of parcels of land and buildings in Paris, some 20 estates, jewels, a
45,000-volume library and a wine cellar.
Eventually, all financial fevers break. In Law's case, professional speculators began selling, but instead of
accepting paper as payment they demanded coin. Law knew his bank would soon be depleted if this
continued, but he also knew it would be suicidal not to honor his redemption promise. Share prices fell, and
attempts to convert paper into gold or jewels accelerated.
At Law's urging, the government introduced a series of edicts aimed at forcing the public to use paper instead
of metal. On Feb. 27, 1720, the government ordered that no person "of whatever estate or condition" should
have more than 500 livres in coins or ingots. The authorities commenced so-called domiciliary visits, resulting
in widespread confiscations. In the cellars of an ex-chancellor named Pontchartrain were found 57,000 gold
coins. On March 11, the government announced that gold and silver could no longer be used to pay debts.
This made France the first country in the civilized world where a commercial transaction couldn't be
conducted with gold and silver.
The public was incensed. Law was given a detachment of Swiss Guards to protect him from enraged crowds.
Once when his empty carriage was recognized on the street, an angry mob tore it to pieces. The windows of
his house were smashed. Yet he still had the ear of the regent. He was, after all, the only person in the world
who understood the system.
On May 21, the government issued the edict that broke the system. It devalued all the company's notes and
shares and fixed their prices. The value of the company's shares was reduced by almost half. The public
outcry was so vociferous that the edict was repealed a week after its publication, but the damage was done.
Law submitted his resignation as comptroller general.
The rush on the bank was so great that it closed for 10 days; it opened again on June 10, but so many people
tried to get in that some suffocated or were crushed to death. On July 17, the crowd arrived at the bank to find
barricades around it. This so incited them that they rushed the barriers; 12 were killed, many others injured.
Meanwhile the price of food was rising, and shopkeepers would accept paper currency only at discounts as
high as 90% and sometimes not at all. The government began to withdraw paper from circulation by
announcing that all notes of 1,000 livres or more were canceled unless they were used to buy government
annuities or for opening accounts at Law's bank. Another edict soon did the same for all other notes. A few
months later, the use of gold and silver was resumed in all commercial transactions.
On Nov. 27, the bank closed its doors for the last time. John Law's experiment with paper money had lasted
less than two years. He left France in December. All his property, except a small amount of cash he carried,
was confiscated by the government. He returned to England, where he asked for and was granted a formal
pardon for his 30-year-old murder conviction.
In 1725, Law moved to Venice, where he made a modest living by gambling. He died of pneumonia in a
lodging house four years later at the age of 58. An inventory of his wealth noted 488 paintings, including
works by Titian, Raphael, Michelangelo and Leonardo da Vinci. A satirical epitaph published in France after
his death read, "Here lies that celebrated Scotsman, that peerless mathematician who, by the rules of
algebra, sent France to the poorhouse."
---
John Law's experiment, like many experiments, was a failure, even a tragedy for some. But his ideas were
like smoke from a bottle: Once out, they could not be put back. He had proved that the value of money is an
agreement among people, not an objective standard measurable in nuggets or ingots, a distinction that
fostered future stages of wealth creation.
---

--- Page 56 ---
Page 56 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
From the book `The Rich and How They Got That Way' by Cynthia Crossen. Copyright 2000 by Dow Jones &
Co. Published by Crown Business, a division of Random House Inc.

Deals & Deal Makers:Bids & Offers
Deals & Deal Makers: Bids & Offers
Compiled by Randall Smith, with contributions from Kara Scannell and William Power
972 words
7 July 2000
The Wall Street Journal
