[Inside the World of Corporate Finance & Wall Street]
The U.S.S. Wall Street? The bulls marched up Wall Street yesterday, . . . well, sort of.
Nine Navy "bull ensigns" took a tour of Wall Street while in town for the OpSail 2000 parade of tall ships and
naval vessels. A bull ensign is the most senior ensign -- itself the lowliest commissioned officer rank-onboard
each ship.
Why Wall Street? Lt. Comdr. Bob Carey, who organized the sailors and seemed to know a thing or two about
stock-market trends, explained: "I thought it'd be a great idea to bring the bulls together, since the U.S. has
enjoyed a bull market" for the past 9 1/2 years.
The squadron of officers started at the famous statue of a bull in lower Manhattan, and headed north to the
Federal Reserve Bank of New York, where they each curled a 28-pound gold bar ("I've never held so much
money before," one Navy man said) and then into the New York Stock Exchange.
The great bull market hasn't left these men completely at sea. During the tour of the New York Fed, more
than one sailor asked about insider trading, and another wanted to know where Fed Chairman Alan
Greenspan sneaks into the building and where he actually sits for policy meetings. Perhaps sonar would help.
The Great Race
With the dollar strong against the euro lately, many Americans are flocking across the Atlantic for summer
vacations. Some Wall Street types, naturally, do things on a grander scale.
Joining the throngs in France starting this weekend will be Thomas Weisel, the San Francisco investment
banker. A longtime cycling enthusiast, he will be there to cheer on an American bicycling team led by Lance
Armstrong, who is defending his 1999 Tour de France title.
It won't all be fun and games for the chairman and chief executive of Thomas Weisel Partners. As chairman
and lead investor of a sports-management company, Disson & Furst Group, that owns and manages the
team, he will be riding in one of two station wagons that follow the team. He will keep in touch with the office
by cell phone and the cyclists by radio, all the while watching the race on a television in the vehicle. The
team's main sponsor is the U.S. Postal Service.
"It's kind of like the trading floor," said Mark Gorski, general manager of the team, of the frenetic atmosphere
in the car. "He has really found a way to incorporate the team as a vehicle for business building" with both
trading and investment-banking clients.
Mr. Weisel, formerly chief executive of the old Montgomery Securities, has close business ties with some of
the cycle team's other sponsors including Yahoo! Inc. and Visa USA Inc., Mr. Gorski adds. Montgomery even
underwrote stock offerings for helmet maker Bell Sports Corp., and bicycle makers Cannondale Corp. and GT
Bicycles.
Mr. Weisel plans to arrive in France this weekend and stay through the end of the 3,630-kilometer race in
Paris on July 23. Last year he hosted a victory dinner at the elegant restaurant at the Musee d'Orsay. With
Mr. Armstrong in 12th place after six stages, it is still too early yet for word on this year's plans.
A Way With Words
Wall Street stock-research reports can, believe it or not, sometimes be witty and almost fun to read.

--- Page 58 ---
Page 58 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Then there is Merrill Lynch & Co.'s research note last week on the stock of Great Plains Software Inc., which
included this charming example of what happens when Wall Street shorthand and jargon runs amok: The
company's earnings, the Merrill analysts wrote, were "in line with the high end of the preannounced shortfall."
Huh? It means Great Plains had a net loss of nearly $2.6 million for the quarter, and the company had
warned of such.
Yankees in San Fran
Greenwich Capital Markets, a bond firm that is well-known on Wall Street and is a unit of Royal Bank of
Scotland, takes its name from the high-toned town in Connecticut where it is based. But that is about as far
as it goes with matching geography and marketing.
Last week, Greenwich Capital trumpeted the opening of a San Francisco branch office. The locals won't likely
catch the Connecticut reference. Some are likely to think the name refers to Greenwich Street, one of San
Francisco's roller-coaster-like streets, but the outpost -- devoted to the sale of mortgage and asset-backed
securities -- will be located instead on Spear Street.
The firm's public-relations crew won't comment.
All-Inclusive NYSE
The New York Stock Exchange, despite its button-down image, is always enthusiastic about press events
and gimmicks to publicize its listed companies. After all, this is the exchange that, in a typical period, will host
a Snoopy mascot on one day (to publicize MetLife) and a Sports Illustrated swimsuit model the next (for Time
Warner).
But even by the NYSE's standards, there has been a striking hodgepodge of celebrities and other characters
lately. The appearances in recent weeks have included the Budweiser Clydesdales, the king and queen of
Romania, Taco Bell's Chihuahua and a "virtual bell ringing" by Victoria's Secret owner Intimate Brands
(televised to the trading floor from France). Perhaps that is why just when things might have been getting too
earthy, yesterday's closing ceremonies were officiated by New York's new archbishop, the Rev. Edward M.
Egan. The session's nearly nonexistent move in the Dow Jones Industrial Average (down 2.13 points, or just
0.02%), was appropriately serene.

Review / Books:`The Father of Modern Finance'
WEEKEND JOURNAL
Review / Books: `The Father of Modern Finance'
By James Surowiecki
1,357 words
30 June 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
W9
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
MILLIONAIRE
By Janet Gleeson
(Simon & Schuster, 303 pages, $24)
John Law was, as the subtitle of Janet Gleeson's new pseudo-biography, "Millionaire," has it, a "philanderer,
gambler, and duelist." More important, he was also one of the most inventive economic thinkers of his day
(his day being the early 18th century). Over the course of a very busy four years, Law set in motion a
far-sighted program of industrial development and public-works projects, tried to give France something like
the world's first paper-money economy and created a stock-market bubble next to which last year's run-up in
Internet shares pales by comparison. This may not make Law, as Ms. Gleeson's subtitle also has it, the man
who "invented modern finance," but it does make him a fascinating subject for a serious intellectual
biography. Unfortunately, Ms. Gleeson hasn't written it.
What she has given us instead is an ill-fitting combination of racy anecdotes, half-baked social history and a
haphazard discussion of Law's intricate attempts to change the way 18th-century Europeans thought about
things like money, value and risk. "Millionaire" feels like a book that wants to be this year's version of Dava
Sobel's "Longitude" or Simon Winchester's "The Professor and the Madman." But Ms. Gleeson doesn't know
enough -- can't know enough, because of the lack of sources -- about Law's life to make "Millionaire"
compelling as narrative, and she doesn't trust the reader's willingness to delve into financial arcana enough to
make it interesting as intellectual history. The result is a book that offers just enough intriguing material to
make you frustrated by how much you're not being told.
It's understandable, of course, that Ms. Gleeson would want to spice up a potentially dry topic with some sex
and violence. But part of the problem is that most of Law's gambling and philandering, and all of his dueling,
were confined to the first part of his adulthood. More substantively, the details of Law's life are, for the most
part, unknown, which means that Ms. Gleeson has to fill in the gaps with annoying suppositions of the kind
that dot all too many popular biographies.
We hear of Law's "probable acquaintances" and people he "must have" run into at one point or another.
When Law kills the notorious dandy Edward Wilson in a duel in 1694, Ms. Gleeson imagines herself into the
head of the constable who came upon the scene and "must have flinched with surprise." Most dubiously, she
indulges in psychologizing without a trace of evidence, suggesting for instance that Law's sometimes erratic
behavior during times of crisis was provoked by the "disturbing memories" of the duel and his subsequent
imprisonment.
There are, of course, certain limitations that have to be accepted in any biography and especially in
biographies of people whose lives were, for the most part, undocumented. One way to get around those
limitations is to use an individual's life to illuminate the world that shaped it. You may not be able to fashion a
classic potboiler out of what we know of John Law's deeds, but Law can be the vehicle to transport the reader
into 18th-century Europe. And Ms. Gleeson does try to use Law in this way. But she prefers to sketch the
world, rather than draw it in real detail, and so even the cultural landscape surrounding Law remains vague.
This is a real missed opportunity, because the early 18th century was a tremendously interesting time,
especially in terms of money. This is when the roots of our modern stock market were planted -- in the form of
a company Law himself created, the Mississippi Co., and the concurrent explosion in joint-stock companies in
England -- and when traditional notions about the centrality of precious metals to any economy were first
meaningfully challenged. It was also a time when ideas about probability began to filter, slowly, down to the
general public. As Ms. Gleeson suggests, Law's success as a gambler as well as his economic innovations

--- Page 60 ---
Page 60 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
stemmed in no small part from his understanding of why certain things were more likely to happen than
others (like why, with two six-sided dice, you're more likely to roll a seven than a 12).
But the sheer radicalness of this understanding seems to elude the author. If Law understood probability but
most of the people he was dealing with -- everyone from his fellow gamblers to royal officials to the investors
in the Mississippi Co. -- didn't, then what did they think he was doing? How did Law's initial success and then
resounding failure -- he created the first French central bank but ran it into the ground, his Mississippi Co.
went under and he ended up in exile -- alter the way Europeans thought about money? These are questions
Ms. Gleeson doesn't really help us answer.
Law's central insight was that money -- whatever the form it takes -- has value only because people believe it
does. I accept a piece of paper with the word "dollar" printed on it in payment for services I render or goods I
deliver because I know (or think I know) that I'll be able to exchange that same piece of paper for someone
else's services or goods later on. Now, you could say that this means that any modern economy depends on
a sort of collective hallucination, since paper has no intrinsic worth. But what Law seems to have recognized
is that nothing has any intrinsic worth -- nothing is, by its nature, inherently valuable -- so hallucination is all
we have. The important question is not whether an economy's foundations are true in some deep sense, but
whether an economy works, which is to say whether it encourages people to work and to invent, to transform
their time into goods and services that others value.
Law ultimately didn't go far enough in this direction. When he ran the French bank, he offered bank notes that
were guaranteed by actual gold and silver in the bank's vaults, so the value of the notes still depended on the
fact that you could get hard currency for them at any moment. At the same time, though, he allowed the
French state to print bank notes seemingly without regard to how much gold and silver really was in the
vaults. This was economically disastrous -- a pure recipe for inflation -- but it was a step toward today's
economy, where the dollar does not need gold behind it, and where the value of a currency changes literally
by the minute on the foreign-exchange market. Law probably could not have imagined an economy as
untethered as ours, but I like to think that if he could have seen it, he would have understood it immediately.
I'd like to think that, but "Millionaire" doesn't really help me decide. In her notes to the book, Ms. Gleeson
writes that she has "deliberately simplified the sometimes mind-bogglingly complex financial details" in order
to keep the material accessible for the general reader. The principle here is admirable. But in practice what
Ms. Gleeson ends up doing is leaving the general reader without any real way of evaluating either Law's
ideas or his intentions, because the information she gives us about Law's economic theories and the way he
ran both the French bank and the Mississippi Co. is presented so haphazardly and unsystematically that it's
impossible to tell what was really revolutionary -- or really preposterous -- about what Law pulled off.
For most of the last 200 years, Law has been seen as a crank and a charlatan. Only recently have
economists started to take him seriously as a visionary of value. "Millionaire" should leave readers with some
idea of whether Law was one or the other. Instead, it's likely to leave readers confused.
---
Mr. Surowiecki is a staff writer for the New Yorker.

EUROPE
France Telecom Sets
Modest Price Range
For Wanadoo IPO
France Telecom said it plans an initial public offering of 10% of its Internet unit Wanadoo, to be listed on the
Paris Bourse on July 19, and set a modest price range of 17 euros to 20 euros a share -- at the low end of
analysts' expectations -- in the hope of reassuring investors still skeptical of Internet valuations. France
Telecom's chairman, Michel Bon, said 100 million shares would be offered, valuing all of Wanadoo at
between 17 and 20 billion euros ($16 billion and $18.9 billion) and giving the business an acquisition currency
to carve out a place among Europe's top three Internet service providers by 2003.
By contrast, the IPO of Deutsche Telekom's Internet unit T-Online, also for 10% of the business, valued
T-Online at 36 billion euros and was considered a low valuation when the unit was floated in April. Traders
said they had expected Wanadoo to be valued at about 25 billion euros.
U.K. Posts Hefty Current-Account Deficit
Britain posted a first-quarter current-account deficit of GBP 4.0 billion ($6.05 billion), the country's sixth
quarterly deficit in a row, the Office for National Statistics said. The deficit in the current account, the broadest
measure of trade, was wider than a revised gap of GBP 1.5 billion in the final quarter of 1999 but narrowed
from the GBP 4.1 billion deficit a year before. The deficit was wider than economists' predictions of a GBP 3.3
billion gap, reflecting the effect on British industry of the pound's strength against the euro. The goods-trade
deficit narrowed to GBP 6.6 billion from GBP 7.2 billion in the previous quarter, while the surplus on trade in
services fell to GBP 2.5 billion from GBP 2.6 billion.
Shell to Use $14 Oil Price for Calculations
Royal Dutch/Shell Group expects oil prices to come off current levels and will keep its target of $14 a barrel
as the basis for its financial policy, its incoming chairman, Jeroen van der Veer, told reporters. He said Shell
plans to keep its working target of $14 a barrel as the basis for its investments, particularly in oil and gas
exploration and development. Separately, Maarten van den Bergh, who will step down as chairman
tomorrow, said the rise in oil prices has led to increased investment by non-OPEC producers, which will
eventually lead to an increase in output.
EU Members to Review Austrian Relations
European Union countries have endorsed a strategy -- but no timetable -- for lifting their diplomatic sanctions
against fellow EU member Austria, imposed after the inclusion of Joerg Haider's far-right Freedom Party in
Austria's coalition government in January. A panel of "three personalities" to be chosen by a Swiss judge --
Switzerland isn't an EU member -- will report on the Austrian government's commitment to "common
European values," particularly "the rights of minorities, refugees and immigrants," as well as on the Freedom
Party's character. After the report, the 14 countries will "re-examine" bilateral relations with Austria.

-- German lawmakers conceded that efforts to work out a compromise agreement on the government's
proposed tax-cut plan before Parliament breaks for the summer have effectively failed, after another fruitless
round of talks yesterday.

-- French steelmaker Usinor, benefiting from robust economic growth in Europe and the U.S., said it swung to

ASIA/PACIFIC
Air France, Delta May Bid for Thai Stake
Air France Group said it is interested in bidding with Delta Air Lines of the U.S. for a 10% stake in Thai
Airways International that the Bangkok-based carrier plans to sell to a strategic partner. A Delta spokesman
in Atlanta declined to comment. Meanwhile, Thai Airways, which is 93%-owned by the government, said it
would delay a planned public offering of 13% of its shares because of poor market conditions. But its
president said the airline will proceed with the planned sale of a 10% stake to a strategic partner. Besides Air
France and Delta, carriers that have expressed an interest in the stake, he said, include Singapore Airlines,
UAL Corp.'s United Airlines, Germany's Lufthansa, Scandinavian Airlines System, Air Canada, British
Airways, Australia's Qantas Airways and SAirGroup.
Japan's Jobless Rate Declines Again
Japan's unemployment rate fell to 4.6% in May, the lowest level since November, as the number of full-time
workers rose for the first time in more than two years, the government said. It was the second straight month
in which the jobless rate fell, after easing to 4.8% in April from 4.9% the month before. The lower
unemployment rate is the latest sign of improvement in Japan's economy, and suggests the recovery in
corporate profits has started to spill over into new jobs. Economists surveyed by Dow Jones Newswires had
predicted a 4.8% jobless rate in May.
Hainan Aims to Resolve Bond Payment
The government of China's Hainan province said it is trying to resolve the financial problems of Hainan
International Trust & Investment, an investment firm that has missed an interest payment on a Japanese
Samurai-bond issue. Hainan International, already struggling under $400 million in foreign debt, on Monday
missed a deadline for an interest payment on a seven-year, 14.5 billion yen ($137.4 million) Samurai bond. A
spokesman for China's central bank said a resolution of Hainan International's problem will be announced
within days.
BRIEFLY:
-- Canada's Newmont Mining said it evacuated women, children and nonessential staff from the area of its
gold mine in Indonesian's Sulawesi province, following what it called intimidation by local protesters who
have blockaded the site. The mine's gold output last year was 11 tons; output this year is targeted at 12 tons.

THE AMERICAS
Brazil Ruling Upheld on Monsanto Seeds
A Brazilian federal court upheld an injunction against the sale of Monsanto's genetically modified soybean
seeds without a prior study of their impact on the environment. Monsanto, a unit of Pharmacia, has sought
since 1998 to obtain Brazilian approval for the sale of its genetically modified Roundup Ready soybean
seeds. According to the director of Monsanto's Brazilian unit, the Brasilia court may soon issue a definitive
ruling on the matter, rather than merely upholding the injunction. Brazil, the world's No. 2 soybean producer,
is the only Western Hemisphere country without genetically modified food.

Canadian engineering and construction company SNC-Lavalin Group announced several contracts for
projects in Russia, in conjunction with a Canadian trade mission to Russia. SNC-Lavalin said it signed a
cooperative agreement with Russian oil company Lukoil for the design, financing and construction of a
reformer unit at Lukoil's Volgograd refinery; the capital cost of the work is estimated at $60 million to $70
million. The reformer will produce high-grade gasoline. SNC-Lavalin said it also entered into an agreement
with the Moscow government to build a rapid-transit system linking the Sheremetevo airport with the city
center. The agreement calls for the system to be built in phases, with the first phase estimated to cost $150
million.
Air Canada, Union Avert Holiday Strike
Air Canada and the union representing about 2,200 of its pilots said contract talks are expected to continue
this weekend. The union said it won't issue a strike notice before Monday, meaning there is no danger of a
strike before the end of the holiday weekends marking Canada Day tomorrow and the Fourth of July in the
U.S. The union must wait 72 hours after giving a strike notice before it can begin a strike.

CBOT Members Vote Overwhelmingly to ChangeStatus to For-Profit, Following Rivals' Footsteps
Commodities
CBOT Members Vote Overwhelmingly to Change Status to For-Profit, Following Rivals' Footsteps
By Peter A. McKay
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
874 words
29 June 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
C17
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Chicago Board of Trade members voted resoundingly yesterday to follow the lead of their two biggest U.S.
competitors by transforming their member-run market into a for-profit corporation.
Unlike similar proposals that won by recent landslides at the New York Mercantile Exchange and the Chicago
Mercantile Exchange, CBOT's plan generated heavy last-minute political squabbling. That left some members
worried yesterday about the proposal's chances of garnering the necessary two-thirds super-majority to pass.
However, those worries proved moot when CBOT members passed the measure by a 90.7% margin. The
referendum, like many issues at U.S. financial exchanges these days, became not only an issue of
management structure in its final days, but also of political control and electronic competition.
"I applaud the CBOT membership for embracing change and coming together in support of this historic
initiative," CBOT Chairman David P. Brennan said in a statement following the vote. "This is a tremendous
vote of confidence in the direction our exchange is headed."
The switch will be complete pending a favorable Internal Revenue Service ruling and related member votes at
an unspecified date, a CBOT spokesman said.
"I would like to see us take the responsibility for moving forward as a business," independent trader Daniel F.
Brophy said. "Instead of the country club that's used to operating a big, fat monopoly, we need that business
footing, which is what this plan does."
A final vote count wasn't available at press time. Former exchange Chairman Les Rosenthal, however, said
he thought the proposal would get a simple but not two-thirds majority because of CBOT traders' newfound
concern about the plan's possible effect on valuable joint trading rights their memberships carry at the
neighboring Chicago Board Options Exchange.
"There are more people against it than who let on," he said yesterday, counting himself among those who
voted no. "They feel there hasn't been enough attention paid to protecting that CBOE exercise right."
Eligible to vote in the referendum were 1,402 full CBOT membership-seat holders who each had one vote,
along with more than 700 holders of partial seats who had fractional votes.
The CBOT's plan differs most dramatically from those of its rivals in that it would split the exchange into two
competing units, one dedicated to open outcry and one to electronic trading.
In previous interviews, Mr. Brennan touted yesterday's vote as only a first step to restructuring, since its
biggest effect would be to move the exchange's charter from Illinois to Delaware for legal and tax purposes.
Members would still have to vote later to merge or partner with another entity, he said.
Many veteran traders say such extreme moves may be necessary in an increasingly electronic era. Hence,
restructuring in recent years at CBOT has become closely linked as a political issue with online trading,
something many floor members fear as a threat to their jobs doing physical transactions.
"If they don't go for-profit, then they are extremely encumbered in the technological revolution," said Joe
Zednick, cochairman of YesTrader Inc., a Chicago exchange software firm based in the CBOT building. "In an
electronic environment, the best move for an exchange is to have terminals on everyone's desk or the
Internet, which is the complete opposite model of having a fixed set of memberships to trade exclusive
contracts."

--- Page 65 ---
Page 65 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The other key issue for CBOT members is the possible value of their seats-turned-shares. As at most
commodity exchanges in recent years, CBOT seat prices have fluctuated wildly.
Instead, many are now hoping to cash in if the CBOT makes a lucrative initial public offering of stock, as it
has said it might consider doing, particularly for its electronic unit.
Mr. Rosenthal remains skeptical of such a move, though, given the recent shakeout in technology stocks.
"All the exchanges have missed the brass ring," he said. "You no longer have those kinds of situations where
you go public at 20 and are at 200 by the end of the week."
In commodity trading yesterday:
ELECTRICITY: Forward prices reached new highs, driven by a heat wave throughout the western U.S., which
is pushing electricity demand for air conditioning beyond utilities' generating capacity. Day-ahead prices on
the California Power Exchange for electricity in that state during daylight hours yesterday averaged $522.55 a
megawatt-hour. The previous record was set June 14 at $464.86/MWh. Unplanned outages of several major
generators, such as a 1,250-megawatt nuclear reactor in Washington state, contributed to the bullish market.
GOLD: Futures rose on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange amid short covering -- the
buying of futures to negate previous sales -- before the Federal Open Market Committee decision on rates,
traders said. The August contract climbed $6.80 to $294.30 a troy ounce. "The shorts were caught in a vice,"
said Bernard Penner, a bullion dealer at Rudolf Wolff in New York. The Fed kept its rates unchanged.
---
David Bogoslaw and Mark Golden contributed to this article.

So Much Money,So Little to Buy
The Outlook
So Much Money, So Little to Buy
By Bob Davis
800 words
26 June 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
WASHINGTON -- Al Gore blasts George W. Bush for proposing to invest Social Security revenue in the stock
market. But Mr. Gore's plan, if taken literally, probably depends more on the market than does Mr. Bush's.
Why? The vice president would pay off the federal debt around the end of the decade, and continue running
surpluses, so he could use them decades later to pay for the baby boom's retirement. The federal
government must invest those trillions of dollars in surpluses somewhere, and corporate stocks and bonds
are the obvious places. "The government would have to buy some nongovernment assets," says Gore
economic adviser Alan Blinder.
This is new budget math. White House economists have focused on paying off the debt but ignored the
question of what happens afterward. "The government would have to invest in America's businesses," says
University of California economist Brad De Long. "It's very weird."
If it happens, it also will be unprecedented. Andrew Jackson was the only president to have paid off the debt
and still collected surpluses. In 1837, he sent the extra money to state governments as bonuses. Twice, the
U.S. came close to eliminating the debt, but the War of 1812 and World War I intervened.
The Gore plan calls for the government to start buying assets around 2010 and to hold them until around
2060. The sums involved are huge. By 2030, the federal government would own assets equal to 27% of the
economy; in today's terms, that's about $2.5 trillion.
The problem may seem academic, but it's already roiling bond markets, which aren't sure how they will
conduct business if the Treasury largely stops issuing bonds. Goldman Sachs economist John Youngdahl
worries that other debt instruments will necessarily be more risky. In case of a financial crisis, he asks, what
will investors turn to for safety? The Federal Reserve also is looking at what other instruments it should buy
and sell.
The changes are critical to Mr. Gore's politics and policy. Seeking debt elimination helps to position him as a
sober economic steward. If the surpluses are spent on other government programs or reduced through tax
cuts, Mr. Gore wouldn't have enough money to keep Social Security solvent for as long as he is now
promising, until 2054.
Mr. Bush doesn't face the same questions about what to do with surpluses because he's promising to cut
taxes and revamp Social Security, not pay off the debt. So what, exactly, to do with the money?
Buying gold, as the U.S. did in the 19th century, doesn't make sense, says Mr. Blinder, the Gore adviser,
because it's "a depreciating asset." Foreign stocks and bonds? "Hard to imagine . . . because of nationalism,"
he says. That leaves U.S. stocks and bonds, he argues.
But that produces other problems for the vice president. Last year, Mr. Gore and the Clinton administration
proposed that the government should use as much as 15% of the Social Security trust fund to buy stocks and
bonds to boost retirement-fund revenue. But Fed Chairman Alan Greenspan criticized the plan because he
believes it would inevitably lead the federal government to intervene politically in markets.
Last month, Mr. Gore said he had taken the Greenspan criticism to heart and no longer backed government
purchases. Trouble is, his advisers acknowledge, the Gore debt-reduction proposal inevitably leads to the
same kind of government stock purchases he has publicly forsworn. "Implicitly, Gore has to rely on private
asset markets," says former Congressional Budget Office chief Robert Reischauer.
Mr. Blinder says the federal government's role could be limited to passive investment, so it wouldn't use its
massive investments for political ends: "A reasonable guess is that the government will start buying index
funds of stocks and bonds, so it doesn't favor one company over another."

--- Page 67 ---
Page 67 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Treasury Secretary Lawrence Summers says the government could chose "high-grade securities that have
risk properties quite similar to Treasury debt."
Another possibility is that Mr. Gore could use the coming surpluses to sweeten his new Retirement Savings
Plus accounts. Under that plan, the federal government would offer generous tax credits for families that set
aside money for savings. By committing more government resources to such accounts, he could reduce the
benefits paid out under traditional Social Security accounts. That option is nearly the same as Mr. Bush's plan
to place a portion of Social Security taxes to personal retirement accounts.
Over the coming years, economists are sure to debate the merits of different ways to handle the burgeoning
surpluses. But for Mr. Gore the lesson is already clear: be careful what you wish for.

Online Booksellers in China Find Success ToughAnd Most Have Postponed Public Stock Offerings
World Stock Markets
Online Booksellers in China Find Success Tough And Most Have Postponed Public Stock Offerings
By Michael Flagg
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,404 words
16 June 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
C12
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
HONG KONG -- At least a half-dozen companies want to be China's Amazon.com. But they will probably
have an even tougher time than the original, the huge U.S. online bookseller that after five years still incurs
millions of dollars in losses annually.
Stocks in Hong Kong posted strong gains yesterday, led by a rebound in property shares. Other Asian
markets were mixed. European markets were mostly lower, though some defensive stocks found buyers.
Overall, the Dow Jones World Stock Index fell 0.69, or 0.28%, to 244.51. Excluding the U.S., the index fell
1.98, or 1.09%, to 179.36.
In the first flush of China's Internet gold rush, people are starting dozens of me-too versions of American
companies in the world's most populous country -- including more than 70 knockoffs of Internet auction house
eBay alone.
But selling books in China is tougher than selling soap or soft drinks. The government bans books it doesn't
like, so these companies have to be careful. Another headache: China is the biggest piracy problem in the
global publishing business. For Chinese publishers, it can be even worse. It is common for millions of pirated
copies of, say, their English dictionaries to be floating around.
And books are inexpensive in China -- as little as $2.50 each -- so the business depends on volume,
something few of these Web sites have yet. That is one reason Hong Kong real-estate developer OceanLand
Group went into business with Xinhua, China's government-controlled news service. Xinhua also is the
mainland's biggest bookseller.
On the other hand, the book market in Taiwan and China, with a population of 1.3 billion, is valued at almost
$13 billion a year, estimates online bookseller CP BooksNet. And readership isn't limited to these two places.
Most Chinatowns around the world have Chinese-language bookstores for the tens of millions of Chinese
who live abroad. More than half of the sales at Hong Kong's Chinese Books Cyberstore, for instance, comes
from the U.S. And while electronic commerce in Asia is puny compared with America's market, Boston
Consulting Group estimates it will more than double this year to $7 billion.
Some of the companies starting online booksellers are Old Economy companies dabbling in the Internet.
Hong Kong real-estate developer Sun Hung Kai Properties, for instance, just sold part of its Internet unit,
SUNeVision Holdings, to the public. SUNeVision launched CP BooksNet in May with what it said were
250,000 titles. SUNeVision owns 25%; the rest is owned by the Internet unit of privately held, centuryold
Commercial Press (Hong Kong), one of Hong Kong's largest publishers and retailers of Chinese books.
Sun Hung Kai has said it might spin off CP BooksNet in yet another public offering. That probably won't be
soon, though, given how skeptical investors have become after tech stocks around the world crashed in
March. SUNeVision itself is selling at several Hong Kong dollars below the HK$10.38 a share at which it
made its debut in March.
Most of these online booksellers, in fact, have had to postpone their dreams of getting rich by selling stock to
the public. Ocean-Land said last year it might sell part of its venture with Xinhua, ebooksChina.com, to the
public this year. That statement was "premature," says Howard Chan, vice president of marketing for Irvine,
Calif.'s ChinaServe.com, the Web site's parent company. "We just opened in April," says Mr. Chan, "and
we're still trying to gear up a marketing program."
SUNeVision and Chinese Books Cyberstore declined to be interviewed. But Beijing's Dangdang.com, which
means "superb" in Chinese, seems to understand "buzz." Run by Peggy Yu, who returned to China after a

--- Page 69 ---
Page 69 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
stint in the investment-banking business in the U.S., and her husband, the company says it is one of the few
Chinese tech start-ups led by a woman.
The company, which is less than a year old, has rounded up impressive investors: Japan's Softbank, one of
the world's biggest Internet investors; and two American companies, technology trade publisher International
Data Group and Luxembourg Cambridge Holdings Group, a tech investor. Together, the three own 55%.
"There are only a handful of big bookstores all over China," says the company's marketing director, Alick Yan,
reciting one of the company's favorite themes. "So China needs an Amazon.com 10 times more than the U.S.
does." The company, which says it offers 200,000 titles, is postponing its plans to go public this year.
Compaq Computer, one of the world's largest computer companies, started one of the oldest Web sites in this
business, Beijing Book Centre Online, in March 1999 with China Information Highway, a consortium formed
by the Ministry of Information Industry.
In yesterday's trading in TOKYO, the Nikkei 225 Index sank 315.72 points, or 1.9%, to 16338.70. Sentiment
was weakened as market heavyweight Sony lost further ground on selling by foreign investors. Sony shares
shed 3% to 9,550 yen. Other blue-chip technology shares fell as well. Tokyo Electron tumbled 7.2% to 12,850
yen, while Advantest plunged 3.8% to 21,640 yen. Kyocera declined 1.8% to 17,290 yen. Bank shares were
battered by news that Asahi Bank will pull out of a planned three-way business integration with Sanwa Bank
and Tokai Bank. Sanwa fell 49 yen to 752 yen, and Asahi shed 30 yen to 427 yen. But Tokai bounced back
from an earlier sell-off to finish 12 yen higher at 515 yen.
In HONG KONG, the Hang Seng Index rose 1.4%, or 223.27 points, to 16080.34. The property index gained
3.6% on optimism that the government will enact some stabilization measures in the property market. Sun
Hung Kai Properties jumped 6.8% to HK$58.75, while Henderson Land rose 3.9% to HK$34.50. China
Telecom gained 2.8% to HK$63.75; the mainland mobile operator said it will acquire more telecom assets in
seven Chinese provinces.
In LONDON, the FTSE 100 Index closed down 45.50 points, or 0.7%, at 6490.80. Technology, telecom and
oil stocks put pressure on the index. Freeserve fell 18.5 pence to 440.5 pence as rumors circulated that one
of its potential suitors, Germany's TOnline, was backing out. Dixons, Freeserve's majority shareholder, also
shed 26.5 pence to 283.5 pence. Oil stocks fell after a recent rally. Shell was off 12.25 pence to 564.25
pence, and BP Amoco was down 15 pence at 618.5 pence.
In PARIS, the CAC 40 Index dropped 95.12 points, or 1.4%, to 6512.98. Utilities-to-media group Vivendi fell
on concerns about its merger talks with pay-TV group Canal Plus and Canada's Seagram. Traders said
investors are unsure how Vivendi will finance the deal. Vivendi declined 4.9% to 98.45 euros, while Canal
Plus fell 1.8% to 200.90 euros.
In SAO PAULO, the Bovespa Index gained 195.27, or 1.2%, to 16,561.37. Bank shares rose on hopes that
the central bank will cut interest rates at next week's meeting. Preferred shares in the country's largest
commercial bank, Banco do Brasil, jumped 7.9% to 8.70 reals. Bradesco shares moved ahead 3.5% to 14.9
reals.
-- Craig Karmin

Gold and Crude Oil Increase on Inflation Worries
Commodities
Gold and Crude Oil Increase on Inflation Worries
By Peter A. McKay
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
688 words
13 June 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
C21
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Gold and crude-oil futures jumped on a combination of inflation worries, tight energy supply
and technical buying.
Analysts have said for some time that the two commodities have been steadily losing their historic price
correlation to one another. However, they said yesterday was a rare demonstration that crude oil can still help
spur sagging gold futures.
Nearby August gold futures rose $2.90 to $286.50 an ounce on the Comex division of the New York
Mercantile Exchange. July crude oil rose $1.54 to $31.74 a barrel after the Organization of Petroleum
Exporting Countries declined to increase production.
The rise translated into the highest close since March for a nearby crude-oil contract on Nymex.
"The U.S. is per capita the largest consumer of energy in the world, so higher energy prices mean more
money flowing out of the U.S. That usually means a weaker dollar, which tends to help gold," said Gil
Atzmon, chief investment strategist for U.S. Global Investors Inc., San Antonio.
Oil's surge was mostly attributable to OPEC's decision not to raise production by an additional 500,000
barrels when the commodity's 20-day average price broke $28 last week. Such a rise should have been
triggered by a sliding "price band" that cartel members recently said they would adopt, allowing them to
automatically increase output at certain price levels without cumbersome meetings.
"They've kind of gone back on their word with respect to the price band," said analyst John Kilduff, of Fimat
USA Inc. "Even if it works, I expect that we'll see increased volatility at the extreme ends of the band, which is
sort of what we're seeing now."
Mr. Kilduff said OPEC is now likely to hold its production steady until its June 21 member meeting.
Algerian Oil Minister Chakib Khelil seemed to back up that expectation by saying in an interview that he
expected the cartel at its next meeting to raise output by 1.2 million to 1.5 million barrels a day, beginning with
September deliveries.
Also spurring energy prices higher were the approach of the U.S. summer driving season and the June 1
implementation of tougher federal standards requiring use of cleaner-burning gasoline in some cities,
analysts said. Any resulting spiral in energy prices would be bad news for U.S. drivers, who saw average
retail gasoline prices of above $1.60 a gallon this weekend.
But it's good news for a gold market starved for any news to damp public confidence in paper investments,
such as dollars. The metal is generally considered a safe haven in such scenarios, which lately have been
rare as the U.S. economy has soared.
"Part of the weakness in gold has been the very proactive stance that [Fed Chairman] Alan Greenspan has
taken against inflation," said analyst Dave Meger of Alaron Trading Corp. in Chicago. "The market believes,
at least for the time being, that he is doing a good job."
In other commodity trading:
SOYBEANS: Futures slid at the Chicago Board of Trade following heavy rains over the weekend and
forecasts for more storms. The July contract lost 10.25 cents to $5.105 a bushel. "There is no underlying
support for beans, the weather premium has been thrown out," said Mark Hanna, floor trader with ED&F Man
on the CBOT floor. "The much-talked-about ridge? Nowhere in site." Mr. Hannah was referring to forecasts

--- Page 74 ---
Page 74 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
last week that called for a high-pressure ridge to move toward the Midwest, blocking beneficial rain for the
crops. The ridge now appears to be moving away from the region, forecasters said.
COTTON: Futures fell on the New York Cotton Exchange division of the New York Board of Trade as rain in
Texas fueled selling by speculators and trade houses, traders said. The July contract dropped 1.74 cents to
56.65 cents a pound.

EUROPE
Germany to Commit
To Airbus Aircraft
For Military Cargo
The German government said it plans to purchase as many as 75 transport aircraft valued at 20 billion marks
($9.84 billion) from Airbus Industrie, which should seal the launch of the European aircraft maker's A400M
military cargolifter. Defense Minister Rudolf Scharping said Germany, which has long wavered over choosing
between the A400M and the cheaper Russian-Ukrainian Antonov An-70, expected to start buying the planes
in 2006-07. Russia and Ukraine have pushed hard for the An-70, saying the plane is already flying, while the
A400M won't be ready for another five years. If the A400M goes ahead, the losers would also include
Lockheed Martin and Boeing, which, respectively, make the smaller C-130J Hercules tactical transporter and
the larger C-17 Globemaster strategic lift aircraft.
Separately, the commercial launch of Airbus's A3XX superjumbo jet was delayed for the second time in a
month. The supervisory board of European Aeronautics Defense & Space, or EADS, which owns 80% of
Airbus, didn't meet yesterday, as had been expected. The meeting was supposed to have included approval
of the A3XX project. EADS said unspecified "outstanding" issues needed to be resolved.
Tate & Lyle May Sell U.S. Sugar Assets
Britain's Tate & Lyle, the world's largest sugar company, warned it saw no sign of recovery in a difficult U.S.
sugar market and didn't rule out a sale of its U.S. business after further review. Despite a generally poor
outlook, the company reported pretax profit, before restructuring and other exceptional items, rose 30% to
GBP 225 million ($342.5 million) for the fiscal year ended March 25. Including one-time items, pretax profit
rose 3.8% to GBP 191 million. Sales fell 6% to GBP 4.09 billion. Tate & Lyle also said it is selling its
Bundaberg cane-sugar operation in Australia and will buy out minority shareholders in its U.S. sweeteners
unit Staley and starch producer Amylum in Europe. Its chief executive said he hopes to report on the review
of U.S. sugar operations in November.
Deutsche Telekom Offering Scores in U.S.
Fueled by surprisingly large demand from the U.S., orders for a global offering of shares in Deutsche
Telekom are expected to exceed the number of shares available. Today is the deadline for individual
European investors who want to qualify for a price discount of three euros a share and a bonus system that
awards one extra share for every 10 held at least 18 months. The German government and state
development bank are selling 200 million shares, or 6.6% of the company, in an offering that concludes June
19, when trading in the new shares begins. U.S. and Japanese investors don't qualify for the price discount
but they can place orders through June 15 and qualify for the bonus system. Deutsche Telekom closed at
67.22 euros, down 52 cents, in German trading.
Zimbabwe Opposition Notes U.S. Action
Zimbabwe's main opposition party welcomed U.S. condemnation of President Robert Mugabe's handling of a
land crisis but warned sanctions would hurt average Zimbabweans. The U.S. Senate Foreign Relations
Committee this week approved a bill to suspend U.S. assistance to Zimbabwe until democracy and rule of
law are restored. Meanwhile, Zimbabwe's gold-mining industry is in danger of collapsing, the industry's
Chamber of Mines said.

BRIEFLY:
-- German industrial production surged 1.5% in April from March, the Finance Ministry said, well ahead of
market expectations of an 0.8% rise. Unemployment continued to drop, by 27,000 in May, on a seasonally

-- Hong Kong conglomerate Hutchison Whampoa said it will team with Sweden's largest investment holding

-- The presidents of Croatia and Romania agreed to work on clearing the Danube River of 1999 war damage
in Serbia to facilitate traffic along the entire river, and discussed building an oil pipeline connecting the

ASIA/PACIFIC
Malaysia Industrial Output Rises 18.6%
Malaysia's industrial production in April rose a preliminary 18.6% from a year earlier, the ninth straight month
of double-digit year-to-year growth in output, the government's statistics agency said. Output declined 0.9%
from the month before. Manufacturing production rose 23.6% from a year before, compared with 24% in
March. Mining output was up 2.9%, compared with 0.2% in March. Growth in electricity output also fell slightly
to 4.0% in April, compared with an annual 5.1% in March.
Qualcomm Signs China Licensing Deals
Qualcomm of the U.S. has signed licensing deals with eight Chinese manufacturers to develop equipment
using its CDMA mobile-phone technology, according to a report in the official China Daily. Financial terms
weren't disclosed. The Chinese companies will develop handsets and wireless base stations that use both
current-generation and third-generation CDMA technology, it said. A Qualcomm official confirmed that deals
had been signed but declined to provide details. The deals come less than a week after China United
Telecommunications, or China Unicom, said it had shelved plans for a network based on Qualcomm's
current-generation technology but would eventually use third-generation CDMA. In response to the setback,
Qualcomm said it would localize production of more components in order to win backing of Chinese
regulators. Eastern Communications, China's largest state-owned mobile-equipment maker and one of the
eight companies licensing Qualcomm's technology, announced its deal with the U.S. company on Tuesday.
Newbridge Bid for Thai Bank Is Rejected
Thailand's central bank rejected an offer by Newbridge Capital of the U.S. to acquire ailing Siam City Bank.
The Thai central bank said it will reconsider what to do with Siam City Bank, which was nationalized by the
government. Newbridge's offer was too low and left unclear what returns the government would receive from
the sale of Siam City, according to a member of the committee reviewing bids for the bank who represented
the Thai attorney general's office. Siam City is the biggest and most difficult to sell of four nationalized banks
put up for auction last year by the central bank. The central bank sold two of the banks in 1999, and last
month announced it had sold a third, Bangkok Metropolitan Bank, to Britain's HSBC Holdings.
BRIEFLY:
-- Singapore Press Holdings said it will apply for two television licenses and one radio-broadcasting license.
The decision comes days after a media-market shake-up by Singapore's government.
-- Seven airlines will be launched this year in Indonesia, despite the local industry's struggle to recover from
the economic crisis that led to a dramatic decline in passenger numbers, the Indonesian National Air Carriers
Association said. Most of the new airlines will operate on domestic routes.

THE AMERICAS
AES Wins Control of Venezuela's EDC
U.S. power company AES said it will take over management of Electricidad de Caracas, or EDC, within 45
days, after winning control of Venezuela's biggest publicly traded electric utility at a cost of more than $1
billion. AES, Arlington, Va., said it had agreed to buy 49% of EDC's shares through a tender offer in the U.S.
that closed Wednesday. Added to the 1.1% of EDC it already owned, it gave the leading U.S. power-plant
developer a majority stake in EDC, which supplies power to the Venezuelan capital. Taking into account a
Venezuelan tender that closes on Monday, AES currently has offers to buy over 57% of Electricidad. This

--- Page 77 ---
Page 77 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
state of affairs puts a price tag so far of $1.18 billion on the hostile takeover, the largest in Venezuelan
history. Separately, EDC's board, conceding AES had won control of EDC, told shareholders to either sell
their shares to AES or subscribe to EDC's own share-repurchase program. "EDC's current board always held
shareholder value as its highest priority, and still does," EDC's acting president said.
Lafarge to Reduce Its Blue Circle Stake
French building-materials company Lafarge said it will reduce its stake in British cement company Blue Circle
to less than 10% over the next two years, following an agreement reached with Canadian competition
authorities. After a failed takeover attempt of the British company, Lafarge holds 19.9% of Blue Circle's
capital, and Lafarge's advisory bank, Dresdner Kleinwort Benson, owns 9.7%. Under Canadian competition
rules, Lafarge can't hold directly or indirectly more than 10% of Blue Circle. Both companies have significant
market shares in Canada. Lafarge said that it has renounced its agreement with Dresdner Kleinwort Benson
regarding Blue Circle shares.
BRIEFLY:
-- Brazilian aircraft maker Empresa Brasileira de Aeronautica, or Embraer, and Swiss engineering group
Liebherr International agreed to create a jointly operated aircraft-equipment company, in which Embraer will
hold 60% and Liebherr 40%. Financial terms weren't disclosed.
-- Mexico's central bank said consumer prices rose 0.37% in May from April, the slowest rate for May in 28
years, and were up 9.48% from a year earlier.
-- Peru's telecommunications regulator proposed that interconnection charges for fixed-line telephone service
should be reduced by 42% to 1.68 cents a second starting in July.

Gold Futures Prices Rise on Weakness in Dollar
Commodities
Gold Futures Prices Rise on Weakness in Dollar
By David Bogoslaw
Dow Jones Newswires
457 words
5 June 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
C19
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Gold futures rallied Friday as weakness in the dollar followed the release of
weaker-than-forecast U.S. employment data.
Although the data were viewed as a sign of the economy cooling in response to higher interest rates,
dealers didn't focus on the reduced appeal that implied for gold as a hedge against inflation. Instead, they
read the fall in the dollar as shoring up demand for the metal in nondollar nations.
Rising prices spurred a scramble by traders to cover short positions, buying futures to cancel out previous
sales, positions which had grown significantly since early May.
At the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange, August gold settled $8.80 higher at $284.20 a
troy ounce.
The move was all the more dramatic in light of gold futures sinking to new lows at $270.70 just one week
ago, pressured by spillover from selling in the cash market, said Leonard Kaplan, president of Prospector
Asset Management in Chicago.
"Too many people were overly negative on the [central bank] auctions, so they were heavily short the
market," said George Gero, senior vice president at Prudential Securities in New York.
Sales of gold by central banks have been a major factor in the long-term bear trend in gold prices.
Mr. Gero explained that options trading contributed to the rise in gold futures, with those traders who were
short in futures buying calls to hedge their positions. Call options on futures confer the right, but not the
obligation, to purchase gold futures.
He expected there to be much more bidding interest in the next Bank of England auction set for July by those
who have bought call options and choose to exercise them.
Friday's Commitments of Traders report from the Commodity Futures Trading Commission showed large
speculators short of gold to the tune of about 40,000 lots as of May 30.
In other commodity markets:
SUGAR: Futures gained on the Coffee, Sugar & Cocoa Exchange, as bullish fundamentals continued to
encourage buying by trade houses and brokers, participants said. The July contract added 0.35 cent to 8.14
cents a pound. Traders also pointed to talk of potential buying from China as a supportive influence.
SOYBEANS: Futures climbed at the Chicago Board of Trade on new forecasts predicting hotter, drier
conditions to hit the Midwest by late next week, which revived worries about crop damage. The July contract
rose 11.25 cents to $5.3375 a bushel. Although agronomists said it's too early to worry about heat and
dryness, traders remain mindful of a U.S. government warning of drought earlier this year.

Silver Refuses to Shine Despite Bullish Outlook
Commodities
Silver Refuses to Shine Despite Bullish Outlook
By Peter A. McKay
Dow Jones Newswires
939 words
30 May 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
C17
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Analysts have touted silver for some time now as the antigold, the metal not plagued by central-bank selling
and investor apathy like its yellow-metal cousin.
But at least for the past year or so, those problems have also dogged silver -- which is why silver futures
prices have hovered around a dreary $5 an ounce despite excess demand.
As described in the industry-sponsored Silver Institute's latest industry survey, published last week, the sale
of 61 million ounces of silver by the People's Bank of China bogged down silver prices in 1999, and
world-wide mine production fell for the first time in five years.
Surely, these issues aren't as acute for silver as for gold, whose price slipped again last week -- and remains
virtually unchanged from a year ago at just more than $272 an ounce -- in part because of another 25-ton
Bank of England auction of that metal.
Yet the institute's findings struck an ironic note for a resource that is being touted as something of a safer
haven than another faltering traditional investor refuge. Many analysts' long-term outlook for silver is still
bullish, and some disagreed with the survey's conclusions even as they acknowledged the data as yet
another sign of China's growing clout in the silver market.
"Their sales were definitely a surprise, a wild card," said Arthur Brown, chairman, president and chief
executive of Hecla Mining Co., Coeur d'Alene, Idaho. "A very good question now is how much more do they
have, and will they continue to sell it at these prices?"
The Silver Institute survey, conducted by Gold Fields Mineral Services, showed a 6% increase in global silver
supply last year to 888.2 million ounces. That number fell short of world-wide demand, however, by 156
million ounces and included 87 million ounces of official sector sales, more than double the previous year's
total.
Most of that came from the Chinese bank, and it helps explain why silver prices have been so dreary, said
Philip Klapwijk, managing director of GFMS.
"It's a bit hard to describe this as a trend because, unlike in gold, there are very few official holders who have
any silver stocks worth talking about," Mr. Klapwijk said. "The Chinese just happen to be the largest one."
The governmental role in China's silver market is quickly fading, however, and may even have been
exaggerated in the Silver Institute report, said Jeffrey M. Christian, managing director of the New York
analysis and consulting firm CPM Group.
He said the People's Bank of China formerly controlled all the nation's silver sales by law, and frequently
bought for about 15% below the world market price and sold for about 15% above. As a result, silver
smuggling by jewelers and refiners to other parts of Asia where they could get full value for their metal
became rampant.
That has been changing since 1997 or so as the Chinese government has begun issuing import waivers to
smelters who promise to later export their finished products and "erase" those sales' price effect within China
itself, Mr. Christian said.
"What's going on is really just a rerouting of metal," said Mr. Christian, who has consulted for a Chinese silver
firm trying to obtain a government waiver. "In general, the people who buy and sell metal globally have
become more sophisticated in masking exactly what they're doing."

--- Page 80 ---
Page 80 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
A CPM analysis earlier this year, written primarily by Mr. Christian, reported much smaller government silver
sales than the Silver Institute of 10 million ounces in 1999.
CPM also reported a 6.4% increase in mine production, compared with a 5% drop reported by the institute.
Metals analyst John H. Hill, Salomon Smith Barney, said he tends to side with the higher government sales
numbers and lower mine output reported by the Silver Institute.
"It's a fact, a new development, and it explains a lot of things about last year," he said. "That's no knock on
CPM, because you have to realize that their report came out in February, when people were more interested
in still talking about 1999."
As China continues to "liberate" its silver industry, it could supplant India as the world's largest consumer,
said Thomas S. Kaplan, chairman and chief executive of Apex Silver Mines Ltd.
"After so many years of controlling the market, it makes sense that the government would have some stores,
which is what we're seeing now," he said.
Nearby July silver futures rose 1.5 cents to $4.963 an ounce Friday on the Comex division of the New York
Mercantile Exchange on news of a weaker dollar and strong buying from India.
In other commodity markets Friday:
ENERGY: Crude-oil and refined-products futures tumbled at Nymex as traders booked profits ahead of the
long holiday weekend. Nearby June gasoline futures climbed to a post-Gulf War high of $1.0284 a gallon in
overnight trading but closed with a loss of 0.97 cent at $1.0024. Crude futures lost some ground, with the July
contract dropping 51 cents to $30 a barrel. Natural gas rose 17 cents to $4.406 per million British thermal
units, capping a 58.1-cent rise for the week on concerns about low reserves heading into the summer.
HOGS: Futures gained on the Chicago Mercantile Exchange, supported by notions that recent selling pushed
prices too low. The June contract rose 0.72 cent to 68.75 cents a pound.

ASIA/PACIFIC
Hyundai Patriarch
Moves to Shore Up
Hyundai Motor Unit
In a surprise move, the founder of Hyundai Group, South Korea's largest conglomerate, bought shares of
Hyundai Motor in a step toward becoming the largest shareholder of the country's No. 1 car maker. The
purchase by Chung Ju Yung, Hyundai's 84-year-old patriarch, shows the importance Hyundai Group places
on its automobile business, because Mr. Chung sold shares in a handful of other Hyundai affiliates, including
Hyundai Heavy Industries and Hyundai Engineering & Construction, to bolster his position in Hyundai Motor.
"The purchase was to actively support the car business, and help tie up an alliance with a top global
automobile maker," a Hyundai spokesman said.
Hyundai Motor has been seeking an alliance with a strategic partner to compete in global markets. A strategic
alliance with a top automobile maker could also help Hyundai in bidding for ailing South Korean auto maker
Daewoo Motor, which Ford Motor and General Motors are also aggressively pursuing.
Vodafone Pacific Abandons Planned IPO
Wireless operator Vodafone Pacific, which is 91%-owned by Britain's Vodafone AirTouch, withdrew its
planned initial public offering. An official of adviser Goldman Sachs Group said "current market conditions are
too volatile." The offering of 17.5% of the company originally was set to raise about three billion Australian
dollars (US$1.72 billion) but the amount had been cut sharply. Vodafone Pacific said it will continue to
"monitor market conditions."
Hughes Seeks to Cancel Japan Rockets
In the latest setback for Japan's aerospace program, U.S. satellite maker Hughes Space & Communications
has told Japan's Rocket System Corp., or RSC, that it wants to cancel an order for 10 rockets that are similar
to a model that has had persistent problems. Hughes Space & Communications, a unit of General Motors'
Hughes Electronics subsidiary, has said it wants to scrap an order for 10 H-2A rockets, each of which costs
between 8.5 billion yen and 9 billion yen ($78.8 million and $83.4 million), according to a spokesman for
Japan's National Space Development Agency. RSC is negotiating with Hughes to convince it to change its
mind, an RSC spokesman said. The orders were placed in 1996. Japan's space program has been plagued
by cost overruns and technical difficulties, especially with the H-2 rocket, the more costly version of the H-2A.
Jakarta May Probe Freeport-McMoRan
U.S. mining company Freeport-McMoRan Copper & Gold may be investigated for alleged human-rights
abuses when an independent commission is set up next month to hear cases in West Papua province, an
Indonesian minister said. Human-rights activists have accused Freeport of providing vehicles and facilities to
Indonesian security forces fighting separatists. A spokesman for Freeport in New Orleans said the company
welcomes an investigation of recent allegations by Indonesia's human-rights commission. "We fully support
and will cooperate with any investigation of those allegations," he said.
China Ad Ban Called `Incomprehensible'
Taiwan's new president, Chen Shui-bian, said it was "incomprehensible" and "regrettable" that China has
banned Sprite ads featuring Taiwan pop star Chang Huei-mei because she sang the island's national anthem

--- Page 82 ---

at his inauguration Saturday. The ads had run on Chinese television before being pulled after her
appearance. Coca-Cola, which makes Sprite, said it won't contest the Chinese ban.

-- U.N. Secretary-General Kofi Annan criticized Fiji's traditional chiefs for suggesting that their prime minister,
held hostage by rebels for the past week, should be replaced by an interim administration.
-- Cable & Wireless HKT's board unanimously endorsed a takeover offer from Pacific Century CyberWorks,

EUROPE
ING Profit Jumps 32% on Bank Earnings
ING Group of the Netherlands said first-quarter profit rose 32% from a year earlier to 1.02 billion euros
($924.2 million), at the high end of analysts' estimates. The banking and insurance company raised its
forecast for full-year net operating profit per share to rise at least 15%, up from 10% forecast earlier. Banking
division profit rose 45% to 497 million euros, while insurance division earnings rose 22% to 526 million euros,
boosted by growing operations in the U.S. and ING's home market.
Deutsche Telekom War Chest Approved
Deutsche Telekom shareholders approved a potential capital increase of 1.5 billion shares that would add
99.9 billion euros ($90.5 billion) to the German telecommunication company's acquisitions war chest.
Deutsche Telekom's chief executive, Ron Sommer, defending his international strategy at the company's
annual shareholders meeting, said the preapproved capital would give Telekom the chance to make a
"megamerger" on the scale of the merger of auto makers that created DaimlerChrysler. "Any U.S. telecoms
company could be on our radar screen," Mr. Sommer said.
Credit Lyonnais Russian Unit Searched
Russian tax police searched the Moscow offices of the Russian unit of French bank Credit Lyonnais,
according to the Russian news agency ITAR-Tass. The tax police said the search was conducted because
the branch, Credit Lyonnais Rusbank, hasn't provided tax authorities with requested documents, the report
said. A bank employee reached by telephone declined to give details of the search. The search follows the
seizure by a French court of assets owned by Eurobank, a subsidiary of Russia's central bank, in response
to a Swedish court ruling in favor of Swiss trading company Noga.
Royal Bank of Scotland to Sell Stake
Royal Bank of Scotland Group said it agreed to sell its interests in the former NatWest Equity Partners for
about GBP 522 million ($771.1 million). NatWest Equity Partners, which has been renamed Bridgepoint
Capital, is a private-equity firm. Royal Bank's holdings are being sold to a consortium led by European
private-equity company Coller Capital. Royal Bank said the consortium will assume its unfunded
commitments for the NatWest portfolios, putting the overall value of the deal at about $985 million.
BRIEFLY:
-- Swiss bank UBS posted first-quarter results that beat market expectations. Profit rose 41% from a year
earlier to 2.22 billion Swiss francs ($1.29 billion). The group's most improved performer was
investment-banking arm UBS Warburg, whose pretax profit rose 86% to 1.32 billion francs.
-- The European Central Bank left interest rates unchanged, in the face of pressure to support the weak
euro by following the U.S. Federal Reserve's recent half-point rate increase.

Brazil Trade Forecast Likely to Be Revised
Brazil's projected trade surplus of $4 billion to $5 billion this year may be revised downward as early as next
month because of unexpectedly high levels of imports, the country's trade minister said. The trade surplus,
which private economists have pegged much lower than the government, has been less than projected
because commodity prices haven't recovered as quickly as had been expected, he said. Brazil's 1999 trade
deficit was $1.21 billion.
Ivanhoe Makes Cambridge Shopping Bid
Ivanhoe of Canada said it intends to make an offer of 12.50 Canadian dollars ($8.28) a share for all of the
shares of Canada's Cambridge Shopping Centres that it doesn't already own. Ivanhoe said it and its affiliates
own about 67% of Cambridge's common shares on a diluted basis. Ivanhoe said its offer price represents a
premium of about 45% to Cambridge's average share price in the 30 days before the offer's announcement.
Ivanhoe said it will also make concurrent offers to acquire all convertible debentures outstanding of
Cambridge, excluding the 6% debentures due June 30, 2007. Ivanhoe said the total value of the
common-share and debenture offers will be about C$700 million. Cambridge Shopping Centres said its board
will meet today to discuss Ivanhoe's offer.
Brazil to Propose Antitobacco Measures
The Brazilian government, taking aim at the tobacco industry, has asked the country's Congress to approve a
surtax on cigarette sales and a ban on all advertising and sponsorship of cultural and sports events by
tobacco companies. Health Minster Jose Serra said the goal is to reduce smoking by 40% within four years
with the new measures, which will be submitted to Congress next Wednesday to coincide with world-wide
antitobacco observances.
BRIEFLY:
-- Ecuadorean President Gustavo Noboa said the cost of pricier fuels will be raised and import duties cut to
help pull the country out of the economic doldrums. "For the well-being of our economy, such products must
be paid for at market value," he said.

ASIA/PACIFIC
Colombian Turmoil,
S&P Downgrade Add
To Currency's Woes
A bitter battle between Colombian President Andres Pastrana and the opposition-led Congress has dealt a
deadly blow to the country's bid -- backed by the International Monetary Fund -- to pull out of the country's
deepest recession on record. The Colombian peso weakened to a record of 2139.00 to the dollar, compared
with 2109.00 on Tuesday, as Standard & Poor's Ratings Services downgraded the country's long-term
foreign-currency credit rating to double-B from double-B-plus because of concerns the government won't
meet deficit-reduction targets agreed to in its $2.7 billion Extended Fund Facility with the IMF. Analysts said
the downgrade had been largely priced into the market because it brought S&P's rating into line with that of
Moody's Investors Service.
The showdown between the Conservative government and the Liberal opposition over an anticorruption
referendum has paralyzed passage of economic reforms needed to trim the budget. Liberals, who control
both houses of Congress, said they would block a series of key reforms, mandated by the IMF, unless the
government agreed to a power-sharing deal.
Latin American PC Shipments Jump 50%
Latin America's personal-computer market showed the strongest first-quarter growth in five years, as product
shipments rose nearly 50% from a year earlier, according to research firm International Data. Desktop,
notebook and PC-server shipments during the quarter totaled 1.5 million units, valued at $1.92 billion. The
80% growth in Brazil's PC market led the region. Mexican PC shipments grew 63%, while Argentina and
Chile saw PC-market growth of 29% and 38%, respectively. Compaq Computer maintained its top 19%
market share. Hewlett-Packard followed with nearly 6%; International Business Machines and Taiwan's Acer
followed.
BBVA Hints at Legal Action on Bancomer
Spain's Banco Bilbao Vizcaya Argentaria raised the specter of legal action in the takeover battle for Mexico's
second-largest bank, Grupo Financiero Bancomer. BBVA's director of Latin American operations said BBVA
is confident its $1.2 billion capitalization plan, with a capital injection of $600 million, is superior to the $2.4
billion Bancomer bid from Mexico's Grupo Financiero Banamex-Accival, or Banacci. Banacci's deal offers an
injection of $300 million in cash. The BBVA official said that, apart from regulatory issues, Bancomer could
face legal consequences if BBVA's offer, which was accepted in March before Banacci's bid, is turned down.
"We have a contract," he said.
Colombian Guerrillas Bomb Power Plant
Colombia's main Pacific seaport, Buenaventura, was left without power after guerrillas entered a hydroelectric
plant and set off a bomb in the control room. Guerrillas from the Revolutionary Armed Forces of Colombia, or
FARC, evacuated the control room before setting off a natural-gas cylinder, and no employees were injured,
said a spokeswoman for energy company EPSA, partly owned by Reliant Energy, Houston. FARC
commandos last year stormed a larger hydroelectric plant nearby and occupied it for five days, holding 145
people hostage.
Colombian Governors Sue Philip Morris

--- Page 85 ---
Page 85 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Colombian officials have sued Philip Morris in federal court in New York, accusing the tobacco company, in a
civil racketeering lawsuit, of scheming with smugglers to cheat state governments and the city of Bogota out
of billions of dollars in taxes. Michael York, an attorney for Philip Morris, called the accusations by 22 state
governors "wacky." He said there "is just no legal or factual basis for this lawsuit."
BRIEFLY:
-- The Argentine government said the country's beef industry completed its first year free of hoof-and-mouth
disease without assistance of vaccinations, opening the way to an improved export-quality designation and
additional markets.

EUROPE
Thyssen Krupp Results Miss Estimates
German steel and engineering company Thyssen Krupp reported fiscal first-half profit before taxes and
minority interests rose 96% to 366.5 million euros ($332.3 million). But the results were below analysts'
forecasts for pretax profit of 484 million euros for the six months ended March 31, and the company's shares
plummeted 16% in Frankfurt trading. The average price of steel produced by its Thyssen Krupp Stahl unit
was slightly below the average price a year earlier. Thyssen Krupp plans to spin off 25% to 35% of its steel
unit this year and to eventually hold only a minority stake, as it focuses on its automotive and mechanical
engineering businesses.
EU to Renew WTO Beef-Hormone Dispute
The European Union is planning to take its dispute over hormone-treated beef back to the World Trade
Organization, claiming it now has the scientific proof and a new legal strategy to force the U.S. and Canada to
drop $124 million in trade sanctions. The EU, armed with a new report that it said confirms the health risks
from eating hormone-treated beef, said it will be ready to challenge the sanctions as soon as July 2001. But it
must first implement cosmetic changes to EU law that it says will allow the EU to comply with WTO rules on
trade and food safety.
German Government Divided on Army
Germany's coalition government is divided over a drastic reform plan for the country's armed forces that calls
for a near-elimination of the draft and has sparked a public debate about the need for mandatory military
service. The pacifist Green Party, which has long called for an end to the draft, welcomed the reform proposal
presented by a high-profile commission led by former German President Richard von Weizsaecker. Their
partners in the national coalition, the Social Democrats of Chancellor Gerhard Schroeder, shy away from
such drastic change. Defense Minister Rudolf Scharping said that the German military wouldn't drop
mandatory service, although he didn't rule out reductions in the number of draftees and overall cuts.
Lloyd's to Launch New Non-U.K. Market
Lloyd's of London said it would open up the world's biggest insurance market to brokers based outside
London next year, citing the need to keep pace with a rapidly changing industry. Lloyd's currently allows full
access only to brokers based in London and directly regulated by Lloyd's. The insurance market said British
and overseas brokers would be able to access the market from Jan. 1, provided they are regulated externally
and met tough new technical and customer-service standards.
Euro-Zone Prices Threatened by Euro
Inflation in the 11-nation euro zone is falling from its March peak, but not as fast or as far as previously
thought. The main reason: the weak euro. Preliminary consumer-price data from Germany were weaker than
expected, indicating an overall rise of around 1.5% for May from a year earlier and about flat compared with
April. But German producer prices were higher than expected. The April figures showed a 2.1% increase from
a year before and a 0.4% rise from March.

after considering postponing the deal -- despite the continuing volatility in tech stocks. Prudential sn't related
to the U.S. insurance company of the same name.

South Korea, Taiwan Lead Asian Growth
Asian first-quarter growth figures reflect the uneven nature of the region's economic expansion. At the head of
the class are South Korea and Taiwan, which have capitalized on strong exports and technology to post
impressive gains in gross domestic product, while close behind are Singapore and Hong Kong. Lagging
behind are Indonesia and the Philippines. The Asian Development Bank projects the four growth leaders will
post average GDP expansion of 6.5% this year. For the rest of the region, excluding Japan, the ADB predicts
average GDP growth of 4.6%. South Korea this week reported first-quarter GDP rose 12.8% from a year
earlier, while Taiwan last week reported a 7.9% GDP rise.
Freeport to Cut Indonesian Mine Output
Freeport-McMoRan Copper & Gold of the U.S. said it agreed to temporarily cut production at its Indonesian
copper and gold mine by about 13% while investigations continue into an accident that killed four workers
and raised environmental concerns. Freeport said that after discussions with government officials, it
voluntarily agreed to limit production to no more than 200,000 metric tons of ore a day at the Grasberg open
pit mine. The mine currently produces about 230,000 tons of ore a day.
Indonesia Minister Seeks Security Funds
Indonesia's defense minister, Juwono Sudarsono, said he is optimistic the country can provide the security
that investors need but warned that it could take years, a bigger defense budget and far more police officers.
His comments came in the wake of a series of protests and lootings that have disturbed production at some
mines and plantations around the country. He said the defense budget is "No. 7 in priority" in the
government's outlays.

record 252.14 billion yen ($2.37 billion). Revenue climbed 19% to 3.719 trillion yen.
