Gold Futures Prices Drop Following BOE Sale
Commodities
Gold Futures Prices Drop Following BOE Sale
By Peter A. McKay
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
778 words
24 May 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
C17
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Another Bank of England gold auction yesterday thumped futures for the metal, which have
languished lately under the U.S. dollar's strength.
The Bank of England sold 25 tons of gold yesterday at an allotment price of $275.25 an ounce. The sale also
had a scaling factor of 73.7%, which is the rate at which the bank could fill orders for given amounts of gold
at its allotment price after selling as much metal as possible above that bid.
Relatively high scaling factors, like yesterday's, mean an auction has been flooded with lowball orders that
signal bearish sentiment. Traders also found yesterday's oversubscription rate of 2.7 times the amount of
gold tendered to be lackluster, compared with previous British auctions.
Nearby June gold futures fell $1.50 to $274.70 an ounce on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile
Exchange.
Afterward, analysts said the British auction's fundamental effect to supply and demand will be nominal, even
though its symbolism helped spark the futures sell-off.
"That amount of gold they auctioned changes hands every 15 or 20 minutes on the commodity exchanges,"
said gold fund manager Harry Bingham, of Van Eck Global. "But when people decide that these are
all-knowing seers who have knowledge of the world, it becomes important."
Gold prices have actually been declining throughout the spring, plunging through $290, then $280, and
leaving the once-reachable benchmark of $300 as little more than a gleam in traders' eyes.
Mr. Bingham and other market watchers blamed the strength of the U.S. dollar as a major culprit in that
longerterm decline. They said that since gold trades in U.S. dollars, it's now attractive for foreign producers
and investors to sell as much gold forward as possible, hoarding dollars instead of their local currencies.
And after the Federal Reserve's latest interest-rate increase, that trend doesn't seem likely to end anytime
soon, said analyst Scott Meyers, of Pioneer Futures Inc.
Fed Chairman Alan Greenspan is "trying to fend off inflation, and doing a great job at it. He's also hurting
metals," said Mr. Meyers, who estimates that gold began its latest downtrend in early October.
A recent report by the industry-funded World Gold Council showed a 29% dip in world-wide investor demand
for gold in this year's first quarter, although a 7% rise in jewelry consumption kept the metal on pace with last
year's world-wide demand of 795.2 tons.
Most of the dip in investor interest came from the U.S. following the smooth year-2000 transition, said WGC
analyst George Milling-Stanley.
He said gold coin sales fell sharply this first quarter, compared with the 1999 period when consumers
seemed to be hoarding them in case a New Year's computer glitch hampered their ability to draw money from
banks.
Mr. Milling-Stanley said, however, that many of those buyers seem to still be holding onto their coins as a
long-term investment, even if a new wave of investors hasn't supplanted them to keep this quarter's demand
high.
"I think we can get somewhat back to normal now," he said.

--- Page 88 ---
Page 88 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In other commodity trading yesterday:
ELECTRICITY: Futures fell, but that only shaved a little off the gains of the previous two days, which were
built on a heat wave in California. Market observers said spiking prices could be a frequent occurrence this
summer because there's a lack of capacity to deal with the usual surge in demand arising from air
conditioners. Higher electricity prices are also feeding into natural gas. The California Independent System
Operator, which is responsible for maintaining the state's grid, bought electricity at an average of $133 a
megawatt-hour, having made purchases at the maximum allowable $750 for much of Monday afternoon. By
comparison, the wholesale price of power last week in California averaged about $40/MWh for daytime hour.
The 12-month forward average price of electricity futures for delivery to the California-Oregon border, which
are traded on Nymex, have risen to $52/MWh from $28.50/MWh a year ago.
SOYBEANS: Futures fell at the Chicago Board of Trade on bearish Midwest weather forecasts and on selling
related to key chart levels, traders said. The July contract fell 4.25 cents to $5.4875 a bushel. Forecasters
called for as much as 1.25 inches of rain over 70% of the Midwest on the coming three-day Memorial Day
weekend, traders said.

EUROPE
Sonera, Russia Firm
Win Mobile License
In Closed Process
A company controlled by Finland's Sonera and Russia's national telecommunications holding company,
Svyazinvest, has won a license to operate a mobile-phone network in Moscow. But critics said the way the
license was awarded -- with no open tender and no public discussion -- undermined the principle of fair
competition that President Vladimir Putin's new government has promised to support. Moscow's cellular
market is split between two companies -- Vimpelcom and Mobile TeleSystems -- which have fought a bitter
price war, and analysts said Sonera's appearance will further squeeze earnings in a tight market. The license
was awarded to Sonic Duo, a company in which Sonera has a 35% stake, with the rest held by a unit of
Svyazinvest, which controls nearly every regional fixed-line telecommunications company in Russia.
Sonera had been the main contender for the third so-called GSM license. But critics said the license award is
also linked with the government's plans to sell a stake of 25% minus two shares in Svyazinvest.
France Telecom to Discuss Polish Stake
Poland's Treasury Ministry said it picked a consortium of France Telecom and Poland's Kulczyk Holding for
exclusive negotiations on the sale of a 35% stake in state-owned Telekomunikacja Polska. As expected, the
group beat out a rival bid from Telecom Italia. The deal would be Central Europe's largest
telecommunications privatization. Poland's treasury minister gave no information on the size of France
Telecom's bid. He said the group has until the end of June to complete talks on a sales agreement with the
ministry.
Britain's Net Imperative Is Web Casualty
Net Imperative, a British online community and information resource for Internet professionals, has collapsed,
according to Internet investment company British Durlacher, holder of a 28.5% Net Imperative stake.
Durlacher said Net Imperative's board "has resolved to commence liquidation proceedings." Net Imperative's
demise follows that of British online fashion retailer Boo.com last week. Durlacher said its investment in Net
Imperative was about GBP 570,000 ($849,000), about 1.9% of its portfolio at cost. Durlacher shares have
fallen about 85% from their recent peak.
EBRD Board Debates Investment Thrust
The European Bank for Reconstruction and Development's board elected French Treasury Secretary Jean
Lemierre as the bank's new president, and immediately thrust him into a debate among shareholder countries
over where the bank should invest. Mr. Lemierre listened at the board's annual meeting in Riga, Latvia, as
non-European Union countries pushed for the bank to direct more resources to less-developed countries of
the former Communist bloc, while most EU states said it should continue to support the most-advanced
candidates for EU accession.

-- French channels Television Francaise 1 and Canal Plus said they will acquire the stakes of ESPN, a unit of
Walt Disney, in sports channels Eurosport International and Eurosport France for $155 million.

SingTel's Fiscal-Year Profit Fell 5.3%
Singapore Telecommunications said it expects to maintain its earnings this fiscal year amid increased
competition and will focus on Asia, despite setbacks in some recent attempts at regional mergers and
acquisitions. The company said earnings for the fiscal year ended March 31 fell 5.3% to 1.85 billion
Singapore dollars (US$1.07 billion), slightly below analysts' expectations, because of a more aggressive
depreciation policy. SingTel's chief executive said it expects to maintain per-share earnings, which fell 5.7%
to 12.09 Singapore cents, in the current fiscal year, taking into account compensation it expects to receive
from the government after deregulation in the telecommunications market.
Two Japanese Banks Swing Back to Profit
Japan's Sumitomo Bank and Sakura Bank swung to fiscal-year profits after posting huge losses for the prior
year. Sakura Bank posted group net profit of 62.58 billion yen ($585.2 million) for the year ended March 31,
compared with a net loss of 479.27 billion yen the year before, when the bank accelerated its write-off of bad
debts. Sumitomo Bank reported group net profit of 61.88 billion for the fiscal year, compared with a net loss of
568.89 billion yen in the prior year, when it also accelerated write-offs of bad loans.
Rio Tinto Reports Lost Indonesian Output
Anglo-Australian mining company Rio Tinto said a road blockade over land rights by Indonesian activists has
reduced gold output by about 20,000 ounces at its Kelian Equatorial Mining unit in East Kalimantan over
three weeks. Protesters last week resumed a siege at the gold mine, which produces 400,000 ounces of
gold annually and has only one access road, after a May 11 agreement to restart talks on land claims failed
to hold.
Korean Air Posts Loss, as Fuel Costs Soar
Korean Air Lines posted a first-quarter net loss of 41.7 billion won ($37.2 million) and said the loss was mainly
the result of increased fuel prices. The South Korean flag carrier, which is also struggling to recover from a
long series of accidents and safety problems, had a pretax quarterly loss of 56.5 billion won, on revenue of
1.23 trillion won ($1.1 billion). Beginning this year, all listed Korean companies are required to report quarterly
results.

THE AMERICAS
Canada's Surplus May Have Doubled
Canada's federal budget surplus for the fiscal year ended March 31 could be more than double the
government's initial estimate of three billion Canadian dollars (US$2 billion), according to the Finance
Department's recent update. The department said it collected C$4.4 billion more in revenue than had been
projected; when final calculations are completed in September, the fiscal-year surplus could be C$7.4 billion
or more. Finance Minister Paul Martin last week said the extra revenue stemmed from the country's robust
economy. He said the surplus would be used to pay down the C$577 billion federal debt and to fund
programs such as health care.
Ecuador Freezes Prices on 120 Products
Ecuador's government and leading business associations agreed to freeze retail prices on 120 consumer
products in order to keep inflationary pressures in check, President Gustavo Noboa said. The price freezes
are necessary to avoid inflation resulting from planned salary increases, he said. The government plans to
announce shortly an increase in workers' minimum salaries, as the Andean country adjusts to the new link
between its currency and the U.S. dollar, and prepares for planned fuel-price increases. The list of products
published yesterday consists primarily of nonperishable foods, such as sugar, and cleaning supplies,
including soap. Under a recent standby-loan accord with the International Monetary Fund, Ecuador has
promised to bring domestic fuel prices to international levels within two years. Under the pact, fuel and
domestic gas prices would rise by 60% and 40%, respectively, in June. Another round of price increases
would occur in October.
Chile Capital-Gains-Reform Bill Planned
Chile's finance minister, Nicolas Eyzaguirre, said reform of capital-gains taxes that have been criticized for
holding down foreign investment were coming "very soon." He told reporters, after a meeting with a Chilean
senator, that it was possible that a clause proposing to modify capital-gains taxes might be tacked onto a
proposal already under way.

Old-Economy Rally: Is It Repeating Its Fade?
Abreast of the Market
Old-Economy Rally: Is It Repeating Its Fade?
By E.S. Browning
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
2,071 words
22 May 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Could this be, in Yogi Berra's timeless phrase, deja vu all over again?
Last year about this time, something very painful happened to Old Economy stocks: Their rebound ran out of
steam.
This year the Old Economy stocks -- old-style industrial and consumer-product companies -- have been
rebounding again, amid the mauling of the technology darlings. But some of last year's pain seems to be
creeping back in. The Old Economy rally has shown signs of weakness. The question is whether those stocks
already are fading again, turning themselves for a second year into one-month wonders.
"That's every value manager's nightmare," acknowledges Robert Morris, chief investment officer at Jersey
City, N.J., fund-management group Lord Abbett.
Value managers are professional investors who buy stocks that have been beaten down, in hopes that they
will rebound. Old Economy stocks, left for dead by go-go technology-stock investors, have become the value
managers' playground. And for a while this spring, anyone who toyed with Old Economy stocks looked like a
genius.
Consider what would have happened if you bought International Paper, a component of the Dow Jones
Industrial Average and one of the Old Economy's staples, back on March 9. In just over a month, through
Friday, April 14, International Paper gained 15%. Anyone lucky enough to make that move at that precise
time had a good laugh at the expense of anyone foolish enough to be holding tech highfliers. Once-brilliant
software-maker Oracle, one of the hottest tech stocks, fell 26% from March 9 through April 14.
But it was a brief window. Since then, although it hasn't gotten a lot of attention, the value stocks haven't held
up as well. Since April 14, International Paper has fallen 2.6%, while Oracle is back up 12%.
Many other tech highfliers, and other value stocks, have followed a similar pattern, disconcerting investors
who thought the industrial and consumer stocks were back to stay.
Now value managers are looking over their shoulders at last year, which isn't a pleasant memory. Last year at
this time, the market was having the same debate. That time, stocks such as International Paper and fellow
Dow Jones industrials Caterpillar and Procter & Gamble stopped advancing and entered a slump. Many
recovered later in the year, only to slump again this year. Their rebound was eclipsed by that of the roaring
technology sector, led by companies such as Oracle, by computer-maker Sun Microsystems and by
semiconductor giant Intel, which is one of the industrial average's four tech stocks.
Trying to figure out how the competition will play out this year is crucially important to investors who are
worrying about what to do with their money in these turbulent times.
The uncertainty played out again Friday, as the industrials, heavy in Old Economy stocks, fell 150.43 points,
or 1.4%, to 10626.85. But over the week as a whole, the Old Economy managed to edge back up. Even with
Friday's fall, the industrials advanced 17.48 points for the week, or 0.2%. The tech-loaded Nasdaq Composite
Index, by contrast, plunged 148.31 points, or 4.19% on Friday, wiping out gains from Monday and Tuesday
and leaving it down 3.9% for the week. It finished at 3390.40, just 69.11 points above its April 14 low of
3321.29.
International Paper staged a particularly strong comeback for the week, advancing 6%, while Oracle sagged
over the week, down 5.6%.

--- Page 93 ---
Page 93 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
That kind of news is making Mr. Morris and other fans of value stocks hope that the Old Economy will hold up
better this year. But having been burned last year, they aren't letting themselves relax. They know that tech
stocks have a habit of rebounding.
"Many people own technology, so they are rooting for it," notes Morgan Stanley Dean Witter investment
strategist Byron Wien. "And secondly, the earnings in technology are terrific, so the fundamentals are there.
There is always a possibility that they could come back."
But Mr. Wien, like Mr. Morris, still prefers the Old Economy. Why should this year be different? One word:
price. Mr. Wien thinks New Economy stocks still are too high priced at a time when interest rates are rising.
Higher rates make the expected big future earnings of fast-growing stocks less valuable, and make
alternative investments, with shorter payoffs, more attractive. Mr. Wien's preference is for drug stocks, oil
stocks, airlines and retailers.
Consider how much some of the big tech names have gained in just one year. Oracle's stock has more than
quintupled in value since this time in 1999, a staggering gain for an established company with more than $9
billion in annual revenue. Even after falling 21% from its high, it trades at more than 100 times expected
earnings for the next 52 weeks.
International Paper, on the other hand, still is 25% below where it was one year ago, despite its recent gains.
It trades at 12 times projected earnings.
Many "tech stocks still are just priced for perfection," says Mr. Morris of Lord Abbett. "That would be my
concern if I were sitting with a boatload of these kinds of exposures. And you see it in the market. There is a
lot of nervousness and people are selling at any sign of trouble."
True, say fans of the big, quality tech stocks. But that is exactly what the lovers of the Old Economy were
saying last year. Last spring, investors were recalling that the Nasdaq composite had soared almost 40% the
year before. Few thought it could do so again. They were wrong; it gained 86% in 1999. And it did so in an
environment of rising interest rates, because investors thought the rate boosts would be temporary.
No one is predicting another 80% year for the Nasdaq, but there are plenty of people who think that the tech
stocks will be leading the market ahead again by fall.
Interest rates won't be going up forever, notes PaineWebber investment strategist Edward Kerschner. "As
rates peak and come down again, the Intels and the Oracles and the Ciscos, because they generate good
growth, will be paid handsomely for that," he says.
After Labor Day, investors will start evaluating stocks based on what they are expected to do in 2001.
Because earnings in Old Economy stocks are up strongly this year, those companies will face tough
"comparisons"; it will be hard to show the same strong growth from the higher profit levels they are likely to
post this year.
"You aren't going to see double-digit growth in the industrial sector and you aren't going to see double-digit
growth in the more defensive food and tobacco group," Mr. Kerschner says. "They've had their pop. But the
tech stocks will have double-digit earnings."
Mr. Kerschner stresses that he has no time for the small "concept" stocks in the tech sector, which trade
mainly on hopes and still don't have earnings. Those will fall farther, he says.
He figures the place to be is stocks such as Hewlett-Packard, International Business Machines, Lucent
Technologies, Cisco Systems, Oracle, Motorola and, yes, even Microsoft.

Sycamore Networks (Nasdaq) stumbled, dropping 11 5/16 to 80 15/16. The Chelmsford, Mass., maker of
optical networking products reported fiscal third-quarter results late Thursday that, as expected, showed a
profit for the period, and a larger one than analysts expected. The stock still crumbled in the face of declines
in technology stocks Friday.

5/16 after ABN AMRO boosted its earnings forecast.

concern, citing a decision Thursday from a federal appeals court that supported Unocal's right to collect
royalty payments from other energy companies that a lower court said violated a Unocal patent in making
lower-emission gasoline for California drivers in 1996.

lost 5/16 to 24 7/16 and Noble Affiliates declined 11/16 to 39 1/2 , after Credit Suisse First Boston lowered its
ratings on the issues.
Owens Illinois lost 15/16 to 13. Credit Suisse First Boston cut its rating on the stock of the Toledo, Ohio,
maker of glass containers.
Calpine dropped 2 1/2 to 106 5/8 , continuing to drop even though the San Jose, Calif., energy company said
its shareholders approved a plan that let it split its stock on a 2-for-1 basis.
Coca-Cola Enterprises rallied intraday but finished down 1 1/4 at 14 5/8 . The stock had lost ground this week
after warning that profits would be weaker than expected, and on signs that the European Union's
investigation of the company's competitive practices may stretch through the end of this year.
ADC Telecommunications (Nasdaq) saw intraday gains disappear by the close. The Minneapolis maker of
communications equipment and software reported fiscal second-quarter earnings late Thursday that topped
Wall Street's forecasts. WR Hambrecht raised its earnings estimates and price target. The stock finished 2
1/8 lower at 58 5/16.
Portal Software (Nasdaq) climbed 8 1/8 to 47 1/8 . The Cupertino, Calif., developer of Internet
management-and-billing software posted a profit for its first quarter. The results prompted Chase H&Q to
reiterate its buy rating and establish a price target of 70 on the stock.
Synopsys (Nasdaq) gained 7/8 to 43 5/8 . The Mountain View, Calif., maker of electronic design automation
models posted fiscal second-quarter earnings late Thursday that beat Wall Street's forecasts. In a research
note, Credit Suisse First Boston, calling the second-quarter results "solid," set a price target of 75 on the
stock.
Nextlink Communications (Nasdaq) moved up 6 1/4 to 59 13/16. Salomon Smith Barney said Friday that the
McLean, Va., provider of broadband communications services has been hurt in recent selling, despite signs
the company has been hitting on all cylinders.
Some market watchers said the regard some investors had for the tech leaders may be tarnishing.
"We finally got some leadership in the Nasdaq composite," Todd Gold, technical strategist at Gruntal, said
Friday, with a trace of gallows humor. "Unfortunately, the leadership turned out to be to the downside.
"I wouldn't rule out the chance of a tradeable rally here in the short term," Mr. Gold added. "But, for any
longer-term perspective, the path of least resistance is to the downside."

Fed Is Wary of Expanding Clout of Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac
Economy
Fed Is Wary of Expanding Clout of Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac
By Jacob M. Schlesinger
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
842 words
19 May 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
WASHINGTON -- Federal Reserve officials seem to have some reservations about helping expand the clout
of Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac, the massive mortgage underwriters.
That hesitancy was implied in the central bank's announcement yesterday that it has launched a
"broad-gauge" study of the types of securities it should use while conducting monetary policy. For decades,
the Fed had bought and sold Treasury securities via dealers on Wall Street for such policy operations, which
allow the central bank to set interest-rate levels. But the shrinking supply of Treasury bonds, bills and notes
is forcing the Fed to seek alternative securities.
Since last summer, the Fed has used Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac mortgage-backed securities during open
market operations and recently decided to continue doing so through early next year.
But Fed officials agreed at their March policy-making session that they considered the use of notes from
those government-chartered institutions for now to be "temporary," according to minutes of that meeting
released yesterday. Officials also "noted" that the current use of Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac securities
"should not be read as indicating in any way how the [Fed] might ultimately choose to allocate the portfolio"
used in conducting monetary policy, the minutes said.
The minutes didn't say that the Fed would ultimately shy away from buying debt of the two companies. Nor
did the report elaborate on the Fed's opinion of Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac debt or the reasons for
conducting the study.
At a minimum, though, the report makes clear that the central bank wants to take its time before giving its
imprimatur to the mortgage companies as investors seek a replacement to Treasurys as the gold standard
for credit markets. Privately, some Fed officials have raised concerns that by using Fannie Mae and Freddie
Mac debt to conduct monetary policy, they would be sending a signal to markets the Fed considers that to
be the case.
"It's not our position to interpret the Fed minutes," said Fannie Mae spokeswoman Janice Daue. "We would
hope that we would be considered one of many alternatives" to Treasurys, she said, adding: "Ultimately, it's
the marketplace where the alternatives will be selected."
The Fed's new expression of caution comes as a growing coalition of policy makers -- from the Treasury
Department to leading congressmen -- have become increasingly vocal in their concern that Fannie Mae and
Freddie Mac are becoming too powerful for the good of the U.S. financial system.
At issue is whether or not the government is giving implicit support to those companies and how the
perception of such backing distorts financial markets.
Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac are now private companies, but their historical ties to the government -- and
their many special breaks granted by the government -- create an impression in financial markets of
suggested government backing.
As a result, Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac securities are treated by many investors as ultrasafe investments,
which lowers the companies' borrowing costs. That further fuels their expansion and allows them to take
greater risks, critics say.
Treasurys remain the Fed's "preferred means" for maintaining the target for the Fed funds rate, the rate
banks charge each other overnight, according to the minutes. But "anticipated paydowns of marketable
federal debt associated with projected budget surpluses" will limit the Fed's ability to keep relying on
government issues "without generating undesirable market repercussions," the minutes added.

--- Page 96 ---
Page 96 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Fed officials haven't given detailed public comments on what they consider the best alternative. In response
to a letter from a congressman in March, Fed Chairman Alan Greenspan noted that "Fannie Mae and Freddie
Mac already have attempted to take advantage of this situation." But he added, "it is possible that . . . entirely
private firms could serve as benchmarks."
The minutes of the March meeting also contain a summary of Fed deliberations on setting interest rates and
reveal how dramatically sentiment has shifted inside the bank in recent weeks about the need to slow the
economy. Fed officials voted Tuesday to raise their key short-term interest rate by half a percentage point,
twice the size of the rate increase approved at the March meeting. Whether or not there was any dissent to
the more aggressive stance won't be publicized until early July.
In March, however, the 10 voting members of the policy committee voted unanimously to raise rates by just a
quarter of a point. A half-point move doesn't appear to have been seriously weighed.
"Some members commented that, although a more forceful policy of [half a point] might be needed at some
point, measured and predictable policy tightening moves . . . still were desirable in current circumstances,
which included somewhat unsettled financial markets," the minutes said.

THE AMERICAS
Major Firms Plan
Web Marketplace
For Mining, Metals
Fourteen of the world's top mining and metals companies said they are forming an Internet-based
marketplace aimed at bringing producers and suppliers in more than 100 countries together online. The
founding shareholders of the procurement Web site include aluminum producers Alcan Aluminium and Alcoa,
gold miners Anglo American and Barrick Gold, mining and steel conglomerate Broken Hill Proprietary,
copper miner Cia. Nacional del Cobre de Chile, diamond giant De Beers Consolidated Mines, nickel miner
Inco and mining companies Cia. Vale do Rio Doce, Newmont Mining, Noranda, Phelps Dodge, Rio Tinto and
WMC. The companies represent more than 60% of the market capitalization of the global mining and metals
industry, which had estimated 1999 procurement spending of $200 billion.
The companies are among the world's largest producers of aluminum, coal, copper, gold, iron ore,
manganese, nickel, silver, zinc, platinum and industrial minerals. Morgan Stanley Dean Witter is the
marketplace's adviser and facilitator.
Brasil Telecom Profit Edges Up 1.4%
Brasil Telecom Participacoes, formerly known as Tele Centro Sul, reported first-quarter earnings rose 1.4% to
28.3 million reals ($15.5 million). But Brasil Telecom said that excluding 52.8 million reals of privatization
goodwill amortized as nonoperating expenses, profit nearly tripled to 81.1 million reals. Revenue rose 24% to
1.14 billion reals. The company said lines in service rose 3.2% in the quarter to 4.87 million, and were up
24% from a year earlier.
AES's Bid for EDC Is Subject to Conditions
U.S. power company AES can go ahead with its bid for a 51% stake in Electricidad de Caracas, or EDC, but
the Atlanta company must abide by certain conditions, a spokeswoman for Venezuela's securities regulator
said Friday. She said AES must guarantee full payment to shareholders and complete the transaction, valued
at $863 million, in two days instead of the 30 days set in the AES offer. If the offer is extended beyond its
current May 30 expiration date, shareholders who have committed to the deal must be allowed to withdraw if
they choose to do so. EDC last week said it plans a $300 million share-repurchase program in the face of
AES's bid. The action must still be approved by a special shareholders' meeting. EDC said it will offer
between 57 cents and $1 a share, above AES's offer of 47 cents a share.
Chile's Capital-Control Change Lifts Peso
The Chilean peso strengthened against the dollar Friday as banks adjusted their positions after Chile's
central bank removed an important capital control, allowing foreign investments to flow more freely in and
out of Chile. The peso closed at 520.80 to the dollar, stronger than 522.50 on Thursday. The central bank
said that it was eliminating a rule that required all foreign investment coming into the country to remain for at
least one year.

Honda Profit Falls 12% on Strong Yen
Honda Motor said group profit fell 12% in the January-March quarter from a year earlier to 62.7 billion yen

telephone lines. The law doesn't change talks under way between the U.S. and Japan on how much the
interconnection fees should be reduced, but it authorizes the Telecommunications Ministry to issue an order
on the size of the cut.

Americas---Above the U.S.:Canadian stocks last year outperformed the U.S. and most of Europe;We're talking a lot more than `rocks and tree...
Global Investing (A Special Report)
Americas --- Above the U.S.: Canadian stocks last year outperformed the U.S. and most of Europe;
We're talking a lot more than `rocks and trees'
By Mark Heinzl
1,559 words
8 May 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
R13
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
It isn't often that Canadians get to boast about having one of the world's top-performing stock markets, but
hot technology and communications stocks have put Canadian indexes in the limelight.
Though Asian markets generally fared even better last year, Canada handily outperformed the U.S. and most
of Europe. Many investors still think of Canada's stock market as a play on natural resources. However,
surging shares of such high-tech names as Nortel Networks Corp., BCE Inc. and JDS Uniphase Corp. (with
headquarters in both San Jose, Calif., and Nepean, Ontario) have pushed Canada's traditional sectors into
the shadows over the past year.
Technology and communications stocks accounted for about 51% of the weighting of the Toronto Stock
Exchange 300 Composite Index at the end of the first quarter. That compared with a weighting of just 15% for
all of Canada's energy, mining and forest-products companies. Though some high-tech stocks have since
plunged amid the sector's wobbles, telecom-equipment giant Nortel has seen its shares hold up well in the
wake of strong earnings.
The weighting of Canada's resource stocks in the TSE 300 has long been declining as commodity producers
disappointed investors and companies in other fields prospered. The combined weighting of resource
companies has fallen from 20% two years ago and 29% a decade ago.
Over the past six months or so, many foreign investors "have woken up to the fact that there's much more
going on here than rocks and trees," says George Vasic, market strategist with Bunting Warburg Dillon Read
Inc. in Toronto. Mr. Vasic says the TSE 300's heavy weighting in technology stocks makes it "perhaps the
second-greatest tech index" behind the tech-drenched Nasdaq Composite Index in the U.S.
The Canadian stock market spent most of the 1990s lagging well behind U.S. and international stock
measures, but the pattern started to change last year, when the widely followed TSE 300 index jumped
29.7%, beating the 19.5% increase in Standard & Poor's 500-stock index in the U.S. In this year's first
quarter, the TSE 300 rose about 12%, setting several records along the way, while the S&P 500 was up
about 2%.
The Canadian gains have been driven by a relatively small group of highly valued stocks, which have masked
sluggish performances by natural resources, financial services and industrial stocks. Canadians are pouring
their money into high-performing technology funds that invest in Canada and abroad, while flows of money
into mutual funds with a broad base of holdings in Canadian companies have slowed to a trickle.
The dichotomy in valuations between technology stocks and others is even more pronounced in Canada than
in the U.S. The split has left value-minded fund managers coping with subpar returns and wondering how to
drum up interest in the overlooked shares of nontech companies.
Irwin Michael, a portfolio manager with ABC Funds in Toronto, says he has rarely seen so many companies
with such low price/earnings and price/book value ratios. "Canada's on sale waiting to be plucked" by foreign
acquirers, he says, pointing to shares of Canadian banks, oil producers and retailers. Some Canadian banks
are going cheaply -- at about nine times expected earnings for this year -- while oil stocks have climbed only
moderately and mining stocks have stubbornly refused to budge, despite a sharp climb in commodity prices
in recent months. And some retailing stocks sell well below their book value, Mr. Michael adds.
Meanwhile, Nortel, which recently traded at a multiple of more than 80 times expected earnings this year, has
been leading the TSE 300's rise over the past year and dominating Canada's technology sector. The
telecommunications-equipment giant has become a top technology pick in Canada and the U.S.,
commanding a market capitalization of about $250 billion Canadian (US$170 billion) recently. That means

--- Page 101 ---
Page 101 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
that Nortel, based in Brampton, Ontario, recently accounted for about one-fifth of the weighting of the TSE
300 index.
While Nortel is an old company on a hot streak, many of the names driving the TSE 300 these days were
virtually unknown a year ago. Take Research In Motion Ltd., a Waterloo, Ontario, maker of wireless
communications devices, including the popular Blackberry wireless paging and electronic-mail device. Partly
due to recent supply agreements with Compaq Computer Corp., Houston, and America Online Inc., Dulles,
Va., shares of Research In Motion more than doubled in the first quarter to C$152, but were recently down
below C$65. The shares traded at about C$10.40 a little over a year ago.
Shares of fast-growing electronic-commerce company BCE Emergis Inc., majority-owned by Montreal-based
telecom holding company BCE, shot up 32% in the first quarter, before giving back most of the gains. And
shares of ATI Technologies Inc., Thornhill, Ontario, which makes computer chips for graphics applications,
surged 35% in the first quarter, as the company announced deals to act as a supplier to Japan's Sony Corp.
and others.
---
Mr. Heinzl is a staff reporter in The Wall Street Journal's Toronto bureau.
---
What You Need to Know
Just a year or so ago, the gap between the sluggish Toronto Stock Exchange 300 Composite Index and the
soaring Dow Jones Industrial Average seemed insurmountable to Canadian investors. The TSE 300
wallowed around 6500, while U.S. investors were celebrating the Dow's first surge through 10000. But the
Canadian index has since been lifted by big gains in technology stocks (even with all the recent turmoil in the
sector), while the U.S. barometer has cooled off. At a level of 9348 at the end of April, the TSE 300 was much
closer to the Dow, at 10734, and Canadian market watchers generally expect further gains as Canada's
economy continues to perform well.
Regulation
Canada has no national securities regulator, so provincial regulators oversee the nation's financial markets.
The largest is the Ontario Securities Commission in Toronto, whose ability to police securities markets has
been questioned by critics. Recently, however, the commission has shown signs that it's getting tougher: It
has brought insider-trading charges against two prominent executives, one from a software company and one
from a mining concern.
Who Can Invest and How
Shares on the Toronto Stock Exchange, Canada's biggest exchange, are available to investors in any part of
the world. Shares of many big Canadian companies, from telecommunications giant Nortel Networks Corp.,
Brampton, Ontario, to transportation concerns Bombardier Inc., Montreal, and Canadian Pacific Ltd., Calgary,
Alberta, have long had listings both in Canada and in the U.S.
Partly because of this ready access, many U.S. investors hold substantial stakes in Canadian companies,
including cable operator Rogers Communications Inc., Toronto; film company Imax Corp., Toronto; and
several natural-resources companies, such as gold producer Barrick Gold Corp., Toronto.
U.S. investors commonly buy shares directly rather than through mutual funds. Fidelity Canada is devoted to
the Canadian stock market, and many natural-resources funds or gold-mining funds in the U.S. have heavy
exposure to Canadian companies.
One easy way to invest in Canada's stock market is to buy shares of iUnits, which represent an investment in
the S&P/TSE 60 Index, launched last year. The iUnits trade on the Toronto Stock Exchange and give
investors a stake in 60 of Canada's biggest companies, as measured by market cap. A similar index fund,
Webs Canada, trades on the American Stock Exchange in New York.
The Upside and Downside
Despite the tech upheaval of recent weeks, Canada's stock market is heavily weighted toward technology
and communications. Nortel alone, for example, hogs about one-quarter of the S&P/TSE 60. This provides
both a risk and an opportunity for investors in the Canadian market, some analysts say. If tech shares hold
their ground, there could be solid returns still to come in the market as valuations firm up in banking, natural
resources and other areas. If tech stocks crumble further, however, Canada's stock indexes will struggle to
hold their gains.

Africa & Mideast---After Apartheid:The arrival of democracy and the end of sanctions were turning pointsfor the South African stock mark...
Global Investing (A Special Report)
Africa & Mideast --- After Apartheid: The arrival of democracy and the end of sanctions were turning
points for the South African stock market; Next on the agenda -- Insider trading and exchange
controls
By Donna Block
2,190 words
8 May 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
R14
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
JOHANNESBURG, South Africa -- The big story in South African stocks used to be commodities. In fact,
much of the time it was the only story. But now this 113-year-old "emerging" stock market is reinventing itself,
with technology and telecommunications gaining influence.
As apartheid ended in 1994 and democracy arrived, capturing the imagination of politicians, the world's
international-fund managers took notice. Drawn to South Africa's infrastructure, its First World veneer and its
sophisticated banking sector, the fund managers decided that many South African stocks were undervalued
compared with those in other emerging markets.
The result has been a remarkable improvement in the Johannesburg stock market in the past few years, as
political and social changes swept through the country. The Johannesburg All Share Index advanced more
than 57% in 1999. Lately some fund managers have been attracted to South Africa as a play on rising
commodity prices.
"The end of apartheid has given the market the ability to unshackle itself from sanctions," says Dana Becker,
head of equity sales at a Merrill Lynch & Co. unit in Johannesburg. "The new government has shown its
integrity and is willing to become a global player. This is hugely important for foreign and local investors."
On the other hand, South Africa still presents problems for investors. With volume thin and insider trading rife,
it remains vulnerable to all the nauseating ups and downs of an emerging market. The government is taking
steps to make the exchange more dynamic and transparent, and foreigners say they like what they see. But it
will take a while before they have enough confidence to reach for their wallets and invest heavily for the long
term.
"There's a lot of foreign interest in South Africa, but the perception is that this is still Africa and still an
emerging market," says Jeremy Gardiner in Capetown, head of unit trusts for Investec Guinness Flight, part
of Investec Group. "So, you have investors whipping in and whipping out depending on whether emerging
markets are in or out of favor."
In fact, the exchange retains some of the vestiges of its more-opaque past. The Johannesburg exchange was
set up in 1887 as the financial playground of the gold and industrial barons -- the so-called Randlords -- who
previously had traded their gold claims in barrooms and hotels. The exchange was dominated then (and to a
lesser degree still is today) by a coterie of institutions and families that run companies such as diamond giant
DeBeers Consolidated Mines Ltd. and gold-mining conglomerate Anglo American Corp. of South Africa.
The Randlords traded among themselves, often tapping old colonial connections in London while expanding
their trade in gold and diamonds internationally. The great wealth they generated gave them tremendous
political influence. But the good times ground to a halt as it became clear to the world that white South African
prosperity was built on black repression. The international community clamped the country under a harsh
regime of sanctions starting in 1986, leaving South Africa in deep recession and its stock market in a funk.
Perhaps more important for the Johannesburg exchange, the apartheid regime imposed strict
foreign-exchange controls limiting the flow of money out of the country. For three decades, this created an
almost impenetrable barrier that had the effect of sealing investment inside South Africa. The controls forced
companies to plow surplus profits into other South African companies, creating a tangled web of
cross-ownership that today is proving difficult to unbundle.
While former President Nelson Mandela presided over the political and social transformation of the country,
Russell Loubser, a merchant banker, took on the task of turning the Johannesburg exchange into a modern

--- Page 104 ---
Page 104 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
trading institution. His first challenge was to persuade the members of the exchange to deregulate the
100-year-old cartel and throw open its doors to corporate membership, negotiated commissions and dual
trading -- brokers now could trade both as principals and as agents. Foreign investment banks saw their
opportunity to get in on the ground floor and scrambled to secure partnerships in local brokerage firms.
The exchange's watershed year was 1996. "Suddenly, the big boys were coming to town, and we wanted
their business," says Mr. Loubser.
The exchange had to deregulate to encourage foreign portfolio investment. But it was an electronic-trading
platform known as JET, for Johannesburg Equity Trading, that was the engine of the market's transformation.
The system, modeled after the Chicago Board Options Exchange, replaced the exchange's traditional
"open-outcry" market.
Until electronic trading got going in 1996, the Johannesburg exchange suffered from chronic low liquidity.
Many stocks barely traded at all. Since the introduction of JET, volume and liquidity have taken off. In 1994,
the number of shares traded through the Johannesburg exchange totaled 5.3 billion. By 1998 it had surged to
34.4 billion.
Even so, the Johannesburg exchange doesn't come close to matching the amount of capital that can be
raised in London or New York, as some of South Africa's large corporations can attest.
In the past year, four major South African companies -- Billiton PLC, a base-metals producer; South African
Breweries PLC, one of the world's largest brewers; South African Mutual, the largest of the country's insurers,
which recently demutualized and is now known as Old Mutual PLC; and Anglo American -- have chosen to
locate their corporate headquarters abroad, all in London, and to obtain primary listings on the London Stock
Exchange. In each case, the companies gained entry to the prestigious Financial Times Stock Exchange 100
Index. Critics have called the moves by South Africa's blue-chip companies "the corporate chicken run."
A more fundamental problem plaguing the Johannesburg exchange is the country's continuing
foreign-exchange controls. While exchange controls have been liberalized, they haven't been done away
with. The remaining controls are almost certainly a factor in the recent groundswell of debate among major
South African companies on whether to move their stock-exchange listings abroad, notably to the U.S. or
U.K.
Exchange controls deter many foreign investors, especially institutional fund managers. Certain global funds
aren't allowed to invest in a country with these kinds of limitations. And the very existence of the controls
implies that South Africa's government recognizes all is not as it should be in the country.
The authorities have committed themselves to eliminating the controls, albeit at a gradual, still-unspecified
pace. The loosening of controls would mean that South African corporations and individuals would be able to
move their money offshore. Many local investors probably would sell holdings in South Africa and move to
diversify.
"Exchange controls contribute to liquidity problems and volatility," says Ms. Becker. "Domestic investors are
restrained from investing offshore; therefore, they are held captive to the market in South Africa. Once those
investors have the capability of moving some assets out of South Africa, selling pressure comes into the
market."
Another chronic problem is insider trading. The practice has gone unchecked despite the best efforts of
various administrations to bring it under control. Mr. Loubser concedes that a perception of the South African
market as a playground for insiders casts a long shadow over the Johannesburg exchange's reputation.
Some fund managers have made it clear that until this practice is curbed, they won't trade securities of
companies in Johannesburg.
In response, South Africa's financial regulators last November created a new agency to combat the problem,
the Inside Trading Directorate. In recent months, the agency has brought a number of high-profile cases.
For all its changes, South Africa's stock market still has one of the highest correlations with commodity prices
of all the emerging markets -- a tempting prospect for those who believe that commodity prices are headed
higher. Foreign portfolio inflows have increased substantially during the past five years.
Local South African investors aren't yet major direct players in the market, preferring to participate through
products sold by financial institutions such as unit trusts and mutual funds. There are signs, however, that
more and more ordinary individuals are buying stock. The demutualization of Old Mutual made shareholders
of thousands of policyholders, for example.

--- Page 105 ---
Page 105 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Mr. Loubser's dream now is to see the Johannesburg exchange itself demutualized, with a stake in public
hands before his tenure expires at the end of next year. "We are doing everything possible not to miss a big
trend in world markets or become complacent," he says.
---
Ms. Block is a free-lance writer in Johannesburg.
---
What You Need to Know
South Africa's stock market is viewed by some investors as a
developing financial powerhouse. The country has come a long way since
the days of apartheid. But much remains to be done.
Regulation
The South African government faces a dilemma in regulating the financial system. Foreign-exchange controls
still prohibit South African companies and individuals from moving assets abroad. Many money managers are
barred from investing in countries that have such limits.
Yet the government fears that easing restrictions could provoke a rash of selling, as South Africans move
money to stock markets that are more stable. So, the government's challenge is to create enough stability in
the South African stock market to prevent such a run -- and keep foreigners interested.
A crucial strategy in attracting investors is to crack down on insider trading. South Africa's financial regulators
last November created the Inside Trading Directorate.
Who Can Invest and How
The foreign-exchange controls apply to domestic investors and corporations in South Africa. Foreign
investors face no such restrictions.
Shares can be purchased through any brokerage firm with a presence in South Africa, including Merrill Lynch
& Co., J.P. Morgan & Co., HSBC Holdings PLC and Investec Group Ltd.
There aren't any curbs on the amount of funds that can be brought in to the country. Dividends are tax-free,
and there's no tax on capital gains. Brokerage rates are fully negotiable.
The Upside
South African equities, while somewhat undervalued, are no longer really cheap, analysts say. There is scope
for share prices to go higher this year, they say. But with prices for industrial and resources stocks
approaching 20 times estimated earnings, any growth is likely to be driven by earnings rather than by an
expansion of trading multiples.
Nevertheless, resource-intensive markets such as South Africa tend to outperform the major markets when
the prices of commodities rise. Johannesburg Stock Exchange shares recently have outperformed U.S.
stocks.
South Africa's prudent fiscal policies have recently been rewarded by improving credit ratings from Duff &
Phelps, Moody's Investors Service and Standard & Poor's. This has helped boost foreign portfolio investment
to $4 billion, including holdings by mutual funds and large individual investors in stocks, bonds and futures.
Interest rates have come down from a high of 25.5% during the emerging-markets crisis in mid-1998 to a
recent 14.5%, encouraging further investment in stocks.
The Downside
Although it is a developed nation, South Africa remains vulnerable to foreign investors' fickle appetite for
emerging markets. And it must deliver more-consistent performance before foreign money pours in.
Weakness in U.S. markets generally has a spillover effect on the Johannesburg exchange; the All Share
Index was recently at its lowest level in nine months. A long-lived major correction in the U.S. would threaten
South African stocks and could short-circuit the growth in global markets, which would be particularly difficult
for the commodity producers that are South Africa's longtime mainstays.

--- Page 106 ---
Page 106 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The rand can zigzag wildly, so investing in South Africa may carry more than usual currency risk. Over the
past few years, the rand has generally fallen against the dollar. Thus, a share price might gain in rand terms
but lose value when translated into dollars.
The Johannesburg exchange embarked on a big reclassification of equity sectors in March 1999, making
historical analysis cumbersome. Data were provided only back to 1996 for many new sectors, giving them a
very short track record.
---

Americas---Fighting for Their Rights:Minority shareholders are clamoring for more protection in emergingmarkets; So far, they've had litt...
Global Investing (A Special Report)
Americas --- Fighting for Their Rights: Minority shareholders are clamoring for more protection in
emerging markets; So far, they've had little success
By Craig Torres
2,107 words
8 May 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
R11
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
One evening in January, Mark Mobius, manager of Franklin Templeton's emerging-markets funds, was about
to give the go ahead to legal teams in Europe and South America to sue Telefonica SA of Spain over a
shareholder-rights issue at its Chilean phone subsidiary.
The case involved Telefonica's purchase, for about $40 million and the assumption of $8 million in debt, of an
Internet unit belonging to the subsidiary, Telefonica CTC Chile SA, based in Santiago. After the deal was
announced in September 1999 CTC's shares tumbled and investors hit the roof, claiming the fast-growing
Chilean Internet unit was worth much more. Mr. Mobius started working with Chilean pension funds to plan a
counterattack.
But just as the court battle was about to begin, the telephone rang. It was Juan Villalonga, Telefonica's
chairman, who informed Mr. Mobius the Spanish phone giant was launching a $21 billion stock swap with
minority shareholders in its principal Latin American subsidiaries at a hefty 40% premium to respective share
prices.
It was good news for Mr. Mobius.
Even though Telefonica CTC Chile won't participate in the swap, Mr. Mobius says the gains on his holdings in
Telefonica's other subsidiaries in Argentina and Peru more than made up for what he lost in CTC's sale of its
Internet unit. Mr. Mobius congratulated Mr. Villalonga on the stock-swap plan and dropped his plans for
litigation.
A spokesman for Telefonica says the buyouts "were not in response to Mobius, but part of a reorganization
[of the company] world-wide."
Nonetheless Mr. Mobius believes his opposition to some of Telefonica's dealings with its Chilean subsidiary
persuaded Mr. Villalonga to take shareholder concerns into consideration, which helped to produce the
buyout offer. "I don't think [the offer] was a coincidence," says Mr. Mobius. "The actions we took really got
them thinking."
As such, Mr. Mobius sees it as another victory in a shareholder-rights battle that Franklin Templeton, a unit of
Franklin Resources Inc., San Mateo, Calif., is fighting on many fronts throughout the world. But Mr. Mobius is
discouraged about the overall progress of shareholder rights in emerging markets. "I can't think of one
[emerging] market where minority shareholders are given good and strong protection," he says. "I don't see
the governments in these countries moving in any direction to protect shareholder rights."
Those words may be the epitaph of emerging markets, if companies and regulators don't make big changes
in favor of minority shareholders.
As emerging-markets pioneers such as Mr. Mobius began venturing into new, or less developed, capital
markets in the early 1990s, other U.S. investors followed.
At the end of 1990, diversified emerging-markets funds had total assets of $118 million, which has swelled to
$20.8 billion today, according to Morningstar Inc., the Chicago-based mutual-fund research company. Such
funds invest throughout the developing world -- in Latin America, parts of Asia, Eastern Europe, the Middle
East and Africa.
Among these regions, Latin America has been a strong magnet for investment. Governments there, as in
Eastern Europe, have made their markets attractive by settling their defaulted debts with commercial
creditors, privatizing state-owned companies and passing laws to fight inflation and, in other ways, stabilize

--- Page 108 ---
Page 108 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
their economies. While funds dedicated solely to Latin America had zero assets at the start of 1990, they
have $2 billion today, according to Morningstar.
In all this euphoria, however, a small hitch went unnoticed: Emerging markets -- almost by their definition as
rough-hewn capital frontiers -- lack market practices and securities laws that defend shareholders.
To redress this, Mr. Mobius and other foreign investors are taking their shareholder-rights fight to the media
and to court, and are trying to rouse local regulators and pension funds to make changes.
Governments in the regions, however, have so far been inattentive, for several possible reasons. Some could
be leery of antagonizing powerful business groups; others may be too busy trying to figure out how to
regulate complex sectors of their transitional economies. (After all, even though companies may be treating
shareholders unfairly, local disclosure laws aren't being violated.) Or they may be suspicious of foreigners,
such as Mr. Mobius, who can seem like troublemakers since the managers of local pension funds aren't
complaining much themselves. (In Chile, however, at least one pension-fund company is pressing on with a
suit against Telefonica over the Internet sale.)
Complacency about shareholder rights, however, could mean that emerging markets remain stuck in the
backwaters of global finance. Already, many foreign investors have grown disgusted with practices that rake
billions of dollars out of companies at their expense, says Edward Schneider, a fund manager at Lloyd's Bank
PLC in Geneva. "Foreign minority investors," he says, "are always giving their pound of flesh."
Mr. Schneider says shareholder rights can be summed up in two golden rules: equal values paid to all
shareholders when a company changes ownership and equal distribution of profits to all shareholders. Both
of these rules are designed to more closely align the financial incentives of controlling and minority
shareholders, and to motivate managers to deploy earnings in ways that will support the company's stock.
As it is now, however, these two rules are hardly the standard in emerging markets. In Latin American
companies, for instance, controlling investors -- often a local management group or family or a foreign
company -- may charge "management fees." This means that, instead of retaining earnings to build the
business, or distributing them to all shareholders equally through dividends, companies make a special
payment to a control group through this fee -- sometimes dubbed a "tax on shareholders" by detractors.
Whether the stock price goes up or down, the control group is usually assured some gain over and above
other shareholders.
To critics, the practice of taking hefty payments from Latin American companies, especially when it is done by
foreign owners, reeks of conquistador capitalism: taking the gold and sending it home.
In the U.S., says Morton Pierce, a securities-law expert at the New York firm Dewey Ballantine LLC, that
could be considered "usurpation of an asset" and a breach of fiduciary duty. U.S. laws "have provided a very
good safety net for investors," says Mr. Pierce, and that's one reason the U.S. has the world's most vibrant
capital markets. In Latin America, by contrast, companies habitually skim profits by charging management
fees -- and so far, no courts or regulators have challenged the practice.
Telefonica -- which doesn't charge such fees on its holdings in Spain -- pulled $431 million in management
fees out of its Buenos Aires-based subsidiary Telefonica de Argentina SA from 1995 to 1998. A Telefonica
spokesman says the fees compensate the Spanish parent company for the use of its management, "whose
experience has been essential in turning these companies around within a short time."
Now, however, while many applaud the 40% premium shareholders in Telefonica's subsidiaries will receive,
some investors say it is a consolation prize for a practice that hurt minority shareholders.
Indeed, while buyouts such as Telefonica's may be a sign that shareholder concerns are being heard,
minority shareholders are also concerned that a lot of long-term value is being lost. Spain's Banco Santander
Central Hispano SA, for example, recently launched a share-exchange offer to minority investors in its
Buenos Aires-based Banco Rio de la Plata SA. Investors will get a 23% premium over Banco Rio's recent
share price. While that return exceeds near-term expectations for the stock, it could easily fall short of returns
investors might have enjoyed over the next few years, given the strong growth potential of Argentina's
banking market.
"Argentine banks have been spending over the past two years and sharing the costs with minority
shareholders," says Dominic Rossi, portfolio manager at Scudder Threadneedle Investment Managers Ltd. in
London. "The return on these investments should start to accelerate over the next couple of years," but that
will be captured by BSCH and not minority shareholders in Banco Rio. "You have to say this is pretty
opportunistic," says Mr. Rossi. A BSCH spokesman says shareholders will capture Banco Rio's potentially
higher future returns when they become investors in BSCH.

--- Page 109 ---
Page 109 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Big fund-management groups can always divest from companies in emerging stock markets and look
elsewhere for better opportunities. The bigger losers are the local pension funds. These were set up to
replace public social-security systems (which are being phased out) and are required to keep a certain
amount of their holdings, long-term, in local stocks. Nevertheless, there are few examples of pension funds in
Argentina and Chile -- the two countries in the region with the most established pension-fund systems --
pushing for changes to increase shareholder value.
An Argentine pension-fund manager says there hasn't been a single instance when Argentine pension funds
have tried to place a director on the board of a publicly traded company. Nor can the fund manager recall any
meeting among pension-fund managers to discuss opposition or even support of a particular corporate
practice. "We don't have clear laws," the fund manager says, even though local regulations do guarantee a
level of accounting transparency.
In Chile, pension funds have placed representatives on the boards of some local companies. But
pension-fund managers can be fined for complaining publicly about management because regulators deem
this "interference." "The government has made it nearly illegal to have shareholder activism" in Chile, says
Christopher Palmer, senior fund manager at Gartmore Asset Management Ltd. in London. "And this is a
government that is trying to build a pension-fund system based on share prices."
A lack of independence is another reason local funds are silent. In contrast to U.S. funds, which are highly
independent, many pension-fund companies in Argentina and Chile are owned by foreign and domestic
commercial banks that have indirect ownership stakes or direct lending relations with many of the companies
in which the funds own stock. Even though individual investment managers might like to push for change at
the companies, they are hushed by higher-ups who want to protect commercial relations. The result:
unresponsive corporate management and poorly performing stocks in the portfolios collectively owned by
millions of citizens.
Local pension funds aren't the only ones to blame for silent complicity. Most U.S. fund managers would rather
sell a stock than engage in a costly fight for shareholder value. Mr. Mobius says very few fund managers
offered to help Franklin Templeton foot the legal bill when the group was contemplating litigation against
Telefonica. "We as fund managers have to stick together," says Mr. Mobius. "Nobody is going to do [this] for
us."
Meanwhile, say fund managers, complacency is hurting the prospects for economic development in the
region. Local pension funds are either getting stuck in a shrinking group of illiquid stocks or, they are
exporting savings -- up to, and pushing, the maximum of their allowable limits -- to more-developed markets
where shareholder rights are protected. This migration of savings defeats one of the central reasons the
private pension-fund systems were created: to develop a pool of domestic money to finance local companies.
Now, the question is whether emerging markets can recover the vitality they displayed in the early '90s.
Investors not only suffered deep losses during 1997 and 1998 as the Asian and Russian economies sank, but
they also began to look closely at how well the companies they owned were being managed. "Investors
realized that, in any case, they had been taken for a ride," says Mr. Mobius.
The reaction was predictable. Foreign investment flows into emerging stock markets declined from an annual
peak of $49.2 billion in 1996 to just $14.1 billion in 1998, according to International Finance Corp. in
Washington, D.C., a division of the World Bank. Estimates for 1999 show a slight increase, but nobody
expects another boom.
"Why is it that an entrepreneur in California can become a billionaire?" asks Mr. Palmer of Gartmore Asset
Management. "The reason is that equity investors are confident enough to invest in a company that will
develop its business to the benefit of all shareholders. These questions of fairness are still over the heads of
many Latin American managements."
---
Mr. Torres is a staff reporter in The Wall Street Journal's Buenos Aires bureau.

Copper Supply Tightness Spurs Two-Cent Rally
Commodities
Copper Supply Tightness Spurs Two-Cent Rally
By David Bogoslaw
Dow Jones Newswires
633 words
8 May 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
C29
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Tightening supplies of copper spurred a two-penny rally in futures prices Friday on the
Commodities Exchange division of the New York Mercantile Exchange, traders said.
Analysts tied that supply tightness to the strength of the U.S. economy, with the continuing decline in
warehouse stocks also showing robust demand.
The nearby May contract rose 1.80 cents to 83.80 cents a pound and the most-active July contract was up
2.10 cents at 84.05 cents.
"Consumers are finding it hard to source material so they have to pay higher prices," said one New
York-based trader.
Comex traders with short positions, or bets for lower prices, also were forced to pay up to close out those
positions by purchasing equal amounts of futures, traders said.
A growing economy and Friday's rally in U.S. equities markets contributed to the price rally, said Dan Horn,
an analyst at Refco Inc.
Marked drawdowns in London Metal Exchange and Comex warehouse stocks support the case for nearby
demand dominating the picture. LME stocks fell overnight 3,775 tons to 679,025 metric tons, while Comex
inventories dropped 2,717 tons to 75,148 metric tons from April 28 to Thursday.
Many traders have mentioned that copper is being taken out of warehouses to fulfill Southwire Co.'s
contractual obligations once it closes its cathode-refining plant in Carrollton, Ga., in July.
But traders disagreed whether the fresh buying that came into the Comex market Friday was all related to
consumption or whether it included speculative trading. They also differed on causes behind changes in
spreads between contract months this week.
"The spreads got quite crazy with the May-July going into contango after being in backwardation yesterday.
And now you have the July-September going into backwardation," said John Tyree, a trader at Fimat Futures.
Backwardation occurs when the nearby contract is priced higher than deferred contracts, suggesting strong
immediate demand for physical material. Contango occurs when deferred contracts are priced higher than
nearby months.
Although immediate demand can pressure prices into backwardation, it may be that inflationary signals will
cause demand to wane for deferred months, said Dave Meger, senior metals analyst at Alaron Trading in
Chicago.
News Friday that U.S. unemployment fell to a 30-year low in April reinforced expectations for a
half-percentage-point increase in interest rates at the Federal Reserve's next meeting May 16, leading to
concerns about an eventual slowdown in the economy and reduced demand for copper.
Mr. Tyree and the other New York trader dismissed the idea that concerns over demand possibly waning later
in the year were pressuring spreads.
"The disparity between the nearby [contract] and next year would be much greater. But when you still have 85
cents [per pound] for next year [contracts], I wouldn't say that's the case," the trader said.
In other commodity markets:

--- Page 111 ---
Page 111 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
SOYBEANS: Futures fell at the Chicago Board of Trade as participants booked profits on gains earlier in the
week. The July contract dropped 6.25 cents to $5.6600 a bushel. Futures soared early in the week on
concern about a possible Midwest summer drought, but eased off Thursday and Friday as forecasters put
more moisture into next week's outlook.
GOLD: Futures fell on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange, giving up earlier gains in
reaction to higher U.S. equities prices, traders said. The June contract dropped $1.10 to $280.10 a troy ounce
after hitting a high of $283.80. Prices had rallied after news that U.S. unemployment fell to the lowest level in
30 years in April, viewed as a sign of inflationary risk.
