Lucky Numbers:Casino Chain MinesData on Its Gamblers,And Strikes Pay Dirt---`Secret Recipe' Lets Harrah'sTarget Its Low-RollersAt the Ind...
Lucky Numbers: Casino Chain Mines Data on Its Gamblers, And Strikes Pay Dirt --- `Secret Recipe'
Lets Harrah's Target Its Low-Rollers At the Individual Level --- A Free-Meal `Intervention'
By Christina Binkley
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
2,149 words
4 May 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
TUNICA, Miss. -- She doesn't know it, but Linda Maranees is the subject of a behavioral experiment that
could change the odds of the gambling business.
The Memphis, Tenn., retiree, her blouse bedecked with sequined cards and dice, has just received invitations
to two nearby slot tournaments, along with vouchers for $200, all courtesy of Harrah's Entertainment Inc.
"Harrah's is savvy," says Ms. Maranees, who admits that once in the casino door, she is bound to spend
much more than what Harrah's has given her.
That is exactly what the Las Vegas-based company is banking on. Over the past two years, Harrah's has
quietly conducted thousands of clinical-style trials to determine what gets people to gamble more. Based on
its findings, Harrah's has developed closely guarded marketing strategies tailored individually to the millions
of low-rollers who make up its bread-and-butter business.
The results are impressive enough that other casino companies are copying some of Harrah's more
discernible methods. Wall Street analysts are also beginning to see Harrah's -- long a dowdy also-ran in the
flashy casino business -- as gaining an edge on its rivals. Harrah's stock price has risen quickly in recent
weeks as investors have received news of the marketing results. And the company's earnings have more
than doubled in the past year.
At the center of Harrah's strategy is a former Harvard professor named Gary Loveman and a vast
mathematical model much like the ones that economists use to predict the gross national product or that
airlines use to fill seats with the highest-paying fliers. But this one scores gamblers on how profitable they can
be to Harrah's. Richard Mirman, the company senior vice president who refined the model, boasts that it is
Harrah's "secret recipe" -- on a par with the famous unrevealed formula of Kentucky Fried Chicken.
The model tells Harrah's marketers how to appeal to gamblers such as Ms. Maranees, based on data
tracking their previous behavior in casinos. Spitting out "behavior modification reports," Harrah's computers
suggest that Ms. Maranees -- an avid slot-tournament player -- will respond best to a cash offer, while Tina
Montgomery, a real-estate agent from nearby Oxford, Miss., is better motivated by a free hotel room. As Ms.
Montgomery gambles downstairs, she explains, "my husband stays in the room."
Drawing on data from electronic frequent-gambler cards that customers present before they play, the model
sets budgets and calendars for gamblers, calculating their "predicted lifetime value" to Harrah's. When a
gambler wagers less than usual -- by skipping a monthly visit for instance -- Harrah's "intervenes" with a letter
or a phone call offering a free meal, a show ticket or a cash voucher. Telemarketers are trained to get
customers to talk about their earlier casino experiences, and then to listen for trigger phrases such as "hotel
room" or "steak dinner" to come up with the most alluring offer.
This "Pavlovian marketing," as Mr. Mirman calls it, is a far cry from the traditional methods gambling
companies have used to attract customers. Casinos have long depended on the inherent sexiness of their
product to reach the low-rolling public. Until Harrah's, they have eschewed the kind of quantitative analysis
employed to great effect by other consumer-oriented industries such as airlines and banks. Instead, they
have focused on high-rollers, doling out VIP perks such as free flights and fine champagne. The masses have
been courted with gimmicky contests and, increasingly, with fantastical top-this-one resorts.
This has cost the gambling industry. Over the past decade, the billions of dollars lavished on hotels, malls
and marble bathrooms have cut casino investment returns in half. So Harrah's, with its solidly proletarian
properties in places like Topeka, Kan. and Joliet, Ill., has had to think differently.

--- Page 113 ---
Page 113 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The marketing push was born when Phil Satre, Harrah's chairman and chief executive, was struggling to
recover from what appeared to be a gross strategic error in the early 1990s: After leading the way among
casinos into new jurisdictions as legalized gambling spread across the country, Harrah's got creamed
whenever competitors put up flashier properties next door. "We were fed up with running our business as a
victim," says Colin Reed, Harrah's chief financial officer.
The only way out was to squeeze more business from existing customers. But the first round of "loyalty"
marketing and technology programs, with names such as Marketing Workbench and WINNET -- which
generally relied on blanketing gamblers with freebies -- not only failed to keep them coming, but also cut into
the company's bottom line.
In 1994, Harrah's hired a chief marketing officer, Brad Morgan, with experience at Visa and Procter &
Gamble, to bring in some outside consumer-marketing savvy. Mr. Morgan spoke of sizing up gamblers
"psychographically" -- rating them according to characteristics such as their careers.
Mr. Morgan also identified a small group of Harrah's customers who produced most of the company's profits.
He says he found that people who spent between $100 and $499 a trip accounted for about 30% of gamblers
but 80% of revenue and, startlingly, nearly 100% of profits. Among themselves, Harrah's officials referred to
these customers as "grazers" for their steady casino habits. Publicly, the company settled on another term for
its core audience: "avid experienced players." "I felt like I'd discovered the Rosetta stone of casinos," Mr.
Morgan says.
"Avid experienced players" became Harrah's target customers.
The company began collecting intimate details on these players when it launched its Total Gold
frequent-gambler card in 1997. The electronic cards, inserted in slot machines or handed to casino
supervisors, gathered minutiae on gamblers' habits in exchange for letting them know how to attain the free
drinks, hotel rooms, show tickets and other "comps."
The idea was that gamblers would bet more if they knew the exact threshold they needed to cross to get a
freebie. But it didn't work out that way. Freebie levels differed from one Harrah's casino to the next, confusing
customers. Casino managers balked at sharing the data with their colleagues at other properties. Getting
them to cooperate "was like herding cats," says Mr. Morgan, who left Harrah's in 1997 and is now a business
consultant in Boulder, Colo.
Meanwhile, Harrah's executives were swimming in information they didn't know how to use. "I went through a
period of frustration," says Mr. Satre. "I said, Why isn't any of this stuff working?' "
Although he didn't fully grasp it at the time, the electronic card was generating some key intelligence for Mr.
Satre. It told Harrah's how fast people pull a slot-machine lever and what their favorite games are. It told them
a gambler's age and gender. It helped the company to identify which neighborhoods around the country
produce the most lucrative customers.
A few weeks before Christmas 1997, Mr. Satre made a pilgrimage to Atlanta to see Sergio Zyman, Coca-Cola
Co.'s marketing guru at the time. In a two-hour conversation in an executive lounge at the airport, Mr. Zyman
talked while Mr. Satre scribbled notes. Mr. Zyman recommended that Mr. Satre hire a chief operating officer
with a marketing background. With authority over all of Harrah's properties -- which now number 21 -- and
over the company's operating vice presidents, the executive could make marketing the driving force at
Harrah's.
In what he calls a "Eureka moment," Mr. Satre thought of Mr. Loveman, the Harvard Business School
professor, who had consulted for Harrah's about marketing and training. A year from becoming eligible for
tenure at Harvard, the affable and rumpled Mr. Loveman says he was "dumbstruck" by the offer. Mr. Satre
presented the hiring to his board as a fait accompli because, as he puts it, "if I said I was looking for
permission, they'd have said I was crazy."
Approaching his $1.3 million-a-year job as one of Harvard's classic case studies, Mr. Loveman decided that a
lack of customer loyalty was Harrah's biggest weakness. Noting that clients spent only 36 cents of every
wagering dollar at Harrah's, he realized that if the company could raise that by a penny, annual earnings
would jump by more than $1 a share. "I'm in the business of fostering customer monogamy," Mr. Loveman
says, "like the Ladies' Temperance Movement."
Mr. Loveman began his push by recruiting outside help. That's a rarity in the insular gambling industry, which
tends to promote from within. One of his early hires was Mr. Mirman, a former University of Chicago math
whiz who favors conservative blue suits over the kind of wide ties and pinky rings often found in casino
executive suites. When he left his management-consulting job at Booz Allen & Hamilton to join Harrah's in
1998, Mr. Mirman says, he shrugged off the reactions of colleagues, as when a senior partner asked if he
was "comfortable with the morals of this."

--- Page 114 ---
Page 114 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Mr. Loveman and Mr. Mirman grouped together the 16 million gamblers in Harrah's database according to
characteristics such as age, how much money they are likely to lose and how frequently they gamble. Then
Harrah's started testing hypotheses against control groups.
One example: Harrah's chose two similar groups of frequent slot players from Jackson, Miss. Members of the
control group were offered a typical casino-marketing package worth $125 -- a free room, two steak meals
and $30 of free chips at the Tunica casino. Members of the test group were offered $60 in chips. The more
modest offer generated far more gambling, suggesting that Harrah's had been wasting money giving
customers free rooms. Thereafter, profits from the revamped promotion nearly doubled to $60 per person per
trip.
In another test, Harrah's focused on a group of monthly gamblers whom the company suspected could be
induced to play more frequently because they lived nearby and displayed avidgambler traits such as hitting
slot buttons quickly (playing at "high velocity" in Harrah's parlance). To entice them to make two back-to-back
visits, Harrah's sent cash and food offers that expired in consecutive two-week periods. The group's average
number of trips per month quickly rose to 1.4 from 1.1.
Harrah's has dropped some old casino standards, such as giving customers bonus points that can be spent in
Harrah's gift shops and restaurants. Research showed that most gamblers weren't motivated by the bonus
points, which cost Harrah's $14 million a year.
In its trials, the company is also studying the widely held belief that gamblers notice slight changes in
slot-machine odds. If that's wrong, as Mr. Loveman suspects, casinos could slightly lower the odds of winning
and reap even bigger profits. "I believe there's a lot of money to be made for the person who has the answer
to that," says Mr. Loveman.
This experimentation is helping Harrah's gain market share around the country. Here in Tunica, chosen as a
key test site because of the "average" characteristics of its gamblers, Harrah's revenue has risen at nearly
double the rate of nearby casinos since the new targeted marketing was introduced last June. Not only that,
the higher volumes came cheap. Profits at the Tunica casino rose sharply, according to Harrah's executives.
Rivals are trying to follow suit. "Harrah's has taken a different tack than everyone else, and they've been
really smart about it," says Marc Grossman, head of investor relations for Hilton Hotels Corp., which spun its
casinos off into Park Place Entertainment Corp. a little more than a year ago.
The approach is also winning some fans among Harrah's old-timers. Tom Jenkins, who oversees Harrah's
Las Vegas flagship, calls Mr. Loveman and his crew "propeller heads." But he says they've helped to more
than double the rate at which people respond to offers that he mails to their homes -- to 8% from 3%.
There are more pitches to come. Harrah's last month launched frequent-gambler cards with gold, platinum
and diamond thresholds, which offer escalating rewards for gambling more. Ms. Maranees, the Memphis
retiree, is a proud diamond echelon player. That means Harrah's expects she'll lose a minimum of $5,000 this
year.
Ms. Maranees says she's not put off by Harrah's "Pavlovian" marketing. "A gimmick to get me to spend more
money?" she asks rhetorically. "Why of course it is."

Business World:No Future in Newspapers? Ask the Politicians
Business World: No Future in Newspapers? Ask the Politicians
By Holman W. Jenkins Jr.
1,108 words
3 May 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
A27
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
There is a theory going around that newspapers don't matter much anymore, and editorial pages matter least
of all.
You might have to conclude otherwise from the furor among Michigan movers and shakers over the ouster of
Tom Bray, the Detroit News opinion editor whose pages have hosted debates on the policies -- privatization,
welfare reform, etc. -- that have breathed life back into the state's economy. He was deposed, after 16 years,
by a lifer from Gannett not known to have much affinity for conservative ideas.
We don't know how the story will play out, but Publisher Mark Silverman has put himself in an interesting
position. Word of Mr. Bray's displacement got around the state almost before Mr. Bray found out. Thus it
wasn't the smartest move for Mr. Silverman to publish on his own front page a version of events that
everyone knew to be false -- namely, that Mr. Bray asked to be relieved.
Let's see if we can connect this embarrassment to larger matters: In a world transfixed by the Internet, we
forget that most wealth creation remains intensely local. And nobody is better positioned than publishers to
influence elections, zoning decisions and all the forms of political favoritism that have a tremendous impact
on local enterprise. Making money from local news may still be a puzzler given the way the Web and direct
mail are cannibalizing ad revenues. But the value is there if someone can figure it out.
That's the lesson of the Detroit News and the concurrent hubbub over another urban paper, the San
Francisco Examiner.
This is a fascinating tale, and the essence is: Hearst Corp. is about to hand over the Examiner, the first paper
run by William Randolph Hearst in the 1880s, along with $66 million in operating subsidies over three years,
to a local Chinese business clan with close ties to Mayor Willie Brown and the city's Democratic machine.
Why? As Hearst said in court this week: "Politics."
Let us start with Ted Fang, the 37-year-old son of successful Chinese immigrants, holder of an ethnic-studies
degree from Berkeley. Mr. Fang is re-enacting a classic American story.
His journalist father, the late John Fang, came to the city from Shanghai, by way of Taiwan, in the 1950s. He
founded a printing company and a national tabloid, Asian Week. His wife Florence rescued a bankrupt
Chinatown restaurant that eventually became a stopping place for George Bush and other Republicans
seeking Asian votes. The Fangs did well for themselves and their community. Eldest son James is an elected
boardmember of Bay Area Rapid Transit, married to the daughter of an up-and-coming member of Jiang
Zemin's Shanghai faction, and aims to run for mayor someday.
In 1987, the family bought Ted a failing free weekly, the San Francisco Independent, which he quickly turned
into a thriving, thrice-weekly with a circulation of 200,000 and a knack for backing winning horses in local
elections.
Such was his success that he was widely credited with putting former police chief Frank Jordan (a
conservative by local standards) in the mayor's office in 1991. His switch to backing Willie Brown was an
early indicator of Mr. Brown's upset win in 1995. Another crony, gleefully promoted with unsubstantiated
innuendo against opponents, is D.A. Terence Hallinan.
Oh yes, in all this we cannot forget the role of a California political consultant and kingpin, Jack Davis, who
calls himself a Fang family "warlord." Half the city elite was at his 50th birthday party four years ago where
live sex acts were performed. In San Francisco that gets you votes.
Mr. Fang's pot of gold is the city's official legal advertising, which he first tried to lock up with an antitrust suit
against the Examiner and the Chronicle, the city's notoriously mediocre mainstream papers. When that didn't
seem to do the job, he promoted a ballot measure that essentially guaranteed the business for "any" paper

--- Page 116 ---
Page 116 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
that happens to be locally based, minority-owned and distributed free -- i.e., the Independent, even though it
consistently overbids its rivals by tens of thousands of dollars.
But Mr. Fang's patrons really came through when Hearst, fed up with the also-ran Examiner, agreed to buy
the dominant Chronicle last year from its family owners for $660 million.
Since 1965, Hearst's Examiner has subsisted on a profit-sharing joint operating agreement with the
Chronicle. By law, Hearst would be under no obligation to subsidize a failing paper and could close the
Examiner down once it took over the Chronicle.
But it needs a perfunctory OK from the Justice Department, and when the Democratic machine, from Mayor
Brown to Senator Dianne Feinstein, called in its chits with Janet Reno, the company saw the logic of buying
off the machine by giving the paper to the Fangs, along with a fat handout to help them keep it afloat, in the
name of "preserving competition." On Monday, Hearst Editor Timothy White even said he offered Mayor
Brown favorable editorial-page coverage in return for help getting the Chronicle sale approved.
The deal is now being held up by a lawsuit by rival political consultant Clint Reilly, also who wanted the
Examiner but demanded a $250 million subsidy. Mr. Reilly has multiple axes to grind: The Davis-Fang team
torpedoed his own mayoral hopes last year by recalling that he once beat up a girlfriend, and he once broke
an ankle in a scuffle with Examiner Editor Phil Bronstein (a.k.a. Mr. Sharon Stone). Were the law to triumph,
Judge Vaughn Walker (previously famous for dismissing Apple's patent-infringement suit against Microsoft)
would probably throw out the Reilly case and say that Hearst is free to fold the Examiner.
More likely, politics will triumph, and Mr. Fang will get $66 million and three years to find out if his brand of
journalism (more Hearst-like than Hearst has been in a century) can turn the Examiner into a paying concern.
Guess what? If he succeeds, we'll be willing to forget the slippery way he came into possession of the paper.
We may not care for his politics, but in a world saturated in information and competing media, a local paper
that knows what it thinks and doesn't care whom it offends might just have the right formula. And when he
gets his brother elected mayor, the Fangs can become a name to conjure with, like the Kennedys.

EUROPE
French Socialists
Back Steeper Taxes
On Options Gains
The French government, in a move criticized by the business community, is backing legislation to increase
already-steep taxes on gains from stock options for those with the largest gains. But the plan drafted by
Socialist Party deputies, which could be introduced as early as this week, would lower taxes for those with
smaller gains and would require more transparency in options grants. The issue has long challenged the
French administration, which seeks to appease a business community that says the current tax system is too
harsh but is beholden to its left-wing allies. Within political circles in Paris, the issue is seen as a critical test
for the recently appointed finance minister and Socialist, Laurent Fabius.
A large majority of Socialist lawmakers, whose party holds a majority in the National Assembly, yesterday
voted in favor of the compromise plan on stock options at a party meeting, resisting pressure from the left to
raise the taxes to the marginal rate of 54%.
Officials Accused in Greek Share Scandal
A public prosecutor has charged an unspecified number of Greek stock-exchange officials and
representatives of some listed companies with misdemeanors related to alleged manipulation of prices on the
Athens Stock Exchange, according to court officials. Officials of the exchange and its watchdog panel were
accused of not taking action against illegal trading; the representatives of companies, which weren't identified,
were charged with encouraging officials not to take action. The accusations are part of a continuing
investigation into allegations of price manipulation, insider trading and money laundering at the Athens
exchange; but no charges related to these allegations directly have been brought. Greek stocks skyrocketed
last year in a rally fed by speculation and followed by a steep correction.
Volvo's Operating Earnings Rise 13%
Sweden's Volvo reported first-quarter pretax profit of 1.89 billion Swedish kronor ($212.5 million), as
shareholders approved the heavy-vehicle maker's planned purchase of the truck division of France's Renault.
The first-quarter result compares with pretax profit of 28 billion kronor a year before, which included a
one-time gain of about 27 billion kronor from the sale of Volvo's car division to Ford Motor. Net income totaled
1.27 billion kronor, compared with 27.56 billion kronor a year earlier. Operating profit, excluding one-time
items, rose 13% to 1.43 billion kronor. The approval by shareholders of the $1.6 billion Renault deal,
announced Tuesday, was expected. Group sales rose 13% to 30.55 billion kronor.
German, French Prices Rise Moderately
German consumer prices were unchanged in April from the month before and were up 1.5% from a year
earlier, the government said, in line with economists' expectations that the year-to-year inflation rate would
fall from March's 1.9%, the largest rise in two years. Meanwhile, France's harmonized price index, which can
be compared with the rest of the European Union, rose 0.5% from February and was up 1.7% year to year,
the French government said.
GN Store Nord to Acquire Beltone of U.S.

Beltone Electronics Corp. of the U.S. for $392.5 million, including $196 million in cash and the rest in new GN
Store Nord shares. GN will also assume $75 million of debt. Beltone, the world's seventh-largest
manufacturer of hearing aids, will be merged with GN's hearing-aid unit.
French Telecom Liberalization Hits Snag
The French government withdrew a legislative amendment that would open France Telecom to new
competition, following a request by Communist Party lawmakers. The Industry Ministry said it would press on
with its plan to require the unbundling of the local loop before next year. France Telecom has a monopoly on
local calling by consumers.

Siam Cement Swings to Quarterly Profit
Siam Cement posted first-quarter earnings of 2.49 billion baht ($65.4 million), a huge swing from a loss of
1.14 billion baht a year earlier, as consolidated revenue of Thailand's largest industrial conglomerate rose
40% to 33.26 billion baht. Siam cement said results were helped by 816 million baht in gains on foreign
exchange, compared with a foreign-exchange loss of 748.4 million baht a year earlier because of the weak
Thai currency. Domestic sales grew 33%, while export revenue jumped 64% on higher demand overseas for
its core products of cement, paper and petrochemicals. Foreign debt accounted for $1.5 billion, or 32%, of
total liabilities in the quarter, down from $3.7 billion, or 75% of liabilities, a year earlier.
KFC Must Depart Imperial Surroundings
Because of a new policy to keep foreign fast-food restaurants out of Chinese imperial-era parks, Kentucky
Fried Chicken will have to leave Beijing's Beihai Park when its lease expires in 2002, the official Xinhua news
agency said. The restaurant is "extremely out of harmony with its surroundings," a member of the advisory
Chinese People's Political Consultative Conference was quoted as saying by Xinhua. Tony Chen, a
spokesman for Tricon Global Restaurants, which owns KFC, confirmed the lease expires in 2002 but said the
company hasn't been told it will have to move.
Indonesia May Impound Suharto Assets
Prosecutors plan to impound former Indonesian President Suharto's assets in connection with an
investigation into corruption during his rule, a spokesman for the Indonesian attorney general's office said. Mr.
Suharto is suspected in a corruption scandal involving the misuse of millions of dollars from charitable
foundations that he managed during his 32 years in power.

BSCH May Have Inside Track for Serfin
With several rivals now out of the race, the Mexican unit of Spain's Banco Santander Central Hispano has
emerged as the most likely buyer of ailing Grupo Financiero Serfin. The BSCH unit, Grupo Financiero
Santander Mexicano, now faces only one competitor for Serfin: HSBC Bank USA, a unit of London-based
HSBC Holdings, which already has a 20% stake in Serfin, which was taken over by the Mexican government.
Both companies have deep pockets, but BSCH has a competitive advantage: a local banking presence that
would open the way for synergies and cost cutting. That could prompt BSCH to place a very aggressive bid,
people familiar with the situation said.
Repsol-YPF Unit Wins Brazil Gas Rights
Gas Natural, a unit of Repsol-YPF of Spain, was awarded natural-gas distribution rights in the southern part

outbid a consortium including Italy's ENI and Portugal's Electricidade de Portugal, which offered 245.8 million
reals, and France's TotalFinaElf, which bid 200 million reals. The price paid by Gas Natural far exceeded
expectations and was more than five times the 95 million real minimum asking price.
Agnico-Eagle Loss Widened in 1st Period
Canadian gold miner Agnico-Eagle Mines posted a steeper first-quarter loss, blaming higher costs of
production. Agnico-Eagle reported a loss of $3.5 million, compared with a loss of $1.7 million a year earlier.
The Toronto company said operating cash flow resulted in a deficit of $2.8 million, compared with a deficit of
$800,000 a year earlier. The company said its cash operating costs to produce an ounce of gold rose to $282
from $216 a year before.

ASIA/PACIFIC
China Creates Office
To Regulate News
On Internet Sites
China has created an office to regulate news on the Internet and to help state media spice up Web sites so
they can better compete, according to Chinese officials and reports in state-run newspapers. Executives of
privately owned Web sites said they were worried by the ambiguous mandate of the Internet Information
Management Bureau, which includes countering what the IIMB described as the "infiltration of harmful
information on the Internet." But other observers said the bureau, controlled by China's cabinet, appeared
more focused on making sleepy state-owned news sites more interesting, rather than reining in popular
commercial sites.
IIMB head Wang Qingcun, in a speech Friday, said the office would promote development of the industry by
establishing copyright standards and stamping out "harmful" information. How the bureau chooses to define
"harmful" could have a profound impact on Web portals. Beijing earlier this year barred portals from
disseminating foreign news on their sites.
Nomura Posts Biggest Profit in Decade
Japan's Nomura Securities said its parent pretax profit for the year ended March 31 rose nearly sevenfold as
the company benefited from a strong rise in trading volume amid a bullish Japanese stock market. Parent
pretax profit for the fiscal year totaled 303.31 billion yen ($2.87 billion), the largest profit since the peak of
Japan's asset-market bubble a decade earlier. The company posted parent net income of 144.18 billion yen,
compared with a net loss of 241.84 billion yen the year before. Operating revenue rose 74% to 624.69 billion
yen. Stock-trading commissions nearly tripled to 215.37 billion yen, accounting for 34% of total revenue.
Sumitomo, Sakura Move Up Bank Merger
Japan's Sumitomo Bank and Sakura Bank unveiled new details on their planned merger, including the
decision to move up the combination, which will create the world's third-largest bank, by one year to April
2001. Sumitomo Bank will be the surviving company, which will be called Sumitomo Mitsui Banking Co. The
banks also said one Sakura share will be equal to 0.6 share in Sumitomo Bank.
IMF's Fischer to Discuss Jakarta Reforms
The International Monetary Fund's acting managing director, Stanley Fischer, said he was encouraged by
Indonesia's economic reforms but warned it must not waver from an IMF program that he will review today
with President Abdurrahman Wahid in Jakarta. An IMF team this week will assess Indonesia's reforms before
the IMF board next month decides whether or not to resume lending to Indonesia. Mr. Fischer also welcomed
the accord last week by the Paris Club of country creditors to reschedule $5.8 billion of Indonesia's debt.
Bangkok Bank Posts Loss on Provisions
Bangkok Bank reported a loss of 22.3 billion baht ($588 million) for the first quarter, the result of taking
provisions of 25.5 billion baht against bad debt. Thailand's largest bank gave no year-earlier comparison for
the first-quarter loss. The provisions will better position the bank to write down losses from problem loans,
which totaled 365 billion baht at March 31. Meanwhile, Thai Farmers Bank said it swung to first-quarter
earnings of 301.4 million baht -- its first profit in two years -- from a net loss of 7.24 billion baht a year earlier.

Norilsk Says Platinum Exports Resume
Russia's Norilsk Nickel has resumed exports of platinum, the Prime-Tass news agency reported the company
as saying. Norilsk, the world's second-largest platinum producer, hadn't been exporting the metal since last
year because it didn't have the necessary government permits. A company spokesman declined to disclose
the volume of the newly resumed platinum exports, the report said. The company was unavailable to confirm
the report Friday.
Zimbabwe Tourism Hit by Violence
Zimbabwe's tourism industry is on its knees, as the southern African country's violent upheaval over the
ownership of farmland and other problems bring an avalanche of cancellations, people in the industry said.
Hotel occupancy at Victoria Falls, one of the world's top tourist destinations, is less than half the usual level
for this time of year, they said.
West African Common Currency Planned
Six West African nations have signed an agreement to adopt a common currency by 2003. A joint declaration
last week by leaders of the English-speaking countries of Ghana, Nigeria, Liberia, Sierra Leone and Gambia,
as well as of French-speaking Guinea, also calls for possible monetary union with eight francophone West
African countries that already share the French-backed CFA franc.
Belgian Prosecutors Close Coke Inquiry
Belgian prosecutors closed the investigation into a health scare involving dozens of people who felt ill last
summer after drinking Coca-Cola, and won't file charges, Belgian media reported Saturday. The scare
prompted the biggest product recall in Coca-Cola's history. The prosecutors' office was closed Saturday and
officials handling the case couldn't be reached for comment, but a Coca-Cola spokesman said it expected the
move after a recent report by Belgian health authorities, who found there was no toxic contamination but that
off-odors in the bottled beverage may have provoked a psychosomatic reaction among students who drank it.

Mexico Targets Aid to Combat Drought
Mexico's Agriculture Ministry has allocated one billion pesos ($106.5 million) to combat drought in the
country's north and center, where much of the region was designated a disaster area last year. The ministry
said the funds would be used for programs to spur seasonal employment, improve irrigation systems, road
reconstruction and technical assistance. The government last week raised to nine the number of states
designated as disaster zones, making them eligible for emergency funding. Agriculture Minister Romarico
Arroyo said these states have endured the worst water shortage of any five-year period in the last half
century. The official said the government, through the various programs, is hoping to encourage less water
use and improve the capture of rain water and better retention of water.
Four-Year Strike Ends at Goldcorp Mine
The longest strike in Canadian history ended after Canadian gold miner Goldcorp and workers at its Red
Lake Mine in northern Ontario reached an agreement, the company said Friday. Goldcorp didn't release
details of the deal that ended the strike, which began in June 1996. Only 100 of the 187 employees who
originally went on strike are still with the company.

`Lopsided' Growth
REVIEW & OUTLOOK (Editorial)
`Lopsided' Growth
878 words
19 April 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
A30
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
With International Monetary Fund headlines going to feckless protests, perhaps someone ought to pause for
a moment to assess how the Fund's doing on its original mandate, to wit, promoting "international monetary
cooperation, balanced growth of international trade and a stable system of exchange rates."
Especially so over the past few years, the IMF's currency management advice has often compounded the
problems of poor nations. The IMF's Articles of Agreement specifically charge it with using its influence to
combat "competitive exchange depreciation," which sounds a lot like the policy it was promoting in the
devaluations that defined the 1997-98 financial crisis. Currency instability in the Asian tigers helped push
millions of people who were starting to emerge into the middle class back into poverty -- certainly a more
salient point of protest than supposed exploitation by Nike and its like.
The IMF's latest view of monetary cooperation comes in a yearly report entitled "Exchange Rate Regimes in
an Increasingly Integrated World Economy." It says, "No single exchange rate regime is best for all countries
at all times."
This is not exceptionally helpful guidance, suggesting as it does that the markets should remain indifferent to
the enormous uncertainty presented by a haphazard approach to currency stability. But we guess it is an
intellectual advance over the de facto policy the Fund followed for many years -- that floating-rate regimes are
the new gold standard. What any economy needs is monetary stability, giving savers and investors some
confidence their capital won't be wiped out by currency depreciation. In small emerging economies, an
exchange-rate anchor is an easy route to stability and the report gives a nod in this direction. Given that most
world trade and investment is denominated in U.S. dollars, the logical thing for an emerging nation is to try to
keep the local currency in some reasonably stable relationship to the dollar -- an option the IMF opposed in
Thailand and elsewhere.
The report and the World Economic Outlook also turn their attention to the developed world, commenting on
the three major currency regions. The WEO complains of "seemingly significant misalignments of several key
currencies." Central banks know how to change currency alignments, by adjusting monetary policy. Yet with
the dollar rising and the Euro sinking, the Fund's top economic counselor specifically said, "We would not, at
this time, want U.S. monetary policy to ease and ECB policy to tighten in an effort to correct that."
Instead, the Fund seems to want to stabilize exchange rates by attacking the "lopsided pattern of growth
among the principal currency areas." Somewhere buried in its Europe comments, the Fund does say "many
economies of the Euro area seem hamstrung by their very high tax rates, which are likely to have negative
effects on incentives and thereby impede growth." But the Fund suggests the immediate priority is to slow
growth in the U.S. The required action therefore is to avoid "significant relaxation in the fiscal stance" -- no tax
cuts, please. And in the short-term the Fed should do "the bulk of the monetary firming."
Pardon us, but surely a firmer dollar would exacerbate those "significant misalignments." At least, most
students learned in Economics 101 that raising interest rates will attract funds and boost exchange rates.
But under the new IMF economics, it seems that higher interest rates will depress the exchange rate by
slowing growth, which by this calculus is a win-win proposition.
While you're pondering that, also note that the IMF acting managing director Stanley Fischer threw cold water
all over the "reform" of the IMF being touted by Treasury Secretary Lawrence Summers. The alleged notion is
that the IMF should move toward short-term crisis management and away from long-term development
lending. Asked about this, Mr. Fischer said, "You will not find support among the developing country members
of the IMF for this proposal." And, "I don't see what is accomplished by turning this over to the World Bank,
taking our macroeconomists, putting them in the World Bank and moving part of the IMF there."
Reading the tea leaves, we'd say the path of IMF reform is more of the same. That is to say, employees of
the IMF will continue to enjoy their tax-free salaries and are not likely to be downsized in an economic

--- Page 124 ---
Page 124 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
contraction. The inconsistencies of IMF economics and the consistencies of its patronage system remind us
of a remark in the April 17 New Republic by former World Bank chief economist Joseph Stiglitz, who as
former World Bank chief economist knew all the players.
"IMF experts believe they are brighter, more educated, and less politically motivated than the economists in
the countries they visit. In fact, the economic leaders from those countries are pretty good -- in many cases
brighter or better educated than the IMF staff, which frequently consists of third-rank students from first-rate
universities. (Trust me, I've taught at Oxford University, MIT, Stanford University, Yale University and
Princeton University, and the IMF almost never succeeded in recruiting any of the best students.)"
(See related letter: "Letters to the Editor: How We're Reforming the IMF" -- WSJ April 28, 2000)

For a Bear in Texas, Stocks' PlungeIs Honey but Not Yet Vindication
Heard in Texas
TEXAS JOURNAL
For a Bear in Texas, Stocks' Plunge Is Honey but Not Yet Vindication
By Jonathan Weil
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,310 words
19 April 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
Texas Journal
T2
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
After last week's technology-stock crash, one might have guessed champagne corks were popping at the
Dallas headquarters of David W. Tice & Associates. No way, says Mr. Tice, one of the nation's most
outspoken bears. He has officially declared his office a no-gloating zone.
Not that he didn't have reason to be pleased. Thanks to the past month's jitters, Mr. Tice once again is getting
his day in the sun. From March 10, the day the Nasdaq Composite Index peaked at an intra-day high of
5132.52, through the end of last week, Mr. Tice's Prudent Bear Fund soared 21.5%, the second-best
performance by any U.S. diversified equity mutual fund over that time period, according to Lipper Inc. That's
because the $215 million fund he manages is geared primarily toward betting that stock prices will drop, or
short selling, which benefits from market downturns. Last week, the fund was up 23.4%, making it the top
one-week performer during a stretch when the Nasdaq Composite fell 25.3%.
Of course, this has been a long time coming. For years, Mr. Tice's bets against the bullish tide have been
dead wrong. That in part explains why he isn't exactly celebrating just yet. And if past investor behavior is any
indication, the good times for Prudent Bear holders could be short-lived. Just Monday, for instance, Prudent
Bear gave up 4.4% of its value, finishing at $4.33 a share, compared with the Nasdaq Composite's 6.6% gain
that day. And yesterday the Nasdaq Composite gained 7.2% to close at 3793.57.
In a memo e-mailed to his 23 employees over the weekend, Mr. Tice cautioned his staff not to rub in the
recent gains when hobnobbing around town.
"I believe I have told you how careful we have to be when the market starts going our way," Mr. Tice wrote.
"Now is that time. It should affect how we act with our friends in a bar or at a restaurant or at a hockey game
because others will be observing. With the Nasdaq market down more last week than any week in history, it's
time to be careful. Lots of bulls have been obnoxious. But we can never celebrate openly."
Nor does he feel anywhere near vindicated just yet. After all, despite its swoon, the Nasdaq Composite at
Friday's close was still up 32% from a year earlier. Mr. Tice, by comparison, has been publicly sounding
alarms about stock-market bubbles since 1994. Indeed, through last week, Prudent Bear had produced an
average annual loss of 17% for the previous three years. This means that an investor who plunked $1,000
into Mr. Tice's no-load fund in April 1997 finished last week with only about $570 to show for it. The fund's
one-year return, through Friday, was a hair under 5%.
"We were so early, it's not like we've been proved right yet," says the 45-year-old Mr. Tice. During the
summer of 1998, when the broader market also was in bearish territory, Mr. Tice was warning that a global
economic meltdown would send shivers through the U.S. economy, pointing to such warning signs as the
Asian financial collapse, economic troubles in Russia, rampant manufacturing overcapacity and sluggish
energy prices that were causing pains for some developing nations.
Today, his concerns are just as pointed: Americans' credit is overextended. Foreign investors may pull out of
U.S. stocks now that there's a chink in the armor. Tech-stock valuations, which he and others believed to be
outrageous and unsustainable a few years ago, are even more so today in his view. Inflation is rising,
signaling future interest-rate boosts and a weaker U.S. dollar. And in a prolonged bear market, the
much-discussed "wealth effect" created by recent market gains could be turned on its head, curbing
consumer confidence and spending.
At least for the past month, Mr. Tice's short-selling strategy has been a winning one. (In a short sale, an
investor bets on falling prices by borrowing shares of stock and immediately selling them, hoping to profit by

--- Page 126 ---
Page 126 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
buying back shares at lower prices at some point in the future.) Among the stocks that fueled Prudent Bear's
recent gains: cellular-phone maker Qualcomm, now down 44% from its 52-week high at $118.188; RF Micro
Devices, a maker of wireless-communications equipment, off 47% at $97.313; chip manufacturer Rambus,
down 68% at $152; and e-commerce reservations booker priceline.com, down 61% at $64.
All four, in Mr. Tice's view, still have much further to fall. Qualcomm, for one, still trades for more than 260
times trailing earnings. And priceline.com continues to use what he sees as aggressive revenue-recognition
practices; namely, it includes the cost of an airline ticket, for instance, in its own top-line revenue results, as
it's collecting the brokering fee for booking a reservation. Priceline contends its accounting is sound because
unlike travel agents, it does take ownership of tickets for a short period during the transaction.
Mr. Tice last year issued a report critical of accounting practices at Tyco International, sending its stock
plunging. Tyco in December disclosed that the U.S. Securities and Exchange Commission had begun an
inquiry into its accounting. The company has denied that its accounting was improper and has said it is
cooperating with the SEC.
Mr. Tice declines to say what other companies his fund currently is shorting, but he does point to several
examples of stocks on which he is decidedly bearish. Mr. Tice says he is sour on International Business
Machines, currently down 17% from its 52-week high at $115.125. He envisions slowing sales and
heightened competition combined with declining profitability from personal-computer sales at IBM.
Chase Manhattan, down 20% from its peak at $80.75, could be headed for more declines, he says, partly due
to downturns in the value of its venture-capital holdings. And he cautions that storage-systems maker EMC,
down 10% from its high at $129.875, remains overvalued at 136 times trailing earnings in an industry also
marked by increasing competition and falling product prices.
If he is right, Mr. Tice promises, there won't be any crowing. "People don't like to be told `I told you so' when
they're losing a lot of money," he says. "History has taught us that excesses in the financial system have to
be wrung out. This is just the beginning. It will take a period of years. And it will be very painful. Because of
that, it's hard to feel celebratory."
---
Rough Landing: Kitty Hawk fell 93.6% last week to 28.1 cents a share. Last week the Dallas air-cargo
company said it will miss a debt payment, blaming high maintenance costs and slower growth in the first
quarter. It is negotiating with lenders and considering selling assets.
---
Safety Plays: Investors last week ducked into some out-of-favor Texas stocks at the same time that recent
highfliers were getting dumped. Service Corp. International, the Houston funeral-home chain, jumped 36.7%
last week to $4.188. And inflation concerns sparked a 25.9% rise in Houston-based Battle Mountain Gold, to
$2.125. Small-capitalization biotechnology companies like Interleukin Genetics, San Antonio, and Energy
Biosystems, The Woodlands, lost more than half their value.
---
Nose Dive: Dallas-based i2 Technologies fell 43% last week to $78.063, reflecting a sectorwide downturn in
business-to-business e-commerce stocks.

EUROPE
Berlusconi's Chief
At Italian TV Empire
Is Expected to Quit
Maurizio Carlotti, a onetime Communist militant who runs Silvio Berlusconi's television empire, is expected to
quit as chief executive of Mediaset as soon as this week amid disagreements about the group's strategy,
people familiar with the matter say. Mr. Carlotti, who couldn't be reached for comment, served as Mediaset's
CEO since 1998, after successfully turning around the Italian company's Spanish network, Telecinco. At
Mediaset's headquarters in Milan, however, an independent-minded Mr. Carlotti repeatedly clashed with
close associates of Mr. Berlusconi, who has founded the TV company and still holds almost half its shares.
Yesterday, as speculation about Mr. Carlotti's departure flooded the market, Mediaset shares sank 6% to
16.9 euros ($16.26) each, cutting the company's market value to 21 billion euros. Still, some investors say
they aren't too alarmed. "Mediaset was well managed even before Carlotti arrived, so its fortunes are not tied
to this CEO," said Stefano Benzi, a fund manager at Fideuram Capital. Mr. Carlotti, in a written statement
faxed to news organizations yesterday, denied that the conflict involves last month's plan by Mr. Berlusconi's
Fininvest holding company to float its own Internet unit, Jumpy.
Gazprom, ENI in Credit Pact on Pipeline
Russian gas monopoly Gazprom and Italian energy concern ENI signed a $1.1 billion credit agreement with a
consortium of Western banks as part of financing for a natural-gas pipeline from Russia to Turkey. The banks
include Banca Commerciale Italiana, Mediocredito Centrale and West Deutsche Landesbank Girozentrale.
The debtor, ENI and Gazprom's joint venture Blue Stream Pipeline, will use the money to build the offshore
section of the pipeline crossing the Black Sea and to procure Italian pipes and equipment to build the
overland section of the project. Gazprom said the long-term credit will be guaranteed by Italy's export credit
agency SACE, but didn't disclose the terms of the loan. Gazprom in February began building the part of the
onshore section of the pipeline that lies on Russian territory. The cost of the entire project is estimated at $3.2
billion.
German M&A Activity Soared in Quarter
The value of German merger-and-acquisition activity more than tripled in the first quarter, excluding the failed
Deutsche Bank and Dresdner Bank merger, a report by Thomson Financial showed. The transaction volume
of M&A activity rose to 30.9 billion euros ($29.7 billion) in the period, from 9.3 billion euros a year earlier.
Germany's first-quarter M&A activity ranked second in Europe behind Britain, where deals with a transaction
volume of 161 billion euros were agreed, Thomson said. Germany was in seventh place in the first quarter of
1999.
Lafarge Blasts Blue Circle's Document
French construction group Lafarge blasted rival Blue Circle Industries' defense document, in which the British
cement maker announced plans to return GBP 800 million ($1.27 billion) to its shareholders. The amount,
equivalent to about GBP 1 a share, "can be financed while preserving the group's ability to pursue its core
cement strategy," Blue Circle said. It said it would return about GBP 400 million as soon as practicable after
Lafarge's bid lapses, with the balance returned by year end. On Monday, the European Commission cleared
Lafarge's GBP 3.4 billion offer for Blue Circle on the condition that Lafarge sell its Danish concrete-tile
business, to which Lafarge has agreed.

Japanese companies expect real gross domestic product growth to come to 0.9% in the current fiscal year

average 1.3% annually, and by 1.5% a year during the next five years, the EPA said.
Asia May Create Energy Commonwealth

--- Page 129 ---
Page 129 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Countries in northeast Asia are considering establishing an energy commonwealth to protect themselves from
a possible oil crisis, said an official with China's State Development Planning Commission. Initiated by
energy-strapped countries China, Japan and South Korea, the organization also will include Russia, Mongolia
and possibly North Korea, said an SDPC senior researcher who is coordinating the program with other
countries for China. The concept is to establish crude-oil reserves jointly owned by member countries to
ensure crude supply in times of shortage. The size of the reserves is proposed at the equivalent of 25 days of
crude imports for each member country.

EUROPE
Russia Moves Closer
To a Restructuring
Of Power Monopoly
Russia edged toward a restructuring of its vast but ailing power network but shied away from the radical
overhaul advocated by Anatoly Chubais, chief executive of power monopoly Unified Energy Systems. In a
heated, late-night meeting at the Kremlin on Tuesday, UES's board and a raft of government ministers
adopted a vague resolution supporting a "market approach" to restructuring the power utility, UES
management said yesterday. But after hours of divisive debate, the group refrained from voting on Mr.
Chubais's plan, which calls for the breakup and sale of regional power-generating plants to private investors,
and sent it back for reworking. A revised plan is expected in June.
UES management predicts the aging network will need $75 billion in investment by 2010 if Russia is to avoid
widespread blackouts. To attract this, the unified network of 70 regional power companies and coast-to-coast
power lines will have to be opened to private strategic investors, including foreigners. But some forces in the
government believe that a sell-off would threaten Russia's national security. The debate will test the
convictions of Vladimir Putin, the newly elected president, who has pledged to deliver a mixture of market
reforms and strong central government.
Andre Board Ousted by Dissident Holders
Rebel shareholders of French shoe retailer Groupe Andre ousted the company's board yesterday and
installed a new slate of directors with a mandate to shake up management. The company's largest
shareholder, United Kingdom investment group NR Atticus, led the coup at a freewheeling annual meeting in
Paris. NR Atticus's founder, Nathaniel Rothschild, was among four Atticus representatives elected to the
board and will act as chairman. The rebellion at Groupe Andre marks one of the first times dissident outside
stockholders have forced a change of control at a French company.
German Greens Overwhelm Web Site
Germany's Green Party overwhelmed the Web site of Juergen Ruettgers, the gubernatorial candidate for the
conservative Christian Democrats in the state of North-Rhine Westphalia, with thousands of electronic-mail
messages to protest an anti-immigration campaign. The e-mails shut down Mr. Ruettgers's mailbox. The
Internet skirmish was the first of its kind in German politics. The mass e-mailing came in response to a
postcard campaign by Mr. Ruettgers's Christian Democratic Union, which calls for "more education instead of
more immigration." The Greens call the campaign populist and xenophobic.
Germany's Adjusted Jobless Rate at 10%
German unemployment rose unexpectedly in March, but prospects are brighter for coming months. The
number of jobless adjusted for seasonal effects rose by 8,000 last month, the Federal Labor Office said. The
rise came as a surprise because dole queues had been shortening for five consecutive months before. The
seasonally adjusted unemployment rate was unchanged at 10%. The disappointing development stemmed
mostly from eastern Germany, where lower government subsidies led to fewer public work programs and
more job seekers.

Japan's Nintendo may delay the launch of its next-generation Game Boy because of a shortage of important
parts for the hand-held video-game machine, a company spokesman said. The Kyoto-based game maker
may push back the August launch of the portable player but no final decision has been made, he said. Last
month Nintendo said it will delay the North American launch of its next-generation home video-game console,
code-named Dolphin and based on an advanced chip from International Business Machines. That console is
still on track to ship in Japan from December but won't make it to the U.S. until early next year. Any further
delays could be troubling news for Nintendo, which hopes to compete with Sony's PlayStation2.
Indonesian Cigarette Maker's Shares Fall
About 17,000 workers have now joined a strike at Indonesia's leading cigarette maker, Gudang Garam,
triggering a further drop in the company's share price. The industrial action began last Thursday when 200
drivers walked out at the company's main plant in Kediri, 370 miles east of Jakarta, in a dispute over layoff
pay, witnesses said. In morning trading, Gudang Garam shares slid slightly more than 5% to 13,050 rupiah
($1.69). The stock price has now fallen almost 13% since the strike began and is now at its lowest level since

Normandy Mining, Australia's largest gold miner, will restructure to refocus operations on gold, jettisoning
some nongold assets and buying full ownership of 57.75%-owned gold miner Great Central Mines. Under the
restructuring, Normandy said it may use any net funds generated from the deals to buy back as much as 5%
of its stock, depending on the share price. The restructuring pleased analysts, who are poised to rerate the
stock and have complained of the company's complex structure. The moves make Normandy a more
attractive takeover target at a time when Tiger Management is looking to sell its 11% stake in the company.

An Argentine judge has ordered that two Paraguayans suspected in the assassination of their country's vice

Mexico to Keep Strict Monetary Policy

10% this year and Armando Baquiero said there is now no evidence that inflationary pressures are growing.
However, he noted that Mexico is expected to receive large flows of capital following its recent upgrade to
investment grade by Moody's Investors Service. That flow of capital could further push down interest rates,
which could spur even greater consumption and cause inflation to rise.

Sugar Rises on Bright Supply, Demand Outlook
Commodities
Sugar Rises on Bright Supply, Demand Outlook
By Brendan Coffey
Dow Jones Newswires

NEW YORK -- Sugar futures broke over six cents a pound for the first time in three months as an unexpected
brightening of supply and demand prospects led speculative commodity funds to buy heavily yesterday on the
Coffee, Sugar & Cocoa Exchange.
The front-month May contract gained 21 points to settle at 6.02 cents a pound, the highest price since Jan. 4,
as funds added long positions, or bets that prices will rise on CSCE, a division of the New York Board of
Trade.
"This market has been working higher since the end of February, so today's move is the latest extension of a
near-term upward move," said Arthur Stevenson, a futures analyst at Prudential Securities Inc. in New York.
Since hitting an 11-month low of 4.62 cents on Feb. 29, sugar prices have staged a 30% rally on improved
near-term demand and longer-range forecasts for reduced supply in the crop year beginning in October.
"Generally there is good support in the market," explained Jack Scoville, a tropical commodity analyst at Price
Futures Group, a Chicago-based brokerage firm. "Japan is buying everything it can out of Thailand, and
Brazilian prices are holding," he said, referring to two of the world's three largest sugar exporters.
Indeed, the rally in sugar is a far cry from the dire predictions of just one month ago, when a sixth-straight
year of oversupply and apparent weaker demand had pundits looking for prices to tumble to the 15-year low
of 4.36 cents set in May.
The key to the rally has been buying from Russia, the world's largest sugar importer. Last year, Russia lifted
sugar off the 15-year low by buying massive amounts, 5.9 million metric tons (6.49 million tons) in all. The
scale of last year's purchases, along with threats by the Russian government of prohibitive tariffs on imports,
left most analysts expecting no buying from Russia at all in 2000. Contrary to expectations, though, Russians
have gobbled up close to two million tons of sugar year-to-date, a significant boost to the market.
Nature also has played a role, with a series of cyclones that have battered the prime growing region of
Australia, the world's second-largest sugar exporter. The storms have damaged crops enough to reduce the
harvest by at least 400,000 tons to below last year's output of 5.0 million tons.
In addition, the woefully low prices of the past two years are being reflected in reduced planting intentions for
sugar cane and sugar beet in the coming crop year, according to analysts.
However, even with all the bullish factors of late, few predict sugar prices can work much higher before
August. Brazil, the world's largest producer, needs to start selling the bulk of its estimated 12 millionton
harvest and will probably find a price of 6.1 cents to 6.25 cents too good to pass up, analysts said.
"With the more attractive price levels, more producers will be seduced to sell," said Prudential's Mr.
Stevenson.
In other commodity markets yesterday:
COFFEE: After sliding to a five-month low of 97.2 cents a pound early, CSCE May coffee futures staged a
furious 530-point rally in the last minutes of trading on news that 11 exporting countries, including Brazil and
Colombia, the world's largest coffee producers, agreed in principal to withhold supply from the market starting
June 1. The May contract settled at $1.018 a pound, up 1.7 cents on the day.
SILVER: May futures fell 2.8 cents to $5.137 per ounce yesterday on the Comex division of the New York
Mercantile Exchange, but maintained most of Tuesday's price gains. "Silver also benefited from the equity

markets' losses, but unlike gold, it will continue to look good as an alternate investment because of the lack
of a central bank threat," said Dave Meger, a senior metals analyst at Chicago-based Alaron Trading.

EUROPE/MIDEAST
United Pan-Europe
To Buy Cable Firm
In German Strategy
United Pan-Europe Communications agreed to buy German broadband company EWT/TSS for 935 million
euros ($894 million), in a bid to gain market share in Germany, the world's third-largest cable market. UPC,
the European cable operator unit of Denver-based UnitedGlobalCom, would pay 237 million euros in cash
and the remaining 698 million euros in stock of its UPC Germany, a UPC spokeswoman said. The deal gives
the Stritzl family, which runs EWT/TSS, a 49% stake in UPC Germany, she said. The Stritzl family will be able
to convert its stake into UPC shares in April 2001.
EWT/TSS is Germany's fourth-largest independent broadband cable operator, with 650,000 subscribers to its
cable video and Internet access services. EWT/TSS's 1999 sales totaled 57.6 million euros, and earnings
before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization were 25 million euros. UPC's acquisition of EWT/TSS is
part of the company's fallback strategy if its efforts to acquire parts of No. 1 cable operator Deutsche
Telekom's cable assets, which the former monopoly is forced to unload to satisfy regulators, fall apart. UPC
has gone on a $5.5 billion buying spree, gobbling up 10% of the European cable market in 12 different
countries.
Turkey's Austerity Plan Shows Progress
Improved budget and inflation figures released yesterday show that Turkey's three-month-old disinflation
program is making headway despite the political turbulence surrounding next month's presidential elections,
bankers and analysts say. The Turkish Treasury said budget revenue in the first quarter was ahead of target,
while wholesale-price inflation in March of 3.1% and consumer-price inflation of 2.9%, respectively, met and
exceeded market expectations. Prices set by private manufacturers rose just 2.5%, the lowest figure since
July 1994. The austerity program agreed to in December with the International Monetary Fund aims to end
nearly three decades of high inflation and roller-coaster growth that last year brought a 6.4% contraction in
the Eastern Mediterranean's biggest economy.
IMF Urges ECB Not to Raise Rates
The International Monetary Fund called the euro's slide against other major currencies "undesirable," but it
urged the European Central Bank to fight any temptation to raise interest rates to shore up the currency. "A
monetary response in the absence of clear risks to price stability would do little to strengthen the currency,"
the IMF's directors said in an annual review of the economic performance of the 11 countries in the euro
zone. The admonition came after the common currency fell below 100 yen Friday for the first time since the
currency was launched 14 months ago. It strengthened to 100.37 yen yesterday in New York. The euro has
lost 26% of its value against the yen since the launch, and 18% against the dollar as of Friday.
Annual Gold Sales Likely to Be 500 Tons
Central banks are likely to release on average 500 metric tons of gold from reserves every year for the next
decade, said Bobby Godsell, chief executive of the world's biggest gold producer, AngloGold, Johannesburg,
South Africa. Mr. Godsell said he believed this amount of gold could be sold every year as central-bank
reserves are run down. Nevertheless, he sees the outlook for the precious metal as stable and expects gold
to trade within a "broad equilibrium price range" of $280 to $320 a troy ounce. Gold was trading at $278 an
ounce yesterday.

BRIEFLY:
-- Officials have found another case of mad-cow disease in the north of France, the 13th afflicted animal
reported in that country this year. Officials slaughtered the milk cow, born in 1994, and its herd of 33, as a
precaution, the Agricultural Ministry said.
-- Shares in Stagecoach Holdings tumbled 42% after the British transport operator sold its train-leasing
operation for less than expected and issued a profit warning.
-- UniCredito Italiano signed an agreement with Gruppo Editoriale L'Espresso to set up an Internet venture to
exploit the five million-strong customer base of Italy's third-largest bank.

ASIA/PACIFIC
Korean Chaebols End Cross Guarantees
Most of South Korea's largest conglomerates have eliminated cross-debt-payment guarantees as part of their
restructuring efforts, the Fair Trade Commission said. Affiliates of the country's 30 largest business groups
were required to eliminate all remaining debt-payment guarantees to their sister companies by the end of
March. They have been banned from providing new guarantees since April 1998. The country's five largest
chaebols, Hyundai, Samsung, Daewoo, LG and SK, have met these requirements. Elimination of cross
guarantees by Ssangyong, Kohap, Haitai, Anam, Dong Ah, Jinro and Shinho have been delayed by a year,
the panel said. Cross guarantees at Saehan, Kangwon Industries, Cheiljedang and Samyang have also been
extended by one year.
Hikari Trims Forecast for Handset Sales
Hikari Tsushin, a Japanese mobile-telephone agent and Internet business investor, has lowered its sales
forecast for cellular-phone handsets in the fiscal year ending Aug. 31 to 3.5 million units from 4 million,
President Yasumitisu Shigeta said. In the first half ended Feb. 29, handset sales totaled 1.7 million. Overly
ambitious targets for volume-incentive subscription sales and "soft management controls" at its main
cell-phone subscription agency business were cited as behind the surprise 13 billion yen ($126 million)
operating loss Hikari now expects to report for the first half.
China, EU May Resume Talks on WTO
The European Union may resume talks in Beijing on Chinese accession to the World Trade Organization
within weeks, a spokesman for EU Trade Commissioner Pascal Lamy said. The latest round of talks broke off
Friday in the Chinese capital after four days, with Mr. Lamy saying not enough progress had been made for a
deal. Spokesman Anthony Gooch said the EU looked for talks to resume "in a matter of weeks, rather than
months, probably in Beijing." Mr. Lamy plans to consult first with the 15 EU member nations on how the group
should approach a new round of talks. Mr. Gooch said a Monday meeting of EU foreign ministers in
Luxembourg "might be the appropriate time to report to member states."
China Bans False-Name Bank Accounts
China has introduced new banking rules that prohibit individuals from opening savings accounts under false
names, a move Beijing hopes will curb corruption and bolster tax management. State banks hold more than
$720 billion in individual savings deposits, one of the highest savings rates in the world and nearly equal to
the value of China's annual economic output. Central bankers say as much as 20% of all deposits in China
are linked in some form to corrupt or undeclared earnings.
BRIEFLY:
-- South Korean farmers reported new outbreaks of what is suspected to be foot-and-mouth disease in at
least two more regions over the weekend, prompting fears that the deadly livestock ailment could become a
national epidemic, officials said.
-- Malaysia's nongovernment domestic bond market is expected to expand a net 20% to 30% this year and
next, said Deutsche Bank's managing director for Asian local markets, David Zezza.
-- LG Chemical of South Korea and Germany's Merck KGAA announced plans to develop, manufacture and
distribute optical films for use in liquid crystal displays.

Mexico's IPAB Begins Sale of Buildings
Mexico's Bank Savings Protection Institute, known as IPAB, began the process of selling off six office
buildings. IPAB said five of the six properties are located in Mexico City with the remaining lot in Tijuana. All
six have a market value exceeding 10 million pesos ($1.1 million), according to the agency. CB Richard Ellis
will be the financial agent for the sale of three of the properties, and CMI Staubach will handle the sale of the
other three.
Westburne Agrees to Buy United Electric
Westburne, a Montreal distributor of industrial parts, said it has signed a letter of intent to acquire United
Electric Group, a St. Louis distributor of electrical supplies with operations in Missouri and Illinois. Westburne
also said it has completed the purchase of Cope & Gurr Machinery, Windsor, Canada, expanding its
distribution of industrial products in southern Ontario. Westburne said the two acquisitions will generate 100
million Canadian dollars (US$69 million) in additional sales. Westburne said its purchase of United Electric
requires due diligence and should be completed by month end. Westburne said Cope & Gurr will be added to
its industrial product group. The company said these deals, coupled with its purchases in January of
Beautyware Plumbing Supply, Denver, and Specialty Plumbing, Phoenix, should generate C$180 million in
annualized sales.
BRIEFLY:
-- Mexican construction giant Empresas ICA received 2.28 billion pesos ($246.2 million) in PICs, which are
bonds denominated in inflation-linked accounting units known as UDIs, as part of the release of assets
linked to the Cuernavaca-Acapulco highway project.
-- Mexico's Grupo Financiero Bancomer and Grupo Financiero BBV-Probursa agreed to a capitalization and
merger program, which formally initiates the merger process between the two financial groups.
