Threat to Prosperity
Threat to Prosperity
By Robert Mundell
2,549 words
30 March 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
A30
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
The launching of the euro on Jan. 1, 1999, may prove to have been the most important event in the history of
the international monetary system since the dollar took over from sterling the role of dominant international
currency. It gave the world economy a tripolar system based on the dollar, euro and yen. Is this world
currency configuration optimal? How many currencies does the world need? Which countries belong in a
currency area? What is the optimum currency area?
My own view is that the biggest danger to world prosperity arises from wide swings, not based in any
economic fundamentals, in the exchange rates of these three currencies; that central banks can and should
conduct their affairs to dampen this volatility, evolving policies that provide for intervention in the foreign
exchange markets; and that these would be salutary steps toward the ultimate development of one currency
managed by a world central bank.
The classical economists were virtually unanimous in their support for a common unit of money. Economists
like John Stuart Mill deplored the nationalism that made it impractical: "So much of barbarism, however, still
remains in the transactions of most civilised nations, that almost all independent countries choose to assert
their nationality by having, to their own inconvenience and that of their neighbours, a peculiar currency of their
own."
For most of history, currency arrangements have coincided with political units. The larger the political unit the
larger the currency area. If the world were ruled by a single power or a world government today, a world
currency would be all but inevitable. If the currency were managed properly, the situation would be ideal. On
this line of reasoning, the next best thing to a properly managed world currency might be a well-managed
system of state currencies firmly loc ked by fixed exchange rates.
Much ink has been spilled on the meaningless debate whether "fixed" or "flexible" exchange rates are better
"systems." In the abstract, nothing at all can be said. A fixed exchange rate is a monetary rule, one approach
to monetary stability. A flexible exchange rate is the removal of a monetary rule, not a policy in itself. Fixed
exchange rates have to be compared with other monetary rules not with flexible exchange rates. A flexible
exchange rate is consistent with either price stability or hyperinflation, depending on what rule is applied.
What is the best approach to price stability? A central bank could try to fix the value of a basket of
commodities (e.g., the consumer price index basket), a basket of money (e.g., M1 or M2) or a basket of
currencies (possibly only one). Taking into account the lags in the effect of monetary and exchange-rate
policy, which target is likely to bring about more price stability in the long run? Briefly, is inflation, monetary
or exchange rate targeting likely to be the best way to achieve price stability?
The answer depends on the situation of a country in relation to the size configuration of countries in the world
economy. The real world of 2000 is divided into economies and currency areas of greatly different sizes. In
round numbers, the U.S. represents somewhat less than 25% of the world economy, Euroland 20%, and
Japan 15%. Together this G-3 "core" represents 60% of world output.
There are economies of scale associated with currency areas. The size of a monetary area determines its
ability to absorb shocks. In a freely floating world of paper currencies, the currency area with the largest
transactions domain is, for a given inflation rate, the most stable. The best way for the small countries to
protect their currencies from speculative attacks is to fix their currencies to one of the G-3 currencies, making
sure that the money supply is allowed to -- and is perceived to be allowed to -- move with the balance of
payments without sterilization. Fixed exchange rates always work and only work when intervention in the
foreign-exchange market determines monetary policy.
The combination of pegged or pseudofixed exchange rates with an independent monetary policy is no doubt
the worst of all exchange-rate systems. Because there is no mechanism of adjustment it is doomed to
destruct from the start. There is no point to a pegged exchange rate managed with no recognition of the iron

--- Page 139 ---
Page 139 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
law that an independent monetary policy is incompatible with fixed exchange rates. By contrast, fixed
exchange rates have built-in mechanisms of adjustment that work automatically -- as economists have known
since David Hume. A currency-board arrangement is one extreme example of a properly working fixed
exchange-rate mechanism.
The creation of the euro area will eventually, but inevitably, lead to competition with the dollar area, both from
the standpoint of excellence in monetary policy and in the enlistment of other currencies. The formation of
the euro area will present an attractive new option for countries in Central and Eastern Europe and
elsewhere. The euro area, measured by transactions domain, will soon exceed the existing dollar area, but
the expansion of the euro area will itself provoke a countervailing expansion of the dollar area in Latin
America and parts of Asia and Africa. Within the coming decade we will see both the European Central Bank
and the Federal Reserve Bank (or the U.S. Treasury) setting up departments of external affairs to deal with
the wider currency areas.
A large state cannot achieve stability by fixing its currency to the currency of a smaller state. By fixing to a
smaller currency area, a large currency area would force its inflation rate on the smaller area. Under present
institutional arrangements the U.S. has no option but targeting inflation rates. The same holds for the euro
and yen because fixing these currencies to the dollar would need or should have the cooperation of the U.S.
But it should be borne in mind that sweeping changes in the dollar-euro and yen-dollar rates could have
devastating effects on the domestic goals of these areas.
Whatever currency areas are formed, the dollar-euro-yen rates will become a matter of great concern to
Europe, the U.S. and the rest of the world. Diversification from the dollar into the euro would create the threat
of a soaring euro and play havoc with the sensitive issues of competitiveness and unemployment in Europe.
The alternative of a falling euro would raise the specter of an outbreak of inflation that would necessitate
corrective policies more deflationary than if the rate had been kept stable. It would be a grave mistake to
believe that the closed nature of the three big blocs would make exchange rates less important, or that the
dollar-euro rate can be treated with "benign neglect."
The dollar has gone down from 250 yen in 1985 to 79 yen in 1995, up to 148 yen in 1998, and down to 105
yen in early 2000. These swings make a currency area built around the yen intolerable. No doubt the
appreciation of the dollar against the yen between 1995 and 1998 had its hand in the so-called Asian crisis in
knocking stabilized currencies off their dollar pegs and aggravating the real burden of these countries' debts.
Because of the volatility of the yen-dollar rate, it seems much more likely that the dollar rather than the yen
will be the main default international currency in Asia. A stabilization of the yen-dollar rate would be a great
benefit for the rest of Asia, just as a stabilization of the dollar-euro rate would be a great benefit to Europe,
Africa and Latin America.
Before the creation of the euro, the mark-dollar rate was the most important exchange rate in Europe.
Between 1971 and 1980 the deutsche mark doubled against the dollar, to $1 = DM 1.7; between 1980 and
1985, it halved, to $1 = DM 3.4; between 1985 and the crisis of 1992, it more than doubled, to $1 = 1.39; and
it has since fallen to $1 = DM 1.9. Comparable movements of the dollar-euro rate would crack Euroland apart.
Especially in the early stages of Europe's monetary union, it will be desirable to minimize changes in the
dollar-euro rate.
Once convergence among the G-3 is achieved, it would not be in any member's economic interest to break
up either a single-currency or a three-currency monetary union. Given the gains and the superiority of such a
union over flexible rates, would it be worth seeking such a change, or would the convergence problem be
insuperable? The most important dimension of convergence is a common inflation rate. Convergence of
inflation rates in Euroland and the U.S. is well within 2%, and with Japan it is within 3%. These differences
are too small to indicate any need for significant exchange-rate changes but, as already noted, the yen-dollar
rate has undergone enormous fluctuations over the past five years. What economic function did the
exchange-rate changes among these islands of stability fulfill? Except for stuffing the socks of hedge funds,
the answer is none.
We are of course a long way from re-creating an international monetary system. If one can be created,
however, it will have to deal with exchange-rate relations among the G-3. One step in the right direction would
be for the three central banks to reassess their habit of benign neglect of the exchange rate.
It is legitimate for a country to neglect exchange-rate fluctuations only if they arise from foreign inflationary (or
deflationary) developments. Central banks should recognize that maintenance of price stability will be
furthered rather than harmed by taking explicit account of the exchange rate to mitigate wild gyrations. A
central bank can tighten or loosen its monetary policy by changing either its domestic assets (typically
government bonds) or its foreign assets (typically foreign exchange but also gold and special drawing rights).
It is currently the conventional wisdom that a large and independent central bank should restrict itself to
altering its domestic assets, to the exclusion of changing its foreign assets. But the rule that only domestic
assets should be changed is correct only for a closed economy. The only closed economy, where there are

--- Page 140 ---
Page 140 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
no foreign assets, is the world economy. A central banker who restricts himself to domestic assets opts for a
corner solution that implies that it is a matter of indifference what happens to the exchange rate, one of the
most important prices for its economy.
It is a common myth that daily turnover in foreign-exchange markets overwhelms central-bank actions with
respect to intervention, leading to the conclusion that the speculators always win and that therefore
intervention is futile. The assumption is false. Intervention always works when monetary policies are
supportive of it. Since the EU-11 countries locked exchange rates in the middle of 1998, there has been zero
speculation against the fix because the markets believed it was permanent. The myth of the futility of
intervention does, however, serve as a warning that a great deal of intervention has been useless, particularly
intervention that has been sporadic rather than systematic and concerted. Successful intervention requires
adherence to three basic principles.
-- The monetary effect of intervention should not be sterilized. For example, if the ECB were to intervene to
support the euro by selling dollars, it would simultaneously tighten liquidity, and, furthermore, the market
would know that liquidity was being tightened. It would be self-defeating for the ECB to imitate the atrocious
practices of the Bank of England and Federal Reserve System in automatically matching a sale of foreign
exchange with a purchase of government assets.
-- Intervention should take place in both the spot and forward exchange markets. Intervention is much less
effective if the forward market is ignored. Support of the spot euro alone would put downward pressure on the
forward euro, encourage outward interest arbitrage and raise interest rates in Europe by more than would be
jus tified by the liquidity-tightening alone.
-- Intervention should be concerted with monetary partners. An exchange rate has two sides to it. Concerted
intervention is more credible than unilateral intervention. G-3 (or G-7) intervention would be more credible
than unilateral intervention.
Durable reform of the financial architecture should be a goal of future policy. The volatility problem could be
addressed best by restoring fixed exchange rates among the Big Three. A single-currency monetary union
among the Big Three would probably not be politically feasible; American and Japanese national pride would
get in the way, and Europe would not be willing to scrap a new currency that it worked so long to prepare. But
a three-currency monetary union or fixed exchange rates around an international currency could be made to
work.
For the time being, it would help ameliorate the situation if the Big Three could be brought to realize that
exchange volatility is counterproductive in areas that have achieved local price stability, and that exchange
rate intervention can serve a useful purpose. Large gyrations of exchange rates can never be justified
between countries that have achieved price stability.
The world currency problem could be solved by creating a world central bank producing an international
asset backed by reserves of dollars, yen, euros and gold. Some such arrangement was proposed at Bretton
Woods but it fell afoul of political difficulties. A pattern recurrent in the history of money is that the dominant
country rejects meaningful monetary reform, probably because it involves power sharing and a weakening of
its monopolistic currency position. But it is not a healthy sign that the only option for smaller countries is to fix
their currencies to one of the G-3, a situation reminiscent of the monetary colonialism of the 19th century. A
multilateral solution based on a world central bank and a world currency would involve some power sharing
as well as a fairer distribution of international seigniorage. The advantages of such a system would be so
great that all countries could gain from it.
Not much can be done if the leaders of the major currency blocs insist upon policies that lead to volatile
exchange rates, But if agreement could be achieved on the desirability of fixed exchange rates among
currency blocs each of which have subdued inflation, it would not be a difficult step to go the extra mile and
build around the G-3 currencies a world central bank and an international currency that would be the
property of all nations.
---
Mr. Mundell is the C. Lowell Harriss Professor of Economics at Columbia University and the 1999 Nobel
Laureate in economics.

Gold Futures Fall on Report of French Bank Sale
Commodities
Gold Futures Fall on Report of French Bank Sale
By Nicholas Elliott
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
618 words
28 March 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
C19
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Gold futures fell to their lowest level this year at the Comex division of the New York
Mercantile Exchange yesterday after a report in a French newspaper that France may sell a part of its
reserves to pay for retirement funds.
Although no confirmation of the report was available and France is part of last year's Washington Agreement
by European central banks to limit their gold sales, the news revived the specter of central-bank sales that
has pressured gold sales in recent years.
Comex's April contract fell $4.60 to $280.50 a troy ounce.
Its low of $280.20 was the lowest price since Dec. 6 last year.
According to a report in the French newspaper Le Monde, French Prime Minister Lionel Jospin may ask the
Bank of France to sell as much as 23 billion euros, or $22 billion of gold. At current prices that would
represent about 85 million ounces.
"It raised a lot of concerns in the market, particularly as France is one of the biggest gold holders out there,"
said Ian MacDonald, manager of precious metals at Commerzbank in New York.
According to the World Gold Council, France holds 3,024 metric tons of gold, or about 97 million ounces,
representing 9% of total world reserves.
Analysts noted that France was part of the Washington Agreement signed last September, in which 15
European central banks agreed to limit their gold sales to 2,000 tons during the next five years. But Mr.
MacDonald said the worry now is that France will try to change that agreement.
David Meger, a metals analyst at Alaron Trading in Chicago, said, "Even though they can't sell for another
four-and-a-half years due to the Washington Agreement, it's another central-bank factor that at this point a
weak market doesn't need to hear about."
George Gero, first vice president at Prudential Securities, said the news from France wasn't the only factor
causing gold's drop. He also pointed to a souring macroeconomic environment for gold, with U.S. stocks
rallying again, energy prices down from their highs, and several U.S. investment banks downgrading their
outlooks for gold stocks.
Indeed, Mr. Gero said, "I'm surprised we didn't go further down."
Yet traders have reported solid physical demand in the past two weeks, particularly from India.
Mr. Meger, of Alaron, said the price fall would be a buying opportunity for some. "As you go below $280, I do
feel you could see a significant amount of bargain hunting and that could very well support the market over
the next couple of days."
In other commodity markets:
COCOA: Futures tumbled for the second successive day on the Coffee, Sugar & Cocoa Exchange division of
the New York Board of Trade as sharp losses in London stoked heavy fund selling in New York, participants
said. The May contract fell $43 to $797 a metric ton, adding to Friday's loss of $36. Speculative commodity
funds were heavily net long of cocoa, or holding a large balance of bets that prices would rise. It's those
contracts they are now selling.

--- Page 143 ---
Page 143 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
PORK BELLIES: Futures gained on the Chicago Mercantile Exchange, as traders viewed key U.S.
Department of Agriculture data released Friday as supportive. The March contract rose by its three-cent limit
to 94.70 cents a pound.
The USDA reported that the U.S. March 1 hog breeding herd was down 5% from a year earlier, compared
with prereport estimates for just a 4% drop.

The Americas:A Comrade From the Coup Challenges Venezuela's Chavez
The Americas: A Comrade From the Coup Challenges Venezuela's Chavez
By Mary Anastasia O'Grady
1,119 words
24 March 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
A19
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
On his Sunday weekly radio address, Venezuelan President Hugo Chavez announced that his country would
not accept a plan by Texas-based Beal Aerospace Technologies to build a satellite-launch facility in the
Essequibo region of Guyana, a territory that Venezuela has claimed to be its own since the 1940s.
Just what Mr. Chavez will do to block the investment is not clear, though he has said that he is willing to
reach a peaceful accord with Guyana over the region itself. Interestingly, there are already a number of U.S.
and Asian gold, diamond and timber investments in Essequibo.
That the former military officer would, in this moment, seize the flag of sovereignty over some long-disputed
territorial claim is entirely predictable and largely reflects his country's growing economic chaos and his own
political insecurity. Even with oil prices at levels not seen for almost 10-years highs, the prospects for a
meaningful recovery in the Venezuelan economy this year still look grim. Saber rattling at foreigners is classic
behavior for a military man who's having a tough time on the home front.
Mr. Chavez's biggest domestic problem is the looming May 28 elections, where, under the rules of the 1999
Venezuelan Constitution, all governorships, mayoral offices and national and state assemblies, as well as the
presidency, will be up for grabs. Last week, Francisco Arias Cardenas, one of Mr. Chavez's comrades in the
now-famous 1992 failed military coup, stepped down as governor of Zulia so that he could run against the
president. In launching his campaign, Mr. Arias cited Mr. Chavez's failure to work toward the goals that the
coup leaders had once taken an oath to die for.
A little known Venezuelan polling company called Grupo Campol released the first sampling of voter
sentiments this week. It showed Mr. Chavez with 50% of the vote and Mr. Arias with almost 39%. That's a
surprisingly strong position for Mr. Arias, but analysts agree that it still is far too early to tell if he has a shot at
beating the heretofore highly popular incumbent.
But win or lose the Arias candidacy marks a turning point in the Chavez era. For the first time since Mr.
Chavez overwhelmed the traditional governing elite in the 1998 election, Venezuela may be witnessing the
birth of a national opposition with some genuine credibility.
Ever since Mr. Chavez took power in 1999 he has encouraged the public to think about him as a messiah for
the poor; he often quotes from scripture and suggests that God is on his side. But while Mr. Chavez may
believe that God helped him, it is clear that his task also was made easier by the old political establishment,
which was morally bankrupt. With an estimated 70% of the population living below the poverty line and well
aware of their country's oil wealth, Mr. Chavez had no trouble winning on a platform that blamed the elite and
their corruption for the country's widespread hardship.
That indictment helped Mr. Chavez sail through his first year with wide popular support. The most prominent
political parties -- notably the Democratic Action Party and Social Christian Democrats -- have been unable to
refute Mr. Chavez's charges of decadence, or to object to his authoritarian style or his hateful rhetoric, without
being shouted down by a fed-up population.
Mr. Arias, on the other hand, is a real challenge. Like Mr. Chavez, he comes from outside the establishment.
He opposed the system when it was still strong and spent time in jail for his role in the coup. While many find
his revolutionary background distasteful, his resume makes him a serious contender for the votes of the
disenfranchised. He therefore is a problem for the Chavez government, which has courted support with its
own revolutionary credentials.
In 18 months of power Mr. Chavez has accomplished little beyond delivering a new constitution. Having
insisted on oil-production cuts, he takes credit for the rebound in oil prices. But the Venezuelan economy
contracted by 7.25% in 1999. The government forecasts growth of 2% this year but that outcome remains to
be seen. Unemployment is officially estimated at 15.4% of the work force, but is no doubt considerably

--- Page 145 ---
Page 145 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
higher. Takeovers of land by squatters is a growing problem, as is violent crime. Last week, Caracas taxi
drivers went on strike to protest the city's crime epidemic.
Mr. Arias, who spent nine years in a Catholic seminary, is widely considered to be the most educated and
most intelligent of the 1992 coup team, which consisted of five Commandantes. The other two surviving coup
leaders -- a third died in a car crash in Paris last year -- are backing his candidacy. Still in the early stages of
his campaign, he is rapidly distinguishing himself from his former ally. While being careful to protect his
outsider status and his goals of social revolution, he emphasizes his democratic moderation and his
self-control, both of which contrast sharply with the fierce, fiery, authoritarian image of Mr. Chavez. And
thanks to his track record in Zulia, where he was elected in December 1995 and re-elected in November
1998, he is already considered a constructive leader.
Locals from Maracaibo, the capital city of Zulia, report that Mr. Arias has dealt effectively with squatters,
understands the problems of entrepreneurs and has been willing to work with the private sector and his
political adversaries. Highway management, which is a constant source of concern for Venezuelans,
reportedly improved under the Arias governorship. He also has received recognition for reducing state
corruption, particularly in making it easier for people to deal with the government without paying bribes.
In a public statement last week, Mr. Arias and the two coup leaders who are supporting him outlined their
disappointment with the Chavez regime. No revolution, they said, can be tied to one person and they rejected
the irrational and disproportionate confrontation that moves away from the possibility of national
reconstruction . . . They also severely criticized Mr. Chavez's consolidation of power in a country that needs
greater decentralization in order to grow and create wealth.
They criticized the concentration of power that only seeks to substitute for the old structures, while at the
same time keeping its practices in all the different levels of traditional government. In other words, says Mr.
Arias, Mr. Chavez is just like those he replaced. Those are strong words considering the president's
messianic posture. But given his record so far, they may find resonance with Venezuela's voters.

Platinum, Palladium Part in a Day of Conflicts
Commodities
Platinum, Palladium Part in a Day of Conflicts
By Peter A. McKay
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
644 words
22 March 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
C21
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- It was a day of conflicts in the precious-metals futures markets yesterday. On the one hand,
interest-rate worries and a ballooning trade deficit boosted gold, but continuing uncertainty over Russian
metal shipments thumped palladium.
In an unusual departure, April platinum futures moved in the opposite direction of palladium, rising $4.40 to
$491.60 an ounce. June palladium futures dropped $23.40, or 3.5%, to $655 an ounce in light trading on the
New York Mercantile Exchange.
Both metals have skyrocketed over the past year as the Russian government has cut off its shipments of
them. However, analysts said Russia is a less dominant producer of palladium, which is a key component of
the catalytic-converter parts that clean car exhaust.
"There's a lot of guesswork that's still going on as to what the intentions of the Russians are," said
independent platinum analyst James Ryan. "They're still playing that for all it's worth, which I don't think is
going to change."
He said if prices remain bloated, car makers might begin switching from palladium back to platinum, which
used to be the primary metal in converters.
Analysts also pointed out that an extraordinary exchange-imposed freeze on palladium trading at the Tokyo
Commodity Exchange has remained in effect since Feb. 23, pushing the metal's New York price down
sharply while leaving platinum free to continue nudging higher.
The pending expiration early next week of some March futures contracts also affected the New York
precious-metals markets yesterday as traders turned their attention to the next available months' contracts.
April gold rose $3.90 to $290.40 an ounce on Nymex's Comex division.
The metal rallied partly because traders were seizing a buying opportunity following yesterday's previously
scheduled Bank of England auction of 25 tons of gold, said John H. Hill, an analyst at Salomon Smith
Barney.
Because the number of bids at the auction was sluggish, it was taken as a bearish sign that drove down
prices in early London trading.
But by the afternoon, Mr. Hill said New York traders had largely shrugged off the auction, focusing instead on
yesterday's interest-rate increase by the Federal Reserve and a new Commerce Department report that the
U.S. trade deficit widened 14% to a record $28 billion in January.
"Macroeconomics 101 says that you can't keep running up an ever-spiraling trade deficit without it affecting
the currency," he said. "That's good news for gold," which historically acts as a safe haven for investors
when money falls in value.
Silver for May delivery was the notable exception to yesterday's otherwise significant moves in the precious
metals. It nudged down nine cents to $5.158 on Comex.
In other commodity trading:
GRAINS: Forecasts for additional moisture in the U.S. Midwest and the Plains and speculative
commodity-fund selling pushed corn and wheat futures lower at the Chicago Board of Trade, market watchers
said. CBOT grain futures have been in retreat this week as forecasts of rain have diluted concerns about

--- Page 147 ---
Page 147 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
drought that pushed prices sharply higher last week. May corn futures fell 3.75 cents to $2.32 a bushel. May
wheat futures fell six cents to $2.6025 a bushel. Those contracts peaked last week at $2.4175 and $2.7300,
respectively. "We're looking at warmer temperatures and a wetter forecast," said Brian Scott, vice president
with R.J. O'Brien on the CBOT floor.
PORK BELLIES: Futures slipped on the Chicago Mercantile Exchange after a U.S. Department of Agriculture
report showed more bellies in storage than expected. The March contract fell 1.55 cents to 93.15 cents a
pound.

Gold Falls Before Bank of England Sale,As Crude Oil Drops and Blue Chips Rally
Commodities
Gold Falls Before Bank of England Sale, As Crude Oil Drops and Blue Chips Rally
By Enza Tedesco
Dow Jones Newswires
634 words
17 March 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
C15
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Gold futures on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange fell as traders
positioned ahead of the last of the five scheduled United Kingdom gold sales, participants said.
As the Bank of England gets ready to sell another 25 metric tons of gold on March 21, participants are
beginning to adopt a more cautious stance as they fear that an expected low demand at the auction will
deepen the erosion in prices.
The leading April contract dropped $2.60 to $287 a troy ounce.
"There's the idea that the sale of another 25 metric tons of gold is not likely to be a bullish event, so why wait
until the auction itself to sell gold," said Tim Evans, a commodities analyst with Pegasus Econometrics in
New York. "I would think that the auction would tend to confirm that the market is vulnerable and even if the
quantities bid for are on the generous side, I would imagine that the prices will not."
Another series of six 25-ton gold sales from the U.K. will start in May.
Ray Streker, a commodities analyst with New York brokerage firm LFG Inc., pointed out that the factors that
had recently sustained gold prices have been fading away, thereby undercutting some sense of fundamental
support.
"You don't have anything driving this market higher as opposed to two weeks ago when you had crude oil
going crazy and you had the stock market with a sharp sell-off," Mr. Streker said.
Indeed, the crude oil's spot month finished at $31.09 yesterday, compared with its post-Gulf War high of
$34.37 on Mar. 8. Also, the blue-chip stocks are starting to show some life as the Dow Jones Industrial
Average yesterday gained nearly 500 points, an all-time one-day record.
The rally in stocks signals that participants aren't as worried about inflation as previously anticipated, thus
lessening the potential for capital flows into the gold market, which has always served as a safe haven
during inflationary times.
Mr. Evans also pointed out that speculative commodity funds currently hold long positions, or bets that gold
prices will go higher. Back when gold spiked in February, it reflected funds moving out of short positions, or
bets the market would decline. With the fund move apparently over, the market can't look for a new wave of
traders to exit shorts.
"That trio of factors have all been reversed from last month . . . there really seems to be no fundamental
reason to rush to the gold market defense," Mr. Evans said.
In other commodity markets:
COCOA: Futures surged on the Coffee, Sugar and Cocoa Exchange on the easing of cocoa arrivals in the
Ivory Coast, the largest cocoa producer, and concern that dry weather may reduce the Ivorian mid-crop,
traders said. The May contract rose $37 to $935 a metric ton, signifying a technical market reversal as prices
rose above $900 for the first time in months. The slowing of weekly cocoa arrivals is seasonal in nature and
signifies the easing of the producer selling that recently pressured cocoa to 27-year lows, traders added.
CATTLE: The nearby April contract at the Chicago Mercantile Exchange slipped 0.65 cents to 72.12 cents a
pound, its lowest close in two weeks, as traders grew nervous about the cash cattle and beef markets. Cash
cattle prices are up nearly 6% in the past three weeks and wholesale beef prices are up more than 10% in the
past month. There is concern that both markets have become too pricey, and could reach a top soon.

Deals & Deal Makers:Zions-First Security Deal Is Dealt a BlowAs Banker Goldman Withdraws Blessing
Deals & Deal Makers: Zions-First Security Deal Is Dealt a Blow As Banker Goldman Withdraws
Blessing
By Steven Lipin
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,213 words
14 March 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
C22
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
The board of Zions Bancorp. is legally committed to backing the company's merger with First Security Corp.
There's only one hitch: Zions's investment banker at Goldman Sachs Group isn't, and that may imperil the
deal.
In a highly unusual move, the blue-chip investment bank withdrew its "fairness" opinion that blessed the
transaction and the value that Zions shareholders are receiving. The fairness opinion was recently mailed to
shareholders as part of the proxy statement for a coming shareholder vote on the deal.
Fairness opinions are ways bankers put their Good Housekeeping seals of approval on mergers. Sometimes
they are nothing more than rubber stamps to deals already crafted by two companies.
But nearly all boards engaging in mergers first want cover from their bankers that what they are doing is right
for shareholders. And investment banks' opinions become quite important in deals that may run into trouble.
In voting for mergers, shareholders rely on advice such as fairness opinions.
The upshot: Because the way the merger agreement is written, Zions must proceed with the merger despite
Goldman's stance. Zions' board is in the unenviable position of attempting to get backing of a merger that its
own advisers find troubling.
"You have a duty to be honest as a director, but [the board] have sort of constrained its ability to talk frankly
with their shareholders," says Victor Lewkow, a partner at the law firm Clearly Gottlieb, Steen & Hamilton,
which isn't involved in the deal.
These types of merger agreements are more common as lawyers try to write airtight merger accords. Now as
a result of Zions, lawyers may revise whether boards can back out of deals if bankers withdraw opinions.
The two Salt Lake City banks announced a merger of equals last year initially valued at $5.9 billion, or $28.90
a share for First Security shareholders. But antitrust regulators were more demanding about asset sales,
delaying the shareholder vote.
Then in December, the Securities and Exchange Commission required Zions to restate earnings based on
accounting changes. In early March, First Security said its earnings would be hurt by rising interest rates and
other factors.
"It's been the deal from hell," a person with knowledge of the deal said.
Indeed, amid the deal's woes and the decline in the financial stocks, the deal is currently valued at just $3.3
billion, or $16.79 a First Security share.
At 4 p.m on the Nasdaq Stock Market, Zions was up 71.875 cents at $37.9688. First Security fell 56.25 cents
to $11.50.
Of course, there are legitimate reasons why deals can't easily be ripped up just because of investment
bankers. In effect, if a banker could kill a deal by withdrawing a fairness opinion, "it's a free option on the
company," one merger adviser says.
At this juncture, the only way the Zions-First Security deal could be scotched is if shareholders vote it down.
That is entirely possible now. In a statement yesterday, Zions said its investment banker "is no longer able to
conclude that the exchange ratio in the merger with First Security is fair, from a financial point of view, to the
Zions shareholders."

--- Page 151 ---
Page 151 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
First Security said it believed there was no basis for the withdrawal of the fairness opinion. It urged Zions to
take the steps necessary to complete the merger. "We believe that Zions is obligated to honor its
commitments under our merger agreement and take all appropriate steps to consummate the transaction,"
Spencer F. Eccles, chairman and chief executive of First Security, said in a statement.
A Goldman spokeswoman declined to comment.
If the merger goes through, Zions shareholders would receive one share of the new company for each share
owned; First Security shareholders would get 0.442 share for each of their shares.
The move by Goldman is highly unusual, but investment bankers may be under more pressure to walk away
from poor deals. While investment banks are legally indemnified from lawsuits over fairness opinions, their
reputations are very much on the line.
Investment banks don't want to be tainted from blessing bad deals, and Goldman itself has come under some
scrutiny for the fairness opinion it wrote for Mattel Inc.'s troubled purchase of Learning Co. for $3.5 billion last
year.
Mattel received a fairness opinion from Goldman that the transaction was fair, even though Learning Co.
restated earnings between announcement and closing. Learning Co. losses sunk Mattel's stock and
contributed to the recent departure of Jill Barad as Mattel's chairman and CEO.
Deal makers include the ability to terminate a merger -- or at least explore other options -- if one side or the
other receives a "superior" proposal.
But this is different. Under state law in Delaware, where First Security is incorporated, boards must inform
shareholders of a change in recommendation by investment bankers even if companies have agreed they
can't terminate deals because of a withdrawn fairness opinion.
There is tension between those two issues, lawyers and scholars say. "A contract that bars boards from
giving an honest but negative recommendation isn't enforceable," says Jeff Gordon, a professor of law at
Columbia Law School in New York. "Advising a board on this case would be very tricky."
Zions wouldn't comment, but its board may be forced into telling shareholders that the board advises
shareholders to vote in favor of the deal because the board is contractually obligated to do so -- not
necessarily because it is a great deal.
"Shareholders need to depend upon the board to assess the changes -- does it still make a deal a good
deal," Mr. Gordon said. "It seems to me the board has a nonwaivable fiduciary duty to give shareholders the
advantage of its best judgement about whether the deal makes sense."
The likely outcome: Either the two sides renegotiate the terms, or Zions will have a hard time getting
shareholder approval.
Yesterday, Zions said it would send supplemental information out to shareholders, and the shareholder vote
would be postponed until March 31. If shareholders end up voting the current deal down, First Security and
Zions may end up in court rather than in each other's arms.

Some Canadian BirdsAre Being Wiped OffThe Face of Currency---Bank Notes Undergo RedesignTo Better Prevent Forgery;Coin's `Loonie' Will ...
Some Canadian Birds Are Being Wiped Off The Face of Currency --- Bank Notes Undergo Redesign To
Better Prevent Forgery; Coin's `Loonie' Will Stay
390 words
13 March 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
B19J
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
OTTAWA (Reuters) -- Some of Canada's best-known birds are set to become extinct -- on the country's bank
notes, at least.
The loon, beloved denizen of Canada's lakes, will keep its safe nesting place on the back of the dollar coin,
commonly known as the "loonie," said an official at the Royal Canadian Mint. But on the nation's bank notes
the belted kingfisher, osprey, common loon, snowy owl and Canada goose will soon be gone, victims of a
redesign of the country's colorful $5, $10, $20, $50 and $100 bills, the Bank of Canada said.
Canada's bills have been bedecked with birds since 1986. They are being redesigned to better prevent
counterfeiting, with the new series set for release in 2001, the central bank said.
"The new design itself is still being worked on and we're not, for security reasons, in a position to announce a
particular content of the design until much closer to 2001," Bank of Canada currency educator Bill Cook said.
He said the bank was polling Canadians about their views on what should be pictured on the back of the bills,
which previously have portrayed allegorical figures, landscapes and industries, as well as birds in the current
series.
Depictions of famous Canadians are an option, Mr. Cook said. The front of the blue, purple, green, red and
brown bills currently depicts stylized numerals as well as a portrait of either Queen Elizabeth or former
Canadian prime ministers.
Mr. Cook declined to say whether the portraits, which were also on the previous series of notes, will be kept,
but he noted that portraits are found on almost every nation's banknotes because they are engraved and thus
difficult to counterfeit.
The central bank reportedly tested the popularity of the removal of the Queen's portrait from the bills through
polling, and found opinion evenly split -- so the Queen will stay on at least one of the notes.
A spokesman for the Royal Canadian Mint, which designs and produces Canada's coin currency, said there
are no plans to shoo the loon from the trademark gold-colored dollar coin.
"We're not removing the loon," Pierre Morin said.

Despite Jittery Market, Gold Isn't Shining
Commodities
Despite Jittery Market, Gold Isn't Shining
By Peter A. McKay
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,086 words
8 March 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
By most measures, gold ought to be golden again.
Demand for the metal surged 21% last year, economists are worried about rising inflation, and other
commodity prices -- especially oil -- are increasing, all of which have been bullish signs for gold in the past.
Volatile stock markets such as the current one have also historically sent some investors to the haven of gold.
But after a run-up to above $300 an ounce a few weeks ago that had many analysts predicting gold finally
had recovered from its long slump, gold prices have fallen and stalled near $290 an ounce.
Why isn't this a golden age? Two reasons emerge, one having to do with fundamentals, the other with
perception. Concerns that there is too much supply in the gold market has hamstrung prices. Meanwhile,
psychologically, investors just have been finding other places to put their money, shying away from gold as a
haven.
Since the summer, when prices surged from 20-year lows caused by oversupply worries, especially the Bank
of England's plan to dump a large amount of gold on the market, the metal has been boosted several times
by one-time events. But invariably, gold has then given back most of the gains.
As usual in the gold market, some conspiracy theories, especially of price manipulation by governments and
gold borrowers, are gaining popularity among a portion of investors to explain the moribund gold price.
Many industry analysts and traders, on the other hand, explain the price stagnation as simply further
evidence of the metal's ever-weakening role as a refuge from inflation.
"Basically, we're right back where we started," says George Gero, a veteran gold trader at Prudential
Securities Inc., New York. "There's been some good news [for prices], but we still don't have all the
ingredients in place to call this a true bull market" for gold.
That much is clear. The latest rally began Feb. 4, after Canadian mining firm Placer Dome announced that it
would halt its gold hedging program, and then several other major producers followed. That meant there
would be less metal on the market, since gold producers' hedging usually involves selling the commodity
"forward" to lock in the current price in case it takes a downturn later.
Gold futures immediately jumped $22.62 to $310 an ounce. But prices have steadily inched down since,
closing at $293.70 yesterday on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange, up $4.30 for the
day, but down $19 from the peak so far this year of $312.70, on Feb. 7.
An even more extreme "false top" occurred in September, when 15 European government banks announced
they would cap their gold sales. The metal leapt $42.25, or 15%, climbing to $324.50 in the next few weeks.
No one is expecting the metal to fall back to the $250 levels seen this summer, although some analysts have
been forecasting the gold price to increase since at least the European banks' announcement.
The metal is "hitting ascending bottoms right now," said gold-fund manager Harry Bingham, of Van Eck
Global in New York. "What it's going to take to really bring up gold is time and a fundamental perception
change among investors. Everybody can't get rich by buying stocks, retiring and becoming a day trader."
So far, though, just the opposite conclusion has been built into gold prices, much to the chagrin of bullish
gold bugs.

--- Page 155 ---
Page 155 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Traders and analysts mostly shrugged off last month's hedging cutoffs as an industry-specific phenomenon,
and then took heart in the dollar's strength overseas and the Federal Reserve's recent interest-rate increases
as ample protection against inflation.
The conspiracy theorists are seizing on the timing of gold's plateaus as evidence of foul play in the market.
Such theorists are given a bit of credence in the gold market, which is less transparent than the stock market.
"Historically, people would tell you that any time gold is trading at a price less than 12-to-1 to the price of oil,
it's a bargain," says Bill Murphy, chairman of the Gold Anti-Trust Action Committee, an investor group that
communicates online and gets some funding from gold companies. "Right now we're at less than 10-to-1,
and no one's saying anything" about the phenomenon.
Mr. Murphy's organization has been lobbying the federal government to investigate whether major gold
borrowers, including some gold companies, are manipulating the metal's price so they can use leased gold
to make other investments, then repay their loans with cheap metal later.
Most Wall Street gold-industry analysts don't buy into that theory outright, although many say the metal's
fundamentals are more positive than its current price might suggest.
World gold demand increased 21% to a record 3,278.4 tons in 1999, according to a recent report by the
World Gold Council trade group.
And World Gold Council analyst George Milling-Stanley says the industry organization doesn't expect any
major production increases in the near future, which would indicate that gold's price should eventually
increase.
However, he said recent episodes of producer hedging and central-bank sales cannot entirely explain the
latest trends in the metal's price. Rather, he said gold's very identity is undergoing a seismic shift perhaps not
seen since the 1970s when the U.S. stopped pegging the dollar to gold and began allowing private
ownership of it.
Mr. Milling-Stanley said the metal's value now is more affected by speculative trading, increasingly complex
hedging programs and foreign-exchange rates in the newly global, electronic economy.
He characterized the fall announcement by the central banks as a step toward eliminating just one specter
hanging over the gold market, albeit an important one.
"You have to look at this as a whole set of circumstances," Mr. Milling-Stanley said. "The market had believed
that there was going to be a major series of central-bank gold sales, but with that fear eliminated by the
September agreement, gold is no longer a one-way bet."
In other commodity trading:
SOYBEANS: Futures slipped at the Chicago Board of Trade, in a correction to early buying by speculative
commodity funds, market watchers said. The May contract fell 5.75 cents to $5.115 a bushel.

P&G's Drop Brings Blue ChipsWith It, Including Coke, Gillette
Abreast of the Market
P&G's Drop Brings Blue Chips With It, Including Coke, Gillette
By Robert O'Brien
Dow Jones Newswires
1,073 words
8 March 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Blue-chip stocks veered sharply lower, dragging almost every sector of the market in their tow,
after Procter & Gamble's surprising earnings news further clouded corporate fundamentals.
P&G plunged 27 5/16 to 60 1/8, after the giant maker of soaps, foods and other consumer products surprised
Wall Street with a warning that first-quarter results won't show the kind of expansion Wall Street has been
expecting.
For blue-chip stocks already hamstrung by concerns about interest rates, P&G's announcement served as a
direct hit on an already disabled market.
A host of issues in the consumer sector suffered big declines: Gillette lost 2 7/16 to 32 7/16, Kimberly-Clark
fell 5 5/8 to 45, Coca-Cola dropped 2 3/16 to 46 11/16, and Johnson & Johnson gave up 3 3/8 to 67 1/8. In
each case, those stocks sank to 52-week lows.
"For the two-thirds of the market operating in the so-called Old World economy, profit margins are going to be
under a lot of pressure," said Richard Cripps, market strategist at Legg Mason. Rising profits had been
counted on to serve as the foil for rising rates and help support whatever valuations remained in the sector.
P&G's warning wiped that out.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average finished the session off 374.47, a drop of 3.68%, to 9796.03.
Procter & Gamble contributed 136.20 points to the industrial average's setback, accounting for 36% of the
loss.
The loss would have been worse absent a contribution from Exxon Mobil, which gained 8 1/16 to 81, as
investors bet that production increases by the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries won't be drastic
enough to cut deeply into commodity prices, which themselves continued to trade sharply higher. Crude-oil
prices reached $34.13 a barrel, and market watchers said they remained optimistic about the outlook.
"We're anticipating a soft landing for oil prices," said Eugene Nowak, an oil-sector analyst at ABN Amro, who
highlighted several names in the group, including Amerada Hess, which gained 3 3/4 to 57 3/4; Kerr-McGee,
which rose 4 1/4 to 51 11/16; and Chevron, up 7 3/4 to 81 3/4.
Of course, rising crude prices, helpful to the sector, contributed to inflation worries in the broad market.
Analysts said higher costs for energy and other commodity products, coupled with higher wage costs, are a
source of pressure on profit margins.
Activity picked up dramatically, even over the frenzied levels of recent trading. Trading on the New York
Stock Exchange reached 1.3 billion shares, making it the second-busiest day ever, behind the 1.34 billion
shares of Dec. 17.
Rising oil prices also leveled transportation stocks. Continental Airlines lost 1 9/16 to 32, AMR declined 4 1/2
to 51 1/2, and Delta Air Lines dropped 2 1/8 to 43 5/8. The Dow Jones Transportation Average traded about
as low as it has since the fall of 1998, sinking to 2263.59, down 112.50, or 4.73%.
High-technology stocks attempted to ignore the sell-off in the broad list. "A day like today tends to accentuate
the move away from traditional stocks into these emerging technology names," said Donald Selkin, chief
market strategist at Joseph Gunnar.
Among the select winners, Juniper Networks gained 8 1/16 to 278 11/16, Ariba added 7 1/2 to 324 1/2,
Broadcom increased 7 13/16 to 243, and Siebel Systems rose 9 9/16 to 161, all on Nasdaq. Rambus

(Nasdaq), the chip maker that has posted impressive gains, notched another one, rising 38 51/64 to 367

Baan Raises Cash,
Considers Floating
Aurum Software
Struggling Dutch software company Baan agreed to a debt-for-equity swap that will add $40.9 million to its
balance sheet. But the company, which expects to report a loss for the first quarter of 2000, is still looking for
other ways to bolster its financial condition, including a possible initial public offering for its U.S.-based Aurum
Software unit. Aurum, a supplier of software that helps companies track interactions with customers, is in one
of the hottest sectors in business-management applications. Analysts think it could bring Baan several billion
dollars on the U.S. stock market. "We are looking at how we can leverage the value that Baan has. A lot of
areas might make sense to float. Aurum is one of the most obvious," Baan's chief marketing officer said.
Under the debt-for-equity deal, a limited number of institutional holders of Baan's 4.5% convertible
subordinated bonds agreed to exchange $40.9 million in notes for 6.54 million common shares.
Eurotunnel Sees Light After `99 Profit
Eurotunnel, the Anglo-French channel tunnel operator, reported earnings tripled last year, helped by financial
restructuring, and predicted a full financial recovery two years ahead of schedule. Profit rose to 334 million
euros ($321.1 million) in 1999 from 111 million euros the year before, as debt-service payments declined.
Operating profit surged 20% to 346 million euros, as revenue rose 5.3% to 969 million euros. The company's
chairman said it expects operating revenue to cover debt repayments by 2002, two years sooner than
forecast. He said Eurotunnel hopes to increase ticket sales via the Internet to 30% within two years, from 3%
currently.

MeritaNordbanken said it agreed, as expected, to acquire Denmark's No. 2 bank, Unidanmark, in a merger
that will cement the Swedish-Finnish banking group's position as the Nordic region's largest retail bank. The
transaction, including a special dividend if the deal goes through, values Unidanmark at nearly $4.6 billion.
Nordic Baltic Holding, the new name for MeritaNordbanken, will offer 12.704 shares for each Unidanmark
share. Goldman Sachs Group represented Unidanmark and Lazard Brothers advised MeritaNordbanken.
Trial Begins in Lloyd's of London Case
Lloyd's of London began the fight of its 312-year life as a trial opened over the British insurance operation's
loss of $12.6 billion from 1988 to 1982 that ruined a number of investors, including 30 who committed suicide.
A lawyer for the investors said Lloyd's concealed $6.3 billion in claims for asbestos-related illnesses, while
reserving a tiny fraction of that amount to pay for them. The lawsuit was filed by more than 230 investors,
known as Names, including 24 Americans. It seeks damages of as much as $237 million and poses a
potentially mortal threat to Lloyd's. "These allegations are a rerun of what this dissident group of Names has
been saying in public on and off for over 10 years and has failed to substantiate," Lloyd's Chairman May
Taylor said.

Philippine Food Prices Damp Inflation
Lower food prices damped Philippine inflation in February, as the consumer price index rose 0.3% from the

Japan's new chief banking regulator said he is optimistic that companies with no previous banking

DaimlerChrysler and Mitsubishi Motors are nearing an agreement in which the German auto maker would
take about a 30% stake in Japan's No. 4 auto company, the Japanese business daily Nihon Keizai Shimbun
reported Tuesday. It said the two companies will likely come to an agreement this month. An alliance
between the two companies would create a group larger than Toyota Motor in vehicle sales and place it third
globally behind General Motors and Ford Motor, it said. A spokesman for Mitsubishi Motors said the report
was based on "speculation." DaimlerChrysler officials couldn't be reached immediately for comment.

Japan will achieve considerable positive economic growth in the January-March quarter, although negative
growth in Japan's gross domestic product in the October-December period is expected, the Economic
Planning Agency's chief, Taichi Sakaiya, told Parliament. He reiterated that 1% GDP growth in the fiscal year
starting in April is possible because the government expects an economic recovery led by private demand
after September. However, Japan's average household spending in January fell by a real 3.2% from a year
earlier to 309,437 yen ($2,873), the Management and Coordination Agency reported yesterday.

Unruly Element:Russian ManeuversAre Making PalladiumEver More Precious---They Force Detroit to PaySoaring Prices for a MetalThat's Used in...
Unruly Element: Russian Maneuvers Are Making Palladium Ever More Precious --- They Force Detroit
to Pay Soaring Prices for a Metal That's Used in Most Cars --- Tapping a Mine in Montana
By Gregory L. White
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
2,452 words
6 March 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
DETROIT -- Here's a million-dollar question: How does the global economy connect American car buyers and
Russian bureaucrats?
One person who can answer is Reg Modlin, an emissions specialist at DaimlerChrysler AG's U.S. unit. Mr.
Modlin, like many other auto-industry executives world-wide, has spent the past several weeks fretting about
the soaring price of an obscure, grayish metal called palladium. Many people haven't even heard of it, but for
anyone who wants to make a cleaner car or sport-utility vehicle, the precious metal is a must-have.
Too bad the main source of the stuff is Russia, which has created chaos on the market for the past few years
by holding up deliveries at the start of each year. The 2000 edition of the palladium crisis is the worst yet, and
it has auto makers reaching for the antacid.
Last month, concerns that political infighting in Moscow could choke supplies drove the price of palladium to
nearly $1,000 an ounce, or about 10 times the levels seen in the early 1990s. Although there is less than an
ounce of palladium in most vehicles -- it is used inside the catalytic converter -- that kind of price surge means
palladium suddenly is becoming a bigticket item for auto companies.
"It's something we don't have any control over," Mr. Modlin says.
Palladium's latest price explosion has added about $100 a vehicle to the company's costs, he says. That
adds up to a pile of money for a company that sold 2.6 million cars and trucks in the U.S. last year.
Fierce competition means auto makers can't raise prices to consumers, and "$100 a car is a lot for an
industry where people get a good pat on the back for saving 50 cents a vehicle," Mr. Modlin says. Although
there is now a huge effort under way at many companies to find ways to use less palladium, changing car
designs in that way will take several years.
"We're pretty well stuck with what the market's going to give us right now," Mr. Modlin says.
With the triumph of global capitalism, Cold War concepts such as strategic commodities were supposed to
have gone the way of backyard bomb shelters. But just as the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries
has gotten the world's attention by driving up oil prices when the cartel was supposed to have lost its teeth,
Russia has demonstrated that even an economic cripple still matters.
Car makers brought much of this on themselves. In the mid-1990s, they agreed to accelerate their adoption
of tighter national emission standards as part of a deal to head off separate state-by-state rules, which would
have played havoc with manufacturing and distribution. Palladium looked like the best solution, since it began
cleaning exhaust sooner after starting up than platinum, then the dominant metal in catalytic converters. Plus,
the price for little-used palladium hadn't gone above $200 a ounce in more than a decade, while platinum had
jumped above $400.
"Everything came together and we said, `OK, we're going to bias toward palladium,'" Mr. Modlin says.
"Recently, we're seriously having to look at was that the wisest choice."
Specialists in the industry recognized that as they embraced palladium, demand for the metal would exceed
world production, which is about five million ounces a year and comes almost exclusively from Russia and
South Africa.
The world's biggest source is Norilsk, an ice-entombed former outpost of the Soviet gulag in the Arctic Circle.
Russia has been producing palladium in Norilsk since 1943, when it started going to the Soviet defense
industry and played a small but important role in the defeat of Nazi Germany.

--- Page 162 ---
Page 162 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Even the Soviet Union's military-industrial complex was too small to consume the large quantities of the metal
buried beneath the snow: Only around 10% of production was actually needed, and the rest -- kept in
concentrate form -- went into government stocks. For decades, the stockpiles grew bigger, but no one paid
much attention. World demand for the metal was small, and Russia was interested more in spreading
revolution than in playing the global market.
But in the 1990s, Moscow's fervor shifted to raising cash, and selling palladium was an easy way to do that.
As auto makers looked at their plans for meeting the new emission rules, they figured that would continue.
"I don't think there was much thought that we were heading for a train wreck," says David Andres, who buys
all the exchange-traded metals for General Motors Corp.
The first signs of an oncoming locomotive in the market appeared in early 1997, as the auto industry was
putting the final touches on its proposals for the new emissions rules.
In January that year, Russian exports ceased. Officials said that the export quotas and licenses -- Russian
bureaucrats require both to let the metal out -- were mysteriously hung up in the approval process.
Prices for palladium, as well as platinum, another big Russian export that was held up, jumped. Speculators
pushed prices higher as the Russian export halt dragged into the spring. Palladium peaked at the unheard-of
level of $239 an ounce.
By spring, however, the Russian government had cleared the paperwork and metal flowed out of the country,
taking some of the pressure off. Prices settled around $200 -- just as auto makers were setting plans to put
more palladium into future models to meet the new standards.
That would prove to be a major miscalculation.
Early 1998 provided a repeat of the previous year. Russian supplies dried up as the government held up
approval of the export paperwork. Wrangling between the Communist-controlled parliament and the
government over the budget added to delays, as did a prolonged confirmation battle for the newly appointed
prime minister, Sergei Kiriyenko.
Palladium prices took off as auto makers scrambled to line up supplies and as investors -- who saw the
chance to profit from the auto makers' misfortune -- pushed the market up. Palladium hit a record of $417 in
May 1998 as the shortage deepened. Russian supplies began to flow again only by early summer. In the
second half of the year, cash-starved Moscow made up for lost time, unloading a record 5.8 million ounces of
palladium. With demand still strong, prices settled around $300.
After two rounds of market chaos, alarm bells were ringing at major auto makers. Many were headed for a big
increase in palladium consumption in 2000, as they began to roll out models to meet the tighter national
emission rules. Regulators were turning their focus to explosively popular trucks and SUVs, which had been
subject to more-lenient rules. Palladium turned out to be particularly good at cleaning their exhaust.
"We did not anticipate that Russia would be as unresponsive as they've been as a producer," says GM's Mr.
Andres.
GM set up a team of two dozen engineers, headed by its chief catalytic-converter man, Michael D'Aniello.
Top executives ordered the team to go back over the company's lineup of nearly 100 models and figure out
how to reduce use of the metal and still meet the 2001 standards.
Mr. Andres, in purchasing, was busy lining up supplies of the metal. In 1998, GM, along with Ford Motor Co.
and Japan's Mitsubishi Corp., signed five-year supply deals with the only major palladium producer outside
Russia and South Africa, Stillwater Mining Co. Stillwater announced plans to more than double production to
1.2 million ounces a year by 2001 at its mine in the Beartooth Mountains of southern Montana.
Economics dictates that other producers should boost output to bring the market into balance in this kind of
situation. But geology makes palladium special. In nature, it occurs mostly with other metals, nickel in Russia
and platinum in South Africa, and in both places there is much less palladium per ton of ore than the other
metals. So even if palladium prices take off, big producers won't add to output, since that would mean
flooding the market for their main product.
Other palladium consumers, meanwhile, have been slow to shift to other metals as prices rise. The
electronics industry has been reducing the amount of palladium it uses in capacitors, but has been making far
more of the parts, which go into cell phones, fax machines and other equipment. The other big user is the
dental industry, which shifted to palladium over the past decade or so for bridgework and fillings, instead of
gold.

--- Page 163 ---
Page 163 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Investors also have become increasingly interested in the suddenly wild palladium market. Although they
account for only about 4% of total demand, according to industry estimates, they can fuel major gyrations in
prices.
For any auto makers that missed the point, 1999 brought another palladium crisis. Prices had begun rising in
late 1998, as investors anticipated what was becoming a routine event. Uncertainty about supplies kept
prices up through most of the spring. Only at the end of April did the price briefly fall below $300, apparently
because Russian sellers were dumping the metal ahead of implementation of a 5% excise tax on
precious-metals exports.
The Russian government also gave RAO Norilsk Nickel, the major producer, a 10-year export quota, a
concession meant to stabilize the market by letting the company make long-term deals with Western
consumers. But the custodians of the stockpile -- Gokhran, which is the state treasury, and the Central Bank
-- still had to go through the annual ritual with the permits. The size of the stockpile is a state secret.
The roller-coaster ride on the market was enough for GM to take the unusual step of imposing specific limits
on how much palladium, as well as platinum and the other clean-air metal, rhodium, engineers could use in
their designs. Typically, the engineers worked to cost and performance targets only.
"I'm like the doctor that keeps going to our engineers and saying, `You're eating too much palladium; you've
got to diet,'" Mr. Andres says. Now, he adds, the engineers are working round the clock "on dialing this down
to a reasonable level."
The problem is, most of the changes take time. A lot of the easy improvements already have been made,
according to industry officials. Mr. D'Aniello says GM now is focusing on new models, where there's more
room to change the design of engines and other components to improve emissions performance, reducing
the need for palladium. Redesigning existing models to, say, move the catalytic converter closer to the engine
so it warms up and starts working earlier, can cost hundreds of millions of dollars. "We would drop a vehicle
early rather than do that," Mr. D'Aniello says.
GM officials say they expect this year to be their peak year for consumption of palladium. They have lined up
supplies to make sure they aren't entirely at the mercy of the market, but the company still feels it when prices
surge.
"You bite your lip and say, `I hope palladium doesn't go to the moon until we get this back in balance,'" Mr.
Andres says.
But this year, the market has been heading into orbit.
At the end of 1999, prices rose again as speculators moved into the market in anticipation of yet another
crisis. Adding to the chaos was the surprise resignation of Russian President Boris Yeltsin on New Year's
Eve.
"I'm sitting at home going `Holy s---, here we go again,'" says Mr. Andres.
Fueled by heavy speculation, palladium trading got so frantic that the Tokyo Commodities Exchange, where
most of the futures trading is done, froze the price at about $700 to steady the situation until Russian
shipments resumed.
The hysteria even made it to the small fry on this food chain. Junkyard operators, who have long put catalytic
converters among the first parts they remove from scrap cars, are salivating at the latest price jumps.
"Every day, hundred-dollar jumps, it's just like a casino here," says Ashok Kumar, who runs A-1 Specialized
Services & Supplies Inc. in Croydon, Pa., which buys old converters from scrap yards to sell to recyclers. Mr.
Kumar says he struggles to explain to sellers looking for that day's top dollar that the metal from their old
parts won't actually be ready for re-use for another six months or so, by which time prices are likely to have
fallen.
And although recycling is a major source of platinum for the auto industry, officials say there isn't much
palladium coming in because cars last about 10 years, so models rich in the metal aren't hitting the scrap
yards yet.
That leaves Moscow in the driver's seat. "The thing that scares the hell out of us is that if the physical
availability goes to zero, then vehicle builds will be affected," Mr. D'Aniello says. Translation: no metal, no
cars.
Russian media reported last week that the country's acting president, Vladimir Putin, had signed a decree
giving new quotas for palladium, platinum and rhodium. Prices eased. But the Kremlin declined to comment,

--- Page 164 ---
Page 164 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
and Alexei Druzhinin, the spokesman for the state treasury, said that "there are no official facts on anything.
This is the only fact." He refused to comment on Detroit's complaints that Russia is playing havoc with world
markets.
Yuri Kotlyar, head of Norilsk Nickel, says he has heard nothing and laments the confusion. While his
company has its long-term palladium quota and could benefit in the short term from stratospheric prices, it
worries that the market chaos will drive its best customers to look for ways to avoid palladium.
"Everything that has happened recently on the market, including the agitated price increases, might have a
negative impact," Mr. Kotlyar says. "Consumers will look for substitutes. . . . Our position is that the market is
a market of agreements between producers and consumers, and of mutual efficiency. This market is very
subtle and has a low capacity. Therefore it demands a delicate attitude."
That's a point many are convinced isn't lost on other Russians in the business, either. "They're learning how
to be capitalists pretty fast," Mr. D'Aniello says.
---
Andrew Higgins in Moscow and Peter A. McKay in New York contributed to this article.

ASIA/PACIFIC
Thais Go to Polls
In Landmark Vote
For Parliament
Thais will vote tomorrow in a ground-breaking election under a new constitution supposed to bring them more
democratic, stable and less corrupt government. The poll gives Thais their first opportunity to elect directly the
200 members of the Senate or upper house of Parliament, previously appointed by the prime minister. But
they will have to make their choice of a single candidate each from a bewildering array of 1,522 candidates,
with 264 candidates running in Bangkok alone for its 18 Senate seats.
In the process, they will test the effectiveness of a constitution adopted in 1997 and designed to change the
distribution of political power. "It's a constitution which empowers the nongovernmental sector, the civil
society, as a check on the power and vested interests of the state and the people who exercised state power
in the past," says Anand Panyarachun, a former prime minister and an important member of the assembly
created to draft the charter. Thailand switched from absolute to constitutional monarchy in 1932.
DirecTV to Give Subscribers to Rival
Hughes Electronics, a major U.S. maker of communications satellites, pulled the plug on its ailing Japanese
satellite-TV venture DirecTV. Under an agreement reached on Feb. 25, DirecTV will effectively hand over its
413,000 subscribers to archrival Sky PerfecTV, which is operated by Japan Digital Broadcasting Services.
DirecTV will halt operations by year end, leaving Sky PerfecTV as Japan's sole communications satellite-TV
operator. In Japan, communications-satellite services are separate from broadcast-satellite services.
Nasdaq Japan Hopes for 100 Listings
The Nasdaq Japan stock market, scheduled to be launched in June, hopes to see about 100 companies list
on the exchange in its initial year, the head of Nasdaq Japan Planning said. Nasdaq Japan Planning is a
50-50 joint venture set up by Softbank and the U.S. National Association of Securities Dealers to design the
proposed market. Nasdaq Japan will function as the Osaka Securities Exchange's new section.
Upbeat Outlook for Australia Exports
The prospects for Australia's commodity exports improved sharply in the second half of 1999 and will brighten
further in the next 18 months, the government's Australian Bureau of Agricultural and Resource Economics
said. The outlook for Australia's mineral and energy industries looks much better than a year ago, thanks to a
stronger world economy and a recovery among most of its main trading partners in Asia. But Abare is less
sanguine about what lies ahead for the farm sector, pointing to important local industries struggling to stay
afloat in the face of oversupplied world markets. Continued high oil prices, from which Australia benefits,
could sow the seeds of a future downturn in the global economy.

Foreign Investment
In Mexico for 1999
Totals $11.6 Billion
Foreign direct investment in Mexico totaled $11.57 billion last year, exceeding government estimates, the
Commerce Ministry said. Mexico captured $4.45 billion in new investment, $2.89 billion in reinvestment of
profits, $2.78 billion in fixed assets for export manufacturing plants known as maquiladoras and $1.45 billion
for transfers between companies. The U.S. and Canada accounted for 67% of the foreign direct investment,
followed by the European Union with about 16%.
The ministry expects investment from Europe to increase after a free-trade agreement between the EU and
Mexico takes effect. Commerce Minister Herminio Blanco said the government was still working on a
preliminary estimate of foreign direct investment for 2000, but that the figure will be "very similar" to that for
1999.

Bancolombia, Colombia's largest bank in total assets, plans to expand its Internet services and complete a

plans to launch a virtual credit card and start offering online corporate banking services and virtual investment
accounts for time deposits in coming months. With the moves, the bank hopes to remain Colombia's top
provider of Internet-banking services despite growing competition. Bancolombia posted losses last year of
197.23 billion pesos, the worst among the country's private-sector banks.

attempt to reduce their debenture and to seek fresh capital injection. As of Dec. 31, Luismin's total liabilities
stood at $71.9 million. Luismin is looking at becoming an independent precious-metals producer based in
