Sheik TellezReadies CurbOn Oil Price
Sheik Tellez Readies Curb On Oil Price
By Robert L. Bartley
1,001 words
28 February 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
A40
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
ALAMOS, Mexico -- Luiz Tellez, Mexico's oil minister, packs off to Paris and London this week to commune
with the Arab sheiks in his new role as strongman of the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries.
Officially, of course, Mexico is not even a member of OPEC, but Mr. Tellez has been the power broker behind
the oil cartel's most effective measures in more than two decades.
A savvy MIT economist, Mr. Tellez took over the oil ministry two years ago, when the oil price was faltering
around $10 a barrel. He brokered production restraints to raise the price, touching base with Norway and
combining with Saudi Arabia and Venezuela in March 1998 to issue the "Riyadh agreement," cutting oil
output by more than 5 million barrels a day, or about 7% of output. For the first time since the 1970s, the
production restraints held, combining with a surging world economy to send oil to its present price near $30.
Now Sheik Tellez has decided this price is actually too high.
With the Riyadh restraints set to expire March 31, Mr. Tellez has suddenly been talking of a lower price.
"We're not a petroleum country anymore," he told a Feb. 18 press conference. "Mexico is mainly an industrial
producer and provider of services." That is, too high an oil price would curb demand for Mexico's other
exports. He's also said -- adding that the oil ministers of Saudi Arabia and Venezuela share his view -- that
"Mexico's main concern is market stability."
First impressions to the contrary, even the most powerful cartel has logical reasons to avoid too high a price.
It can choose to sell fewer units at a higher price, or more units at a lower price. OPEC plus Mexico may find
that its current revenue-maximizing price is below $30. Besides, the current price is already producing a
surge in oil exploration around the world, and a lower price would dull these incentives. What the oil
producers most have to fear is a surge of new output they do not control, setting off production wars that will
collapse the price back to $10.
These points are elementary to a Ph.D. in economics, but not always evident to Mexican nationalists.
Especially so since north of the Rio Grande the current oil price has become a political problem for the
Clinton administration, setting off the usual cries for relief from oil-heated New England. Energy Secretary Bill
Richardson has been touring oil ministries to jawbone for a lower price; he visited Mr. Tellez last week.
Cartoons in the Mexican press have depicted Mr. Tellez as a puppet of the U.S.
What's more, Mexico's presidential campaign is just now moving into high gear, with the election scheduled
July 2. Cuauhtemoc Cardenas, the principal candidate of the left, has denounced a cut in the oil price as
"absurd, idiotic and contrary to our national interest." Mr. Cardenas is the son of former President Lazaro
Cardenas, who nationalized the oil industry in 1938. He seems to be running a distant third in this campaign,
however, to Francisco Labastida, the candidate of the incumbent party, and Vicente Fox of the center-right
opposition.
Both of the leading candidates can be counted as modernizers. Indeed, in his previous tenure as under
secretary of agriculture, Mr. Tellez led groundbreaking reform of the ejido -- the Aztec communal land tenure
system that, like oil, has been an untouchable icon of Mexican nationalism. Gathered at an annual confab in
this remote village of restored silver-mining mansions, Mexican and gringo free-marketeers are bemused that
he has emerged in the forefront of the world's most famous cartel. But he does face certain imperatives.
Clearly the Mexican economy is no longer an oil economy. Oil now accounts for merely 7% of its exports,
down from 78% in 1982. Yet the government depends on PEMEX, the national oil company, for a third or so
of its revenues. The bonanza of revenue resulting from Mr. Tellez's agreements has smoothed the final two
years of the administration of President Ernesto Zedillo. A collapse in the price would spell serious difficulties
for any incoming government.
---

--- Page 172 ---
Page 172 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
As the most important resource base for the world economy, oil has long been a singular commodity. In
particular, there has always been a counterpoint between oil prices and monetary policy. When the U.S.
broke up the Bretton Woods monetary system by closing the gold window in 1971, an OPEC communique
quickly labeled it a de facto devaluation of the dollar, in which oil is priced. The 1973 oil boycott redeemed
OPEC's threat to recapture this loss, and went further to capture the lion's share of gains from an inflation
caused by an explosion of world monetary reserves associated with the Bretton Woods collapse.
Fed Chairman Paul Volcker collapsed the oil price and broke OPEC's power with tight money in 1981-82. The
cartel found its pricing power limited because oil price increases would produce a monetary reaction, slow the
world economy and implode the demand for oil. Today, with oil at $30, we find Alan Greenspan raising U.S.
interest rates even though there is no other sign of inflation.
When the Bretton Woods system officially pegged gold at $35 an ounce, the price of oil was set by the Texas
Railroad Commission, which stabilized the oil price by raising or limiting "liftings" from Texas fields. That is, it
did precisely what Mr. Tellez is trying to do this week; he, the Saudis and Mr. Richardson are bargaining
about the price. Mr. Tellez has mentioned a price of $25, though he has also noted that the Mexican budget is
based on an average price of $16. In 1965, the Texas Railroad Commission had oil at $2.75, which in current
dollars is just shy of $15 a barrel.
---
Mr. Bartley is editor of the Journal.

Gimme Shelter---Shelter vs. Shelter:401(k) plans; IRAs; Roths; They all shield retirement money from taxes;But they aren't all the same
Personal Finance (A Special Report)
Gimme Shelter --- Shelter vs. Shelter: 401(k) plans; IRAs; Roths; They all shield retirement money
from taxes; But they aren't all the same
By Jeff D. Opdyke
2,155 words
28 February 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
R14
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
There are more options than ever for sheltering your retirement cash from the tax man. How do you pick the
one that works for you?
If you're like Laureen Delaney, a 38-year-old advertising rep in Waltham, Mass., your retirement nest egg is
spread across an abundance of accounts: a company-sponsored 401(k) plan, a rollover individual retirement
account from a previous job or, maybe, a traditional IRA you've had for years. You might even have one of
the new Roth IRAs. In short, you have a fistful of money in a gaggle of retirement accounts.
But have you ever stopped to think how that money is invested?
Sure, these accounts all offer certain tax advantages that have made them immensely popular and a fixture
on the retirement scene. But the advantages, and the structure of the accounts themselves, differ slightly.
Thus, to build the biggest buck while avoiding the most taxing bang, financial planners say, investors should
step back and take stock of how they've allocated assets for their gray years.
"I see people with accounts all over the place, and they're trying to figure out what goes where and if they're
properly allocated," says Michael Rubin, vice president of the tax and financial-planning department at New
York's Bessemer Trust Co. Mr. Rubin knows the situation well; he's a member of the gang, with a
profit-sharing plan, a Roth IRA, a rollover IRA and a traditional IRA. "I've got them all."
Lucky for him, he deals with this for a living. But for people like Ms. Delaney, the landscape can be a "a
maze," she says. "I didn't have a concept that there are special objectives investing in these things."
There are. So, here's a simple guide to retirement-account asset allocation, from the ins and outs of the
various plans to what those features mean for managing your money.
---
What Do The Plans Offer?
401(k) Plans
These provide tax-deferred investing and have the benefit of lowering your taxable salary by as much as
$10,500 a year, the maximum contribution for 2000. Once you begin withdrawals, the money -- both
contributions and profits -- is taxed as ordinary income based on your tax bracket.
But these plans typically are limited in the investment choices they offer -- usually a few mutual funds from a
single fund family, and often the employer's stock.
Rollover and Traditional IRAs
These provide tax-deferred investing, like 401(k)s, but offer broader investment choices, including the
universe of mutual funds and individual securities. Rollover IRAs are for money transferred in from a 401(k) or
other employer-sponsored plan when you retire or change jobs. Traditional IRAs allow contributions of as
much as $2,000 a year per person, or $4,000 per couple as long as there's one working spouse, and may be
tax-deductible for the year you make the contribution, depending on whether you -- or, if married, you and
your spouse -- are covered by a retirement plan at work and, if so, your income. Withdrawals -- both any
principal for which you take a deduction, and all investment income -- are taxed as ordinary income.
Roth IRAs

--- Page 174 ---
Page 174 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
These relatively new creations allow no tax deductions on the way in, but the principal and profits compound
tax free, meaning "what you see on your statement is exactly the money you have to play with when you start
pulling it out," says Michael Chasnoff, president of Advanced Capital Strategies Inc., a Cincinnati
financial-planning firm.
Like their traditional and rollover cousins, Roths are limited to $2,000 a year and $4,000 per couple with at
least one working spouse, and are open to a world of investment options. Eligibility to contribute is governed
by a wage test: Single people earning $95,000 a year begin losing access to Roths, and are completely cut
off at $110,000. For married people, the phaseout range is $150,000 to $160,000.
---
What Should Go in Each Account?
401(k) Plans
Since these accounts typically are limited in their choices, financial planners such as Gregory Fenton,
principal at Cambridge Cape Cod Advisors in Sandwich, Mass., usually start their decision making here,
picking the one or two best choices available. That usually means U.S. equity growth or income funds and,
maybe, a broad-based international fund. If so, Mr. Chasnoff says, consider a 75/25 split, with the bias on the
domestic fund. That gives you large exposure to U.S. growth stocks, which have performed well historically,
and a small dollop of exposure to growth outside U.S. borders.
In recently reviewing Ms. Delaney's 401(k) account, Mr. Fenton, her adviser, moved her into a couple of T.
Rowe Price funds available in her plan, including the Stable Value fund and New Horizons. Prior to that, she
had 100% of her 401(k) in a Standard & Poor's 500 index fund, which Mr. Fenton considered a "bad idea"
because index funds, for the most part, tend to be tax-efficient to begin with.
Others argue that index funds "are fine for IRAs," says Jeffrey N. Mehler, an adviser in Centerbrook, Conn.,
who routinely earmarks part of his clients' IRA money for index investments. "I'm of the mindset," he says,
"that you can't beat the market, you can only hope to do as well as the market."
For those investing in company stock, "limit your exposure to no more than 25%," says Mr. Fenton. "People
overinflate the value of their employer. If the stock crashes, you don't want it to destroy your retirement
savings."
Rollover and Traditional IRAs
These are core accounts, so use the money "to complement what you've done in the 401(k)," says Peggy
Ruhlin, a principal at Budros & Ruhlin Inc., a financial-planning and investment-management firm in
Columbus, Ohio. Look to own mutual funds run by strong managers with long track records that you don't
have access to in your 401(k).
If, for example, your 401(k) is partly in the Templeton Fund, a widely available 401(k) offering and a solid,
large-cap international value manager, "you might want to expand your exposure with a fund like Artisan
International, which has more of a growth orientation, but is not as aggressive" as emerging-country funds,
says Mr. Chasnoff.
If you're going to own individual equities in this account, "own blue chips" you're going to hold or companies
whose "product you like and use," says Mr. Rubin, the trust-fund adviser. In his IRA: Seattle coffee vendor
Starbucks Corp., bought several years ago.
Roth IRAs
This, planners universally agree, should be home to your most aggressive assets -- shares of the next
Microsoft, sector funds or emerging-market funds, for instance. The rationale: The Roth "is not taxed in the
end, so you want the most growth you can possibly get to happen in this account," says Steven Enright,
president of Enright Financial Advisors Inc., in River Vale, N.J.
The Roth also is the best place to temper the otherwise encouraged buy-and-hold strategy, for those who are
so inclined. If you're confident in your or your adviser's stock-picking skills, this is the account where you
might own shares of glamour stocks, such as Internet or tech issues, or small-cap companies primed to soar.
Mr. Rubin has his Roth invested in souped-up funds such as Janus Global Technology, up about 180% in
1999, while Ms. Delaney owns some of the Street's favorite tech belles: Cisco Systems Inc., Sun
Microsystems Inc. and 3Com Corp.
---

--- Page 175 ---
Page 175 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
What About Other Investments?
Well, if you're buying taxable bonds, such as Treasurys or corporate obligations, "you want those in your
traditional or rollover IRA," Mr. Enright says, "because you want to shelter the income from taxes while it
grows." Municipal bonds, meanwhile, should go into a standard taxable brokerage account since they're
already kicking off tax-free income.
Likewise for annuities, which are structured as tax-free investments to begin with. Many people these days
are buying variable annuities in their tax-deferred accounts, "and that's just ridiculous," says Steven Norwitz,
a vice president at Baltimore fund family T. Rowe Price. "You're buying a tax shelter within a tax shelter, and
that's not very efficient."
That's what Marge and Charles Valentino, a husband and wife team in Clinton, Conn., faced. She's a
49-year-old child-care administrator for a nonprofit organization; he's a 52-year-old state social worker. They
moved their 403(b) accounts -- essentially a 401(k) for government and nonprofit workers -- into a rollover
IRA, loaded with variable annuities.
Their adviser, Mr. Mehler, has been moving them out of those investments and into diversified international
equity funds such as Artisan, a Vanguard intermediate bond fund and, to a small degree, the Scudder Gold
fund, "just as an inflation hedge," Mr. Mehler says.
Funds that track the S&P 500 or some other broad stock index can be an excellent choice in a taxable
investment account. As Ms. Delaney learned from her adviser, Mr. Fenton, turnover in those funds is limited,
so they're going to kick off minimal taxable income. The same holds true for funds such as Vanguard's
Tax-Managed Capital Appreciation fund, designed to limit the government's tax bite.
And don't overlook one clear advantage of taxable accounts. If you sell an investment at a loss, you can use
that loss to offset gains realized from the sale of other investments. Losses that exceed gains can be used to
reduce taxes by offsetting up to $3,000 of wages and other ordinary income.
---
What If You're Already Retired, Or Close to It?
Individual securities should be in a taxable brokerage account, says Christine Fahlund, a senior financial
planner at T. Rowe Price. When those securities are passed to heirs, they're subject to "step-up" rules,
meaning the cost basis (the cost used as a basis for calculating capital gains) is adjusted to current market
values. That, for instance, would allow heirs to sell stock in a taxable account immediately, if they wanted,
and escape taxes on accumulated capital gains. In an IRA, the cost basis remains at its original level and
capital gains are taxed at the heirs' ordinary rate.
For retirees already taking minimum required distributions from a traditional IRA, Ms. Fahlund recommends
keeping two years' equivalent of distributions in fixed-income instruments such as a short-term bond fund to
"cushion the impact of any downturn in the stock market that might occur." Intermediate-term money, she
says, is best funneled into equity-income and intermediate bond funds, where it can grow.
Meanwhile, Ms. Fahlund says, higher-risk investments "that you need only as a last resort" should be in a
Roth IRA. That way, heirs inherit a tax-free pot of money from which they can withdraw money tax-free over
the course of their lives. (A note for heirs: Do not take a lump sum, Ms. Fahlund urges -- you'll have to park it
in a taxable account. In the Roth, "it continues to grow tax-free for the rest of your life.")
If you're approaching retirement and you have a 401(k) loaded with company stock, Ms. Fahlund says,
consider rolling your 401(k) into a rollover IRA, and in the process moving your company stock into a
traditional brokerage account.
This maneuver, so long as it's a lump-sum distribution, is legal and generally doesn't trigger any taxes typical
when you move assets into a taxable account from a tax-deferred one. You do have to pay taxes at ordinary
income rates on the stock's original cost basis, but that is likely substantially less than its current value. And
you or your heirs ultimately have to pay capital-gains taxes when you sell the stock, but, because the shares
are subject to the step-up rule, the taxes are based only on the appreciation prior to moving the stock into the
brokerage account.
For example: If you have one share of stock, with a cost basis of $1 and valued at $20 when you move into a
taxable account, on the move you're taxed on the $1 cost. When you sell or your heirs inherit the share, say
at $30, the capital-gains are levied on the original $19 gain.
"It's a darn good deal," Ms. Fahlund says.

---
Mr. Opdyke is a staff reporter in The Wall Street Journal's New York bureau.

False Negatives:Signs of a Market TopCome and Go; the BullMostly Just Pushes On---Low Dividends, Hot IPOs,Ominous Chart PatternsHaven't...
False Negatives: Signs of a Market Top Come and Go; the Bull Mostly Just Pushes On --- Low
Dividends, Hot IPOs, Ominous Chart Patterns Haven't Put Off Investors --- But What of the Latest Dip?
By E.S. Browning
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
2,608 words
25 February 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
The roaring Nasdaq Composite Index scored its single biggest one-day point gain ever on Wednesday,
reaching an all-time high. Then it set another record yesterday. This in the face of sliding industrial stocks and
the Federal Reserve's clear resolve to raise interest rates and slow the nation's economy. It has earmarks of
the final blowout that often marks a stock-market top, doesn't it?
Maybe, and maybe not.
In case you've forgotten, pundits have sighted the market peak many times over the past nine-year stretch.
"Classic sign of a top" is the phrase often used. But that has been the same stretch that saw the Dow Jones
Industrial Average pass through the 4000 mark, then 5000 and on through 11000, while the Nasdaq
Composite soared through 600, 1000, 2000 and -- well, you get the picture.
There was the end of 1992, when stocks' average dividend yield dipped below 3%. It meant stocks were
clearly too pricey and must be near a peak, some experts said.
Ditto for the 143% leap in the price of an initial public offering, Boston Chicken, on Nov. 9, 1993. Such an IPO
frenzy had to mean the market had gotten "frothy." Then came the craze for Iomega, a littleknown company
that soared 3,600% in the 14 months through May 1996, buoyed by eager small investors with scant
knowledge of the market.
Next thing you knew, your cabbie and your hairdresser were recounting how they had poured their savings
into stocks they heard about on the Internet -- another worrisome symptom. Depending on which of those
classic signs of a market top prompted you to sell your shares, you either missed a little of the market run-up
or you really, really missed it.
Now, the industrial average is flirting again with the psychologically important 10000 level -- but going the
wrong way. It traded below 10000 yesterday before closing at 10092.63, down more than 133. Market experts
are asking themselves again: Is the bull market finally over?
It could be. The bull market is old, and it has to end sometime. Indeed, some analysts maintain that, by
certain measures, we are in a bear market already. Stalwarts such as Coca-Cola and Merck are down more
than 20% from their highs. The majority of stocks are down since the start of this year, in fact. It is a narrow
group of highfliers, mainly technology companies, that is fueling the Nasdaq Composite's surge and keeping
the other indexes from going any lower.
With the industrial average now 13.9% below its Jan. 14 summit of 11722.98, "I think the market is going to
get its critical test right now, and I think it will flunk," says portfolio manager Michael Metz at Oppenheimer
Investment Advisors, who, like Fed Chairman Alan Greenspan, worries about rampant borrowing to buy
stocks. Mr. Metz could well be right. But before you sell everything, keep in mind that he also saw a risk in
1997, warning that "something as serious as what happened in 1987 could happen again."
Just how tough this long bull market has been to call is painfully clear from a random walk, so to speak, down
Wall Street's memory lane:
Dying Dividends.
One of investing's cardinal rules was breached as far back as the end of 1992, when the 1990s bull market
was just over two years old. The rule: A sound market pays sound dividends. Back in 1982, the stocks in the
Standard & Poor's 500-stock index were paying dividends that totaled close to 7% of the companies' stock
prices. By late 1992, this so-called dividend yield was below 3% of stock value -- a sure sign stocks were
getting overvalued.

--- Page 178 ---
Page 178 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Within a year, a newsletter called Hussman Econometrics began forecasting a "market peak," its author
explaining that "with the S&P 500 dividend yield near a historically low 2.7%, we are no longer willing to
speculate on a significant advance in stocks."
He wasn't, but others were.
An Amazing IPO.
As some investors were lamenting the death of the dividend, others were celebrating the birth of a new fad,
rotisserie cooking; 1993 was the year when chicken moved the market. On Nov. 9, 1993, a barely profitable
restaurant chain called Boston Chicken went public and saw its stock post the biggest openingday jump for a
new issue in seven years.
"The IPO calendar is overheating," fretted a Lehman Brothers analyst. Skeptics saw a clear sign of excess.
They weren't altogether wrong: The chicken company itself eventually got plucked, sagging in value and
finally agreeing to sell its main assets to McDonald's.
But other stocks barreled ahead.
Technical Tops.
To many technical analysts, whose arcane craft involves mapping market trends, the bull market was starting
to look old by the latter part of 1993. Technical analysts use a unique jargon, finding in stock charts such
shapes as "domes" or a "head and shoulders" or the ominous "double top."
In October 1993, analyst P.Q. Wall opined that "the meeting of bowl and dome just here shows we are at the
climax of the bull market right now. We expect three crashes, not one." Robert Prechter, who tracks
something called the Elliott Wave, said in 1993 that "selling at this peak may turn out to be a feat equivalent
to buying at the 1932 low."
The market did have a flat year in 1994, as the Fed raised interest rates, but it never came near a crash and
soon was on its way higher again. In the years since, various technical analysts, using various disciplines,
have seen other signs that the market finally was about to crack.
The Story Stocks.
Certain stocks for which an intriguing argument could be made -- called story stocks -- began responding
largely to chat-room comment and newsletter hype. Beginning in 1994, companies such as Presstek, Iomega
and Diana rose tenfold over a period of months, far outpacing rational expectations of earnings growth. All
wound up plummeting, but they were replaced in investors' hearts by new story stocks with their own
chat-room boosters, in fields from software to genetics to fiber optics.
Richly valued stocks left conservative money managers at a loss. "There's very little that is compelling at all.
That's why we think we are so close to a market top," lamented money manager Eric Ryback in the spring of
1997. He and others didn't sufficiently appreciate that many investors simply wanted to own what was hot,
regardless of traditional valuations.
More Amazing IPOs.
Even before Mr. Ryback's remark, and well before Iomega and Presstek peaked, another wave of IPOs
began hitting the market, dazzling investors and provoking still more talk of wretched excess. Many had no
profits at all. Netscape, a poorly understood company that helped people get access to a techno-fad called
the Internet, more than doubled in value on Aug. 9, 1995, its first day of trading. The next year, Yahoo! outdid
Netscape, rising 154% on its first day.
Froth and excess, said the experts. "It's certainly not the sign of a market bottom," commented Salomon
Brothers market strategist David Shulman, after Netscape went public. Actually, it was something of a bottom.
The market never looked back.
Dying Dividends II.
By the end of 1996, the dividend yield wasn't only under 3% but below
2%, and still falling. But after years of worrying about this trend,
analysts were shifting their focus. Some began explaining that a low
average dividend yield no longer was troublesome because so many good
companies didn't pay dividends at all, using their cash instead to buy
back stock or invest in new technologies.
The Need for a Pause.

--- Page 179 ---
Page 179 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Since the launch of the Dow Jones Industrial Average in 1896, it rarely had posted annual gains of as much
as 20% in two consecutive years, and never for three straight. So when the industrials and the S&P 500 both
gained 20% in 1995 and again in 1996, the experts said the market was overdue to "take a rest." Instead, the
indexes gained more than 20% each for a third year, in 1997. When both indexes then posted double-digit
gains in 1998 -- and again in 1999 -- a lot of the experts turned their attention elsewhere.
John Bogle, then chairman of the Vanguard Group of mutual funds, thought the industrial average must have
peaked late in 1995, when it crossed 5000. He thought the same when it crossed 6000 in 1996. After that, he
just laughed at the question.
Earnings.
Perhaps the longest-used indicator of market health is simply corporate earnings. One of the oldest rules of
thumb holds that stock prices shouldn't trade much above 20 times the past year's earnings.
Number-crunchers such as Richard Bernstein of Merrill Lynch throw inflation into the equation: If inflation is,
say, 2%, stocks can trade as high as 18 times earnings, for a total of 20. If the total gets above 23, the market
is looking toppy.
With the P/E of the S&P 500 at 15 in 1995 -- roughly the historical average -- Mr. Bernstein was a bull. But in
1997, the P/E hit 24. In 1998, it crossed 25. Mr. Bernstein became a bear in the summer of 1998. His other
indicators also were over the top. One of them -- which signals excess euphoria when too many other
strategists are bullish -- flashed red in 1998.
Mr. Bernstein looked brilliant when stocks plunged after the Russian debt crisis hit. But he didn't put his bull
clothes back on, even though the market resumed its climb in the fall of 1998.
Why weren't high P/Es a problem? Some analysts now say stocks can tolerate higher P/Es than usual
because inflation is so tame. Others point out that many investors are so eager to own hot tech stocks that
they don't care if the stocks' P/E ratios are 100 or more -- or infinity, for that matter, if there are no earnings.
Still More Amazing IPOs.
Netscape and Boston Chicken, stand aside. In November 1998, an Internet company called theglobe.com
went public and soared 606% on its first day of trading. If this wasn't a sign of a giddy market top, nothing
was, pundits said. "This is another example of market mania similar to tulip-mania or the South Sea Bubble,"
warned John Schott, a money manager who specializes in market psychology, referring to legendary periods
of excess from the 17th and 18th centuries.
Theglobe eventually fell back to earth. The market didn't. It produced another amazing IPO, that of software
company VA Linux, which leapt almost 700% on its first day. It, too, later gave up much of this gain -- but
without dimming tech investors' appetite.
Online Trading.
The rage for online day-trading, with ordinary people imitating the pros by moving repeatedly in and out of
stocks, seemed yet another sign of trouble. But even after a disconsolate day-trader took a gun and shot
co-workers one July day in Atlanta, day-trading continued, and so did the bull market.
The Press.
Consider Wall Street's version of the Sports Illustrated effect. Just as appearing on the magazine's cover
supposedly kills an athlete's winning streak, big stories about the bull market are supposed to jinx it. They
haven't. But what about advertisements? As long ago as 1993, money manager and newsletter writer Martin
Zweig tracked bullish ads in Barron's as a sign of "excessive speculation." That year, he warned that "the
current score of 22 is double the warning level."
But if anything, the press has been overly cautious about this market. Articles in Forbes and, most notably, in
Barron's have repeatedly cited signs of a market top. One headline in The Wall Street Journal in 1995 read,
"Stocks Cross 4000 for First Time, but Visit There May Be Brief."
Shoe Shines and Taxis.
Legend has it that when a renowned Wall Street investor -- some say it was Bernard Baruch -- got a stock tip
from the guy who shined his shoes in the 1920s, he knew it was time to sell. The idea is that if everybody is
already bullish and in the market, there isn't that much money left to put in to drive stocks higher. But in the
current market, shoe-shiners and taxi drivers have been talking about stocks for years, with no apparent
damage.

--- Page 180 ---
Page 180 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Some pros now think heavy participation by ordinary people -- who aren't spooked by alleged signs of a top --
helps explain why this market has defied the experts. Money manager Steve Leuthold in Minneapolis is no
bull; he long has warned that highfliers and the major indexes will have to fall someday as much as 50% to
get back to where they belong. But "as long as public confidence remains strong," he says, "this market can
continue on no matter what happens with interest rates or the Fed or anything like that, because Main Street
is really what is fueling this market."
At its January high, the industrial average was up 390% since late 1990. That isn't as much as the 497% the
industrials gained, according to Ned Davis Research, before the 1929 crash, when they were the hot index
with the sought-after stocks. But the hot index of this decade is the techheavy Nasdaq Composite, and it is up
more than 1,200% since 1990, surpassing anything that happened in the 1920s.
Careers have been rerouted by the current bull market. Respected experts such as Mr. Metz and Mr.
Shulman quit trying to be market strategists and focused on picking stocks or managing money. Longtime
bear Charles Clough left Merrill Lynch last year after infuriating brokers by advising investors to buy bonds
instead of stocks. Then again, Mr. Greenspan's job is safe despite his now-famous comment about "irrational
exuberance" in December 1996.
Maybe the bears will be proved right. The Nasdaq Composite has turned especially volatile. Eight of the 10
biggest point gains it has ever logged have come this year, as have six of its 10 biggest point losses,
although in percentage terms most of those moves aren't in the top 20.
Turmoil in the market has led Morgan Stanley Dean Witter's Barton Biggs to compare stocks to the Titanic, to
suggest that people buy gold, and to note that "a bear market is likely" during which stocks "could go down
30% or 40%."
Oh, wait. Mr. Biggs didn't say those things this year. The gold suggestion came in 1996, the bear-market
warning in 1997 and the mention of the Titanic in 1998.
For the record, what Mr. Biggs says now is that the odds are very good that the Dow industrial average and
the S&P 500 "are in a cyclical bear market, which would be a decline of 20%." The Nasdaq Composite could
follow those indexes into bear territory within three to six months, Mr. Biggs adds.
Is he right this time?
---
Aaron Lucchetti contributed to this article.

Forget Oil;It's the CenturyOf the Electron
Forget Oil; It's the Century Of the Electron
By Mark P. Mills
699 words
23 February 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
A22
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
With oil prices nearing $30 a barrel, is the "new economy" in trouble thanks to old-economy troubles? Will oil
prices fuel inflation, undermine consumer and business confidence and put a drain on capital? Will oil
markets drag down the fortunes of high-tech companies?
Don't count on it. The epicenter of power in energy markets has shifted from Abu Dhabi, where oil lies
beneath the sand, to Silicon Valley, where electrons flow through computer chips made of sand. Three trends
in the digital economy are diminishing the importance of oil prices and the OPEC.
First, it has accelerated wealth creation. The U.S. Commerce Department estimates that at least one-third of
the nation's economic growth is driven by information technology. That means silicon has injected $800 billion
into the U.S. economy in the past decade. As a result, oil prices matter less. The share of gross domestic
product absorbed by oil purchases is 30% lower now than in 1990, despite the current high oil prices.
And prices will drop. During the past century, oil prices oscillated between $14 and $27 a barrel (in today's
dollars), with only two very brief exceptions. There will be more oil price spikes; such is the nature of any
commodity. But they will matter even less as the economy keeps expanding.
Second, the silicon revolution has made electricity the most important form of energy. The price of a
kilowatt-hour now matters more than the price of a barrel of crude. Transportation uses two-thirds of all oil
consumed, but constitutes only 10% of the economy. The other 90% of the economy gets 55% of its energy
from electricity. More than 80% of the growth in U.S. energy demand since 1990 has been met by electricity.
The tools of the new economy are leading the way. More than 60% of all capital spending, or some $450
billion a year, is on information-technology equipment. Every piece of IT equipment, from the PC to the
wireless base station, uses only one kind of energy: electricity.
Even as oil prices yo-yo, the average retail price of a kilowatt-hour has dropped 10% since 1990, and
wholesale prices are in free-fall. With market pressures from expanding utility deregulation and new
technologies, the electric price trend is locked in a downward cycle. Only 4% of the nation's electricity is
generated by oil, compared with 52% by coal, 15% by gas, 19% by nuclear reactors and 10% by
hydroelectric plants.
Third, computers themselves help hold down energy costs. Remote imaging and supercomputers have made
exploration so efficient that finding costs for a barrel of oil are down by more than 75%. Computer-based
design has radically improved equipment used to drill, extract and process oil. Smart drill bits, with fiber-optic
real-time links, direct serpentine drilling paths to chase down black gold. The Oil & Gas Journal forecasts at
least $11 billion annual savings just from Web-driven operational efficiencies yet-to-be implemented by oil
companies.
Everything from power plants to paper mills can or will arbitrage, in real time, so as to take advantage of
episodic oscillations of commodity prices. Aircraft and truck fleets have gained fuel savings from intelligent
routing and dispatch. We are only at the beginning of a world of smart imbedded and networked chips in
practically everything from refrigerators to machine tools. The collective effect of such efficiency is to build in
more resilience to energy commodity price swings.
The 20th century belonged to oil. The 21st century will belong to the electron, and this is OPEC's worst
nightmare. True, oil will remain the dominant transportation fuel for a very long time. Economic growth will
boost oil demand. But the economy is far less dependent on oil than it once was. The stubborn refusal of
inflation to kick in at $30-a-barrel oil proves it that the era of oil-price-driven inflation is over.
---

Mr. Mills is a senior fellow at the Competitive Enterprise Institute and co-editor of The PowerReport, a
newsletter published by GilderTechnology.

Crude Futures Skid as OPEC OfficialsRaise Prospect of Increasing Oil Supply
Commodities
Crude Futures Skid as OPEC Officials Raise Prospect of Increasing Oil Supply
By Masood Farivar
Dow Jones Newswires
711 words
18 February 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
C19
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Crude and products futures posted sharp losses at the New York Mercantile Exchange after
comments by key ministers from the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries raised the prospects of
putting more oil on the market.
The sell-off was across the board. The nearby March crude contract, which expires Tuesday, dropped to as
low as $29.05 before settling at $29.46 a barrel, down 59 cents. The April and May crude contracts, which
had outperformed the March contract during the previous two sessions, fell more sharply.
Products futures followed crude lower, surrendering some of their recent gains. Gasoline futures, which have
surged recently because of concerns over historically low U.S. inventories, tumbled, with the March contract
posting a 1.05-cent loss to 83.49 cents a gallon. March heating oil dropped 1.15 cents to 75.06 cents.
The recent surge in crude prices, which lifted the March above $30 Monday for the first time since the 1991
Gulf War, began to taper off Wednesday as profittaking and the expiration of March crude options put
pressure on prices.
The correction extended into yesterday after the oil ministers of Mexico, Venezuela and Saudi Arabia -- the
original architects of the output-reduction efforts of the past two years -- suggested in separate interviews that
they may be ready to back a production increase to ease prices.
Venezuelan Oil Minister Ali Rodriguez said Wednesday it was time for OPEC to take "corrective" steps to
damp rising oil prices, though he added that the cartel, not individual members, would decide a change in
production policy.
Saudi Arabia's oil minister Ali Naimi, speaking in South Korea yesterday, called $20 to $25 a barrel of crude
oil the ideal price level.
Mr. Naimi didn't say whether he backed raising OPEC output, but he told his South Korean counterpart that
he would try to stabilize oil prices. He added that crude prices have peaked and that he expects them to fall.
Mr. Naimi and Mr. Rodriguez, along with Mexico's influential oil minister, Luis Tellez, engineered a series of
efforts by major oil-producing countries over the past two years to remove more than 5.2 million barrels of oil
from the world market in an effort to boost prices.
Mr. Tellez, for his part, said yesterday in Mexico City that Saudi Arabia and non-OPEC Norway share
Mexico's view that producers should raise output after the production-cut agreements expire at the end of
March.
While traders seem to be increasingly betting that more oil will find its way to the market in the next quarter --
whether from major producers or from the U.S. Strategic Petroleum Reserve -- prices are expected to remain
volatile in the run-up to OPEC's March 27 ministerial meeting in Vienna, where OPEC ministers will decide
whether to extend the output-reduction accords.
"People feel reasonably secure that OPEC's plan for the time being is to increase production in March. The
comments calmed people down," said Chris Schacte, an analyst at GSC Energy, a trading firm in Atlanta. But
he added, "Certainly, the market has been volatile of late, and I don't think that is going to change" before
OPEC decides whether to boost output.
In other commodity markets:

--- Page 184 ---
Page 184 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
GOLD: Futures fell at the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange, giving up early gains. The
April contract dropped $1.20 to $303.80 a troy ounce, having risen to $312.50. William O'Neill, chief futures
strategist at Merrill Lynch, said prices fell after Federal Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan's Humphrey
Hawkins testimony, in which he warned about the economy overheating. That raised the prospect of higher
interest rates, which would make gold less attractive relative to interest-bearing assets.
PALLADIUM: Prices fell as profit-taking eroded a rise to a record in midsession. The March contract fell $5.60
to $705.55 a troy ounce, after reaching $747. Dealers said profit-taking set in after the earlier gain. The Tokyo
Commodity Exchange and the New York Mercantile Exchange raised margins on futures contracts, requiring
higher deposits for trading.

`There Goes the Neighborhood'
REVIEW & OUTLOOK (Editorial)
`There Goes the Neighborhood'
635 words
16 February 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
A26
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Remarking just the other day on "aggregated demand," Alan Greenspan went on to tell Congress we ought to
be thinking about "expanding the number of people we allow in." The Fed Chairman's persistent,
pro-immigration stance isn't what made him famous, but in our view will add to the luster of his legacy. But
even in the lofty world of monetary authorities and Congressional committees the talk is mostly about allowing
in more "skilled workers." It's never been entirely clear to us what that means.
Our impression has been that the "less skilled" and even the "non-skilled" contribute. Maybe the
Congressional solons should go on a local field trip to find out. All around them, from Washington, D.C., to the
suburbs of Maryland and Virginia, there's a motherlode of evidence that immigrants of all kinds are pure gold
to an urban economy.
We're not referring here to the wonderful cultural diversity that the Vietnamese, Ghanaians and Cubans have
brought to workaday D.C., although there is that. As the Old Cold Warrior policy types used to say -- "lose a
country, gain a restaurant." We're talking about the kind of bonus cherished by even the most staid citizens:
property values. Immigrants, it turns out, are very, very good for both residential and commercial property
values.
The power of this relationship comes clear in a new study by researchers Bronwyn Lance, Margalit Edelman
and Peter Mountford of the Alexis de Tocqueville Institute. They surveyed property values across D.C.
census tracts from 1980 to 1998 and found that spurts in immigration correlated heavily to increases in
residential property values.
How heavily? In Northwest D.C.'s Adams-Morgan area, long a neighborhood in transition, a 78% increase in
foreign-born population was accompanied by a 21% increase in property value. North Georgetown saw a
similar ratio: a 72% rise was matched by a 24% rise in prices. Overall, high immigrant areas saw property
values improve 13.77%, far above the 2.36% average increase for the city.
But the shift was not confined to the District. The immigrant-value connection also showed up in Northern
Virginia, where a 113% increase in foreigners accompanied a 37.5% increase in overall property values.
Typical in its transformation is Bailey's Crossroads, Va. Vietnamese first came here via a 1975 airlift; a
second wave of Vietnamese, Boat People this time, arrived at the end of the decade. In short order the
newcomers bought up the shops, strip malls and parking lots of Bailey's Crossroads and converted them to
thriving enterprises.
Ethiopians, Hispanics and Somalis worked similar magic in a relatively rough corner of Prince Georges
County, Md. In 1991, for example, investor Mike Rubin bought an abandoned shopping mall there, Riggs
Plaza, for a sum he describes as "almost nothing." Immigrant tenants rented the vacant stores, cleaned off
the graffiti, and made the mall a success. As one real estate agent summed up things: "There goes the
neighborhood -- up!"
Unfortunately, the flip side of the logic also holds. The Alexis de Tocqueville researchers found that
neighborhoods where the number of foreign-born decreased also usually saw values drop. The 10 tracts with
the most dramatic decreases in immigrant population, an average decline of 39%, saw home values drop
7.6%. One desolate section of Anacostia lost 76% of its immigration population; its property values fell by
18%.
All of which puts the lawmakers' quibbling about targeting skilled labor in perspective. If workers of all races,
backgrounds and skill levels have proven such a plus for Washington, why pretend that micromanagement is
the best immigration policy? Willing immigrants of all sorts are an asset to the country -- and for that matter,
to the neighborhood.

Gold Is Pitted Against a Vital Resource---In Nevada, Factions Decry Mines' Diversion of Big Amounts of Water
Economy
Gold Is Pitted Against a Vital Resource --- In Nevada, Factions Decry Mines' Diversion of Big Amounts
of Water
By Jim Carlton
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,306 words
16 February 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
BATTLE MOUNTAIN, Nev. -- In the high desert country of northeastern Nevada, almost 500 miles north of
Las Vegas, a war is brewing over gold. But it has little to do with the precious metal itself.
The battle is over the huge amounts of water being diverted by numerous gold mines in northern Nevada.
Since most of the mines are dug deeper than the water table, the water has to be pumped out. Scientists
recently found that the groundwater level has dropped more than 1,000 feet in places over the past decade --
believed to be the largest such fall in the world.
That disclosure has escalated a David vs. Goliath conflict between the region's farmers, ranchers,
environmentalists and Native Americans -- who want to keep this region largely agricultural -- and the state's
second-richest industry: mining.
"It's uncalled for, the destruction that is being caused here," fumes Carrie Dann, a Western Shoshone
rancher.
Since most of Nevada is desert, underground aquifers such as the one being drawn down are considered
vital sources of year-round water for the environment and for agricultural irrigation. Allowing the mining
industry to expand, say the critics, will cause further destruction.
But agricultural interests have an uphill battle against Nevada's fast-growing gold industry, which produces
half of the nation's gold supply and claimed revenue of $2.6 billion in 1998, nearly 5% of state domestic
product. In 1980, revenue from gold mining was just $200 million. Agriculture now ranks fourth behind
manufacturing, mining and, of course, gaming/tourism.
Officials of Eureka County recently commissioned an economic-impact study to buttress its objections to
expanded mining in the area. Preliminary results of the study indicate the rural county will suffer long-term
declines in its groundwater reserve.
Local Western Shoshone and their supporters, meanwhile, are also worried about the possible toxic effects of
the activity. As the pits refill with groundwater after the mines close, they say, the result could be a toxic
hazard as water mixes with minerals. Similar worries are fueling a battle in Sutton Summit, Ariz., over a
proposed copper mine nearby.
"To me, this is almost like nuclear waste," says Tom Myers, director of the Great Basin Mine Watch, a
conservation group in Reno.
Mining executives say their gold mines are equipped with sophisticated technology designed to prevent any
catastrophes, with computer models in place that foresee no major problems with either quantity or quality.
"We've got $3 million invested in our groundwater model, and I'd put it up against any in the world," says John
Mudge, environmental coordinator for Denver-based Newmont. The companies and government officials also
say they have corrective plans in case problems crop up.
Although both sides agree it is still too early to gauge the full impact, some early signs aren't encouraging. As
many as a half-dozen springs have dried up, with dozens more considered threatened. A portion of one creek
went dry before mining engineers could restore the flow. The underground water bodies feed into the
Humboldt River, which serves as the main source of water for the high desert of northeastern Nevada.
Over time, two creeks could dry up in nonrunoff times from all the dewatering activity, while flows may
diminish as much as two thirds in four others, according to a report by Newmont Mining Corp., which

--- Page 188 ---
Page 188 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
operates several of the mines here. The report also predicts that the Humboldt River's flow will fall by as
much as 25% at one location.
Newmont opened the first of the mines in 1965, using the open-pit technique of excavating massive amounts
of dirt and rock rather than the more traditional one of underground drilling. The gold here is embedded in
microscopic fragments and has to be sifted out.
Since open-pit mines have to be dug steadily deeper as more rock is uncovered, they eventually hit the water
table, which typically runs 100 feet to 300 feet deep here. Because this water has to be pumped out to keep
the pit dry, Newmont officials say they began conducting a detailed environmental review of the surrounding
Humboldt River watershed, which stretches on mostly public lands about 200 miles between Elko and
Winnemucca, Nev.
The impacts were minimal early on, because there were only a few mines. However, a 1980s boom in gold
prices touched off a mining frenzy. Mine engineers didn't foresee a problem pumping water out of the pits,
having concluded through studies that the Humboldt River and its tributaries are largely fed by spring runoff.
Environmentalists argue that groundwater acts as a principal source of recharge during the dry summer
months.
In the early 1990s, some Western Shoshone began noticing diminished flow in their springs, which are
spiritually important to the tribe. "When one dries up, for us it is cultural genocide," says Bernice Lalo, the
tribe's environmental coordinator.
Critics of dewatering rallied behind a group called the Western Shoshone Defense Fund, an environmental
group dedicated to helping the tribe. "The local people who go out to hunt and fish couldn't help but notice
less water in the springs and streams," says tribal chief Elwood Aaron Mose.
Since no one had measured how much water was being taken out, the Shoshone petitioned for an updated
study by the U.S. Geological Survey. Survey scientists returned in 1993 to wells they had measured a decade
before, and discovered the water level had dropped as much as 800 feet. Expanded surveying at about 500
wells found the declines had increased to more than 1,000 feet in places by 1996. By then, the water being
displaced by the mines had reached 200,000 acre feet a year, or about the amount of annual consumption of
Las Vegas and Reno combined.
Barrick Gold Corp. officials, meanwhile, discovered in about 1996 that one spring had dried up along a
truck-hauling road near the company's 1,300-foot-deep Betze-Post mine, which pumps water out at a rate of
about 50,000 gallons a minute.
At another mine, Newmont's Lone Tree, an environmental-impact statement in 1996 disclosed that
dewatering contributed to at least two nearby springs' drying up. At one of the springs, called Brooks, the
company laid a pipe to refill it with water pumped out of the mine. That same year, a portion of the Maggie
Creek running by Newmont's 1,600-foot-deep Gold Quarry mine also went dry after a sinkhole appeared,
causing the water flow to disappear underground. Newmont responded by diverting the creek around the hole
until it could be plugged with concrete two weeks later.
While mining officials call these isolated occurrences, environmentalists worry they are harbingers of bigger
problems to come. "This is really a big experiment," says Chris Sewell, an activist with the Western Shoshone
Defense Fund.
Activists have pushed for such measures as making the mines put more water back into the ground. That is
not proving so easy, though. As part of its regulatory permit, for instance, the Pipeline mine included plans to
re-inject water into the ground. But the mine, operated by Placer Dome Inc. of Vancouver, British Columbia,
has had to increase to about 900 acres from 120 acres the land needed for holding ponds from which to put
water back. They say the water being pumped from the pit proved too much for the smaller area.
Whether or not such measures will succeed remains to be seen. Meanwhile, seven more mines are proposed
for the region, all of which the opponents are trying to limit until the dewatering impacts become clearer.

Many Technology Issues Post Solid Increases,But Europe and Asian Indexes Finish Lower
World Stock Markets
Many Technology Issues Post Solid Increases, But Europe and Asian Indexes Finish Lower
A Wall Street Journal News Roundup
1,278 words
15 February 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
C14
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
The technology fever that has swept Wall Street is also leading the overseas markets.
While most Asian markets finished down for the day, many tech stocks broke from the pack and posted solid
performances. European markets were hit by a bout of profit taking in growth sectors and investors used that
cash to pile into companies with close ties to the Internet.
"Owing to much of the dot-com hysteria, many people compare the Internet business to a gold rush," said
Michael O'Sullivan, European equity strategist at Warburg Dillon Read. "The Internet winners of the future will
need to be the first movers possessing strong brand names and client bases."
Investors are pinning hopes that Spanish bank Banco Bilbao Vizcaya Argentaria can become one of the
major players in online banking with its recently announced alliance with Telefonica. Shares of BBVA jumped
12% on the news, while Telefonica rose just 1.4%. Analysts believe that BBVA has more to gain from the
deal because Telefonica already has a well-developed online plan. Investor excitement over the deal helped
keep Madrid's IBEX 35 index in record territory at 12458.60.
Overall, the Dow Jones World Stock Index fell 2.03, or 0.83%, to 241.99. Excluding the U.S., the index fell
2.70, or 1.4%, to 186.67.
The division between market-leading technology names and old-line blue chips is perhaps most apparent in
South Korea, where investors are spurning the benchmark Kospi Index for the tech-heavy Kosdaq. The
blue-chip Kospi plunged 4.4%, while the Kosdaq eked out a gain for the day. Investor interest in tech is so
strong that volume on Kosdaq was triple the value of the Kospi market.
"Retail money continues to pour into the Kosdaq," said Richard Samuelson, research director at Warburg
Dillion Read in Seoul. Retail investors are chasing the prospect of dramatic gains in tech-concept stocks and
there is no sign that it is going to change in the near future, he said.
European markets started the day on a sour note because of Wall Street's weak performance Friday. The
Dow Jones Industrial Average and Nasdaq Composite lost more than 2%. But sentiment shifted into positive
territory once Wall Street opened up on the plus side yesterday.
In TOKYO, the Nikkei 225 Index dropped 153.56 to 19556.46. Toshiba rallied 49 yen to 924 yen as the
consumer-electronics company drew interest for its new Internet strategy. Softbank surged 21,000 yen to
169,000 on the news that it will team with the World Bank to invest $500 million in Internet start-ups in
developing nations. The recent flurry of hacker attacks on Web sites lifted shares of onlinesecurity firms.
Secom, a security-services provider, gained 860 yen to 9,750 and Trend Micro, an antivirus-software maker,
climbed 2,000 yen to 29,800. The market was closed Friday for a holiday.
In HONG KONG, the Hang Seng Index shed 191.34, or 1.1%, to 17188.96. Investors cashed in on the
market's sharp gains the previous week. Despite the overall down day, investors were still excited over
Friday's news of a rival bid by Pacific Century CyberWorks for Cable & Wireless HKT, Hong Kong's dominant
telecommunications company. Cable & Wireless HKT jumped 22% to 26.40 Hong Kong dollars, which follows
a 23% rise Friday. Trading Pacific Century CyberWorks shares was suspended after it announced a HK$7.87
billion share placement. Bank stocks finished lower, dragged down by a quarterpercentage point interest rate
boost in Hong Kong on Friday.
In SEOUL, the Kospi Index dropped 4.4%, or 42.35 points, to 910.87. Wall Street's fall on Friday coupled with
a strong won vs. the dollar sparked worries over exports and led the market lower. Large capitalization stocks
took it on the chin. Pohang Iron & Steel fell 11% to 110,000 won and Samsung Electronics slipped 6% to
269,000 won. Investors flocked to the over-the-counter Kosdaq index pushing it up 4.63 points to 266.71.

--- Page 190 ---
Page 190 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In LONDON, the FTSE 100 finished down 124.70 points, or 2.1%, to 6068.6. The benchmark index is down
13% from its peak at the start of the year. The session's retreat was led by Vodafone AirTouch, which fell
6.8% to 330 pence in heavy trading. Traders were anticipating a round of buying of the telecom giant since its
acquisition of Germany's Mannesmann boosted its weighting in the index. But institutional investors have

Scotland, the winner in the NatWest battle, shed 7.3% to 807 pence.
In FRANKFURT, the Xetra DAX Index posted a 33.25 point gain to close at 7644.80. Technology stocks were

finished up 8.2% to 33.10 euros amid rumblings that the utility group is expected to introduce an Internet

euros. BASF retreated 2.1% to 43.20 euros after it said that selling its pharmaceuticals arm wasn't the sole
option it had to counter industry consolidation. BMW dropped 1.2% to 25.25 euros on reports that it may team
up with Volkswagen and close a plant at its money-losing Rover unit in the United Kingdom.
In MADRID, the IBEX 35 Index rose 26 points, or less than 1%, to a record 12458.60. Banco Bilbao Vizcaya
Argentaria was the day's big winner, climbing 12% to 15.65 euros. Rival Banco Santander Central Hispano
rose 4.8% to 10.41 euros. Profit taking in other blue-chip issues kept the overall market lower.
In SAO PAULO, the Bovespa retreated 1.5%, or 264 points, to 17819. Trading was thin as investors are
cautiously approaching a central bank decision on interest rates and the release of U.S. inflation data later
in the week. Market bellwether Telebras receipts fell 1.5% to 261 reals and preferred shares in Eletrobras
slipped 3.65% to 34.20 reals. The market opened 30 minutes late because of a power outage, which can
partially explain the session's light volume.
Meanwhile, Mexico's IPC Index shed 0.9%, or 65.24 points to 7558.23. Argentina's Merval Index dropped
2.94 points to 634.09 giving back some of last week's gains.

MozambiqueHailed as ModelOf Recovery---Leaders Hope ProsperityWill Spread, but CriticsFault Economic Policies
Mozambique Hailed as Model Of Recovery --- Leaders Hope Prosperity Will Spread, but Critics Fault
Economic Policies
732 words
14 February 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
MAPUTO, Mozambique (AP) -- Gleaming new cars fill a showroom on Karl Marx Avenue, sparkling symbols
of Mozambique's rapid turn to capitalism from communism after a devastating civil war.
Formerly a poster child for strife-torn Africa, Mozambique is now hailed as a model of recovery and promise.
The economy is averaging 10% growth annually, inflation is near zero and the government has privatized
state industries and eliminated subsidies.
Despite that performance, everyone agrees the southern African nation has a long way to go.
Most of Mozambique's 19 million people still live in straw huts and subsist on less than a dollar a day. They
haven't felt a ripple from a boom signaled by construction of tourist hotels along the Indian Ocean coast and
multibillion-dollar industrial projects.
The former Marxist revolutionaries who run the government hope the prosperity will spread, but critics
contend the country has gone too far in adopting noninterventionist economic policies.
The United Nations Development Program says the government should do more to boost earnings of the
poor, but it didn't suggest any specific actions in a recent report.
If anyone embodies the government's about-face from Marxism to capitalism, it is Mario Machungo.
Now chairman of the International Bank of Mozambique, Mr. Machungo was a young activist for the Front for
the Liberation of Mozambique, dodging Portuguese secret police and fomenting rebellion against colonial
rule. When Frelimo, as the movement is known, took power in 1975, Mr. Machungo became a cabinet
minister and then served as prime minister in 1986-1994.
Mr. Machungo noted that the new government faced daunting problems: The departing Portuguese had
sabotaged the infrastructure. Few blacks were educated. White-ruled South Africa and Rhodesia began
backing right-wing Mozambican rebels, who waged a 15-year war that killed 600,000 people before it ended
in 1992.
Frelimo raised an army, imposed a centralized economy, even dictated what professions people should
pursue.
"We needed strong hands to guide society," Mr. Machungo said over cups of espresso. "We were criticized
because that collided with personal liberties, but it was our only way out at the time."
By the mid-1980s, rail and other services barely existed in the war-ravaged country, and most imports,
especially oil, were beyond reach. The country had stagnated at the bottom.
"We said, 'Look, we can't continue to insist on a Marxist-Leninist model that's not working. We need to bring
in other actors from the private sector because the state can't handle it alone,' " Mr. Machungo said.
In 1986, Mozambique agreed to liberalize its economy in order to attract foreign companies, create jobs and
build roads and other infrastructure.
The response was significant. In 1985, foreign investment had totaled just $26 million. By 1998, the yearly
total had soared to $837 million.
Among the main development projects now under way:
A $1.6 billion, natural-gas-fueled factory to produce steel slabs for export. A principal investor is Enron Corp.,
a Houston energy company.

--- Page 192 ---
Page 192 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
A $1.3 billion aluminum smelter scheduled to open this year. Investors include Mitsubishi of Japan and Billiton
of South Africa.
A superhighway being built from the seaside capital of Maputo to southern Africa's economic center,
Johannesburg, South Africa.
Such projects are reviewed by the government's Center for Promotion of Investments. Under the contracts,
foreign companies usually are required to invest heavily in improving basic economic services and facilities,
such as rehabilitating Maputo's port.
If the economy keeps growing at current rates and the wealth is distributed evenly, Mozambicans' income will
double in just over seven years, the U.N. Development Program says.
The boom has already touched Josep Machagai, who as a young man left for neighboring South Africa's gold
mines to find work. Now 69 and suffering from a lung ailment, he is a security guard at an apartment building.
He sells cigarettes on an upturned cardboard box to make a little extra.
His two grown sons didn't have to leave Mozambique to find jobs. They are both construction workers, putting
up houses, shops and restaurants.
"They're working because the economy is doing well," Mr. Machagai said. "I don't know if it will continue, but
for now my sons have a better life."

Euro Again Hits Wall at 99.50 Cents;Dollar Weathers Strong Japanese Data
Foreign Exchange
Euro Again Hits Wall at 99.50 Cents; Dollar Weathers Strong Japanese Data
By Michael Casey
Dow Jones Newswires
681 words
11 February 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
C18
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- The euro made yet another test of parity against the dollar but again failed to get clear of
99.50 cents, inducing another quick unwinding of its earlier gains.
It was the third consecutive day in which the euro had faltered around the 99.50 cents level. Meanwhile, the
dollar was steady against the yen, despite some spectacularly strong economic data out of Japan earlier in
the day.
Late in New York, the euro was at 98.49 cents, down from 99.32 cents late Wednesday. The dollar was at
109.14 yen, up from 109.06 yen Wednesday. The British pound was at $1.6092, down from $1.6170
Wednesday.
The run of upside failures for the euro suggests that the market needs to shake out some long euro positions
before it can make a decisive move in either direction, said Marc Chandler, chief currency strategist at Mellon
Financial Corp.
"At these levels it is neutral -- neither the bulls nor the bears are getting hurt," Mr. Chandler said. "We need to
see some pain."
The European Central Bank's monthly report gave some support to the euro and was cited as a reason it
bounced off its intraday low of 98.33 cents. The report highlighted the inflationary risks posed by a weak euro
and noted that "an upswing is well under way" in the euro-zone economy, references that traders took as a
hint that additional ECB rate increases are in the works.
At the same time, however, the dollar continues to get support from signs of strength in U.S. markets.
Analysts note that the U.S. currency has been moving in close correlation with the booming Nasdaq
Composite Index, which surged 122.39 points to a record yesterday.
Mike Rosenberg, Deutsche Bank's head of global foreign-exchange research, said the market's nonreaction
to news that Japan's machinery orders grew 16.1% in December, way above economists' expectations for a
gain of 0.2%, indicated that traders are looking for more consistent numbers in Japan before they decide on a
trend for the economy.
The machinery-orders data, used as a leading indicator for capital expenditure, came just days after
Economic Planning Agency chief Taichi Sakaiya painted a far more gloomy picture, warning that Japan's
economy may have contracted in the fourth quarter.
Meanwhile, the British pound got no boost from a rate increase by the Bank of England yesterday. The
quarter-percentage-point rise in its key lending rate was widely expected.
The Australian dollar and the South African rand traded in contradiction with their typically strong link with
gold prices. As gold reached a four-month high just below $316 a troy ounce, the Australian dollar briefly
sank below 63 cents, and the rand hit a 17-month low of 6.3750 to the dollar.
Both so-called commodity currencies later recovered slightly, but that did little to outweigh evidence that each
currency has been trading on the back of fund flows, rather than metal price issues.
On the other hand, the Canadian dollar, which is also often included in the commodity-currency category,
hasn't matched the weakness of its Australian and South African counterparts. It eased slightly against the
U.S. dollar but continued to hold fairly steady.
Late in New York, the U.S. dollar was at C$1.4487, up from C$1.4448 Wednesday.

--- Page 194 ---
Page 194 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Asian currencies got some support from the yen's modestly stronger position, but the Indonesian rupiah lost
some of the ground it gained Wednesday. Late in New York, the dollar was at 7,315 rupiah, up from 7,255
rupiah late Wednesday.
The South Korean won, meanwhile, made solid gains after the Bank of Korea said it will raise its one-day call
rate by a quarter percentage point in February. Late in New York, the dollar was at 1,120.50 won, down from
1,123.50 won Wednesday.

NEW YORK -- Technology stocks rallied sharply once again, but blue-chip names set their own course
downward.
Semiconductor stocks paced the technology rise, as investors reacted favorably to fourth-quarter profit
showings in the group. Credence Systems (Nasdaq), a maker of testing systems for chip-equipment makers,
rose to 114 15/16, up 11 11/16, outstripping its previous 52-week high, after the company reported fiscal
first-quarter earnings late Wednesday that blew past analysts' forecasts. Credit Suisse First Boston issued an
upbeat research note on Credence, saying that even at current prices, the stock's valuation looks reasonable.
Teradyne, a rival to Credence, rose to 78 15/16, up 3 15/16, after Lehman Brothers, in its own research note,

name in the sector, LTX (Nasdaq), added 6 3/16 to 35 11/16.
Chip-equipment stocks rallied, with Applied Materials (Nasdaq) ahead 11 1/64 to 174 3/4, and Novellus
Systems (Nasdaq) ahead 6 1/2 to 59 3/4, while chip makers Broadcom (Nasdaq), ahead 12 1/16 to 349, and
STMicroelectronics, which rose 5 15/16 to 215 1/8, also notched sharp gains.
While the Nasdaq Composite Index rose 122.39 points, the Dow Jones Industrial Average danced to a
different beat. Even with a solid contribution from its four technology components, the industrial average
ended lower, down 55.53, or 0.52%, to close at 10643.63.
Absent technology, nearly every other sector of the broad market fared poorly. Financial stocks reacted
poorly, and probably appropriately, to another dismal session in the government-securities market. J.P.
Morgan fell 2 9/16 to 114, Wells Fargo lost 1 1/8 to 36 3/4, and Bank of America lost 1 3/16 to 44 7/8, and
traded within a point of the 52-week low set in October.
As happened Wednesday, fixed-income investors reacted poorly to a leg of the Treasury Department's
quarterly refunding effort, causing government-bond prices to drop, raising yields. The prospect of rising
interest rates damaged several growth stocks in the session, including General Motors, which lost 2 1/4 to
76 7/8, as well as Merck, which weakened again, losing 2 1/4 to 69.
In fact, though, losses spread pretty evenly across blue-chip names. Consumer names such as
Kimberly-Clark, off 2 3/8 to 58 5/8, and Procter & Gamble, which fell 2 5/16 to 91 7/16, declined again.
Cyclicals, such as Caterpillar, down 1 to 38 1/8, and trading at a 52-week low, stumbled again, while DuPont
dropped 2 3/4 to 53 15/16.
Transports fell sharply, sending the Dow Jones Transportation Average to a loss of nearly 3% for the session;
it lost 75.05 points, a fall of 2.94%, and with its finish at 2475.27, slid to its 52-week low.
Gold and oil stocks, two groups that bucked the trends of the session, did manage to move higher. Newmont
Mining, for example, added 3/4 to 24 5/8.
Oil stocks also improved, as Wall Street turned more upbeat on their profit outlook, citing continued strength
in crude prices. Kerr-McGee gained 1 3/16 to 47 3/4, Texaco rose 1 1/16 to 50 5/8, and Atlantic Richfield
increased 3 3/4 to 67 1/8. The gains came after ABN Amro, in a research note, said it expected industry
earnings to rise more sharply than previously anticipated this year.
Among individual issues, UnumProvident fell 10 1/8 to 15 9/16, after the Portland, Maine, insurance provider
reported fourth-quarter results that fell well-short of what analysts anticipated. The company issued its
earnings statement after the close of trading Wednesday. Analysts at J.P. Morgan and at Morgan Stanley
Dean Witter cut their ratings on the stock.

to expect.
AppNet (Nasdaq) rose 18 5/8 to 70, and set a 52-week high. The Bethesda, Md., provider of Web-hosting

Boston, in a research note, called the quarterly results steller, and raised its 12-month price target on the
stock to 100.
Network Solutions (Nasdaq) increased 36 5/16 to 292 5/16, after the company, which registers
Internet-domain names, reported better-than-expected fourth-quarter earnings.
"All we're seeing in the market is the excitement for the future of technology, which I admit, is very bright," Bill

Gold Surges on Bullish Plans on HedgesFrom South African, Canadian Mines
Commodities
Gold Surges on Bullish Plans on Hedges From South African, Canadian Mines
By Janet Whitman
Dow Jones Newswires
609 words
11 February 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
C15
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- More bullish pronouncements from gold mines about trimming their hedge books triggered
another surge in the price of gold.
South African gold producers Anglogold Ltd. and Western Areas Ltd. yesterday announced they have cut
hedges or intend to hedge less of their future production through forward sales. That came on the heels of
similar announcements recently from Canadian mines Placer Dome Inc. and Barrick Gold Corp.
On the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange, the active April gold contract leapt $10.10 to
$318.70 a troy ounce, its highest settlement since the market's surprise spike back in the fall.
"The fact that producers are pulling back on their hedging activity is driving a lot of fears about liquidity drying
up and that's pushing the market higher," said Ian MacDonald, manager of precious metals with
Commerzbank in New York.
Gold mines typically hedge future production by borrowing gold from specialist bullion banks and then selling
that gold forward, adding liquidity to the market.
The announcements from the gold producers "are bullish because they reinforce that the gold price has
reached bottom and is more likely to rise from where it is today," said Jeffrey Christian, general manager with
New York-based research firm CPM Group.
Sentiment began to change in the market in late September following the Washington Accord -- an
announcement by 15 European central banks that they intend to limit their gold sales and freeze
gold-lending levels over the next five years.
"With the Washington Accord, central-bank selling has ceased to be the bugaboo that was scaring investors
away," said Mr. Christian. Now, the producer announcements have "created an environment where the
momentum is for higher prices," he said.
The question now is whether speculative investors can be lured back into the market, analysts said.
"Miners hope speculators will believe them when they say they'll [say no to hedging] no matter what the price
does," said Andy Smith, analyst with Mitsui Bussan in London.
The substantial gains in the price of gold over the past week also have attracted investor interest in the
market, traders and analysts said.
"We're getting into a situation where gold looks good and people are saying, `Maybe I should buy some,'"
said CPM's Mr. Christian. "They're looking at options, which are too expensive right now. So they're just
buying gold outright."
In other commodity markets:
GASOLINE: Futures rose at Nymex as tight U.S. supplies stirred speculation of higher prices to come. The
March contract gained 2.09 cents to 81.75 cents a gallon. Inventory data this week showed a sharp drop in
U.S. refinery-utilization rates, leading the market to factor in the possibility of a supply crunch heading into the
peak demand season in spring and summer. "We're rallying more on what's not happening, than what is.
We're not building gasoline inventory, as we would normally do this time of year," said Tim Evans, an energy
analyst with Pegasus Econometrics Group, a New York-based trading advisor.

--- Page 199 ---
Page 199 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
WHEAT: Futures gained at the Chicago Board of Trade as concerns about dryness in the U.S. Winter Wheat
Belt spurred buying by speculative commodity funds, market watchers said. The March contract rose 6.25
cents to $2.7175 a bushel. Weekly export sales reported by the U.S. Department of Agriculture also boosted
prices, showing sales of 684,400 metric tons in the week to Feb. 3, a much higher figure than expected.
-- Matthew F. Gallagher
