Blue Chips Slide 258.44 Points as Bonds Tumble---Rate Fears, HackersFuel Bearish SessionTouching All Sectors
Wednesday's Markets
Blue Chips Slide 258.44 Points as Bonds Tumble --- Rate Fears, Hackers Fuel Bearish Session
Touching All Sectors
By E.S. Browning
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
879 words
10 February 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Bond ghouls and cyber hackers got to the financial markets.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average took its third-biggest fall of the year in point and percentage terms, led
down by interest-rate-sensitive stocks and by technology bellwethers Microsoft and Intel. It fell 258.44 points,
or 2.36%, to 10699.16, finishing at a level it first crossed on April 22, 1999. The roaring Nasdaq Composite
Index, heavy in technology stocks, gave back 64.26, or 1.45%, to 4363.24.
"It looks like there are still a lot of interest-rate fears around," said Bob Basel, co-head of listed stock trading
at Salomon Smith Barney. "The market was down right out of the chute, and never really looked back. It just
shows you how fragile the whole marketplace is."
Unexpected comments yesterday about Treasury-bond issuance from Treasury Secretary Lawrence
Summers, together with fears about hackers interfering with Internet commerce, made a bad day worse for
stocks and bonds.
The news that future issuance of 30-year bonds might not be as limited as thought helped drive the bond
down 1 6/32 yesterday, or more than $11 for a $1,000 bond. The bond's yield, which moves inversely to
price, rose to 6.32%.
That came on top of a report that inventory growth was weak late last year -- a sign of a strong economy.
Bond ghouls, so-called because they fear economic strength will produce inflation and higher interest rates,
drove bonds down across the board.
The combination of inflation fears and market confusion hurt financial stocks.
Traders said stocks already were showing signs of natural profit-taking in the wake of Tuesday's big gains,
because interest-rate fears still haven't gone away.
The Federal Reserve already has boosted rates a quarter-point this month, and "the large majority of people
feel that rates are going to go higher," Mr. Basel said.
Contributing to the morning jitters was news that hackers, or computer aficionados who use phone lines to
break into other people's computers, temporarily shut down Amazon.com, Buy.com and eBay, after attacking
Yahoo! earlier.
"That is a little scary to people who think we will be doing all our business on the Internet," said Alfred Kugel,
senior investment strategist at Chicago fund management group Stein Roe & Farnham.
Concerns spread beyond the stocks affected, because of a belief that more and more individuals and
companies will be using the Internet for their business.
The businesses the hackers hit "are sort of the backbone of the technology market, and if they are vulnerable
to these kinds of attacks, it is a little bit frightening to people who invest in these Internet stocks," noted Dan
Mathisson, head stock trader at D.E. Shaw Securities.
But traders said a combination of profit-taking after the market's big gains and the persistent fear of
interest-rate boosts to come, may have been as important as the day's specific events. Oil and gold prices
and market interest rates all rose during the day, all of which is bad for stocks and bonds, Mr. Mathisson
noted.

--- Page 201 ---
Page 201 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Reports that European officials are considering tighter regulation of Microsoft hurt technology stocks.
"We did already have some profit-taking in the tech stocks early in the year, after their December runup, and
perhaps we are having more profit-taking now after the February runup," Mr. Kugel said. On top of that, "the
gyrations in the bond market have caused people to wonder what is happening, so they all stand aside for a
while and wait to see what is happening."
Even news of Cisco Systems' strong profit, which came after the close Tuesday, wasn't enough to turn the
tech sector around. Cisco itself posted a 2% gain, but it closed well below its highs for the day as it too was
hit by the market pullback.
Some analysts reported that Cisco had passed General Electric as the nation's second-biggest company
behind Microsoft, based on market value, which is the stock's price times the number of shares outstanding.
That calculation was based on diluted shares, including both actual stock and options. Cisco, like most tech
companies, is a heavy issuer of options. Based only on actual shares outstanding, or "basic" shares, GE
remained slightly larger than Cisco.
The average share of stock listed on the New York Stock Exchange fell 80 cents, or 1.78%, to $44.25.
Outside the U.S., stocks advanced in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
rose 1.56 points to 191.18.
In major market action:
Stocks and bonds retreated. On the Big Board, where 1.04 billion shares traded, 1,002 stocks advanced and
1,995 declined.
The dollar fell. Late in New York, it traded at 99.32 cents to the euro and 109.06 yen to the dollar, from 98.63
cents and 109.46 yen.

Clinton's Surplus Army
REVIEW & OUTLOOK (Editorial)
Clinton's Surplus Army
905 words
9 February 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
A26
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Surveying the discussion around Bill Clinton's final budget, the first thought that comes to mind is that when
"reducing the national debt" lands atop Washington's to-do list, times have somehow changed for the better.
The second thought that comes to mind is, Can we trust the politicians with even this? Well, remember those
"spending caps" they imposed on the budget a few years ago? They're gone. Mr. Clinton's spending plans --
in toto, a mind-boggling array of politicized initiatives -- are scheduled to rise at the projected inflation rate,
about 2.5% annually. Congress's GOP leadership is of course complicit in tossing over this automatic
discipline on their "discretion."
The fact remains that we are passing through one of the most intriguing periods in the history of our political
economy, that crucial switching station between the ability of a nation's people to produce wealth and the
belief among the nation's political class that it is their solemn duty to . . . well, that is the big question today.
The politicians' answer for virtually the entire postwar period has been simple: Tax and spend. Especially
through the 1960s and '70s this solemn duty essentially became a political pig-out, as Congress added a
skein of grandiose entitlements such as Medicaid and Medicare to the already existing Social Security
program, dubbed this stuff "nondiscretionary" spending and declared their hands tied as entitlement outlays
produced deficits.
In the 1980s, Ronald Reagan cut taxes (and regulations) to incentivize the economy, but as importantly to
choke off the revenue oxygen to planet Congress. He also jacked up defense spending to choke off the
Soviet Union. Both Congress and the Communists threw in the towel.
Yes, the victorious defense spending didn't thwart the federal deficit, but what Ronald Reagan did accomplish
was a sea-change in the tax-and-spend mind-set. He overturned the automatic, rarely questioned legitimacy
of ever-rising domestic outlays. When in 1992 the Governor of Arkansas happened along with the Democratic
Presidential nomination, he ran, in a bow to the changed status quo, as a fiscally responsible New Democrat.
Looking back from the vantage of a Dow 11,000 over the past seven years of Bill Clinton's Presidency, we
feel secure in a few observations: There would be no boom of this scale and a smaller budget surplus if a) a
bipartisan Congress hadn't defeated the Clinton plan to nationalize 14% of the economy through HillaryCare
and if b) Newt Gingrich hadn't ended the Democrats' 40-year control of the House in 1994.
These two events shut down Mr. Clinton's compulsion to spend on a grand scale. The fabulous economy now
spread before us has been a case history in what a former President just turned 89 said would happen if ever
Washington just "got out of the way." That includes the contribution of a depoliticized Fed willing to keep
inflation low and prices steady.
The conventional wisdom now is that Americans are enamored with the prosperity unfettered economic
activity can produce and don't much care about politics. If this means people think they can stop watching
Washington, then they're going to be fleeced, eventually. Nothing makes that clearer than the political debate
over the budget and the "surplus."
The vaunted, poorly understood surplus is merely the product, in large part, of multiplying the
worker/employer Social Security tax rate of 12.4% times everyone's individual income up to $76,200. With
growth came the Social Security revenue gusher, the result of tax tables on automatic pilot, not, mirabile
dictu, some overt act of spending control. Look in the Clinton Budget's Historical Tables, and you'll see tax
receipts as a percentage of GDP for this year at 20.4%. Guess when last the government's piece of the action
was that big -- 1945. You're in Bill Clinton's Army now, bub.
Having this sudden pot of gold fall in their laps, and with a public largely unaware that the surplus is being
siphoned straight out of their paystubs, has caught Washington's politicians so off-guard there's been no time
to come up with a new spending strategy that won't get them thrown out of office. Bill Clinton has come

--- Page 203 ---
Page 203 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
closest with his wonderful assertions that the meaning of spending is "investments." Can't wait for for
prescription-drug IPO.
The President has given up on fixing the structural flaws in Social Security and Medicare; indeed, his budget
proposes using the general-revenue surplus to keep Medicare on life-support ("solvent") until 2025. His actual
spending is a last will and testament for Al Gore and Dick Gephardt's candidates. To keep from falling asleep,
Mr. Clinton will play during working hours with the Republicans' hopes for an election-year tax cut.
The public may enjoy Washington's disarray while it last, because it won't last long. Whatever their failings,
politicians understand why God createdtax receipts. Until the public puts someone in the White House
dedicated to giving the gusher back, Washington won't. It will spend the "surplus" on its own obsessions.
Anyone out there, including Alan Greenspan, who truly thinks it's all going to be poured down a hole called
the "national debt" should talk to Hillary Clinton about turning the Brooklyn Bridge into a windfall.

Brinker Is Out of Favor Right Now,Which May Be Just the Time to Buy
Heard in Texas
TEXAS JOURNAL
Brinker Is Out of Favor Right Now, Which May Be Just the Time to Buy
By Jonathan Weil
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,521 words
9 February 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
Texas Journal
T2
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Just when Brinker International's business has really started cooking, investors have lost their appetite for
restaurant stocks. Now, the question is, will they come back for another bite?
Shares of the Dallas casual-dining chain, whose restaurants include Chili's Bar and Grill, Romano's Macaroni
Grill and On The Border Mexican Cafe, have languished for the past year, trading now at $26.438, about a
couple bucks below where they were at the end of 1998.
It isn't that folks suddenly have started cooking at home more. To the contrary, Brinker's sales and earnings
have never been stronger. But in a market where the mantra for investors has been "nothing but Net" (or at
least nothing but technology), retail dining establishments just don't have the spice they used to.
Witness such recent lackluster performers as Applebee's and Darden Restaurants, both of which are near
their 52-week lows. After all, the conventional wisdom goes, there isn't much sense in buying profitable,
established companies that offer merely long-term growth when you can take a shot at a nascent Web stock
that could triple in a day based on some hyped-up press release.
So what will it take for Brinker and its like to stage a comeback? Probably some sort of shift in market
psychology "where technology investors will realize how expensive those stocks are and then go looking for
more-attractive valuations," says Lynne Collier, a restaurant analyst in Dallas at Stephens, an investment firm
based in Little Rock, Ark.
For long-term investors who believe a big tech correction is imminent, Ms. Collier says, the time to start
looking within the downtrodden restaurant sector is now. She recommends keeping an eye toward companies
that consistently report strong same-store sales growth and have a long record of beating the Street's
quarterly earnings estimates.
As for Brinker: "Of all the companies I follow, this one has the strongest same-store sales numbers and the
greatest ability to beat earnings estimates," says Ms. Collier, who rates Brinker a "buy" with a $34 12-month
price target.
Certainly the company is performing well on those two fronts. According to First Call, Brinker has beaten
analysts' average earnings estimates for nine consecutive quarters, including last month's report that
earnings for the fiscal second quarter, which ended Dec. 29, were 38 cents a diluted share, four cents better
than Street expectations. At Chili's, which accounts for nearly two-thirds of the company's 1,000 restaurants,
sales at locations open at least 12 months were up a healthy 5.4% from year-earlier results, while same-store
sales at Macaroni Grill were up 4.5% and at On The Border 4.4%.
The stock does look cheap. Brinker trades for just under 18 times its earnings for the past four quarters. That
is roughly the same multiple it fetched during the mid-1990s, when the restaurant industry was in the midst of
a development glut and the company was regularly posting disappointing quarterly earnings and declining
sales at its older stores.
"The stock is trading as if same-store sales had already dropped off," says Stacy Jamar, an analyst at Banc
of America Securities in New York, who has a "buy" on Brinker with a $35 12-month price target. "By any
measure, it's at the low end of its historical range."
Yet the company's operations have improved considerably in the past couple of years. Last quarter was the
11th in a row that the company posted at least 15% year-over-year earnings growth. For calendar 1999, the
most recent four quarters, earnings climbed 30% to $1.48 a diluted share, while sales jumped 19% to $2

--- Page 205 ---
Page 205 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
billion. Analysts are projecting earnings will grow 22% to $1.80 a share for calendar 2000, a rate well above
the company's forward-looking price/earnings ratio.
So why buy now? At Dain Rauscher Wessels in Minneapolis, analyst David Geraty says it's the old credo of
picking up stocks when they're most disfavored because when the turning point in investor sentiment arrives,
it'll probably be too late to grab any bargains. He has been aggressively touting Brinker and a select few other
restaurant stocks since October, when he raised his rating on the company to "strong buy" from "buy."
"To make money, you've got to buy it when the valuation is attractive," says Mr. Geraty, who has a $37
six-to-12-month price target. "It's a frustrating time, but we do think that investors who are buying here will be
rewarded if they're patient. These types of stocks pop very quickly, and in a matter of months they can be up
50% to 100%."
To be sure, there are a few niggling issues that have given investors plausible excuses to stay away from the
sector. Rising labor costs and a worsening shortage of workers nationally have fueled some worries that
operating margins may come under pressure. Still, that is an issue that Brinker has been dealing with,
successfully, for years. And to date the trend hasn't had a material impact on either the company's earnings
or its ability to find workers to open new stores; it expects to finish its June 2000 fiscal year with 136 new
store openings, mostly Chili's locations.
Rising beef prices also have been a cause for concern, though so far the increase in commodity prices there
has been largely offset by declining poultry prices. Additionally, rising short-term interest rates haven't
helped, though that is a factor that affects all stocks, not just Brinker.
And even when some recent experiments haven't fared well -- namely Eatzi's, a prototype specialty-food
retailer that recently flopped in New York City after an initial success in Dallas, and Corner Bakery, a 56-store
chain featuring fresh-baked breads among other things -- analysts credit the company for cutting back on
expansion plans until the concepts can be better refined.
"We want to make sure we get these things right before we roll these out and ramp up," says Brinker's chief
executive and vice chairman, Ron McDougall.
As for the company as a whole, Mr. McDougall says Brinker's long-term target is 15% annual growth in both
revenue and earnings. Asked why the stock isn't reflecting the company's growth, he cracks, "because our
name isn't Brinker-dot-com," a common refrain these days among brick-and-mortar executives. "Right now,
everyone's playing the techs and highfliers with the high P/E ratios," he says. "Everything's going just about
perfect right now, except our stock price."

Gold Rush: Battle Mountain Gold, a Houston gold-mining concern, jumped 33% to $2.50 last week,
reflecting last week's surge in gold prices. Gold closed Friday at $310.40 a troy ounce, up $27.40, or nearly
10%, putting the precious metal within striking distance of its 52-week high of $324.50. The strong news was
tempered somewhat by Battle Mountain's quarterly release of financial results Monday. Citing a write-down of
assets, a change in value of an equity stake and historically low gold prices, the company reported a
fourth-quarter loss of $80 million, or 36 cents a diluted share, compared with a loss of $222.7 million, or 98
cents a share, a year earlier. The stock closed Monday at $2.188.
---
Old Sparky: Shares of Integrated Electrical Services, a Houston consolidator of electrical-contracting
companies, short-circuited last week in reaction to a major profit warning. The stock closed the week at $6,
down 32.4%. Integrated Electrical said earnings for its fiscal first quarter ended Dec. 31 will come in at about
six cents to eight cents a share. That would be 75% to 82% below what analysts had been projecting,
according to First Call, which had an average estimate of 33 cents. The company did say revenue for the
quarter would be strong and in line with expectations, at about $325 million to $335 million.

its parent, Thermo Instrument Systems, will acquire for $17 a share the remaining interest in ThermoQuest
that it doesn't already own. Thermo Instrument, based in Waltham, Mass., holds more than 90% of
ThermoQuest.

Merger DayLifts NasdaqTo a Record
Monday's Markets
Merger Day Lifts Nasdaq To a Record
By Greg Ip
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
586 words
8 February 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
A slew of technology-company merger announcements helped the Nasdaq Composite Index rally to another
record, but elsewhere, blue chips fell back.
Treasury-bond prices also fell sharply, but began to reverse the unusual behavior that dominated debt
markets last week. Gold gave up some of Friday's gains.
The Nasdaq composite climbed 77.63 points, or 1.83%, to its first close above 4300, at 4321.77. The gains
put the tech-dominated index up 6.2% for the year. But the Dow Jones Industrial Average eased 58.01 points,
or 0.53%, to 10905.79, leaving it down 5.1% for the year.
Spurring the Nasdaq were four acquisitions in the sector: Akamai Technologies said it would buy Intervu for
$2.77 billion; Corel said it would acquire Inprise/Borland for $1.07 billion; Kana Communications said it would
buy Silknet Software for about $3.94 billion; and New York Stock Exchange-listed Lucent Technologies said it
would buy Ortel Corp. for $2.92 billion. Although stock in all four of the acquiring companies fell, the mere fact
investors are accepting the stock of companies like Akamai, which has a market value of $21 billion but
negligible sales, as currency is important, traders said.
"People say the valuation is inflated but in many cases the currency is working," said Michael Molnar, head of
retail sales and trading at Salomon Smith Barney.
Meanwhile, the 30-year government Treasury bond fell 29/32 point, or $9.0625 per $1,000 bond, to push up
the yield to 6.337%. It continued to give up the price gains it made through Thursday on panic buying by
investors alarmed that the Treasury Department's planned debt buyback would create a shortage of the
securities. Bonds of shorter maturities fell by less. As a result, 30-year yields are still below shorter-term bond
yields (an "inverted yield curve"), but not as much as they were last week. Furthermore, the prices of riskier
bonds, such as corporate bonds, rallied yesterday, also reversing last week's trend.
"The market is trying to get back to normal," said David Ging, Treasury market strategist at Donaldson, Lufkin
& Jenrette, who attributed yesterday's weakness in Treasury prices to the government's refunding operation
this week. But he added, "The lack of supply in the long end is not going away," and the Federal Reserve has
been raising interest rates. "So there will be a natural tendency for the curve to stay inverted for the
foreseeable future."
Gold, which shot up $23.20 an ounce Friday on news that one gold producer, Placer Dome, would reduce its
sales in the forward market, initially continued the rally yesterday. But after another producer, Barrick Gold,
announced it would reduce forward sales by less than expected, the near-month February contract finished
$8.50 lower at $301.90 an ounce.
Outside the U.S., stocks declined in dollar terms. Excluding U.S. stocks, the Dow Jones World Stock Index
fell 1.30, or 0.69%, to 187.21.
In major market action:
Stocks were mixed and bonds fell. On the Big Board, where 915.1 million shares traded, 1,801 stocks fell and
1,207 advanced.
The dollar rose. Late in New York, it traded at 98.04 cents to the euro compared with 98.27 cents Friday, and
108.682 yen compared with 107.17.

Italian Shares Hit Records Amid SpeculationMore Firms Will Join European Merger Frenzy
World Stock Markets
Italian Shares Hit Records Amid Speculation More Firms Will Join European Merger Frenzy
By Deborah Ball
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,343 words
7 February 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
C18
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
MILAN -- Could a brave new world be dawning in Italy?
Judging from last week's rallies in the Milan stock market, some believe so. The MIBtel index hit records in
four consecutive sessions, reflecting a number of themes that suggest this year may be marked by important
shifts in Italy's corporate world.
On one front are important changes to the traditional mainstays of Italy's economy. Another front is marked by
the arrival of technology-driven developments that have kicked up so much dust elsewhere. The result is a
stock market that many say likely will continue to rise.
"We think the market should rise by about 10% in the first half," says Alessandro Fugnoli, head of research at
brokerage firm Caboto Holding in Milan. "Some overshooting could be possible, but we don't think there will
be serious corrections."
Last week, the MIBtel rose more than 5% on record volume. Driving stocks higher was speculation regarding
a possible accord between Italian car maker Fiat SpA and German rival DaimlerChrysler AG, which pushed
up the Italian group's shares 5% despite denials from both parties. At the same time, telecommunications
concerns Olivetti SpA and Telecom Italia, which is 55% owned by Olivetti, as well as a slate of media and
technology-related shares all moved higher.
The busy week was topped off Friday with the announcement of a takeover of chemicals group Montedison
SpA by the industrial group Compart SpA, its biggest shareholder, thus shortening a chain of command that
had long hobbled the valuations of both. Mediobanca SpA, the powerful bank that controls both companies,
saw its stock rise sharply on rumors of the death of its 92-year-old founder and honorary chairman, Enrico
Cuccia, which proved false.
While it is early, analysts discern in last week's development the beginnings of a structural shift in Italy's
corporate world. Many analysts think Fiat won't be able to hold out much longer before making a big deal that
could mean the end of Italian ownership of the country's largest and most powerful industrial group.
Furthermore, many hope that the Compart-Montedison move could pave the way to a gradual elimination of
the Byzantine ownership structure that characterizes many big companies and protects them from predators,
thus capping market valuations. While Mr. Cuccia is said to be chuckling at reports of his demise, some in the
Milan financial community say the eventual retirement of the man who helped devise this tangled ownership
structure likely will lead to a host of changes among Italy's biggest companies.
"The thing that penalizes Italy is the lack of companies that can be put into play," says Mr. Fugnoli. "But this
will change. . . . It isn't a process that will happen in the space of a few months, but it is gathering strength."
Other analysts are less optimistic, seeing change coming more slowly, if at all. "The Montedison operation
looks like a one-off deal, and I don't see a follow-up of hostile bids or other operations," says Michele
Pezzinga, head of equity research for Eptasim, a brokerage house in Milan. "As for Fiat, there is still the
question of whether they're really going to do a deal. At the end of it all, they could come up with nothing."
Elsewhere, some analysts hope the takeover of German telecommunications concern Mannesmann AG by
rival Vodafone AirTouch, of the United Kingdom, will force the Italian government to think twice about its
protection of oil concern ENI SpA, which has been the odd man out of the oil sector's consolidation dance
because it is a state-owned company. "The Mannesmann deal is certainly a positive sign," Mr. Pezzinga
says. "It could send a warning to the government to stop thinking that companies like ENI should be
untouchable."

--- Page 211 ---
Page 211 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The financial sector also could see a much-needed boost this year. Italian banking stocks have suffered not
as a result of interest-rate increases in recent months, but also from the consensus that the Bank of Italy will
block any hostile takeover attempts that might shake up a long-protected sector. While the central bank's
attitude isn't expected to change, banks and insurers could strike gold with the expected approval of a law
that would free up employees' severance pay to be invested in pension funds, thus releasing a flood of
liquidity into the sector.
---

Deustche Telekom. Industrial concern Siemens gained 6% to 161.50 euros, fueled by talk that it may buy
Mannesmann's automotive and engineering unit.
In MEXICO CITY, the IPC Index gained 193.44, or 2.8%, to 7236.54, following a 4% advance on Thursday.
Telmex's L shares rose 7.2% to 30.40 pesos after the telecommunications concern reported
better-than-expected fourth-quarter profit.

How a Drop in Stocks Could Slam the Economy
Abreast of the Market
How a Drop in Stocks Could Slam the Economy
By Greg Ip
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,650 words
7 February 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Investors usually thank the remarkable economic boom, which last week became the longest in U.S. history,
for making the bull market in stocks possible.
But maybe it is the economy that should be thanking the bull market instead.
In a reversal of how most investors have viewed the situation, soaring stocks appear to be driving many of
the positive economic forces at work, rather than the other way around. They have stoked consumer
spending, padded corporate profits, kept a lid on inflation and chipped away at the budget deficit. So here is
a disturbing thought: If the market falls, do these factors switch into reverse -- further undermining stocks, in a
vicious spiral?
"Despite the positive impact of technology on our economy and financial markets, we have a bit of a Ponzi
scheme going here," William Gross, chief investment officer of Pacific Investment Management Co., wrote
this past fall. In other words, "At least some of our prosperity is based upon prosperity itself."
The traditional view is that economic factors drive stocks, not vice versa. But then, the stock market has
never been as big a part of Americans' lives -- or so exuberant -- as it is now. Last week, the Federal Reserve
Board raised interest rates one-quarter of a percentage point and hinted more increases would come, but
stocks enjoyed yet another positive week. The Dow Jones Industrial Average, which fell 49.64 Friday, or
0.45%, to 10963.80, ended the week up 224.93 points, or 2.1%. The Nasdaq Composite Index, which rose
33.16 points Friday to a record 4244.14, rallied 9.2% on the week.
Robust consumer spending has been the bulwark of the economic boom, but a lot of that consumption is
coming out of investors' stock winnings. Indeed, Fed Chairman Alan Greenspan says this "wealth effect" is
estimated to have contributed a percentage point of the 4% annual growth in gross domestic product since
late 1996.
What happens if this wealth effect goes into reverse? Consider that when stocks corrected in the fall of both
1998 and 1999, consumer confidence took a hit. When stocks rebounded in both of the following Januarys,
so did confidence.
"The stock market has played a large role in the spending binge that's going on," says Mark McClellan, a
senior editor at Bank Credit Analyst Research Group in Montreal. If and when stocks correct, "you'll get a
retrenchment in the household sector. Once people realize they can't get 20% on stocks year after year,
they'll finally realize, `Hey, I have to save a little bit to reach my retirement goals.'"
Hunkered-down consumers won't do much for Wal-Mart Stores' profits. Perhaps Microsoft and General
Electric don't have to worry as much, but then slumping stocks will do different damage to their profits. They
and many other companies have booked hundreds of millions of dollars in profits by selling stakes in
Internetrelated start-ups. Should the Internet stock sector deflate, so will the opportunity to repeat those large
profits.
In addition, some big, mature companies like Lucent Technologies have enjoyed windfall stock gains in their
employee pension plans, enabling them to book pension income instead of expense. Without that pension
benefit, profits of Standard & Poor's 500-stock index companies would have been 3% lower in 1998, Bear
Stearns estimates. Falling stocks will squeeze that income, as well, though it would take awhile to show up.
To be sure, these are small factors in the overall profit picture, but they won't do much to restore confidence
in stocks.
Even if the stock market takes the wind out of consumer spending and profits, it should have the benefit of
easing some of the demand-driven pressure on wages and prices, enabling the Fed to cut interest rates. But

--- Page 214 ---
Page 214 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
there might be a perverse countertrend. The stock market has actually helped in several ways keep a lid on
price and wage increases, a contribution that might disappear in a bear market.
For example, many companies have substituted stock options for wage increases. A team of Fed economists
figure employment costs, which grew 3.1% from 1994 to 1998, would have grown 3.4% if options were
included.
The beauty of performance-based pay like options, of course, is that when times are tough, companies can
reduce their labor bills without layoffs. At such times, employees usually agree to put job security ahead of
wage gains. On the other hand, it's also possible, especially in the Internet-related sector, that "employees
who are working in companies expecting big checks from options, will say, `I'll leave the clicks and go to the
bricks, where it may be less money but at least I'll get it,'" says Chris Varvares, an economist at
Macroeconomic Advisors in St. Louis. If so, to keep valued employees, some companies will be forced to
replace their worthless options with cash.
Even with higher costs, companies may not be able to raise prices, given how fierce competition now is,
especially from Internet competitors. But a stock slump might actually restore some pricing power. Many
start-up companies can sell books, drugs and personal computers at a loss over the Web thanks to cash
infusions from initial public offerings of stock or venturecapital funds, forcing traditional competitors to match.
When red-ink-soaked Amazon.com started selling bestsellers for 50% off, Barnes & Noble's online affiliate
had to match.
If these upstarts disappear, will traditional competitors try to raise pricesmuch as big airlines do once a
discount airline abandons a route? One example: Low-end personal computer prices have risen in recent
months, as some moneylosing vendors were forced to stop giving away PCs or selling super-inexpensive
models.
A falling stock market could also weaken the dollar, whose strength against the euro is highly correlated with
stocks. Trevor Greetham, global strategist at Merrill Lynch, says a rising Dow Jones Industrial Average lifts
the dollar both because foreigners are buying U.S. stocks and because it suggests the U.S. business sector
is a good place to invest. Conversely, "Whenever the Dow slumps you get a big drop in the dollar."
So if stocks go into a protracted decline, so might the dollar -- removing one of the factors that has kept
imports inexpensive and limited the ability of U.S. companies to raise prices. "It does have an impact at the
margin on inflationary pressures," says Mr. Greetham, adding, "The Fed wouldn't be keen to see a
weakening dollar while oil prices are rising."
Even the budget surplus, which has helped hold down bond yields as last week's Treasury buyback
announcement demonstrated, could be eroded by a bear market. Since 1994, when the bull market's most
powerful phase began, the Congressional Budget Office estimates the federal government's capital-gains-tax
take almost tripled to an estimated $100 billion last year. That increase is equal to half the $124 billion fiscal
1999 surplus. A 20% drop in stocks would still leave investors with enormous paper profits, but the Treasury
would nonetheless see an important source of revenue start to shrink.
Taking these factors together, it is possible to imagine a falling stock market weakening the economy and
cutting profits, but at the same time putting upward pressure on inflation and interest rates -- further
undermining stocks. This would clearly be the opposite of historical experience, where a weak economy
paves the way for lower inflation and interest rates and, eventually, a rallying stock market.
But both the market and the economy have broken a lot of the old rules on the way up. They might break
some rules on the way down, as well.
---

This Week Should Tell Whether GoldCan Sustain Uptick After Wild Friday
Commodities
This Week Should Tell Whether Gold Can Sustain Uptick After Wild Friday
By Peter A. McKay
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
774 words
7 February 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
C22
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- The gold market wasn't moving much going into 2000 -- sort of like the giant, millennial Ferris
wheel in London that stalled at the Y2K turnover.
Both rides finally started last week.
Now the far more interesting attraction for investors may be the gold-futures market, which took them on a
roller-coaster ride through 1999 to 20-year lows near $250 an ounce, then back over $325, then to a close
above $285.
After wild trading sessions in New York and London on Friday, the metal's first significant surges of 2000,
analysts said today's trading may be the real harbinger of whether gold can sustain its latest uptick, possibly
with implications for U.S. inflation.
A buying surge, supposedly led by gold producers, started in London, where the metal rose $9.20 to $294.20
an ounce at the London Bullion Market Association. In New York a few hours later, trading became even
more frenzied shortly after the opening, when the Labor Department reported greater-than-expected U.S. job
growth in January, a possible sign of inflation.
Front-month April gold futures rose $23.10, or almost 8%, to $313 an ounce on the Comex division of the
New York Mercantile Exchange, at one point nearing a four-month high.
But with many traders out on a cold, snowy Friday in New York, and sparse volume and open interest in the
weeks leading up to the rally, many traders and analysts said this week will be the real time to watch the gold
market.
"You had everyone trying to walk through the door at the same time" Friday, said a skeptical George Gero,
senior vice president at Prudential Securities. "There were several reasons why, but those factors were hitting
a limited audience. {Today} will be the real test."
Daily gold volume just before Comex's close was about 85,000 contracts, more than double Thursday's total.
But Mr. Gero said that increase still represented just a fraction of the number of futures that changed hands
during the most volatile parts of last year.
Still, Friday's trading came as a surprise in a market that had languished between $280 and $290 for the past
few months.
"What the reduced volume and open interest suggest is that the shorts were getting out of the market and
liquidating their short positions," said senior metals analyst Dave Meger, of Alaron Trading, referring to bets
that prices would fall. "Now, the fact that volume is going back up shows that they're adding longs," or bets
that prices will rise.
Part of the speculation about producer buying was confirmed Friday afternoon when Canadian gold miner
Placer Dome announced it would trim its gold hedge book by at least two million ounces, citing higher gold
prices. Expectations that others might follow then helped to spur the metal's prices higher.
Producer hedging has been a significant factor in the recent bear market in gold, with the forward sales
contributing to price weakness.
Other market observers pointed to bond market tremors as the significant underlying factor in gold's gains.
The benchmark 30-year U.S. Treasury bond soared Thursday, partly on talk that some traders had been
caught with short positions they were unable to fill with cash bonds.

--- Page 217 ---
Page 217 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"Gold does best in times of uncertainty," said Jeffrey Christian, managing director of CPM Group, a New York
research and consulting firm. "There are a number of things that are going to cause people to say, 'I want
some gold this year,' and this is one of them."
In other commodity markets Friday:
HEATING OIL: Futures gained at Nymex, due to soaring cash prices caused by tight supplies, traders said.
The nearby March contract climbed 0.88 cent to 78.78 cents a gallon. Crude oil prices were also carried
higher, with the March future rising 79 cents to $28.82 a barrel. "Crude traders will look at heating oil traders
and say, 'Well, if you're not dropping, neither are we,' " said Victor Yu, an energy analyst at Refco Inc. in New
York.
NICKEL: The three-month contract rose to a 4 1/2-year high at the London Metal Exchange, underpinned by
strong consumer demand and a lack of producer selling, said dealers. The contract rose $75 to $9,060 a
metric ton after hitting $9,120. Demand is expected to exceed supply in 2000 amid buying by the
stainless-steel industry.
-- Matthew F. Gallagher, Janet Whitman

Mixed Blessing: Cutting National Debt Sounds Cool but Upsets Some Well-Laid Plans --- Treasurys Are a Mainstay Of Conservative Investors And...
Mixed Blessing: Cutting National Debt Sounds Cool but Upsets Some Well-Laid Plans --- Treasurys
Are a Mainstay Of Conservative Investors And Serve as Benchmark --- Job Retraining for Traders?
By Gregory Zuckerman and John D. McKinnon
Staff Reporters of The Wall Street Journal
2,027 words
3 February 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
During the 1990s, John Mitchell drove a truck through the lower 48 states towing a "National Debt Clock," a
25-foot computerized scoreboard that totted up second-by-second increases in the federal debt.
Today, the clock sits in a Storage USA shed in Fort Washington, Md. The national debt now is shrinking, not
growing. And Mr. Mitchell is just one of many trying to adjust to the change. "I had a good time," he says,
recalling his days as a harbinger of giant deficits. "I met a lot of people I never would have met."
His nostalgia is shared in the bond-trading rooms of Wall Street, which got a jolt yesterday when the Treasury
Department announced that it was slashing the number of debt auctions it holds. Undersecretary for
Domestic Finance Gary Gensler said the department will now sell one-year Treasurys only four times a year,
instead of every four weeks. It will also reduce the number of new 10-year notes and 30-year bonds it sells.
The government plans to reduce publicly held debt by $17 billion in the current quarter and $152 billion in the
following quarter, the largest debt reduction in U.S. history. Details of the plan sent bond traders scurrying to
buy up remaining government securities yesterday, an action that pushed interest rates on long-term bonds
down on a day when the Federal Reserve was trying to nudge short-term rates up. Thirty-year bonds ended
the day much stronger, with the yield falling to 6.28%.
That exacerbated an anomalous situation known as an inverted yield curve -- lower yields on long-term
Treasurys than on many shorter-term ones -- that is throwing investors for a loop.
For consumers, the dwindling supply of Treasurys is likely to be a benefit, on balance. For one thing, it is
helping to offset at least some of the flagging demand that investors -- smitten by stocks in recent years --
have had for Treasurys. If it weren't for this slipping supply, bond yields would probably be closer to 7%,
analysts say, pushing consumer rateswhich are still tied to Treasurys -- higher.
Further, corporations are issuing bonds and other debt securities at a record pace. Because the Treasury
market has shrunk, investors are increasingly buying up corporate debt, keeping the rates that corporations
must pay lower than they would otherwise be, and helping corporate profits. More investors are also
switching to bonds such as mortgage-backed securities, helping keep a lid on mortgage rates for
homeowners.
The picture looks different to those on trading desks. While the budget deficits of yore caused concern on
Main Street, they meant profits on Wall Street. The government-bond trading business of the 1980s was
portrayed as part of a "modern gold rush" in Michael Lewis's book "Liar's Poker," which both glorified and
gored the business. The Treasury market attracted some of the brightest minds on Wall Street and also
prompted some of the biggest scams, such as Salomon Brothers' 1991 scheme to rig Treasury auctions.
But now that great money machine is being scaled back. Fabled firms have had to downsize. Lehman
Brothers Holdings Inc., which as part of the old Shearson Lehman Brothers employed 56 traders and
salespeople in its Treasury operations, now has fewer than 30 in the group. Japan's Nikko Securities
International, which led a rush by foreign firms to become "primary dealers" in Treasury debt, de-emphasized
the business a year ago.
"We decided to focus on greater opportunities in asset management," says Masao Matsudo, Nikko
International's president. The total number of primary dealers in Treasury securities has dropped to 30 from
46 during the peak debt years.
As the supply of Treasurys has declined, so has trading of the securities. In the 1999 fourth quarter, an
average of about $175 billion worth of Treasury securities changed hands each day, compared with $240

--- Page 219 ---
Page 219 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
billion in the first quarter of 1998, according to Stone & McCarthy Research Associates. Like steel and auto
workers of earlier decades, once-cocky bond traders are being sent off for retraining.
Consider Julie Glennon's situation. She was until last year a veteran Treasury trader at Citigroup Inc.'s
Salomon Smith Barney, the current incarnation of the old Salomon Brothers bond-trading powerhouse. Then
she was switched to trading the bonds of quasi-governmental agencies such as Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac.
"I was asked to make the change, and there are aspects of the market I'm still not familiar with," she says. But
at least this market is growing, she adds.
Experts on the government's debt once were lured out of the Federal Reserve and the Treasury and paid big
bucks for their expertise. No more. Louis Crandall, an authority on the Treasury's bond sales, says even his
market-leading newsletter, Wrightson Associates' Money Market Observer, is seeing subscriptions drop.
While his phone lit up yesterday in the wake of the Treasury's announcement, he knows the attention won't
last.
"It's Indian summer for folks like us," he says. "It feels like a last hurrah. It's wonderful that America is cutting
its debt, but it's terrible for guys like us."
The shrinking government debt is a problem for investors, as well as traders. Treasurys have long been
considered the safest investment in a world of volatile markets. In the summer of 1998, after the Russian debt
crisis, investors from all over the world dumped stocks and corporate bonds and rushed into Treasurys.
But now, with Treasurys in shorter supply, investors are searching for a new benchmark of risk-free
investments. "We've been cut loose, we're drifting," says Gary Goodenough, a bond manager at Loomis
Sayles & Co.
Conservative investors are having to consider riskier investments. Charles Cox, the director of finance for
Farmers Branch, Texas, currently keeps about 35% of his city's $60 million portfolio in Treasurys. But he is
preparing for a day when the city will have to look elsewhere. "We've got a real conservative investment
policy," he says. "If we have to give up Treasurys, it could be a problem."
Where would he turn to replace them? Among possible alternatives, says Mr. Cox, are the securities of
agencies such as Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac, which raise money to buy home mortgages. Both already
are courting investors seeking safety. Fannie Mae, for instance, recently launched a new bond-issuance
program called Benchmark that mirrors traditional Treasury operations in its size and regularity.
Meanwhile, Leland Brendsel, chief executive of Freddie Mac, has gone from quasi-government bureaucrat to
the Ricky Martin of the bond world -- everyone wants a piece of him. "I went to Singapore in December, and
we had no problem getting meetings with the top investors," Mr. Brendsel says. "I travel abroad to meet with
investors every three months. I never used to go abroad."
Efforts by Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac to become bigger players, however, raise concerns among some
policy makers. The two firms are privately owned. Their securities are considered safe largely because the
markets assume that the government, which created both agencies, will stand behind them. If this is true, it
means taxpayers are assuming an ever larger risk as Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac expand, while the
companies' shareholders enjoy profits at taxpayers' expense.
Some big companies are also looking to get a piece of the lucrative business. Ford Motor Co., for instance,
made a splash recently by boosting its bond issuance.
And it means more business off Wall Street, even in a city such as Memphis, Tenn., that was once better
known as a home of boiler-room operators known as "bond daddies."
"As the national debt gets paid off and Treasurys shrink, it plays into our hands," says Deke Iglehart, senior
vice president at First Tennessee Bank near Memphis, one of the biggest underwriters of agency securities
such as Fannie Maes. "Big accounts who used to hang up on us 10 years ago now take our calls, and foreign
central banks are calling us up," says Mr. Iglehart, whose firm's offices share a lobby with a local Navy
recruiting station.
Indeed, foreign investors may be the most affected by the shrinkage of Treasury debt. Such investors,
including central banks, own about 40% of Washington's $3.2 trillion in accumulated marketable debt, up from
just 20% five years ago.
Some foreign investors are already making the move to agency debt and high-grade corporate debt,
according to David Hale, chief economist at Zurich Financial Services of Chicago.

--- Page 220 ---
Page 220 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
As for interest-rate benchmarks, Richard Medley of Medley Global Advisers suggests that an index based on
a mix of government, agency and corporate securities could be used to substitute for the Treasury's role in
determining these. "There's some interest in not doing a single-bond index but doing an index out of several,
so you don't . . . make the world a hostage to the management decision of, say, a Ford," he says. (He notes
that in the 1950s, when government debt was scarce, bonds of a handful of corporations formed the basic
benchmark, with General Motors the prototype.)
For the Federal Reserve, the shrinkage of government debt is particularly tricky, since the Fed uses
Treasurys in conducting open-market operations. The Fed sells Treasury securities to banks when it wants to
drain money from the banking system and thus raise interest rates. And it buys Treasurys when it wants to
increase money in the banking system and lower interest rates. Recently, it has begun using more of the
debt of Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac, as well as mortgage-backed securities issued by those two agencies,
to do its business.
Some economists worry about the Fed giving its stamp of approval to certain types of corporate debt. But
Stephen G. Cecchetti, an Ohio State University economics professor, notes that "we're unusual in the U.S. in
relying on U.S. Treasury issues" in conducting monetary policy. "In most countries, it's done with privately
issued securities, as well as sovereign. . . . Any potential concern about conducting monetary policy is not,
in my view, something to worry about."
For his part, Treasury Secretary Lawrence Summers says that shrinkage of the government debt market is "a
high-class problem compared to the large-scale crowding out of private investment that has been a concern
of past Treasurys." He and many other economists believe the reduction in government borrowing helps keep
down interest rates and means more money is available for private investment.
Martin Baily, chairman of the president's Council of Economic Advisers, says that by his calculation,
inflation-adjusted interest rates "have been 30% to 50% lower" in this expansion than in the debt-ridden
1980s. As a result, he says, investment and growth have been higher, as has consumer confidence.
But not everyone is confident. In midtown Manhattan, not far from Times Square, another digital National
Debt Clock continues to tick away, adding about $1,000 a second, impervious to reports that the national debt
is going down. Douglas Durst, a New York real-estate executive whose father dreamed up the clock, says it
measures total debt, which includes securities held by the Social Security system. The amount of those
securities is still growing, along with the Social Security surplus, which is invested in them. "The debt is still
growing," Mr. Durst insists. "People don't realize it."
Economists argue that securities purchased with surplus Social Security funds are simply a government
bookkeeping transaction, and have no real effect on the economy. The more relevant measure is debt held
by the public, they say, which is declining.
But James Glassman, an economist at Chase Manhattan Bank, says the still-ticking clock is a reflection of
"how deeply the fear of the debt was ingrained in our whole psychology."

The Fed Has No Excuse to Raise Rates Again
The Fed Has No Excuse to Raise Rates Again
By Charles Kadlec
958 words
2 February 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
A26
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Today the Federal Reserve is expected to raise interest rates again. U.S. interest rates are already among
the highest in the developed world. What justification could the Fed possibly have for raising them even
higher?
Record federal deficits were the favorite reason Fed Chairman Alan Greenspan and his predecessor, Paul
Volcker, cited for why interest rates did not decline as fast as inflation in the 1980s and '90s. But now the
$290 billion deficit of 1992 has given way to $150 billion surpluses. So how come the yield on 10-year
Treasury bonds remains as high as 6.75%? What's more, the 11 countries in the European Economic and
Monetary Union have a 10-year government bond yield more than a percentage point below what the U.S.
government has to pay, even though most of the run huge deficits. Why?
If you've been listening to Mr. Greenspan, you may think America's interest rates are high because the U.S.
economy is growing faster, and the unemployment rate is lower, than in Europe. But this excuse is no more
persuasive than the old one. Those who believe high growth causes inflation have it backwards. When
prices are stable, it is easier to do business. Hence, more business gets done, spurring economic growth.
Between 1951 and 1966, the U.S. economy grew at a 3.9% average annual rate. Over this 15-year period,
consumer prices rose only 1.5% a year. Between 1962 and 1966, growth accelerated to a 5.6% annual rate,
but inflation averaged only 1.3%. The yield on 10-year government bonds, first issued in 1953, averaged
less than 4% and never rose above 5%.
Interest rates and inflation did head higher beginning in 1967, as the U.S. began to equivocate on its
commitment to maintain the dollar's buying power in terms of gold. They went higher still in the 1970s, with
the dollar's devaluations and the breakdown of the international monetary system. During the 15 years
beginning in 1967, inflation averaged 7.1%, but growth averaged less than 3% a year. Yet today Mr.
Greenspan interprets the economic growth that has resulted from his success in restoring price stability as a
warning of impending failure.
We should be just as skeptical of the claim that low unemployment augurs inflation. The decline in the
unemployment rate to 4.1% coincides with a six-year decline in manufacturing unit-labor costs. Relative to its
output, the cost of labor in the U.S. today is 10% less than it was when the unemployment rate was 6.9%.
Unemployment is down and labor-force participation is up because U.S. workers are winning the world-wide
competitive battle.
In any case, rising prices, including wages, do not cause inflation. A general increase in prices is inflation.
Blaming inflation on rising prices is like blaming rain on falling water. And there is no reason to think
businesses will be able to pass on higher wages in the form of higher prices, any more than last year's
increase in energy prices led to a generalized increase in the price level. An increase in the price of labor
relative to capital squeezes profit margins. But that is a matter best left to the private economy to sort out.
The Fed has no business taking the side of business relative to its employees.
Even worse than these three excuses is Mr. Greenspan's concern that stock prices are too high. In December
1996 he issued his first warning about "irrational exuberance." Since then, the Dow Jones Industrial Average
has risen 80%. True, the overall market's price/earnings ratio has increased to 30 times current earnings from
17, suggesting that stocks may be overvalued. But virtually all of this increase in ratio can be attributed to the
25 stocks with the biggest market capitalizations in the Standard & Poor's 500 index. Since 1996 the median
p/e of these 25 mega-cap stocks has increased to 48 from 19. By contrast, the p/e ratio for the remaining 475
stocks has risen to only 20 from 17. In other words, Mr. Greenspan is threatening to slow the growth of the
U.S. economy and throw people out of work because 25 stocks may be overvalued.
Booms and bull markets never die of old age. They always succumb to a policy error, like Smoot-Hawley in
the 1920s and tax increases and devaluations in the 1960s and '70s. The economic progress of the 1980s

--- Page 222 ---
Page 222 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
and the 1990s, in large measure, has simply repaired the damage produced by the great inflation of the
1970s. The Dow Jones Industrials, for example, rose above 1000 for the first time on Feb. 6, 1966. The next
day Lyndon Johnson called for an income-tax surtax to finance the Vietnam war. The Dow did not cross 1000
for good until 1982. It did not reach the equivalent of 1000 in 1966 dollars until 1995.
Now is the time for Mr. Greenspan to articulate a policy by which the Federal Reserve can give the markets
confidence that price stability will persist even after the end of his next term. Such a policy should include a
return to the low and stable interest rates that have prevailed for virtually all of peacetime U.S. history. The
Senate should put the issue squarely on the Fed's agenda before it confirms Mr. Greenspan for another
four-year term.
---
Mr. Kadlec is managing director of J. & W. Seligman & Co., a New York investment management firm, and
author of "Dow 100,000: Fact or Fiction" (Prentice Hall, 1999).

The Power Brokers, the Plutocrats and the Prognosticators
The Power Brokers, the Plutocrats and the Prognosticators
1,869 words
1 February 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2000 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Corrections & Amplifications
A karat is a unit of measure for the fineness of gold, while a carat is a unit of weight for precious stones. An
earlier version of this article incorrectly used carat when referring to gold. (WSJ Jan. 30, 2016)
JUST WHEN IT SEEMS AS IF INTERNET STOCKS and beach houses can't get any more expensive, they
do. Incredible, we say, shaking our heads. We've never seen anything like it!
In fact, the current expansion is the nation's 32nd since such records have been kept, starting in 1854. And
many booms have had a great deal in common: the middle class embracing new luxuries, tycoons reaping
prosperity's rewards with new industrial empires, plutocrats feasting upon one wretched excess after another.
The bygone booms all came to an end. As the current one breaks an endurance record, here's an instructive
look at the ones that came before it.
Late 19th Century
-- Boom time: 1880 to 1900. With the economy expanding rapidly in this period, the U.S. overtakes Great
Britain as the world's largest manufacturer. But the nation never actually enjoys more than three years without
a recession. In total, the two decades have six expansions and six recessions.
-- The boom's engineS: Recovery from the 1873-1879 depression, the longest economic contraction in
American history; immigration and industrialization; development of national railroads and telegraph system.
-- Power brokers: President Grover Cleveland and influential banker J. P. Morgan work together to protect the
gold standard, and with it European investment in American industry.
-- Mid-expansion scare: Railroad overbuilding leads to scores of train-related bankruptcies and financial panic
in 1893.
-- Tycoons: John D. Rockefeller's Standard Oil becomes the model for industry-controlling trusts. Andrew
Carnegie's steel empire exemplifies vertical integration, from extraction to finished goods.
-- New bounty for the middle-class: The electric light, from Thomas Edison's General Edison Electric Co.;
Alexander Graham Bell's telephone.
-- Reading list: "Social Statics" by Herbert Spencer, coining the phrase "survival of the fittest" (later picked up
by Charles Darwin) about social inequality as a natural outgrowth of competition.
-- Wretched excess: New York's nouveaux riches build Fifth Avenue chateaus. Plutocrats smoke cigarettes
rolled in $100 bills. One tycoon presents his dog with a $15,000 diamond collar.
The 1920s:
-- Boom time: Though hailed in history books as the "Roaring '20s," the decade is actually punctuated by two
recessions. The expansions last from July 1921 to May 1923, July 1924 to October 1926, and November
1927 to August 1929.
-- The boom's engines: Recovery from post-World War I depression; productivity burst resulting from the
spread of electricity and electric-powered motors for manufacturing.
-- Power brokers: Treasury Secretary Andrew Mellon pushes tax cuts to stimulate business investment.
Federal Reserve Bank of New York President Benjamin Strong pushes easy money policy until his death in
1928. President Calvin Coolidge declares, "The chief business of the American people is business."

--- Page 224 ---
Page 224 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
-- Tycoons: David Sarnoff, whose Radio Corporation of America popularizes home radios while the
company's stock becomes a high-tech speculative bubble.
-- New bounty for the middle-class: Car ownership jumps from seven million in 1919 to 23 million in 1929.
-- Mid-expansion scare: The Florida land bubble -- in which one lot in a Miami Beach business center
reportedly surged in value from $800 to $150,000 -- bursts in 1926, following a severe hurricane.
-- Reading list: Ladies Home Journal article "Everybody Ought to be Rich," by business leader and
Democratic National Committee chair John J. Raskob; F. Scott Fitzgerald's "The Great Gatsby," on empty
lives of tycoons and flappers.
-- Theme song: Folks can't stop humming "Ain't We Got Fun."
-- Wretched excess: It's Prohibition, and speak-easies flourish. The famous grande dame of the business,
"Texas" Guinan, earns $700,000 in a 10-month period.
-- Other signs of silliness: The nation obsesses over setting wacky records. The Saturday Evening Post
observes Americans are "first in War, first in peace, first in tree-sitting, gum-chewing, peanut-pushing and
bobbing up and down in water." "Shipwreck" Kelly spent 145 days on various flagpoles in 1924.
-- Wise men turn cocky: Eminent Yale University economist Irving Fisher declares in mid-October 1929:
"Stock prices have reached what looks like a permanently high plateau."
-- Causes of death: Slowing industrial production; October 1929 stock market crash; a monetary policy
made overly tight in part because of the gold standard, bank runs, credit crunch.
The 1960s:
-- Boom time: February 1961 to December 1969
-- The boom's engineS: A productivity surge, fueled by private sector embrace of war-time technologies such
as plastics; massive government stimulus resulting from JFK's tax cut and heavy spending on Vietnam War.
-- Power brokers: Lyndon B. Johnson expands government entitlements to include Medicare and Medicaid.
White House economic advisor Walter Heller advocates testing the limits of how low unemployment can go
before it triggers inflation.
-- Mid-expansion scare: In 1966, an interest-rate rise and stock-market swoon create what's widely called a
"crisis of confidence" in the economy.
-- Tycoons: International Telephone & Telegraph Corp., headed by Harold Geneen, symbolizes big
business's infatuation with conglomeration, acquiring Avis Rent-a-Car, Levitt home builders, Continental
Baking, a motel chain and a publishing company.
-- New bounty for the middle-class: America becomes a suburban nation. For the first time, more people live
around cities (38%) than in them (31%) or in the country (31%).
-- Reading list: "The New Industrial State," by John Kenneth Galbraith, argues that big government is needed
to counter the influence of a business sector grown so big that it begins to resemble Soviet-style central
planning. "Le Defi americain" ("The American Challenge"), by Jean-Jacques Servan-Schreiber, warns against
Europe becoming an economic colony
of the U.S.
-- Wretched excess: With "Dr. No" in 1962, movie audiences get a taste of the 007 lifestyle -- martinis,
electronics, tuxedos, casinos. The appetite will last for years.
-- Theme Song: For many weeks, it's hard to turn on a radio without hearing the Beatles' 1964 hit "Can't Buy
Me Love."
-- Wise men turn cocky: LBJ envisions "an end to poverty . . . in our time"; the Commerce Department in 1968
changes the name of its monthly periodical "Business Cycle Developments" to "Business Conditions Digest,"
declaring the business cycle passe.
-- Cause of death: Deficit spending and 3.5% unemployment rate push up inflation, prompting the Fed to
raise rates. The central bank's role is to "take away the punch bowl just when the party gets going," says
Chairman William McChesney Martin, Jr.

--- Page 225 ---
Page 225 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The 1980s:
-- Boom time: November 1982 to July 1990
-- The boom's engine: Recovery from shock of high oil prices and deep recessions; stimulus from heavy
defense spending and Reagan tax cuts; deregulation; stock-market boom
-- Power brokers: Fed Chairman Paul Volcker's 13% interest rates -- which pushed the prime rate to 21.5%
-- quash double-digit inflation. His successor, Alan Greenspan, responds to the 1987 stock-market crash
with easy money. President Reagan offers fiscal stimulus and a symbolic attack on Big Labor by breaking
air-traffic controllers' strike.
-- Tycoons: Leveraged-buyout king Henry Kravis and junk-bond pioneer Michael Milken.
-- Mid-expansion scare: Stock-market crash of Oct. 19, 1987, when the Dow Jones industrials plummet 508
points.
-- New bounty for the middle class: Among U.S. households, VCR ownership rises to 58% from 1% during the
period. The personal computer is in 45 million households, up from two million.
-- Reading list: Paul Kennedy's "The Rise and Fall of Great Powers," tracing the decline of great Western
nations from the Hapsburg empire to the present, is widely interpreted as suggesting that America's turn is
next.
-- Wretched Excess: The gold Rolex and the BMW emerge from the '80s as the period's ultimate badges of
wealth.
-- Theme Song: Madonna's "Material Girl"
-- Wise men turn cocky: President Bush and aides dismiss signs that economy is losing strength. Five months
after expansion ended, chief of staff John Sununu says in a speech, "There's not a single piece of legislation
that need be passed in the two years" left in the administration. "In fact, if Congress wants to come together,
adjourn and leave, it's all right with us," he says.
-- Cause of death: The Fed raises rates to quell rising inflation. Meanwhile the nation is hit with a credit
crunch, a surge in oil prices and a plunge in consumer confidence from Iraq's invasion of Kuwait.
The 1990s:
-- Boom time: March 1991 to ?
-- The boom's engine: Burst in productivity from spread of computers, restructuring of major manufacturers,
expansion of financial markets; low interest rates from elimination of federal budget deficit; globalization.
-- Power brokers: Fed Chairman Greenspan successfully engineers 1994's famous "soft landing," taming
inflation without triggering a broad downturn. Later, he tolerates low unemployment and manages the 1998
Asian crisis. Treasury Secretary Robert Rubin persuades new Democratic President Clinton to push deficit
reduction over big government spending.
-- New bounty for the middle class: More than 70 million Americans surf the Internet in December, and during
1999 22 million buy something online -- two activities unheard of at the beginning of the current boom. The
percentage of households owning stock rises to 48.2% in 1998 from 32.5% in 1989, fueled in part by the 75%
rise in 401(k) retirement-plan ownership, to 28 million participants, from 16 million.
-- Mid-expansion scare: The 1997-1998 Asian crisis, which causes a short-term seizing up of U.S. credit
markets and a brief stock-market dive.
-- Tycoons: Microsoft Corp.'s Bill Gates becomes the world's richest man by dominating software. Sunbeam
Corp.'s "Chain Saw Al" Dunlap personifies new labor-market flexibility by eliminating half the company's work
force four months after taking over -- and eventually gets fired himself.
-- Reading list: "The End of History and the Last Man," by Francis Fukuyama, argues that the ideological
struggle defining the 20th century's latter half is over, as the world embraces democratic free-market
capitalism.
-- Wretched excess: Financier Ira Rennert plans a 60,000-square-foot ocean-front palace in Southampton,
N.Y., at a cost of $100 million. A couple in the Santa Barbara, Calif., area builds a 10,000-square-foot
ballroom for their home, complete with 24-carat gold leafing and orchestra pit.

--- Page 226 ---
Page 226 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
-- Theme song: "It's All About the Benjamins," a rap hit referring to $100 bills, by Puff Daddy & the Family
-- Wise men turn cocky: Market experts justify Yahoo! price/earnings ratio of 735. With the Dow hovering
around a record 11,000, books titled "Dow 36,000" and "Dow 100,000" get published; President Clinton
declares in his final State of the Union address that "the state of our union is the strongest it has ever been."
-- What might cause its death?: Inflation sparked by 4% unemployment; stock- market crash; capital flight
from foreign investors.

A Century of Booms, and How They Ended---If the U.S. Economic ExpansionSeems Prolonged, ConsiderSome of the Marathons Abroad
A Century of Booms, and How They Ended --- If the U.S. Economic Expansion Seems Prolonged,
Consider Some of the Marathons Abroad
A Wall Street Journal News Roundup
1,034 words
1 February 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
The nine-year U.S. economic boom, impressive as it is, has been a leisurely trot beside marathon growth
spurts overseas in the past 50 years.
Japan and South Korea have at different times racked up decade-plus runs of growth as high as 10% a year
or more. America in the 1990s hasn't come close. Starting in 1983, Chile went 16 years without a major
downturn. Economies in Asia and Western Europe after World War II boomed while playing catch-up with the
U.S. As Richard Reid, an economist at Donaldson, Lufkin & Jenrette noted, "It's pretty easy to grow if your
economy is starting at zero."
Zero is the place many European countries and Japan found themselves following World War II, when Japan
and its "economic miracle" of the 1950s and '60s set a standard that's unlikely to be eclipsed in the developed
world soon. If a boom is defined as a period of uninterrupted economic growth, Japan boomed for about 20
years straight between the early 1950s and the early 1970s -- and then again for another 15 years until the
early 1990s. The benchmark streak was the 57-month "Izanagi boom" of November 1965 to July 1970,
named after the god said to have created the islands of Japan.
In those years, Japan was building its industrial base and borrowing advanced technologies from the U.S.
and Europe. The nation had all the ingredients for growth: a well-educated work force, peace at home,
political stability. Companies poured money into advanced factories for electronics, chemicals and steel.
Workers streamed into factory cities; productivity skyrocketed. Japan's whole economy was built on the
assumption that everything -- incomes, productivity, population -- would expand indefinitely. Every
able-bodied person was virtually guaranteed a job.
Then came the famed "bubble economy" of the late 1980s, officially the second-longest postwar boom in
Japan.
Stoked by cheap credit from the central bank, which spurred real-estate and stock bull runs and investment
in still more factories, the era was marked by glittering excess. Department stores thrived, peddling diamonds
and Italian suits. Restaurants sprinkled gold flakes over soba noodles.
The bubble burst in 1990, when Japan's economy reached maturity and its growth formula soured. In the
Tokyo neighborhood of Sendagi, a long-abandoned real-estate office still posts a relic from the go-go days --
an ad for a small three-bedroom apartment, priced at $1.8 million. Today, that property would go for a few
hundred thousand dollars.
Booms in South Korea, meanwhile, transformed that country in about three decades from one of the poorest
to the 11th-largest economy in the world before the Asian financial crisis. Since 1963, when the economy
grew 9.1%, South Korea has experienced only two years of economic contraction -- the worst being 1998,
when the economy sank 5.8%.
In a steroidal version of the Japanese model, South Korean dictator Park Chung Hee created a system
placing the country's resources under state control. The government decided which projects and industries to
pursue, chose the companies to pursue them, and allocated financing through a state-controlled banking
system. This created sprawling, family-run conglomerates that became household names around the world --
Hyundai and Samsung, for example.
South Korea followed a typical formula, initially exporting labor-intensive products like textiles and wigs,
before moving into automobiles and steel and then computer chips. South Koreans went from wading through
paddies to driving Hyundais through cities lined with skyscrapers. In 1965, 55% of all South Koreans lived on
farms. By 1997, less than 10% did.

--- Page 228 ---
Page 228 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
But all this fell apart in the Asian economic crisis of 1997. Government planners made too many wrong bets,
the conglomerates' debts grew unsustainable and the system crashed. South Korea is growing again, but
only after a painful restructuring.
A similar scenario unfolded in Europe after the war, as Western countries rebuilt their economies. U.S.
investment and a bulge in the population of work-age adults sparked a virtuous cycle, in which labor was
cheap, tax revenues were high and individual spending escalated.
While Germany, Britain and France were the chief drivers of the boom, other European countries experienced
similar growth, which Mr. Lewis estimates at 5% or more during 1948 through the mid-'60s.
The monuments to this growth are easily recognizable consumer durables. "In Italy, I'd say it was the Vespa
motor scooter," says Deutsche Bank economist Michael Lewis, while in the United Kingdom in the 1950s, the
low-slung sportscar became a symbol of consumer-driven prosperity.
Then the population bulge disappeared, leading to looming government pension problems and a shrinking tax
base. European currencies, pegged to the dollar, suffered inflationary pressures. And an oil-price spike in
1973 drove a stake through the heart of the postwar European boom. In 1975, the region's economy
contracted for the first time in a generation.
Since 1981, the expansion of the European Union economy has dipped into the red only once, in 1992. But
growth has been far less robust than it has been in the U.S. Mr. Lewis says pension and tax reforms in
European countries could foster another period of stunning growth if sweeping industrial deregulation
continues apace.
"But something tells me the pace of reform will be painfully slow in the midterm," he says.
In Latin America, booms have been susceptible to bruising busts. Chile's long period of growth ended last
year because of falling prices for the raw materials it produces.
Last year marked the end of an even longer growth streak in Colombia. The Colombian economy contracted
by 5% in 1999, the first year of negative growth since 1931.
While Colombia has free-market economic policies, analysts cite another reason for the strong economy over
the decades: cocaine.
---
This article was reported by Christopher Cooper, Peter Landers, Matt Moffett and Michael Schuman.
(See related article: "The Usual Causes of Death -- Inflation and Inept Policies -- Aren't Afoot in America Yet"
-- WSJ Feb. 1, 2000)
