Dollar Leaps Against Major Currencies;Euro at Low as U.S. Asset Markets Climb
Foreign Exchange
Dollar Leaps Against Major Currencies; Euro at Low as U.S. Asset Markets Climb
By Jennifer Barrett
Dow Jones Newswires
584 words
1 February 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
C21
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Fueled by a rally in U.S. asset markets, the dollar continued to surge against most major
currencies, building on gains made last week.
The dollar rose to a 10-year high against the Swiss franc and hit its highest levels in more than three months
against the yen. The euro, meanwhile, fell further, tumbling to an all-time low against the dollar, as investors
flocked to dollar-denominated assets ahead of an expected U.S. interest-rate rise Wednesday.
Late yesterday, the dollar was trading at 107.38 yen, up from 107.12 yen late Friday. The euro was at 97.05
cents, down from 97.54 cents Friday. The dollar also was at 1.6571 Swiss francs, up from 1.6475 francs
Friday, while the U.K. pound was at $1.6155, down from $1.6191 Friday.
Against the Swiss franc, the dollar finished only slightly lower than its high for the day of 1.6616 francs.
Europe's common currency, meanwhile, suffered its worst losses yet, skidding to a record low of 96.75 cents
midway through the New York session after a brief morning rally. It had managed to claw its way up to 98.27
cents after the European Central Bank's president, Wim Duisenberg, warned a weakening euro could spark
euro-zone inflation. The comments prompted some analysts to speculate the ECB will decide to raise
interest rates at its meeting Thursday.
Still, more analysts believe the central bank will leave rates alone. Even if the ECB raises rates, an
increasing number of market participants say that may not be enough to stimulate a sustained euro recovery.
"A lot of people are just throwing in the towel now," said Tom Devine, vice president at Dresdner Bank's
foreign-exchange desk in New York.
Meanwhile, commodity-sensitive currencies continued to struggle against the dollar, after posting some of
their worst losses in months Friday.
The South African rand and the New Zealand, Australian, and Canadian dollars were hit hardest after gold,
silver and copper prices dropped sharply Friday as the U.S. dollar surged.
While the currencies managed a partial rebound, their recovery stalled as many market participants stepped
to the sidelines in anticipation of the meeting of the Federal Reserve's Federal Open Market Committee on
Tuesday and Wednesday.
"People are licking their wounds from Friday; they don't know what to do," said Andrew Busch, director of
foreign ex change at Bank of Montreal in Chicago.
The Fed is widely expected to raise interest rates at least 25 basis points, or hundredths of a percentage
point, a move some analysts believe could be matched soon after by central banks in other dollar-block
countries.
While commodity-sensitive currencies came off Friday's lows, Mr. Busch said their movements this week
largely will depend on what action the Fed takes after it ends its meeting Wednesday.
Late yesterday in New York, the dollar had slipped slightly from Friday's levels. It was quoted at 1.4458
Canadian dollars, little changed from C$1.4453 late Friday. The dollar was at 6.3075 South African rand,
down from 6.3383 rand Friday.
The Australian dollar was buying 63.69 U.S. cents, up from 63.24 cents Friday. The New Zealand dollar was
at 49.60 U.S. cents, up from 49.19 cents Friday.

Dollar's Gains Expected to Continue;Euro Woes Further Sign of U.S. Strength
Foreign Exchange
Dollar's Gains Expected to Continue; Euro Woes Further Sign of U.S. Strength
By Jennifer Barrett
Dow Jones Newswires
671 words
31 January 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
C19
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- The dollar is expected to strengthen against the euro, yen and other counterparts this week,
building on sharp across-the-board gains late last week.
Interest-rate increases in the U.S., Europe and dollar-block countries -- as well as the continued weakness of
the euro -- will be the top issues for currency markets this week, with many players taking their cue from the
U.S.
The Federal Reserve Open Market Committee is expected to raise its benchmark lending rate by
0.25-percentage point at its midweek meeting. Speculation regarding whether central banks in the European
Union, Australia, and Canada will follow suit led to wild swings for their currencies during the past week.
Most believe the Bank of Canada and the Reserve bank of Australia will mirror the Fed's rate increase.
Analysts are divided over what action -- if any -- the European Central Bank will take at its meeting
Thursday. Developments late last week may cause consternation in the loftiest of financial circles.
U.S. trading turned very volatile Friday when at least one hedge fund was forced to unwind long positions in
currency and fixed-income markets, dealers said. During the flight to safety, the dollar advanced significantly
on its counterparts, yet finished off intraday highs.
Hedge-fund moves weren't the whole story. The U.S. currency gained nearly three yen Friday to peak at
107.26 yen, its highest level of the week. Strong technical resistance remains at 108 yen and 109.20 yen. On
Friday the dollar was at 107.12, compared with 105.16 Thursday.
Weighed down by evidence that the U.S. economy will outpace the euro zone's 11 countries this year,
Europe's single currency plummeted to all-time lows against the dollar and other major currencies this past
week.
"It's about the growth differential between the U.S. and Europe," said Stephen Jonathon, director of currency
desk at Merrill Lynch in New York.
He said the better-than-expected 5.8% increase in the U.S. fourth-quarter gross domestic product offers "a
heck of a lot more" in terms of outlook for return on investments than the euro zone's 2.5%3.0% growth-rate
range.
Friday's data sent speculative buyers scurrying to sell euros for dollars. As dealers struggled to cover short
dollar positions, the euro skidded to an all-time low of 97.54 cents.
"Partly it's a weak euro, but it's more a dollar strength story," said Marc Chandler, chief currency strategist at
Mellon Financial Corp. in Pittsburgh.
He said on a trade-weighted basis, the U.S. dollar rose 2% against most major currencies, according to the
Bank of England index. Even as U.S. stocks fell heavily, the dollar gained on just about every major currency
Friday.
Analysts expect the dollar to advance this week as the U.S. economy chugs along despite the threat of a Fed
tightening. The expected rate increase might slow growth, but the U.S. economy is expected to outpace other
major countries, including the euro zone's 11 members.
Sales of gold and other commodities to buy dollar-denominated assets caused commodity-sensitive
currencies such as the Australian and the Canadian dollar to tumble Friday, with the U.S. dollar trading as
high as 1.4505 Canadian dollars before closing at 1.4453.

--- Page 232 ---
Page 232 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Analysts dismissed the move as a knee-jerk reaction to the strong economic news out of the U.S., calling it a
"temporary setback" for the Canadian currency, which has been one of the top performers against the U.S.
dollar this year.
Meanwhile, the Australian dollar dropped more than 4% against the dollar Friday after Australian Consumer
Price Index figures came in below projections. That diminished expectations that Australia's central bank
would increase rates by half a percentage point at its meeting next week.

How the Longest Boom Transformed Main Street---B.F. Goodrich Rolled Out --But Roaring Economy HelpsRubber Capital Bounce Back
How the Longest Boom Transformed Main Street --- B.F. Goodrich Rolled Out -- But Roaring Economy
Helps Rubber Capital Bounce Back
By Timothy Aeppel and John McKinnon
Staff Reporters of The Wall Street Journal
4,290 words
25 January 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
AKRON, Ohio -- Keep the champagne glasses handy. Next week will be cause for yet another celebration.
On Tuesday, the current economic boom becomes the longest in the nation's history, entering its record
107th month as measured by the National Bureau of Economic Research, which looks at industrial
production, employment, and gross domestic product. The longest previous stretch of growth unbroken by
recession was the 106-month run from February 1961 through December 1969, which ended when Vietnam
War spending pushed inflation out of control.
This boom has been remarkably resilient, given that the decade kicked off with the recession of 1990-1991
and the Gulf War. The current expansion, which began in March 1991, survived a string of potential
catastrophes: the Asian financial crisis, Russia's economic meltdown, a presidential impeachment,
derivatives, droughts, hurricanes and mad cows.
Yet the good times rolled. Stocks have gone through the stratosphere, 20 million new jobs have been created
and the numbers of wireless phone users in the U.S. has increased more than tenfold. This boom predates
"ER" and has outlasted "Seinfeld."
And wealth did more than trickle down. It gushed -- through Silicon Valley and Wall Street, splashing right
onto Main Street, to rich families and those not so well off, to high-tech start-ups and plastic recyclers. If the
'80s, the so-called Decade of Greed, showed that wild and notorious fortunes enriched a few, the '90s
showed that the spread of wealth was, if not purely democratic, at least more egalitarian.
That went a long way in supplanting the insecurity of the 1980s, when the whole notion of employment for life
was shattered, with something else: a sense of well-being. The old security has by no means returned --
companies still lay off workers in droves, many people can still find only "temp" jobs, and the number of
families without health insurance grows every year. Yet that kind of churning seems less threatening than it
did at the decade's dawn. If a big company is taken over now, something else, a computer start-up or a
finishing plant, often pops up.
One corner of the country that embodies the reach and flavor of this historic boom is a 38-acre tract off South
Main Street here in Akron called Canal Place. By the late 1980s, the complex -- the size of 44 football fields,
where B.F. Goodrich made rubber bands, golf balls and space suits -- closed its doors, the victim of foreign
competition and modern regional plants.
"Operation Green Grass," an oddly upbeat name for a demolition project, began bulldozing the last buildings
of the onetime largest tire plant in the world, with 28,000 employees. Akron's unemployment rate reached
7.9% at the height of the last recession, in no small part because of Goodrich's blow.
An investor group specializing in industrial conversion saw a bargain, and snatched up the 27 buildings that
survived the wrecking ball for $2.5 million. Small manufacturers, lured by cheap rent and low-interest city
loans, were among the first tenants, along with a short-lived flea market. Just over a decade later, occupancy
now exceeds 90% and annual revenue from rent has reached $5 million.
The 106 tenants represent the confluence of the old and new economy. A thriving gourmet food operation is
next door to a plastic lumber manufacturer. A Time Warner Inc. cable business and Cable & Wireless PLC
opened offices here, as have five Internet-service providers and an administrative office for Bank One Corp.,
the nation's fourth-largest banking company. Amid the roaring market for business and consumer loans,
particularly auto loans, Bank One has doubled, to more than 600, the number of its Canal Place workers
since 1992. The mix is neither all goods nor all services, but a healthy blend of both.

--- Page 234 ---
Page 234 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Indeed, one reason this boom has been sustained is because all types of businesses have grown and
become stronger. Ohio's gross state product has grown 47% from 1991 to 1998. But manufacturing remains
about the same level -- 26.2% compared with 27% in 1991. Same for services, which make up 18% of the
state's gross state product, up from 17% in 1991.
That manufacturing-service equilibrium intact, all these businesses have transformed the South Main Street
area into the hot end of town. In addition to new businesses, a baseball park and restaurants, there are plans
for a multi-screen theater and retail complex.
The boom has left some gaps in Akron. Despite the city's redevelopment heroics, some downtown blocks, not
far from Canal Place, remain run-down. While unemployment has fallen to 3.8%, and wages have risen, large
segments of the population continue to struggle.
But there are also striking examples of bouncing back, like Dave Gronauer, here in the Rubber Capital of the
world. Laid off at age 50 when Goodrich left, the former information-services manager gambled his pension
money to start a computer graphics company, Enterprise Information Services. Searching for office space, he
wound up in the same Goodrich factory building where he'd first gone to work, fresh out of Michigan State, in
1962. "It was a little strange," he says.
But the downtown location turned out to be perfect for hopping onto major highways. At first, his new
business grew rapidly, thanks largely to a nearby lift-truck maker that needed lots of technical manuals. Then
in 1995, the client abruptly pulled up stakes, taking three-quarters of EIS's revenue with it. So he took a
second gamble, deciding to hang on to the core of his team. He was fearful he'd never be able to rebuild it,
and worried about casting his young staff adrift.
The next year, Time Warner selected EIS as a contractor for a project to test a cable modem called
RoadRunner in Akron-area homes. Since then, EIS has turned the Internet into a new core business, winning
important Web-page design work for companies like Rubbermaid and Goodyear.
Aside from keeping his business on a roll, Mr. Gronauer takes satisfaction from helping employees get
ahead. A receptionist he hired with a cancer diagnosis has recovered, and married another of his employees.
"Even if we go bankrupt tomorrow, everybody here can find another job somewhere else and make more
money," he says. "What more can you ask?"
While it's true that incomes between the affluent and the poor widened as a whole during the 1990s, the
boom still hit people who were used to being bypassed.
John Hosbach, for one, can scarcely believe that his income has nearly tripled. "If you told me five years ago,
I'd be where I am here, making what I am, I would have said you're crazy," says Mr. Hosbach, a 29-year-old
factory manager at Plastic Lumber Co., which opened here in 1989.
His employer thrived as a strong economy and environmental sensitivities generated demand for its products,
such as picnic tables made of recycled plastic. The labor market was tight, making good workers like Mr.
Hosbach all the more valuable.
He started in 1993, after showing up for an interview and getting an offer on the spot. He worked an overnight
job on the factory floor, but was quickly promoted. At one point, he was putting away $250 from each
biweekly check into savings. He splurged $1,000 on a golf vacation to Hilton Head, S.C.
Yet for him and thousands of other lower-skilled workers, the percentage gains are impressive but don't
translate into easy street, especially when his base was so low -- $5.50 an hour.
Mr. Hosbach feels frustrated. He hasn't put a dent in his and his wife's debt, including $20,000 in credit-card
bills. He has a lot of company, given the rising levels of household debt overall. And he isn't living large. He
bought a modest house, doing much of the initial fix-up work himself. And his mother-in-law sometimes helps
out. After Thanksgiving -- when some of the family was relegated to folding chairs in the Hosbachs' dining
room -- she bought them a new dining room set.
He doesn't have stock or mutual-fund investments, but that part of the boom has swept up millions of others.
A record number of Americans at all income levels now own stock, sparking sharp increases in the net worth
of most families, and letting them indulge whims like never before.
One is Brad Sacks, whose More Than Gourmet food business is next door to Plastic Lumber. Every day, Mr.
Hosbach drives his 1988 Beretta, with 140,000 miles, right past Mr. Sacks's black Mercedes E430.
Mr. Sacks bought property where he's building a turreted, five-bedroom, four-car garage house, in part out of
the 50% return he saw on his investment portfolio in just six months last year. In 1997, he began plowing
savings from his $100,000-plus a year salary into five mutual funds. The 33-year-old entrepreneur is amazed

--- Page 235 ---
Page 235 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
that the neighborhood is filled with young families and small children. "People just aren't waiting for things the
way they used to," he says.
He launched More Than Gourmet in 1994 with the help of his father, who has an international trading
company that buys spare parts for foreign industrial companies and serves as a manufacturer's rep for tire
equipment. More Than Gourmet specializes in selling demi-glace, the highly concentrated veal stock used in
fine sauces. It caters to restaurants and high-end food stores nationwide with names like Glace de Poulet
Gold. Its containers are adorned with an image of the company's imaginary mascot, "Chef Louie." And the
business has flourished in this era of high-end consumption. He can afford to trade in sleek cars after a year,
collect expensive wines and build his dream home. It's supposed to be finished in June -- but there might be a
snag. "There's a shortage of bricks," he says, the result, his builder tells him, of the current boom in
construction.
His gourmet business has outgrown its space in Canal Place. The Sacks are in the process of building new
space on the outskirts of Akron for their food and international trading business. Their Canal Place office
space won't be vacant for long. Plastic Lumber plans to take it over and use it for a front office.
---
Flying High
The stock market isn't the only thing that's soaring. U.S. carriers
have seen an increase in total miles flown from 4.3 billion in 1991 to
about 5.8 billion last year, according to the Federal Aviation
Administration. While the length of the average trip has remained
relatively steady, people are flying more than ever before: Travelers
boarded planes 629,396 times last year, up from 452,781 in 1991.
Love Those
SUVs
"Bigger is better" has been the rule when it comes to cars during
this economic boom. Car sales have remained fairly flat over the
decade, according to Autodata Corp., but sales of light trucks have
nearly doubled, thanks largely to the now ubiquitous sport-utility
vehicle. Despite rising gasoline prices, SUV sales topped three
million in 1999, up from 909,925 in 1991.
The Big Splurge
The booming economy put many Americans in a mood to splurge.
Champagne sales to retailers bubbled from 945,000 cases in 1991 to an
estimated 1.7 million in 1999, according to the Impact Databank. That
was no millennial fluke: revellers bought more than 1.4 million cases
in 1998.
The jewelry sector is sparkling too. Sales rose 45% from 1991 to
1998, the Census Bureau's retail survey found. Last year's sales
through October -- $15.4 billion -- outpaced all of 1991. Once the
glittering holiday season is tallied, 1999 will be another gem of a
year.
The cigar craze seems to be burning out, however. Imports of premium
cigars quadrupled from 1991 to 1997, according to the Cigar
Association of America. They fell 21.7% in 1998.
Bigger Castles
The size of the average new home has expanded along with the economy.
Square footage grew 5.5% from 1991 to 1998, says the National
Association of Home Builders. Some 16% of houses are now built with
garages for three or more cars.
Home ownership hasn't grown as fast: the portion of Americans who own
their homes is up just 2.2 percentage points to 66.3% since 1991, the
Census Bureau says.
Americans are spending more on home additions and improvements --
$325 billion last year, nearly twice as much as in 1991. But we're
spending less on maintenance and repairs: $157.3 billion last year,
down from $199.9 billion seven years earlier. The average price of a
new home now exceeds $200,000.
Bullish on Stocks
The bull market has drawn more Americans into stock ownership. Nearly
49% of US households owned equities last year, up from 36% in 1992,
according to the Investment Company Institute. The typical equity
owner is 47 years old, and has a household income of $60,000.The same
study found that 11% of investors traded over the Internet during 1998.

--- Page 236 ---
Page 236 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Insufficient Funds
Personal income has grown -- but not as fast as some people have
spent it. Bankruptcy filings rose 52.8% from 1991 to 1998, reaching
1.4 million, according to the American Bankruptcy Institute. The
number of businesses seeking
bankruptcy protection actually dropped
during that period. But consumer filings rose and now make up 96.9% of
total bankruptcies.
Jobs, Jobs, Jobs
Economic expansion has created 20 million new jobs since January
1993. In fact, the
Department of Labor says a larger percentage of
the population is employed than ever before. Even so, the portion of
Americans with health insurance has fallen 2.2% since the start of the
boom.
You've Got Gadgets!
Prosperity and technology have grown hand in hand, sometimes to the
annoyance of restaurant patrons trying to hold conversations at the
next table. The army of cell phone users in the U.S. is now 86.4
million strong -- up tenfold during the 1990s, according to the Yankee
Group.
Shipments of personal computers have reached 14.9 million annually,
with the average price falling to $1,200, says the Consumer
Electronics Manufacturers Association. Widespread computer ownership
has helped feed the Internet frenzy. Nielsen/NetRatings estimates that
119.2 million people had access to the Internet during the month of
December 1999, with 74.1 million logging on.
-- Nicholas Kulish
---
Five Tales of Prosperity
Over the past decade, a shuttered rubber factory in Akron, Ohio, has
reemerged as an industrial complex, becoming home to a bank, a
computer-graphics company, Internet service providers and changing
many lives.
CANAL PLACE
1990
-- B.F. Goodrich's
rubber plant in downtown Akron, once the world's
largest with some 28,000 workers, has been shut down since 1987.
Several of the buildings have been demolished. The 27 that remain have
been purchased for $2.5 million by a group of investors specializing in
industrial conversions. They name the project after the Ohio & Erie
Canal.
1992
-- JULY: Fast-growing Banc One leases almost all of Building 10 as
part of a local consolidation, investing $4.5 million in improvements
and adding about 300 workers.
-- OCTOBER: Ken and Margarita Roberts move from Los Angeles to take
over management, touting low lease rates and better service.
1993
-- AUGUST: Canal Place sells Building 5 to the city of Akron for an
industrial incubator, uses the proceeds to create a park behind the
main buildings. Complex scores a small coup when the law firm of
Baker, Hardesty & Kaffen takes over the wood-paneled former Goodrich
executive suites, known as `Mahogany Row.'
1994
-- FEBRUARY: Michelin/Uniroyal Goodrich, one of Canal Place's early
tenants, vacates when its lease expires. Canal Place steps up
marketing efforts. The project's annual income drops to about $3
million from $3.8 million in 1993. On-site gas wells are drilled to
help pump up revenues.
1995
-- AUGUST: A major industrial tenant, Advanced Elastomer Systems,
takes space in a former Goodrich building that had been bought by
other investors. Leasing in Canal Place picks up speed.
-- DECEMBER: Rental income recovers to about $3.2 million.
1996
-- FEBRUARY: City of Akron provides substantial covered parking to

--- Page 237 ---
Page 237 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Canal Place.
-- DECEMBER: Canal Place annual income surges to $3.9 million, as
Internet service providers, led by LEK Internet Services, cluster
there.
1997
-- APRIL: City opens its new Canal Park baseball stadium, home to the
minor-league Akron Aeros, a few blocks away. Several new restaurants
and bars follow.
1998
-- DECEMBER: Annual income hits a peak of $5.3 million and occupancy
reaches more than 95%.
1999
-- MAY:
GOJO, a skin-care products maker, starts renovations in
another former Goodrich building across Main Street from Canal Place,
with plans to move in in spring 2000.
-- DECEMBER: Occupancy exceeds 90%, with 106 tenants, annual rental
income of $5.1 million and annual payroll of more than $50 million.
JOHN HOSBACH
A former vacuum salesman becomes a plant manager, takes a rowdy
vacation and juggles debt
1990
-- JANUARY: Living with his parents and working construction for $7
an hour, but his employer often asks him to wait several days before
cashing his check. Switches to another construction job.
1992
-- JANUARY: Begins selling Kirby vacuum cleaners door-to-door and
splurges on a 1988 Chevrolet Beretta, which costs $6,500. He still
drives it (140,000 miles).
-- JUNE: Highpoint as a vacuum salesman: selling six machines in one
week, which translates to about $2,000 in sales commissions or about
20% of his year's earnings.
1993
-- AUGUST: Quits the Kirby job and bounces through a series of
fruitless interviews. His parents ask him to move out and he
eventually moves in with his grandmother.
-- OCTOBER: Interviews at a small factory that makes plastic `lumber'
in Canal Place and is offered a job on the spot. Starting pay is $5.50
an hour, working the night shift from 6 p.m. to 6 a.m.
1994
-- DECEMBER: Having worked his way up to $7.75 an hour, he grosses
more than $17,000 for the year.
The overnight shifts mean he's usually too tired to spend money.
He creates a separate bank account for savings, putting aside $250
from each biweekly paycheck.
1995
-- CHRISTMAS EVE: First formal date with Georgiann Lance, his future
wife. They go tobogganing.
1996
-- WINTER: Switches to working days. Soon thereafter, the factory
converts to 8-hour shifts. He becomes a floor supervisor, which bumps
his hourly pay up to $10.25 by the end of the year.
-- JUNE: Spends $1,000 on a golf vacation to Hilton Head with five
buddies. They get kicked out of two hotels for being rowdy.
1997
-- BIG YEAR: He marries in August; buys a house in October (a 1923
two-story in nearby Barberton, Ohio, for $72,000); takes a salaried
position at the plant in November. Daughter Rebecca is born in
December. He earns nearly $32,000.
1998
-- WINTER: Unaccustomed to having more than a handful of bills as a
single wage earner, he bounces several checks. Georgiann takes over
juggling their finances.
-- DECEMBER: He earns nearly $40,000 for the year, the biggest
one-year jump in pay he's ever seen. But bills are mounting. Their
combined credit card debt alone is $20,000.
1999

after birth for an infection, which leads to some $4,500 in medical

BRAD SACKS
A food entrepreneur turns concentrated veal stock into a gourmet
gold

ENTERPRISE INFORMATION SERVICES
A laid-off Goodrich manager gambles his savings to start a computer
graphics company
1990
-- DECEMBER: Dave Gronauer, a laid-off information-systems specialist
at B.F. Goodrich, founded EIS in 1989 in the same factory building
where he started his career. By the end of its second year, EIS has
hired two employees. Total revenues: almost $200,000.
1991
-- Inspired by a new employee's dabbling in computer diagramming, Mr.
Gronauer makes a concerted effort to market interactive-playbook
software to NFL teams. A year later, EIS drops the idea, one of
several disappointments in high-end computer simulation and animation.
1992

--- Page 239 ---
Page 239 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
-- The company, its print business growing rapidly, branches into

Within three years, that client makes up 75% of EIS's business.
1995
-- MIDYEAR: EIS has grown to 17 employees and $1.9 million in sales.
Mr. Gronauer splurges on a twin-turbo Toyota Supra.
-- DECEMBER: The lift-truck maker abruptly moves its operations to

leaving EIS scrambling and forcing staff reductions. Mr.

-- APRIL: Time Warner, testing what would become the RoadRunner cable

1997
-- MARCH: EIS bids for a contract to provide in-store computer kiosks

Web page instead. That's followed a year later by a contract to do an

1999
-- APRIL: EIS wins a second major client, Akron-based Goodyear, which
wants an improved look and feel for its Web site. EIS also gets the job
of designing a Web page for Goodyear's chemical division.
-- DECEMBER: Rubbermaid has pulled the plug on its e-commerce
operation, leaving EIS with an almost-complete next-generation page
design it now plans to market to other companies. Sales have almost
recovered to their 1995 levels.
BANK ONE
The roaring auto-loan business brings more jobs to the nation's
fourth-largest bank
1992
-- JULY: Columbus, Ohio-based Banc One, growing rapidly in the
Midwest through acquisitions and expansions, leases five of the six
floors of Building 10 for an operations center for its Akron-area
branches. The bank brings 300 new jobs to Canal Place.
1995
-- FEBRUARY: Its stock price lagging, Banc One begins consolidating
operations nationally, leading to several waves of expansions at Canal
Place.
1996
-- SUMMER: Three major departments -- including telephone banking and
customer service -- leave Canal Place as part of the consolidation,
taking 250 jobs. But auto loan collections are consolidated in Canal
Place, adding 175 positions.
1997
-- SUMMER: Management of troubled business loans is added, bringing
22 jobs to Canal Place, eventually growing to 61.
1998
-- FEBRUARY: Banc One, entering the subprime auto lending market,
adds more jobs.
-- APRIL: Banc One merges with First Chicago NBD to form Bank One.
1999
-- JANUARY: Auto loan office adds about 100 jobs at Canal Place.
-- SUMMER: With auto loans booming, Bank One adds 40 more workers to
enter applications data.
-- OCTOBER: Bank One jobs at Canal Place total 610.

Plunging Mercury Has Oil Prices on the Rise
Commodities
Plunging Mercury Has Oil Prices on the Rise
By Matthew F. Gallagher
Dow Jones Newswires
908 words
19 January 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
C23
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Severe cold weather in the U.S. extended crude-oil gains to a sixth straight session at the New
York Mercantile Exchange and, at one point, to $29 a barrel for the first time since December 1990.
Bitter cold is expected to remain in the Northeast U.S., the world's largest heating-oil market, through next
week, leaving some analysts worried the rapid depletion of U.S. heating-oil inventories over the past several
weeks may have left the market vulnerable to shortages.
"The Northeast is having the coldest weather of the last few years," said John Kilduff, senior vice president of
energy-risk management at Fimat USA Inc. "We're seeing heating-oil futures exerting their influence as
seasonal mover."
Signs of strength from heating oil were hard to miss. February heating oil jumped 3.23 cents to 77.04 cents a
gallon, the highest settlement price for the nearby contract since November 1990. The gain came on top of a
4.53-cent rally on Friday, giving the contract an 11% gain in just two sessions.
Heating oil outpaced the 2.68-cent rally in February gasoline to 77.2 cents a gallon, even though the gasoline
market was worried about unconfirmed reports of closed gasoline production at a Venezuelan refinery in
Amuay Bay. For the first time in more than a year, heating-oil prices were at near-parity with gasoline prices.
February heating oil outgained March heating oil by 1.34 cents, expanding February's premium over March to
3.88 cents. Such a large premium gives a sense of the immediate-supply concern in the market, as traders
are willing to lock in heating-oil barrels now at a high price, rather than waiting for less-expensive barrels in
March.
The market also sent signals to refiners that more heating-oil inventory is needed, by expanding the margin
between crude-oil and heating-oil prices. On the basis of a 42-gallon barrel, February heating-oil's gains led
February crude's by more than 50 cents. February crude ended the session at $28.85, with an 83-cent gain.
By the barrel, February heating oil ended at $32.35.
But overall refining margins remain fairly weak, and it's not likely refinery output will rise until there is more
marked improvement in them, traders said.
The heating-oil rally saw little resistance in a market that is already concerned about dwindling crude supply,
as the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries appears likely to extend its output-reduction pacts
beyond a March 2000 phase-out date.
Demand for crude tends to ease in the first half of the year, from strong fourth-quarter demand, but several
analysts said it won't drop enough to offset the continued restriction of OPEC barrels. "I don't see crude
demand falling to the point where we'll start seeing a surplus of oil," said Tim Evans, an energy analyst with
Pegasus Econometrics Group, a New York trading advisor.
Mr. Evans added that the market could be set up for supply squeezes, if refiners fail to build gasoline
inventories in time for the peak driving season in spring and summer. "There's a real potential for this bull
market to perpetuate itself without much additional effort from OPEC," he said.
For this reason, though, OPEC skeptics abound. Some doubt that OPEC will go ahead with a cut extension at
its meeting in March if prices hold at current levels, or they see the group raising production some time in
spring.

--- Page 241 ---
Page 241 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"OPEC doesn't want to stall economic growth in Asia, or force non-OPEC countries to tap petroleum
reserves. And oil prices near $30 will greatly accelerate the pace of stepped-up non-OPEC output, something
the group doesn't want," an analyst said.
For now, though, analysts see momentum carrying oil prices toward $30 a barrel. Many expect a correction
soon, but few said they were willing to go against the market at this point. "I think we would have seen lower
prices for crude today if the heating-oil market hadn't taken off. I think the correction will wait for a return to
above-normal temperatures in the Northeast," said a West Coast oil analyst.
In other commodity markets:
GOLD: Futures rose on mounting inflation fears, position squaring ahead of next week's United Kingdom
gold auction and speculation that miners are buying back hedged gold, traders and analysts said. The
February future on the Comex Division of Nymex leapt $4.70 to $289.60 an ounce. Soaring crude-oil prices
have heightened inflation fears, which could prompt a shift of capital into gold and other precious metals
from equities, analysts said. David Rinehimer, senior futures strategist with Salomon Smith Barney in New
York, said "the price ratio between crude and gold is a little extreme."
SOYBEANS: Futures gained at the Chicago Board of Trade, turning around dramatically from sharp early
losses in a surge of technical buying. The March contract rose six cents to $5.0225 a bushel. Prices fell in
early trading after news of showers in South American producing regions, but speculative commodity funds
then began buying aggressively after the contract fell to what traders identified as key support at $4.90.

Nasdaq Falls As Deal Gets Second Look --- Drop of 128.48 Points Is the Fifth Biggest; Bonds Also
Play a Role
By E.S. Browning
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
872 words
12 January 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Wall Street woke up with a hangover after its big Monday merger celebration.
Tech stocks, which soared Monday on news of America Online's merger with Time Warner, plummeted,
pulling the Nasdaq Composite Index down 128.48 points, or 3.17%, to 3921.19. It was the fifth-biggest
Nasdaq point-loss ever, although not among the top 20 in percentage terms. And continuing the stock
market's volatility in 2000, it was the Nasdaq index's fifth 100-point move this month -- three down and two
up.
Yesterday's stock slump was sparked partly by second thoughts about the merger, and partly by another big
drop in the bond market. The bond slump in turn was set off by a Federal Reserve official's hint that the Fed
may well push interest rates up at its policy meeting at the start of next month.
Other major stock indexes also fell, but less sharply. The Dow Jones Industrial Average gave up 61.12 points,
or 0.53%, to close at 11511.08. The dollar was mixed.
The gray day dumped cold water on hopes that the tech sector's new year's slump was over. On a normal
day last year, such a huge decline in the Nasdaq composite, which is dominated by the leading tech stocks,
would have been considered chilling; after two even bigger declines already this month, and two bigger gains,
it looked like just another sign of tech-stock volatility.
"I call it the yo-yo principle," said Bob Bissell, president of Wells Capital Management, Wells Fargo's
fund-management arm. "To the extent that it can go up, it can go down just as much." Mr. Bissell said he
doesn't think tech stocks have given up market leadership, but "there is going to be a lot more volatility and it
is going to be hard to predict trends off of it."
After driving Time Warner up 39% Monday on the merger news, investors began wondering whether the deal
would be carried off as smoothly as hoped -- not to mention some of the imagined deals that had propelled
other Internet, entertainment and media stocks upward. Investors took profits in some, but not all, of
Monday's big gainers. Yahoo!, Lycos, Viacom and News Corp. all fell. But rumored takeover targets CBS,
Seagram and Walt Disney eked out additional gains. Time Warner gave back one quarter of Monday's gain,
and AOL, which had declined on Monday, fell some more.
The continued bond gloom also took a toll. Interest-rate futures now reflect an expectation that the Fed will
raise its benchmark rates by one-half percentage point soon; bond traders are debating whether it will come
all in February, or in two steps, in February and March.
"The question has turned from `will the Fed tighten on Feb. 2,' to `how much will the Fed tighten on Feb. 2,'"
said Bill Hornbarger, bond strategist at A.G. Edwards in St. Louis.
Bond investors were spooked by a Monday evening speech by Richmond Fed president J. Alfred Broaddus,
a voting member of the Fed's policy committee. Mr. Broaddus stressed the risk of an overheating economy
and suggested that the best response is to raise rates ahead of actual signs of inflation, to prevent it from
occurring.
Rising prices of oil, gold and other commodities also boosted inflation fears. Continuing its relentless
decline, the bellwether 30-year Treasury bond fell 1 3/32, or $10.9375 for a $1,000 bond. That pushed up
market interest rates. The bond's yield, which moves inversely to price, rose to 6.672%, once again marking
its highest level since September 1997.

--- Page 243 ---
Page 243 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Another source of worry was the anticipation, during the normal trading day, of the after-hours earnings
announcement from Internet blue chip Yahoo. As was feared, Yahoo's profit came in slightly lower than the
most optimistic "whisper" numbers; the stock initially fell in after-hours trading but then traded slightly above
its regular 4 p.m. close.
Worry during the trading day about the coming Yahoo earnings helped push down the 40-stock Dow Jones
Internet Index by 23.37 points in regular trading to 389.00, more than erasing Monday's gain of 22.44.
But in another sign of tech-stock volatility, Intel bucked the market trend and closed at a record high after an
analyst upgraded it.
The average share of stock listed on the New York Stock Exchange fell 40 cents, or 0.86%, to $46.11.
World-wide, stocks fell in dollar terms. Excluding U.S. stocks, the Dow Jones World Stock Index rose 1.72
points to 188.27.
In major market action:
Stocks and bonds sagged. On the Big Board, where 993.7 million shares traded, 994 stocks advanced and
2,109 declined.
The dollar was mixed. Late in New York, it traded at $1.0333 to the euro and 106.06 yen to the dollar,
weakening from $1.0258 and strengthening from 105.19 yen.

Ecuador's Leader Scrambles to Fill a Credibility Gap---Shift to U.S. DollarIs Bold, but May BeToo Late to Help

Ecuador's Leader Scrambles to Fill a Credibility Gap --- Shift to U.S. Dollar Is Bold, but May Be Too
Late to Help
By Thomas T. Vogel Jr.
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
967 words
12 January 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
A16
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
The success or failure of Ecuadorean President Jamil Mahuad's push to dollarize his country's economy
depends less on the technical details than on his ability to do something he has failed to do since taking office
in August 1998: gain credibility.
Late Monday evening, Ecuador's Central Bank approved Mr. Mahuad's proposal to replace Ecuador's
troubled currency, the sucre, with the U.S. dollar. The president of Ecuador's Central Bank, Pablo Better,
resigned in protest. Mr. Better was the only member of the Central Bank's board to oppose the measure.
As Mr. Mahuad has struggled to deal with such multiple crises as widespread bank failures, a deep
recession, foreign-debt defaults, rising inflation and unemployment, he has been dogged by criticism for
acting too slowly and too late. His proposal to dollarize Ecuador's economy stands out as the boldest move
he has made so far as president. The risk, say critics, is that Ecuadoreans and investors have lost so much
faith in the beleaguered president that even this measure may not be enough to save his presidency. Mr.
Mahuad's move puts Ecuador at the center of a growing and controversial movement in the developing world
toward dollarization or so-called currency boards as pressures on weak currencies push countries to link
them to stronger currencies. Some economists have expressed concern over the potential dangers of
developing nations sacrificing control over monetary policy to the central banks of stronger, industrialized
nations, whose economies have little in common with poor countries like Ecuador.
Mr. Mahuad's proposal to dollarize is meant to end the currency crisis and silence growing calls for his
resignation. Last week, the president declared a state of emergency to keep protests against his government
from getting out of control. Yesterday, there were announcements of a new wave of protests by leaders of
Ecuador's indigenous groups who were angered by the dollarization move.
"It's not such a bad idea," says Michael Henry, Latin America economist at ING Baring. But Mr. Mahuad "has
to build some credibility and break the cycle of low expectations" that has plagued his government almost
from the start. "If this is enough to shock Ecuadorean politicians and get them to make the necessary
changes" in how the country's economy is run, "then it's a good idea."
The sucre dropped about 20% in value last week against the U.S. dollar as panic about Ecuador's economic
and political future sparked a frenzy of sucre exchanges for dollars by Ecuadoreans. Ecuador devalued the
sucre three times in 1998 and early 1999 before floating the currency last February. But the currency dropped
more than 70% over the past 12 months despite the measure. In late trading in New York yesterday, the
sucre was quoted at 24,825 to the dollar, 0.3% weaker than Monday's level of 24,750 sucres to the dollar.
Traders and investors are reluctant to participate in the foreign exchange market for the sucre because of
confusion over how dollarization will be implemented.
Yesterday, there were some signs of confidence in Mr. Mahuad's proposal. Investors in Ecuador's defaulted
dollar-denominated international bonds, for example, haven't rushed to unload their holdings. Prices have
remained fairly stable since Mr. Mahuad's dollarization announcement late Sunday evening. A poll published
yesterday by Ecuadorean newspaper El Diario Hoy showed that 58% of the 640 people interviewed in
Ecuador's biggest cities of Quito and Guayaquil supported the dollarization proposal.
What's more, Ecuador's largest political party, the Social Christian Party, or PSC, which has opposed Mr.
Mahuad, announced it would support dollarization. PSC had proposed dollarization last March but Mr.
Mahuad and his supporters opposed the idea at the time. PSC's support will be key to pushing a laundry list
of legal measures through Ecuador's Congress to implement dollarization.

--- Page 245 ---
Page 245 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
But there remain many questions about how Mr. Mahuad intends to implement dollarization. The president
and other government officials have offered few details about how the move will affect the servicing of some
$15 billion in domestic and foreign-debt obligations, for example.
Ecuador has about $1.2 billion in foreign reserves but only about $850 million of that is readily available to
cover the sucre at the exchange rate set by the president of 25,000 sucres per dollar. There is about $540
million of so-called base money in circulation in Ecuador, ING Baring's Mr. Henry estimates. But if long-term
bank deposits are included, the amount of sucres to be backed by the government jumps to about $2.9
billion. The shortfall means that Ecuador will be forced to restructure deposits and perhaps force depositors,
many of them influential and wealthy Ecuadoreans, to accept losses.
Mr. Mahuad has said that Ecuadoreans will be able to withdraw about $4,000 from certain bank accounts by
March and the rest of their deposits will remain frozen. Savers looking to cash out will be given long-term
government bonds instead, which can be traded for cash in what the government hopes will be a liquid
market, said government officials.

EUROPE/AFRICA
National Power
Of U.K. Will Expand
Its U.S. Capacity
Britain's National Power said it will invest $600 million in two gas-fired power plants in Texas and
Massachusetts that will increase the company's overall U.S. investment to $1.9 billion. The investment will be
used to build a second power station at Midlothian, Texas -- alongside the plant that will begin operation this
summer -- and a plant in Bellingham, Mass. After completion of the new projects in late 2001, National Power
will have total U.S. generating capacity of about 4,000 megawatts, it said. The projects will be managed by
the company's American National Power unit and will be funded entirely by National Power.
The company's plants now being developed at Blackstone, Mass., and San Marcos, Texas, are scheduled to
begin operating by the summer of 2001.
Harmony Gold Bids for Randfontein
South Africa's Harmony Gold Mining is bidding to snatch Randfontein Estates from the jaws of fellow gold
miner Western Areas. If successful, the move would nearly double Harmony's annual gold output to 2.2
million ounces. Harmony's offer of a share swap and a cash alternative valued at 780 million rand ($128.6
million) represents a 36% premium to Western Areas' bid last month. Harmony's chief executive, Bernard
Swanepoel, said its acquisition drive isn't over. Pointing to a chart ranking Harmony third among South
African gold miners by cost efficiency, he said any of the mines with higher costs could be targets, including
the Freegold mines of Anglogold.
Nike Probes Soccer-Shirt Allegations
U.S. athletic-gear company Nike said it is investigating allegations that its jerseys made for the popular
German soccer club Borussia Dortmund may contain a toxic chemical. German department stores didn't wait
for the results and pulled the shirts off shelves. The actions followed a report by a German public- television
show that said a laboratory it commissioned had found the jerseys to contain tributyltin, or TBT, a
heavy-metal compound used in ship paint. TBT is also used to kill bacteria and quell the smell of sweat. But
British and Dutch tests have shown TBT can cause mutations in marine snails, and the World Wildlife Fund
has called TBT to be banned. Nike officials in Beaverton, Ore., promised a speedy investigation. "We're doing
independent tests on our end to ensure there is no risk," a spokesman said. "As of right now, there's no
legitimate reason to believe there's any risk."
Freeserve Turns in Upbeat Results
Britain's Freeserve, which connects customers to the Internet for the cost of a phone call, said its subscriber
base rose 12% to 1.6 million during the 12 weeks ended Nov. 13; by Jan. 2, it had added 100,000 more
subscribers. Revenue during the fiscal second quarter rose to GBP 3.8 million ($6.2 million), compared with
GBP 3.4 million in the 16-week fiscal first quarter. The company's operating loss before exceptional items
narrowed to GBP 4.8 million from GBP 5.2 million in the first quarter.

-- France's food-safety agency recommended a ban on the sale of shellfish, salt and seaweed from coasts in
western France most affected by oil spilled from the tanker Erika, which sank Dec. 12.

Sony Shares Fall After News Report
Shares of Japan's Sony fell 7.2% in Tokyo trading following a news-service report that the president of the

GE Forms Nuclear-Fuel Joint Venture
General Electric of the U.S. and Japan's Hitachi and Toshiba said they completed establishment of a joint
venture to combine the companies' nuclear-fuel operations. The venture, announced last April, integrates the
companies' nuclear-fuel marketing, engineering, design, development and manufacturing operations into one
company. The joint venture, called Global Nuclear Fuel, is based in San Jose, Calif., and will have annual
sales of 50 billion yen ($479 million), the companies said. GE owns a 51% stake in the venture, while Hitachi
and Toshiba each hold 24.5% of the company.
Ford to Conduct Daewoo Due Diligence
Korea Development Bank said Ford Motor of the U.S. plans to send a team next week to conduct due

Motor, Korea Development Bank said. General Motors has already submitted a proposal to the Korean
government and Daewoo Motor's creditors to purchase all of the ailing company's domestic operations and
many of its overseas assets.

system appeared to be manageable.
Venezuela Maintains Currency Band
Venezuela's Central Bank said it will maintain the foreign-exchange band guiding the convertibility rate for
the country's currency, the bolivar. The bank said its policy was guided "by the objectives of price stability and
external equilibrium of the economy." Under the band system, the dollar is allowed to fluctuate within 7.5% of
either side of the central parity level, which slides at a monthly pace of 1.28%. The Finance Ministry's
research unit has estimated the bolivar is overvalued by nearly 30%, while some private economists figure it
may be overvalued by 50%. Economists note that inflation still outpaces the rate of devaluation of the
bolivar.

Farmers WinCourt BattleVs. Miners
THE WALL STREET JOURNAL / CALIFORNIA
Farmers Win Court Battle Vs. Miners
By Marc Lifsher
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,082 words
5 January 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
CA1
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
[In celebration of California's 150th anniversary as a state, the following article is based on source material
from the mid-1800's]
NORTH BLOOMFIELD, Jan. 7, 1884 -- A federal judge in San Francisco yesterday handed California
agriculture a major victory over the state's mining industry.
U.S. Ninth Circuit Judge Lorenzo Sawyer, while acknowledging the "disastrous consequences" his order
would have on miners, nonetheless ordered them to stop dumping debris into waterways -- a practice that
farmers say has ruined their business by causing floods and mayhem downstream from the mineral
extraction installations.
Miners here at the world-renowned Malakoff Diggins assailed the decision, which they say will force them to
abandon modern hydraulic extraction methods -- and will make unprofitable the industry that made California
great, gold mining.
As the miners' attorney, Jason K. Byrne, argued during the trial: "Never in the history of this state has an
injunction been asked for which . . . would be so disastrous in its effects and so far-reaching in its
consequences." He predicted the court order would lead to "widespread ruin and disaster" for the Mother
Lode, which has been "one of the most populated, enterprising and prosperous sections of the state."
Already, after decades of round-the-clock labor, the nine-inch nozzles used here have stopped pulverizing the
steep cliffs with jets of super-high-pressure water fed by a vast mountain system of reservoirs, dams, flumes
and tunnels. Armies of miners, their supervisors and the San Francisco investors who bankrolled them have
been stalking through this ramshackle village, drunk and bitter. In nearby Nevada City, the hub of the Mother
Lode, dozens of normally lively saloons have taken on the atmosphere of funeral parlors.
And these dark days may be only the beginning, if Mr. Byrne's trial arguments prove prescient. The end of
hydraulic mining, Mr. Byrne warned, will "depopulate villages, towns and cities, and cause schools and
churches to go to ruin and decay and even cause the disorganization of county governments."
Down in Sacramento Valley farming communities, however, residents are taking to heart the message from
their attorney, George Cadwalader, who telegrammed sup
George Cadwalader, who telegrammed supporters: "You can have a big time. Success attends us at every
point."
At Marysville, the commercial-farming town at the confluence of the Yuba and Feather rivers, massive
bonfires illuminated the night, while fireworks exploded and bands paraded around the courthouse square. In
Sacramento -- a farm town as well as the state capital -- revelers have been firing cannons and toasting the
health of Judge Sawyer, an original forty-niner who panned his share of gold before studying for the bar.
"That the practice of hydraulic mining produces a nuisance of the most insufferable kind is incontrovertible,"
says Mr. Cadwalader. "What does it do to civilization? It drives it away. What does it propose to do in this
state? To devastate it. . . in the name of profit."
The townsfolk and farmers say the ruling will end the wrath and natural spoilation annually visited upon them
by the hydraulic miners at Malakoff and dozens of other mountain churning sites throughout the Sierra
Nevada foothills. Judge Sawyer, reading his 255-page decision from the bench, rejected the miners'
insistence that they have a historic right to dump millions of tons of debris, called tailings or "slickens," into
the Sierra's creeks and riverbeds. The mass of rock, mud and boulders has caused horrendous floods down
below, covering thousands of acres of valued agricultural bottomlands with thick concrete-like crusts,

--- Page 250 ---
Page 250 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
hampering crucial steamboat navigation, and destroying countless homes and commercial properties, the
judge found.
The judge, ruling on a lawsuit filed more than two years ago, Edwards Woodruff vs. North Bloomfield Gravel
Mining Co., gave the agriculturalists a victory they had sought for more than 10 years -- something that
neither the state courts nor the Legislature would deliver in the face of fierce opposition from the mining
industry.
In reading his opinion, the judge noted the widespread devastation he had personally witnessed on inspection
tours and contemplated upon in 20,000 pages of testimony. He particularly emphasized the damage suffered
to property in Marysville during the massive flood of 1875. Such acts, he said, "constitute a grievous and
far-reaching public nuisance." And so, "with a painfully anxious appreciation of the responsibilities resting
upon us, and of the disastrous consequences to the defendants, we can come to no other conclusion than
that the complainant is entitled to a perpetual injunction," Judge Sawyer said.
Though he did not ban hydraulic mining outright, Judge Sawyer's ruling seems likely to have that effect
because it prohibits the miners from dumping mountainsides full of gravel, the byproduct of massive blasting
and sluicing, in watercourses. For example, Malakoff poured 41 million cubic yards of gravel in Humbug
Creek between 1866 and this year. That massive earth moving yielded one part gold per every 12 million
parts gravel, or approximately $3.5 million of gold at an average price of $17 an ounce. Those tailings quickly
filled up the Humbug bed, and with spring runoff, roared down the Yuba River to the Feather and Sacramento
rivers.
During periodic floods, which flatlanders say have been arriving with increasing frequency, the debris easily
smashes through protective levees surrounding valley towns, entombs farms and orchards and creates
dangerous bars that infuriate steamboaters on the state's most important fluvial artery, the Sacramento. As
Capt. John R. Farris, reputedly the oldest pilot on the Sacramento River, describes it, the shifting bars to
navigation "are the whole of the river, from side to side and from top to bottom, so that steamers have great
trouble in getting over them, if they succeed in so doing."
The question remains whether the judge's order will be enough to stop hydraulic mining -- or whether miners
will contest the ruling or simply ignore it.
And in the meantime, the decision only promises to heighten the antipathy between these two crucially
important sectors of the state's economy.
Miners, for instance, have been accusing Marysville merchants of turning against them. In court arguments,
Mr. Byrne derided the merchants for "growing rich and waxing fat upon the pecuniary harvest which they
were reaping from the toil of the honest gold miner." Meanwhile, Mr. Cadwalader countered that miners have
made their water "unfit for any use" and their lands "untillable."

Gold Futures and Crude-Oil Prices Finish Lower
Commodities
Gold Futures and Crude-Oil Prices Finish Lower
By Peter A. McKay
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
673 words
5 January 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
C21
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Gold futures tumbled in the New York Mercantile Exchange's first session this millennium,
and crude oil was also down sharply before a late recovery.
Analysts and traders pegged much of the sell-off to the world's relatively smooth year-2000 rollover, lacking
any computer-driven monetary crisis or major transportation slowdown. Gold fell $5.90 to close at $283.70 an
ounce on Nymex's Comex division.
Crude-oil futures finished five cents lower, at $25.55 a barrel, after falling as much as 89 cents during the
session on New Year's jitters. In the end, natural gas took the energy complex's biggest hit, falling 15.3 cents,
or 6.6%, to $2.176 per million British thermal units.
Warm Northeastern weather, higher interest rates and the possibility of increased oil production by the
Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries also helped nudge the contracts lower, analysts said. But it
was the pre-January hoarding of benchmark commodities -- or in gold's case, the perception of it -- that
dominated traders' attention. Even with the day's losses, commodity prices overall remain well above
year-earlier levels, and analysts said they could yet go higher if worries of inflation and an end to the bull
stock market prove true. "There was definitely fear of non-Y2K compliance in producing countries that just
didn't pan out," said Fimat USA Inc. energy analyst John Kilduff of yesterday's oil trading. "That took some of
the fear premium out of the market, and it was the same for gold."
The declines came one week after the federal Energy Information Administration estimated that 17 million
barrels of crude oil and refined products had been stockpiled in the U.S.
For gold, analysts said the evidence that consumers had been squirreling away metal was sketchier, given
light trading last week on Comex. Yet the metal's reputation as a hedge against emergencies still led it lower
when no calamity emerged over the weekend.
"Yes, Y2K explains the psychology behind the market today, but I don't know how much that's supported by
the fundamentals," said gold analyst Matthew Ford, of U.S. Global Investors. "If there was no buying last
week, it would be hard to use Y2K to reasonably justify the selling now."
Unlike most U.S. commodity exchanges, the Nymex closed both Friday and Monday as a precaution against
computer problems when the date changed from 1999 to 2000.
The later opening meant that by the time Nymex trading resumed yesterday, members had witnessed not
only a New Year's weekend free of major "Y2K bug" mishaps, but also an uneventful first business day in the
U.S. and the reopening of foreign exchanges that had waited the extra day.
The oil market also appeared to be bracing for bearish OPEC data. Last week, a report from a
Washington-based oil consultant put OPEC compliance with output reduction pacts at around 70%.
Traders are worried that other OPEC surveys due this week will show a similar slide in compliance. Over the
past several months, the group's compliance has been between 80% and 90%.
"With Y2K out of the way, the market will be looking for more fundamental news like the OPEC numbers to
really make a big move," Mr. Kilduff said.
Regardless of what short-term change it makes up or down, crude oil's recent ascent has been dramatic, its
price more than doubling since the beginning of 1999.

--- Page 252 ---
Page 252 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Not so with gold. The metal sank to 20-year lows near $250 an ounce in the spring, then shot above $320 in
the summer before leveling off at year's end to close only 40 cents higher on the year.
Analysts said that has left a murkier picture than when they seemed to be almost unanimously bearish near
gold's dramatic lows, or bullish during its extreme highs.

Greenspan Re-ups
REVIEW & OUTLOOK (Editorial)
Greenspan Re-ups
870 words
5 January 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
A22
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Maybe Alan Greenspan isn't God after all. The Federal Reserve chairman was reappointed yesterday and the
financial markets that supposedly think he walks on water promptly staged one of their largest selloffs ever,
the Dow falling by 359 points, the invincible Nasdaq by 229.75, its largest one-day point slide. Maybe there's
a lesson or two in here somewhere.
Markets closed the millennium at extraordinary heights, of course, and a retreat is in itself no great cause for
alarm. In the longer run the Greenspan appointment is reassuring, if only by foreclosing President Clinton
from bandying the appointment around as this campaign's Lincoln bedroom. By reappointing Mr. Greenspan
six months before his third term expires, Mr. Clinton is wrapping himself and his Vice President in the
Greenspan legacy. Mr. Clinton is pulling out all the stops to elect Mr. Gore, against both the insurgent Bradley
campaign and the gathering Republicans. One such effort is to attach the Vice President to today's
prosperity; yesterday Mr. Gore all but nominated Mr. Greenspan for sainthood while endorsing his
reappointment, an attempt at virtue by association.
Mr. Greenspan indeed has an estimable record, one we've repeatedly commended. The Fed boss's
commitment to price stability over the past 12 years is a large reason for the 1990s' run of prosperity. Not all
that long ago there were still politicians who listened to economists who thought inflation had its benefits. In
addition to monetary stability, we've also just run an experiment in what kind of economy results when a
compulsively intrusive Presidency is checked and thwarted from doing anything significant, such as
HillaryCare, for eight years. Quod erat demonstrandum: When Washington spins its wheels, the economy
roars forward.
For all of that, yesterday's plunge was strikingly sharp, and it's pretty clear that lately markets have begun to
wonder what's driving the invisible head behind Mr. Greenspan's visible monetary hand. To judge by its public
explanations, the Fed now seems bent upon raising interest rates, but for the odd reason that the country is
too prosperous. In raising rates last November (for the third time last year), the Fed explained that "the
expansion of activity continues in excess of the economy's growth potential." This hardly sounds like a
concern over rising prices. Instead it sounds suspiciously like a Greenspan version of the old, discredited
Phillips Curve, which held that too many people working causes inflation.
It is after all hard to detect any signs of inflation, past or future. The dollar price of gold is back down around
$285, about where it was a year ago and well off its 1999 high near $325. Other commodity prices also seem
in check, with the exception of oil, which can be explained by OPEC's success in controlling production. The
30-year bond has been creeping higher for the past year, but not unusually so during such a long expansion.
The Fed deferred another rate increase in December, but mainly, it said, to provide enough liquidity to ensure
"a smooth transition into the Year 2000."
The millenium having arrived without the Apocalypse, or even any bank runs, markets can be forgiven if they
now conclude that the Greenspan Fed will once again return to raising rates. So better sell equities. All the
more so once the chairman has been safely reappointed and can do as he pleases.
Clearly Mr. Gore has no interest in further Greenspan rate increases, though their full economic impact
probably wouldn't hit until after November. But the veep does have a political need to deflect any Republican
tax cut this year, especially with George W. Bush having put one on the table. Without suggesting any quid
pro quo, we doubt it was lost on the Clinton-Gore team that last year the Fed chairman publicly groused
about Congress's tax cut. We predict Mr. Gore will be quoting Mr. Greenspan before the year is out to bash
Mr. Bush on taxes. This means even less chance for growth-producing fiscal policy in the next year.
Our point here isn't to grumble about the Greenspan regency. Paul Volcker aside, we can't think of anyone
better to run monetary policy from his hip pocket. But that's what he does, no more evidently so than in the
past year. Having dismissed the Phillips Curve many times himself, Mr. Greenspan now seems to be invoking
it in so many words to justify higher rates. No wonder markets are so jittery.

--- Page 254 ---
Page 254 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Mr. Greenspan, we should add, is also 73 years old. The Greenspan legacy would be much enhanced if he
could in his fourth term as chairman do more to publicly explain and institutionalize the Fed's
inflation-fighting obligation. In particular this would mean speaking up in favor of Florida Senator Connie
Mack's legislation making price stability the Fed's statutory policy goal.
Mr. Greenspan seems as vital as ever, but not even Fed chairmen live forever. A Fed chairman endorsed by
both George W. Bush and Al Gore has the political capital to do more than govern by the seat of his pants.

Year-End Review of Markets and Finance 1999---What Was News:The Events That Shaped End of the Millennium
Year-End Review of Markets and Finance 1999 --- What Was News: The Events That Shaped End of
the Millennium
Compiled by Sheila Dougherty, Robin Frost and Stephen Schurr
11,694 words
3 January 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
R12
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
For markets world-wide, 1999 was the year of rocketing high-tech stocks and initial public offerings, as
interest in the Internet and telecommunications soared.
It was the year of megamergers, the euro's launch and Asia's recovery from financial crisis. Politically, as
President Clinton's impeachment trial ended in acquittal, the focus quickly shifted to the next presidential
race.
Telecommunications deals fueled the high-tech surge, including a huge hostile bid by Britain's Vodafone for
Germany's Mannesmann. Though high-tech IPOs generally were the hottest, United Parcel Service's was the
biggest.
The courts were busy with a federal judge saying Microsoft was a predatory monopolist, and a federal lawsuit
accusing the tobacco industry of deception.
Inflation remained under control; barriers separating banking and securities firms were lifted, and major U.S.
markets began extended trading.
Overseas, Washington and Beijing signed a deal that could make China a WTO member, even though there
were accusations China stole arms technology. The IMF approved loans to Russia, while investigations were
launched into suspected Russian money laundering. And on the last day of the year, President Boris Yeltsin
unexpectedly quit.
Here's a summary of the news events chronicled on the front page of The Wall Street Journal. The dates
reflect when the items were published and include events of Dec. 31.
JANUARY
4
The euro is launched. European monetary union represents the first postwar challenge to the U.S. dollar's
dominance of international trade and finance.
A big Midwestern snowstorm snarls air and road travel with drifts as high as two feet. Frigid weather is
blamed for more than 90 deaths.
The U.S. economy is expected to grow at a slower pace in 1999 than last year, according to most economists
participating in a semiannual survey. Global uncertainties are making forecasting trickier, economists say.
5
Vodafone offers to acquire AirTouch, hoping to break up AirTouch's merger talks with Bell Atlantic. Before
month's end, AirTouch agrees to be bought by Vodafone for $58 billion in stock and cash, creating the world's
biggest wireless-communications company.
Clinton sets plans to allow a wider range of contact between Cubans and Americans while rejecting calls for a
broader review of Cuba policy. An aide stresses Clinton wants to show support for the people of Cuba, not
weaken the 37-year-old embargo against the Communist island. As a centerpiece, the Baltimore Orioles
agree to play a Cuban exhibition game.
7
China received data in the 1980s on the most advanced U.S. nuclear warhead. U.S. officials say the top
suspect is a Department of Energy scientist who has been removed from sensitive projects.

--- Page 256 ---
Page 256 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
U.S. arms inspectors employed an eavesdropping device that automatically sent signals from Saddam
Hussein's communications network to the National Security Agency via satellites, officials say. The spy
agency decoded the encrypted data, then returned some of it to the inspectors. The U.S. used some
information in planning recent airstrikes.
The NBA and its players settle a six-month-old lockout. Days later, five-time MVP Michael Jordan retires. A
truncated season will begin in February.
8
The Senate is sworn in as the jury for the impeachment trial of Clinton. Chief Justice Rehnquist administers
the oath in the first trial of a president in 130 years.
Abby Joseph Cohen, a Goldman Sachs market strategist noted for her enthusiasm for stocks, slightly cuts
her recommended stock position for the first time in almost four years.
13
Testimony against Microsoft draws to a close after nearly 12 weeks of trial, with antitrust chief Joel Klein
saying the U.S. presented clear evidence of "predation, exclusion and coercion."
Lucent agrees to acquire Ascend, paying more than $19 billion in stock in hopes of dominating the next
generation of telecom networks.
14
Brazil devalues its currency, the real, and its central bank head quits, raising worries about renewed global
financial turmoil.
18
MarketWatch.com jumps 474% in one of the largest one-day gains ever for an initial public offering.
20
The president delivers his State of the Union address amid his impeachment trial in the Senate, offering his
most ambitious agenda since the GOP won Congress. He proposes to invest some Social Security funds in
the stock market and to create individual savings accounts.
25
Welfare rolls have fallen to their lowest level in 30 years, Clinton announces. About eight million people were
on welfare as of September, down 44% from 14.3 million in 1994.
26
The Colombia earthquake death toll rises to nearly 1,000, in the worst such disaster in the nation in more
than a century.
27
U.S. jets over northern Iraq again fire on air-defense sites as Clinton's national security adviser acknowledges
the president approved more-aggressive rules of engagement after last month's four-day bombing campaign.
Meanwhile, the Pentagon confirms an errant U.S. missile struck near Basra Monday. Iraq says 11 civilians
died.
29
Ford agrees to pay $6.47 billion for the automotive business of Sweden's Volvo. The deal fills a gap in the
U.S. auto maker's product line and leaves Volvo with funds for expanding its remaining commercial-vehicle
business.
FEBRUARY
1
Monica Lewinsky begins her deposition for Clinton's Senate trial. House prosecutors say they hope to use her
testimony, plus that of Vernon Jordan and Sidney Blumenthal, to bolster their obstruction-of-justice charge.
2

--- Page 257 ---
Page 257 of 277 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Clinton presents a $1.766 trillion budget, spurning Republican calls for big tax cuts. He proposes raising taxes
on business and investors by $78.4 billion over the next five years.
France's Paribas and Societe Generale announce plans to merge in a $17 billion stock deal, creating
Europe's second-largest bank.
3
Online abortion foes are ordered by a federal jury in Oregon to pay $107 million for operating a Web site that
put abortion doctors on "wanted" posters and crossed out their names when they were killed. The ruling has
constitutional implications because the site carried no explicit threats.
8
King Hussein dies, putting his eldest son, Abdullah, on the throne of Jordan. Hussein survived assassination
attempts, revolts and Mideast wars to die of cancer at age 63 after a 45-year reign in the Hashemite kingdom.
9
USA Networks will merge its cable-television Home Shopping Network unit with Internet portal Lycos,
creating an electronic-commerce giant with ties to an entertainment empire.
10
A Utah Olympics inquiry finds the campaign by state officials seeking to land the 2002 Games for Salt Lake
City included improper, but not criminal, dealings with more than one-fifth of the 114 International Olympic
Committee members.
11
American Airlines pilots are ordered back to work by a federal judge, but the move fails to prevent travel
disruptions over the Presidents Day holiday weekend.
Philip Morris is assessed $50 million in punitive damages stemming from a suit by an ex-smoker with lung
cancer. A day earlier, the San Francisco jury awarded the plaintiff $1.5 million in compensatory damages.
The Nasdaq composite soars 96.05 points, or 4.16%, to 2405.55, its biggest point move to date and its
ninth-best in percentage terms. Fed Chairman Greenspan helped spark the rally with positive comments on
banking reform and praise for the economy's strength.
12
Clinton's impeachment trial in the Senate, the first such trial since 1868, ends in acquittal by votes of 55-45
against the perjury and 50-50 against the obstruction-of-justice charge.
19
Aegon agrees to pay $9.7 billion in stock and cash for life insurer Transamerica, the newest deal in a wave of
insurance-industry consolidation. The Dutch insurer's purchase also marks the latest European acquisition of
a well-known U.S. company.
22
Olivetti launches a hostile $58 billion bid for its larger rival, Telecom Italia.
The trade gap widened to $168.6 billion, the U.S.'s worst trade performance ever, in 1998.
24
The Texas dragging-death jury convicts John William King of murdering a black man, James Byrd Jr., by
chaining him behind a racing pickup truck. King may face execution. Two others await trial.
Many Boeing investments will yield little, according to a study by the aerospace giant that will pave the way
for a financial overhaul of the company. The findings show that one-fourth of the $13 billion that Boeing is
investing in its aircraft, defense, space and service businesses will produce no meaningful returns.

1

Gen. Olusegun Obasanjo, a former military ruler, wins Nigeria's presidency in a disputed election. He returns
to power with 63% of the vote.

The U.S. mounts the most extensive raids on Iraqi targets since mid-December. More than 30 laser-guided
bombs are dropped on 12 sites around Mosul, in the northern zone from which Iraqi military flights are

Hewlett-Packard, bidding to regain its footing in the computing business, plans to spin off its $7.6

Stanley Kubrick, visionary director of such films as "A Clockwork Orange" and "2001: A Space Odyssey," dies
at 70 in Hertfordshire, England.

Intel agrees to settle FTC charges that it used monopoly power to bully customers with which it had patent
disputes. With the move, the computer-chip maker avoids a trial that was about to begin.
Joe DiMaggio, baseball great who epitomized grace and sportsmanship in a 13-season New York Yankees
career, dies of lung cancer at 84 in Hollywood, Fla.
10
RJR Nabisco agrees to sell its overseas cigarette unit to Japan Tobacco for $7.8 billion and plans to spin off
its Reynolds U.S. cigarette business. The restructuring plan closes a chapter on one of the glitziest trophy
deals of the 1980s, the $25 billion auction for RJR Nabisco.
Banque Nationale de Paris has launched a bid valued at an estimated $37 billion for Societe Generale and
Paribas, seeking to thwart the planned merger of the rival French banks.

Fleet sets deal to buy BankBoston for $16 billion in stock, in a move that would create a New England
powerhouse and the eighth-largest U.S. bank.
The U.S. prison population hit a record 1.8 million last year, having doubled since 1985, the Justice
Department says.
16
The 20-member EU Commission resigns after coming in for scathing criticism in an independent panel's
report that accuses the executive body of cronyism and financial bungling of the EU's $100 billion budget.

The Dow Jones industrials shoot above 10000 for the first time, but fall back as computer-driven sell

Microsoft plans to begin settlement talks with the Justice Department and 19 states, seeking to end the
antitrust trial that has shed a harsh light on its business practices.

NATO begins bombing Serbia with cruise missiles and planes, including B-2s. Targets include military sites

Amazon.com sets plans to enter the online-auction market, widening its selling repertoire and competing with

stands at more than 300% above its level of October 1990, reflecting both a long period of economic health

A computer virus called Melissa doesn't cause serious damage, but it spreads considerable alarm quickly,
and security experts warn of future threats.

Philip Morris is hit with a record $80.3 million in damages by a state-court jury in Portland, Ore., in a case
